The scheme was jotted down in 1890, from notes made in 1887\r\nand onward, some of the circumstances being suggested by the death of a\r\nwoman in the former year. The scenes were revisited in October, 1892;\r\nthe narrative was written in outline in 1892 and the spring of 1893,\r\nand at full length, as it now appears, from August, 1893, onward into\r\nthe next year; the whole, with the exception of a few chapters, being\r\nin the hands of the publisher by the end of 1894. It was begun as a\r\nserial story in HARPER\xe2\x80\x99S MAGAZINE at the end of November, 1894, and was\r\ncontinued in monthly parts.\r\n\r\nBut, as in the case of _Tess of the D\xe2\x80\x99Urbervilles_, the magazine\r\nversion was, for various reasons, abridged and modified in some degree,\r\nthe present edition being the first in which the whole appears as\r\noriginally written. And in the difficulty of coming to an early\r\ndecision in the matter of a title, the tale was issued under a\r\nprovisional name\xe2\x80\x94two such titles having, in fact, been successively\r\nadopted. The present and final title, deemed on the whole the best, was\r\none of the earliest thought of.\r\n\r\nFor a novel addressed by a man to men and women of full age, which\r\nattempts to deal unaffectedly with the fret and fever, derision and\r\ndisaster, that may press in the wake of the strongest passion known to\r\nhumanity, and to point, without a mincing of words, the tragedy of\r\nunfulfilled aims, I am not aware that there is anything in the handling\r\nto which exception can be taken.\r\n\r\nLike former productions of this pen, _Jude the Obscure_ is simply an\r\nendeavor to give shape and coherence to a series of seemings, or\r\npersonal impressions, the question of their consistency or their\r\ndiscordance, of their permanence or their transitoriness, being\r\nregarded as not of the first moment.\r\n\r\nT.H.\r\n\r\n\r\n_August_, 1895.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart First AT MARYGREEN\r\n\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cYea, many there be that have run out of their wits for women, and\r\nbecome servants for their sakes. Many also have perished, have erred,\r\nand sinned, for women\xe2\x80\xa6 O ye men, how can it be but women should be\r\nstrong, seeing they do thus?\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94ESDRAS.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe schoolmaster was leaving the village, and everybody seemed sorry.\r\nThe miller at Cresscombe lent him the small white tilted cart and horse\r\nto carry his goods to the city of his destination, about twenty miles\r\noff, such a vehicle proving of quite sufficient size for the departing\r\nteacher\xe2\x80\x99s effects. For the schoolhouse had been partly furnished by the\r\nmanagers, and the only cumbersome article possessed by the master, in\r\naddition to the packing-case of books, was a cottage piano that he had\r\nbought at an auction during the year in which he thought of learning\r\ninstrumental music. But the enthusiasm having waned he had never\r\nacquired any skill in playing, and the purchased article had been a\r\nperpetual trouble to him ever since in moving house.\r\n\r\nThe rector had gone away for the day, being a man who disliked the\r\nsight of changes. He did not mean to return till the evening, when the\r\nnew school-teacher would have arrived and settled in, and everything\r\nwould be smooth again.\r\n\r\nThe blacksmith, the farm bailiff, and the schoolmaster himself were\r\nstanding in perplexed attitudes in the parlour before the instrument.\r\nThe master had remarked that even if he got it into the cart he should\r\nnot know what to do with it on his arrival at Christminster, the city\r\nhe was bound for, since he was only going into temporary lodgings just\r\nat first.\r\n\r\nA little boy of eleven, who had been thoughtfully assisting in the\r\npacking, joined the group of men, and as they rubbed their chins he\r\nspoke up, blushing at the sound of his own voice: \xe2\x80\x9cAunt have got a\r\ngreat fuel-house, and it could be put there, perhaps, till you\xe2\x80\x99ve found\r\na place to settle in, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA proper good notion,\xe2\x80\x9d said the blacksmith.\r\n\r\nIt was decided that a deputation should wait on the boy\xe2\x80\x99s aunt\xe2\x80\x94an old\r\nmaiden resident\xe2\x80\x94and ask her if she would house the piano till Mr.\r\nPhillotson should send for it. The smith and the bailiff started to see\r\nabout the practicability of the suggested shelter, and the boy and the\r\nschoolmaster were left standing alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry I am going, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the latter kindly.\r\n\r\nTears rose into the boy\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, for he was not among the regular day\r\nscholars, who came unromantically close to the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s life, but\r\none who had attended the night school only during the present teacher\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nterm of office. The regular scholars, if the truth must be told, stood\r\nat the present moment afar off, like certain historic disciples,\r\nindisposed to any enthusiastic volunteering of aid.\r\n\r\nThe boy awkwardly opened the book he held in his hand, which Mr.\r\nPhillotson had bestowed on him as a parting gift, and admitted that he\r\nwas sorry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Phillotson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you go, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94that would be a long story. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand my reasons,\r\nJude. You will, perhaps, when you are older.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I should now, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of this everywhere. You know what a university is,\r\nand a university degree? It is the necessary hallmark of a man who\r\nwants to do anything in teaching. My scheme, or dream, is to be a\r\nuniversity graduate, and then to be ordained. By going to live at\r\nChristminster, or near it, I shall be at headquarters, so to speak, and\r\nif my scheme is practicable at all, I consider that being on the spot\r\nwill afford me a better chance of carrying it out than I should have\r\nelsewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe smith and his companion returned. Old Miss Fawley\xe2\x80\x99s fuel-house was\r\ndry, and eminently practicable; and she seemed willing to give the\r\ninstrument standing-room there. It was accordingly left in the school\r\ntill the evening, when more hands would be available for removing it;\r\nand the schoolmaster gave a final glance round.\r\n\r\nThe boy Jude assisted in loading some small articles, and at nine\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock Mr. Phillotson mounted beside his box of books and other\r\n_impedimenta_, and bade his friends good-bye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t forget you, Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smiling, as the cart moved off.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe a good boy, remember; and be kind to animals and birds, and read\r\nall you can. And if ever you come to Christminster remember you hunt me\r\nout for old acquaintance\xe2\x80\x99 sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cart creaked across the green, and disappeared round the corner by\r\nthe rectory-house. The boy returned to the draw-well at the edge of the\r\ngreensward, where he had left his buckets when he went to help his\r\npatron and teacher in the loading. There was a quiver in his lip now\r\nand after opening the well-cover to begin lowering the bucket he paused\r\nand leant with his forehead and arms against the framework, his face\r\nwearing the fixity of a thoughtful child\xe2\x80\x99s who has felt the pricks of\r\nlife somewhat before his time. The well into which he was looking was\r\nas ancient as the village itself, and from his present position\r\nappeared as a long circular perspective ending in a shining disk of\r\nquivering water at a distance of a hundred feet down. There was a\r\nlining of green moss near the top, and nearer still the hart\xe2\x80\x99s-tongue\r\nfern.\r\n\r\nHe said to himself, in the melodramatic tones of a whimsical boy, that\r\nthe schoolmaster had drawn at that well scores of times on a morning\r\nlike this, and would never draw there any more. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him look\r\ndown into it, when he was tired with his drawing, just as I do now, and\r\nwhen he rested a bit before carrying the buckets home! But he was too\r\nclever to bide here any longer\xe2\x80\x94a small sleepy place like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA tear rolled from his eye into the depths of the well. The morning was\r\na little foggy, and the boy\xe2\x80\x99s breathing unfurled itself as a thicker\r\nfog upon the still and heavy air. His thoughts were interrupted by a\r\nsudden outcry:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring on that water, will ye, you idle young harlican!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt came from an old woman who had emerged from her door towards the\r\ngarden gate of a green-thatched cottage not far off. The boy quickly\r\nwaved a signal of assent, drew the water with what was a great effort\r\nfor one of his stature, landed and emptied the big bucket into his own\r\npair of smaller ones, and pausing a moment for breath, started with\r\nthem across the patch of clammy greensward whereon the well\r\nstood\xe2\x80\x94nearly in the centre of the little village, or rather hamlet of\r\nMarygreen.\r\n\r\nIt was as old-fashioned as it was small, and it rested in the lap of an\r\nundulating upland adjoining the North Wessex downs. Old as it was,\r\nhowever, the well-shaft was probably the only relic of the local\r\nhistory that remained absolutely unchanged. Many of the thatched and\r\ndormered dwelling-houses had been pulled down of late years, and many\r\ntrees felled on the green. Above all, the original church, hump-backed,\r\nwood-turreted, and quaintly hipped, had been taken down, and either\r\ncracked up into heaps of road-metal in the lane, or utilized as pig-sty\r\nwalls, garden seats, guard-stones to fences, and rockeries in the\r\nflower-beds of the neighbourhood. In place of it a tall new building of\r\nmodern Gothic design, unfamiliar to English eyes, had been erected on a\r\nnew piece of ground by a certain obliterator of historic records who\r\nhad run down from London and back in a day. The site whereon so long\r\nhad stood the ancient temple to the Christian divinities was not even\r\nrecorded on the green and level grass-plot that had immemorially been\r\nthe churchyard, the obliterated graves being commemorated by\r\neighteen-penny cast-iron crosses warranted to last five years.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nSlender as was Jude Fawley\xe2\x80\x99s frame he bore the two brimming\r\nhouse-buckets of water to the cottage without resting. Over the door\r\nwas a little rectangular piece of blue board, on which was painted in\r\nyellow letters, \xe2\x80\x9cDrusilla Fawley, Baker.\xe2\x80\x9d Within the little lead panes\r\nof the window\xe2\x80\x94this being one of the few old houses left\xe2\x80\x94were five\r\nbottles of sweets, and three buns on a plate of the willow pattern.\r\n\r\nWhile emptying the buckets at the back of the house he could hear an\r\nanimated conversation in progress within-doors between his great-aunt,\r\nthe Drusilla of the sign-board, and some other villagers. Having seen\r\nthe school-master depart, they were summing up particulars of the\r\nevent, and indulging in predictions of his future.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who\xe2\x80\x99s he?\xe2\x80\x9d asked one, comparatively a stranger, when the boy\r\nentered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell ye med ask it, Mrs. Williams. He\xe2\x80\x99s my great-nephew\xe2\x80\x94come since you\r\nwas last this way.\xe2\x80\x9d The old inhabitant who answered was a tall, gaunt\r\nwoman, who spoke tragically on the most trivial subject, and gave a\r\nphrase of her conversation to each auditor in turn. \xe2\x80\x9cHe come from\r\nMellstock, down in South Wessex, about a year ago\xe2\x80\x94worse luck for \xe2\x80\x99n,\r\nBelinda\xe2\x80\x9d (turning to the right) \xe2\x80\x9cwhere his father was living, and was\r\ntook wi\xe2\x80\x99 the shakings for death, and died in two days, as you know,\r\nCaroline\xe2\x80\x9d (turning to the left). \xe2\x80\x9cIt would ha\xe2\x80\x99 been a blessing if\r\nGoddy-mighty had took thee too, wi\xe2\x80\x99 thy mother and father, poor useless\r\nboy! But I\xe2\x80\x99ve got him here to stay with me till I can see what\xe2\x80\x99s to be\r\ndone with un, though I am obliged to let him earn any penny he can.\r\nJust now he\xe2\x80\x99s a-scaring of birds for Farmer Troutham. It keeps him out\r\nof mischty. Why do ye turn away, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, as the boy,\r\nfeeling the impact of their glances like slaps upon his face, moved\r\naside.\r\n\r\nThe local washerwoman replied that it was perhaps a very good plan of\r\nMiss or Mrs. Fawley\xe2\x80\x99s (as they called her indifferently) to have him\r\nwith her\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cto kip \xe2\x80\x99ee company in your loneliness, fetch water, shet the\r\nwinder-shetters o\xe2\x80\x99 nights, and help in the bit o\xe2\x80\x99 baking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fawley doubted it. \xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t ye get the schoolmaster to take\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee to Christminster wi\xe2\x80\x99 un, and make a scholar of \xe2\x80\x99ee,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued,\r\nin frowning pleasantry. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99 took a better one.\r\nThe boy is crazy for books, that he is. It runs in our family rather.\r\nHis cousin Sue is just the same\xe2\x80\x94so I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard; but I have not seen the\r\nchild for years, though she was born in this place, within these four\r\nwalls, as it happened. My niece and her husband, after they were\r\nmarried, didn\xe2\x80\x99 get a house of their own for some year or more; and then\r\nthey only had one till\xe2\x80\x94Well, I won\xe2\x80\x99t go into that. Jude, my child,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you ever marry. \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t for the Fawleys to take that step any\r\nmore. She, their only one, was like a child o\xe2\x80\x99 my own, Belinda, till\r\nthe split come! Ah, that a little maid should know such changes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude, finding the general attention again centering on himself, went\r\nout to the bakehouse, where he ate the cake provided for his breakfast.\r\nThe end of his spare time had now arrived, and emerging from the garden\r\nby getting over the hedge at the back he pursued a path northward, till\r\nhe came to a wide and lonely depression in the general level of the\r\nupland, which was sown as a corn-field. This vast concave was the scene\r\nof his labours for Mr. Troutham the farmer, and he descended into the\r\nmidst of it.\r\n\r\nThe brown surface of the field went right up towards the sky all round,\r\nwhere it was lost by degrees in the mist that shut out the actual verge\r\nand accentuated the solitude. The only marks on the uniformity of the\r\nscene were a rick of last year\xe2\x80\x99s produce standing in the midst of the\r\narable, the rooks that rose at his approach, and the path athwart the\r\nfallow by which he had come, trodden now by he hardly knew whom, though\r\nonce by many of his own dead family.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow ugly it is here!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\nThe fresh harrow-lines seemed to stretch like the channellings in a\r\npiece of new corduroy, lending a meanly utilitarian air to the expanse,\r\ntaking away its gradations, and depriving it of all history beyond that\r\nof the few recent months, though to every clod and stone there really\r\nattached associations enough and to spare\xe2\x80\x94echoes of songs from ancient\r\nharvest-days, of spoken words, and of sturdy deeds. Every inch of\r\nground had been the site, first or last, of energy, gaiety, horse-play,\r\nbickerings, weariness. Groups of gleaners had squatted in the sun on\r\nevery square yard. Love-matches that had populated the adjoining hamlet\r\nhad been made up there between reaping and carrying. Under the hedge\r\nwhich divided the field from a distant plantation girls had given\r\nthemselves to lovers who would not turn their heads to look at them by\r\nthe next harvest; and in that ancient cornfield many a man had made\r\nlove-promises to a woman at whose voice he had trembled by the next\r\nseed-time after fulfilling them in the church adjoining. But this\r\nneither Jude nor the rooks around him considered. For them it was a\r\nlonely place, possessing, in the one view, only the quality of a\r\nwork-ground, and in the other that of a granary good to feed in.\r\n\r\nThe boy stood under the rick before mentioned, and every few seconds\r\nused his clacker or rattle briskly. At each clack the rooks left off\r\npecking, and rose and went away on their leisurely wings, burnished\r\nlike tassets of mail, afterwards wheeling back and regarding him\r\nwarily, and descending to feed at a more respectful distance.\r\n\r\nHe sounded the clacker till his arm ached, and at length his heart grew\r\nsympathetic with the birds\xe2\x80\x99 thwarted desires. They seemed, like\r\nhimself, to be living in a world which did not want them. Why should he\r\nfrighten them away? They took upon more and more the aspect of gentle\r\nfriends and pensioners\xe2\x80\x94the only friends he could claim as being in the\r\nleast degree interested in him, for his aunt had often told him that\r\nshe was not. He ceased his rattling, and they alighted anew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor little dears!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cYou _shall_ have some\r\ndinner\xe2\x80\x94you shall. There is enough for us all. Farmer Troutham can\r\nafford to let you have some. Eat, then my dear little birdies, and make\r\na good meal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stayed and ate, inky spots on the nut-brown soil, and Jude enjoyed\r\ntheir appetite. A magic thread of fellow-feeling united his own life\r\nwith theirs. Puny and sorry as those lives were, they much resembled\r\nhis own.\r\n\r\nHis clacker he had by this time thrown away from him, as being a mean\r\nand sordid instrument, offensive both to the birds and to himself as\r\ntheir friend. All at once he became conscious of a smart blow upon his\r\nbuttocks, followed by a loud clack, which announced to his surprised\r\nsenses that the clacker had been the instrument of offence used. The\r\nbirds and Jude started up simultaneously, and the dazed eyes of the\r\nlatter beheld the farmer in person, the great Troutham himself, his red\r\nface glaring down upon Jude\xe2\x80\x99s cowering frame, the clacker swinging in\r\nhis hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Eat my dear birdies,\xe2\x80\x99 is it, young man? \xe2\x80\x98Eat, dear birdies,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nindeed! I\xe2\x80\x99ll tickle your breeches, and see if you say, \xe2\x80\x98Eat, dear\r\nbirdies,\xe2\x80\x99 again in a hurry! And you\xe2\x80\x99ve been idling at the\r\nschoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s too, instead of coming here, ha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t ye, hey? That\xe2\x80\x99s how\r\nyou earn your sixpence a day for keeping the rooks off my corn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst saluting Jude\xe2\x80\x99s ears with this impassioned rhetoric, Troutham\r\nhad seized his left hand with his own left, and swinging his slim frame\r\nround him at arm\xe2\x80\x99s-length, again struck Jude on the hind parts with the\r\nflat side of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s own rattle, till the field echoed with the blows,\r\nwhich were delivered once or twice at each revolution.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ee, sir\xe2\x80\x94please don\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ee!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the whirling child, as\r\nhelpless under the centrifugal tendency of his person as a hooked fish\r\nswinging to land, and beholding the hill, the rick, the plantation, the\r\npath, and the rooks going round and round him in an amazing circular\r\nrace. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I sir\xe2\x80\x94only meant that\xe2\x80\x94there was a good crop in the ground\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nsaw \xe2\x80\x99em sow it\xe2\x80\x94and the rooks could have a little bit for dinner\xe2\x80\x94and you\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t miss it, sir\xe2\x80\x94and Mr. Phillotson said I was to be kind to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94oh, oh, oh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis truthful explanation seemed to exasperate the farmer even more\r\nthan if Jude had stoutly denied saying anything at all, and he still\r\nsmacked the whirling urchin, the clacks of the instrument continuing to\r\nresound all across the field and as far as the ears of distant\r\nworkers\xe2\x80\x94who gathered thereupon that Jude was pursuing his business of\r\nclacking with great assiduity\xe2\x80\x94and echoing from the brand-new church\r\ntower just behind the mist, towards the building of which structure the\r\nfarmer had largely subscribed, to testify his love for God and man.\r\n\r\nPresently Troutham grew tired of his punitive task, and depositing the\r\nquivering boy on his legs, took a sixpence from his pocket and gave it\r\nhim in payment for his day\xe2\x80\x99s work, telling him to go home and never let\r\nhim see him in one of those fields again.\r\n\r\nJude leaped out of arm\xe2\x80\x99s reach, and walked along the trackway\r\nweeping\xe2\x80\x94not from the pain, though that was keen enough; not from the\r\nperception of the flaw in the terrestrial scheme, by which what was\r\ngood for God\xe2\x80\x99s birds was bad for God\xe2\x80\x99s gardener; but with the awful\r\nsense that he had wholly disgraced himself before he had been a year in\r\nthe parish, and hence might be a burden to his great-aunt for life.\r\n\r\nWith this shadow on his mind he did not care to show himself in the\r\nvillage, and went homeward by a roundabout track behind a high hedge\r\nand across a pasture. Here he beheld scores of coupled earthworms lying\r\nhalf their length on the surface of the damp ground, as they always did\r\nin such weather at that time of the year. It was impossible to advance\r\nin regular steps without crushing some of them at each tread.\r\n\r\nThough Farmer Troutham had just hurt him, he was a boy who could not\r\nhimself bear to hurt anything. He had never brought home a nest of\r\nyoung birds without lying awake in misery half the night after, and\r\noften reinstating them and the nest in their original place the next\r\nmorning. He could scarcely bear to see trees cut down or lopped, from a\r\nfancy that it hurt them; and late pruning, when the sap was up and the\r\ntree bled profusely, had been a positive grief to him in his infancy.\r\nThis weakness of character, as it may be called, suggested that he was\r\nthe sort of man who was born to ache a good deal before the fall of the\r\ncurtain upon his unnecessary life should signify that all was well with\r\nhim again. He carefully picked his way on tiptoe among the earthworms,\r\nwithout killing a single one.\r\n\r\nOn entering the cottage he found his aunt selling a penny loaf to a\r\nlittle girl, and when the customer was gone she said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, how do you\r\ncome to be back here in the middle of the morning like this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m turned away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Troutham have turned me away because I let the rooks have a few\r\npeckings of corn. And there\xe2\x80\x99s my wages\xe2\x80\x94the last I shall ever hae!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe threw the sixpence tragically on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said his aunt, suspending her breath. And she opened upon him a\r\nlecture on how she would now have him all the spring upon her hands\r\ndoing nothing. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you can\xe2\x80\x99t skeer birds, what can ye do? There! don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nye look so deedy! Farmer Troutham is not so much better than myself,\r\ncome to that. But \xe2\x80\x99tis as Job said, \xe2\x80\x98Now they that are younger than I\r\nhave me in derision, whose fathers I would have disdained to have set\r\nwith the dogs of my flock.\xe2\x80\x99 His father was my father\xe2\x80\x99s journeyman,\r\nanyhow, and I must have been a fool to let \xe2\x80\x99ee go to work for \xe2\x80\x99n, which\r\nI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99 done but to keep \xe2\x80\x99ee out of mischty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore angry with Jude for demeaning her by coming there than for\r\ndereliction of duty, she rated him primarily from that point of view,\r\nand only secondarily from a moral one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot that you should have let the birds eat what Farmer Troutham\r\nplanted. Of course you was wrong in that. Jude, Jude, why didstn\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\noff with that schoolmaster of thine to Christminster or somewhere? But,\r\noh no\xe2\x80\x94poor or\xe2\x80\x99nary child\xe2\x80\x94there never was any sprawl on thy side of the\r\nfamily, and never will be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is this beautiful city, Aunt\xe2\x80\x94this place where Mr. Phillotson is\r\ngone to?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the boy, after meditating in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord! you ought to know where the city of Christminster is. Near a\r\nscore of miles from here. It is a place much too good for you ever to\r\nhave much to do with, poor boy, I\xe2\x80\x99m a-thinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will Mr. Phillotson always be there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I tell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould I go to see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, no! You didn\xe2\x80\x99t grow up hereabout, or you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask such as\r\nthat. We\xe2\x80\x99ve never had anything to do with folk in Christminster, nor\r\nfolk in Christminster with we.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude went out, and, feeling more than ever his existence to be an\r\nundemanded one, he lay down upon his back on a heap of litter near the\r\npig-sty. The fog had by this time become more translucent, and the\r\nposition of the sun could be seen through it. He pulled his straw hat\r\nover his face, and peered through the interstices of the plaiting at\r\nthe white brightness, vaguely reflecting. Growing up brought\r\nresponsibilities, he found. Events did not rhyme quite as he had\r\nthought. Nature\xe2\x80\x99s logic was too horrid for him to care for. That mercy\r\ntowards one set of creatures was cruelty towards another sickened his\r\nsense of harmony. As you got older, and felt yourself to be at the\r\ncentre of your time, and not at a point in its circumference, as you\r\nhad felt when you were little, you were seized with a sort of\r\nshuddering, he perceived. All around you there seemed to be something\r\nglaring, garish, rattling, and the noises and glares hit upon the\r\nlittle cell called your life, and shook it, and warped it.\r\n\r\nIf he could only prevent himself growing up! He did not want to be a\r\nman.\r\n\r\nThen, like the natural boy, he forgot his despondency, and sprang up.\r\nDuring the remainder of the morning he helped his aunt, and in the\r\nafternoon, when there was nothing more to be done, he went into the\r\nvillage. Here he asked a man whereabouts Christminster lay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristminster? Oh, well, out by there yonder; though I\xe2\x80\x99ve never bin\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94not I. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never had any business at such a place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man pointed north-eastward, in the very direction where lay that\r\nfield in which Jude had so disgraced himself. There was something\r\nunpleasant about the coincidence for the moment, but the fearsomeness\r\nof this fact rather increased his curiosity about the city. The farmer\r\nhad said he was never to be seen in that field again; yet Christminster\r\nlay across it, and the path was a public one. So, stealing out of the\r\nhamlet, he descended into the same hollow which had witnessed his\r\npunishment in the morning, never swerving an inch from the path, and\r\nclimbing up the long and tedious ascent on the other side till the\r\ntrack joined the highway by a little clump of trees. Here the ploughed\r\nland ended, and all before him was bleak open down.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nNot a soul was visible on the hedgeless highway, or on either side of\r\nit, and the white road seemed to ascend and diminish till it joined the\r\nsky. At the very top it was crossed at right angles by a green\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cridgeway\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the Ickneild Street and original Roman road through the\r\ndistrict. This ancient track ran east and west for many miles, and down\r\nalmost to within living memory had been used for driving flocks and\r\nherds to fairs and markets. But it was now neglected and overgrown.\r\n\r\nThe boy had never before strayed so far north as this from the nestling\r\nhamlet in which he had been deposited by the carrier from a railway\r\nstation southward, one dark evening some few months earlier, and till\r\nnow he had had no suspicion that such a wide, flat, low-lying country\r\nlay so near at hand, under the very verge of his upland world. The\r\nwhole northern semicircle between east and west, to a distance of forty\r\nor fifty miles, spread itself before him; a bluer, moister atmosphere,\r\nevidently, than that he breathed up here.\r\n\r\nNot far from the road stood a weather-beaten old barn of reddish-grey\r\nbrick and tile. It was known as the Brown House by the people of the\r\nlocality. He was about to pass it when he perceived a ladder against\r\nthe eaves; and the reflection that the higher he got, the further he\r\ncould see, led Jude to stand and regard it. On the slope of the roof\r\ntwo men were repairing the tiling. He turned into the ridgeway and drew\r\ntowards the barn.\r\n\r\nWhen he had wistfully watched the workmen for some time he took\r\ncourage, and ascended the ladder till he stood beside them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my lad, and what may you want up here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to know where the city of Christminster is, if you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristminster is out across there, by that clump. You can see it\xe2\x80\x94at\r\nleast you can on a clear day. Ah, no, you can\xe2\x80\x99t now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other tiler, glad of any kind of diversion from the monotony of his\r\nlabour, had also turned to look towards the quarter designated. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t often see it in weather like this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe time I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnoticed it is when the sun is going down in a blaze of flame, and it\r\nlooks like\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe heavenly Jerusalem,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested the serious urchin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94though I should never ha\xe2\x80\x99 thought of it myself. \xe2\x80\xa6 But I can\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nno Christminster to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy strained his eyes also; yet neither could he see the far-off\r\ncity. He descended from the barn, and abandoning Christminster with the\r\nversatility of his age he walked along the ridge-track, looking for any\r\nnatural objects of interest that might lie in the banks thereabout.\r\nWhen he repassed the barn to go back to Marygreen he observed that the\r\nladder was still in its place, but that the men had finished their\r\nday\xe2\x80\x99s work and gone away.\r\n\r\nIt was waning towards evening; there was still a faint mist, but it had\r\ncleared a little except in the damper tracts of subjacent country and\r\nalong the river-courses. He thought again of Christminster, and wished,\r\nsince he had come two or three miles from his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s house on purpose,\r\nthat he could have seen for once this attractive city of which he had\r\nbeen told. But even if he waited here it was hardly likely that the air\r\nwould clear before night. Yet he was loth to leave the spot, for the\r\nnorthern expanse became lost to view on retreating towards the village\r\nonly a few hundred yards.\r\n\r\nHe ascended the ladder to have one more look at the point the men had\r\ndesignated, and perched himself on the highest rung, overlying the\r\ntiles. He might not be able to come so far as this for many days.\r\nPerhaps if he prayed, the wish to see Christminster might be forwarded.\r\nPeople said that, if you prayed, things sometimes came to you, even\r\nthough they sometimes did not. He had read in a tract that a man who\r\nhad begun to build a church, and had no money to finish it, knelt down\r\nand prayed, and the money came in by the next post. Another man tried\r\nthe same experiment, and the money did not come; but he found\r\nafterwards that the breeches he knelt in were made by a wicked Jew.\r\nThis was not discouraging, and turning on the ladder Jude knelt on the\r\nthird rung, where, resting against those above it, he prayed that the\r\nmist might rise.\r\n\r\nHe then seated himself again, and waited. In the course of ten or\r\nfifteen minutes the thinning mist dissolved altogether from the\r\nnorthern horizon, as it had already done elsewhere, and about a quarter\r\nof an hour before the time of sunset the westward clouds parted, the\r\nsun\xe2\x80\x99s position being partially uncovered, and the beams streaming out\r\nin visible lines between two bars of slaty cloud. The boy immediately\r\nlooked back in the old direction.\r\n\r\nSome way within the limits of the stretch of landscape, points of light\r\nlike the topaz gleamed. The air increased in transparency with the\r\nlapse of minutes, till the topaz points showed themselves to be the\r\nvanes, windows, wet roof slates, and other shining spots upon the\r\nspires, domes, freestone-work, and varied outlines that were faintly\r\nrevealed. It was Christminster, unquestionably; either directly seen,\r\nor miraged in the peculiar atmosphere.\r\n\r\nThe spectator gazed on and on till the windows and vanes lost their\r\nshine, going out almost suddenly like extinguished candles. The vague\r\ncity became veiled in mist. Turning to the west, he saw that the sun\r\nhad disappeared. The foreground of the scene had grown funereally dark,\r\nand near objects put on the hues and shapes of chimaeras.\r\n\r\nHe anxiously descended the ladder, and started homewards at a run,\r\ntrying not to think of giants, Herne the Hunter, Apollyon lying in wait\r\nfor Christian, or of the captain with the bleeding hole in his forehead\r\nand the corpses round him that remutinied every night on board the\r\nbewitched ship. He knew that he had grown out of belief in these\r\nhorrors, yet he was glad when he saw the church tower and the lights in\r\nthe cottage windows, even though this was not the home of his birth,\r\nand his great-aunt did not care much about him.\r\n\r\n\r\nInside and round about that old woman\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cshop\xe2\x80\x9d window, with its\r\ntwenty-four little panes set in lead-work, the glass of some of them\r\noxidized with age, so that you could hardly see the poor penny articles\r\nexhibited within, and forming part of a stock which a strong man could\r\nhave carried, Jude had his outer being for some long tideless time. But\r\nhis dreams were as gigantic as his surroundings were small.\r\n\r\nThrough the solid barrier of cold cretaceous upland to the northward he\r\nwas always beholding a gorgeous city\xe2\x80\x94the fancied place he had likened\r\nto the new Jerusalem, though there was perhaps more of the painter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimagination and less of the diamond merchant\xe2\x80\x99s in his dreams thereof\r\nthan in those of the Apocalyptic writer. And the city acquired a\r\ntangibility, a permanence, a hold on his life, mainly from the one\r\nnucleus of fact that the man for whose knowledge and purposes he had so\r\nmuch reverence was actually living there; not only so, but living among\r\nthe more thoughtful and mentally shining ones therein.\r\n\r\nIn sad wet seasons, though he knew it must rain at Christminster too,\r\nhe could hardly believe that it rained so drearily there. Whenever he\r\ncould get away from the confines of the hamlet for an hour or two,\r\nwhich was not often, he would steal off to the Brown House on the hill\r\nand strain his eyes persistently; sometimes to be rewarded by the sight\r\nof a dome or spire, at other times by a little smoke, which in his\r\nestimate had some of the mysticism of incense.\r\n\r\nThen the day came when it suddenly occurred to him that if he ascended\r\nto the point of view after dark, or possibly went a mile or two\r\nfurther, he would see the night lights of the city. It would be\r\nnecessary to come back alone, but even that consideration did not deter\r\nhim, for he could throw a little manliness into his mood, no doubt.\r\n\r\nThe project was duly executed. It was not late when he arrived at the\r\nplace of outlook, only just after dusk, but a black north-east sky,\r\naccompanied by a wind from the same quarter, made the occasion dark\r\nenough. He was rewarded; but what he saw was not the lamps in rows, as\r\nhe had half expected. No individual light was visible, only a halo or\r\nglow-fog over-arching the place against the black heavens behind it,\r\nmaking the light and the city seem distant but a mile or so.\r\n\r\nHe set himself to wonder on the exact point in the glow where the\r\nschoolmaster might be\xe2\x80\x94he who never communicated with anybody at\r\nMarygreen now; who was as if dead to them here. In the glow he seemed\r\nto see Phillotson promenading at ease, like one of the forms in\r\nNebuchadnezzar\xe2\x80\x99s furnace.\r\n\r\nHe had heard that breezes travelled at the rate of ten miles an hour,\r\nand the fact now came into his mind. He parted his lips as he faced the\r\nnorth-east, and drew in the wind as if it were a sweet liquor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, addressing the breeze caressingly \xe2\x80\x9cwere in\r\nChristminster city between one and two hours ago, floating along the\r\nstreets, pulling round the weather-cocks, touching Mr. Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nface, being breathed by him; and now you are here, breathed by me\xe2\x80\x94you,\r\nthe very same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly there came along this wind something towards him\xe2\x80\x94a message\r\nfrom the place\xe2\x80\x94from some soul residing there, it seemed. Surely it was\r\nthe sound of bells, the voice of the city, faint and musical, calling\r\nto him, \xe2\x80\x9cWe are happy here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had become entirely lost to his bodily situation during this mental\r\nleap, and only got back to it by a rough recalling. A few yards below\r\nthe brow of the hill on which he paused a team of horses made its\r\nappearance, having reached the place by dint of half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nserpentine progress from the bottom of the immense declivity. They had\r\na load of coals behind them\xe2\x80\x94a fuel that could only be got into the\r\nupland by this particular route. They were accompanied by a carter, a\r\nsecond man, and a boy, who now kicked a large stone behind one of the\r\nwheels, and allowed the panting animals to have a long rest, while\r\nthose in charge took a flagon off the load and indulged in a drink\r\nround.\r\n\r\nThey were elderly men, and had genial voices. Jude addressed them,\r\ninquiring if they had come from Christminster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid, with this load!\xe2\x80\x9d said they.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe place I mean is that one yonder.\xe2\x80\x9d He was getting so romantically\r\nattached to Christminster that, like a young lover alluding to his\r\nmistress, he felt bashful at mentioning its name again. He pointed to\r\nthe light in the sky\xe2\x80\x94hardly perceptible to their older eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. There do seem a spot a bit brighter in the nor\xe2\x80\x99-east than\r\nelsewhere, though I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99 noticed it myself, and no doubt it\r\nmed be Christminster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere a little book of tales which Jude had tucked up under his arm,\r\nhaving brought them to read on his way hither before it grew dark,\r\nslipped and fell into the road. The carter eyed him while he picked it\r\nup and straightened the leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, young man,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99d have to get your head screwed on\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other way before you could read what they read there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they never look at anything that folks like we can understand,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthe carter continued, by way of passing the time. \xe2\x80\x9cOn\xe2\x80\x99y foreign tongues\r\nused in the days of the Tower of Babel, when no two families spoke\r\nalike. They read that sort of thing as fast as a night-hawk will whir.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis all learning there\xe2\x80\x94nothing but learning, except religion. And\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s learning too, for I never could understand it. Yes, \xe2\x80\x99tis a\r\nserious-minded place. Not but there\xe2\x80\x99s wenches in the streets o\xe2\x80\x99 nights\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nYou know, I suppose, that they raise pa\xe2\x80\x99sons there like radishes in a\r\nbed? And though it do take\xe2\x80\x94how many years, Bob?\xe2\x80\x94five years to turn a\r\nlirruping hobble-de-hoy chap into a solemn preaching man with no\r\ncorrupt passions, they\xe2\x80\x99ll do it, if it can be done, and polish un off\r\nlike the workmen they be, and turn un out wi\xe2\x80\x99 a long face, and a long\r\nblack coat and waistcoat, and a religious collar and hat, same as they\r\nused to wear in the Scriptures, so that his own mother wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know un\r\nsometimes. \xe2\x80\xa6 There, \xe2\x80\x99tis their business, like anybody else\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how should you know\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow don\xe2\x80\x99t you interrupt, my boy. Never interrupt your senyers. Move\r\nthe fore hoss aside, Bobby; here\xe2\x80\x99s som\xe2\x80\x99at coming\xe2\x80\xa6 You must mind that I\r\nbe a-talking of the college life. \xe2\x80\x99Em lives on a lofty level; there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno gainsaying it, though I myself med not think much of \xe2\x80\x99em. As we be\r\nhere in our bodies on this high ground, so be they in their\r\nminds\xe2\x80\x94noble-minded men enough, no doubt\xe2\x80\x94some on \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94able to earn\r\nhundreds by thinking out loud. And some on \xe2\x80\x99em be strong young fellows\r\nthat can earn a\xe2\x80\x99most as much in silver cups. As for music, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeautiful music everywhere in Christminster. You med be religious, or\r\nyou med not, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t help striking in your homely note with the\r\nrest. And there\xe2\x80\x99s a street in the place\xe2\x80\x94the main street\xe2\x80\x94that ha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nanother like it in the world. I should think I did know a little about\r\nChristminster!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time the horses had recovered breath and bent to their collars\r\nagain. Jude, throwing a last adoring look at the distant halo, turned\r\nand walked beside his remarkably well-informed friend, who had no\r\nobjection to telling him as they moved on more yet of the city\xe2\x80\x94its\r\ntowers and halls and churches. The waggon turned into a cross-road,\r\nwhereupon Jude thanked the carter warmly for his information, and said\r\nhe only wished he could talk half as well about Christminster as he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99tis oonly what has come in my way,\xe2\x80\x9d said the carter\r\nunboastfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never been there, no more than you; but I\xe2\x80\x99ve picked\r\nup the knowledge here and there, and you be welcome to it. A-getting\r\nabout the world as I do, and mixing with all classes of society, one\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t help hearing of things. A friend o\xe2\x80\x99 mine, that used to clane the\r\nboots at the Crozier Hotel in Christminster when he was in his prime,\r\nwhy, I knowed un as well as my own brother in his later years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude continued his walk homeward alone, pondering so deeply that he\r\nforgot to feel timid. He suddenly grew older. It had been the yearning\r\nof his heart to find something to anchor on, to cling to\xe2\x80\x94for some place\r\nwhich he could call admirable. Should he find that place in this city\r\nif he could get there? Would it be a spot in which, without fear of\r\nfarmers, or hindrance, or ridicule, he could watch and wait, and set\r\nhimself to some mighty undertaking like the men of old of whom he had\r\nheard? As the halo had been to his eyes when gazing at it a quarter of\r\nan hour earlier, so was the spot mentally to him as he pursued his dark\r\nway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a city of light,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tree of knowledge grows there,\xe2\x80\x9d he added a few steps further on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a place that teachers of men spring from and go to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is what you may call a castle, manned by scholarship and religion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter this figure he was silent a long while, till he added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would just suit me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\n\r\nWalking somewhat slowly by reason of his concentration, the boy\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nancient man in some phases of thought, much younger than his years in\r\nothers\xe2\x80\x94was overtaken by a light-footed pedestrian, whom,\r\nnotwithstanding the gloom, he could perceive to be wearing an\r\nextraordinarily tall hat, a swallow-tailed coat, and a watch-chain that\r\ndanced madly and threw around scintillations of sky-light as its owner\r\nswung along upon a pair of thin legs and noiseless boots. Jude,\r\nbeginning to feel lonely, endeavoured to keep up with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my man! I\xe2\x80\x99m in a hurry, so you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to walk pretty fast if\r\nyou keep alongside of me. Do you know who I am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think. Physician Vilbert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m known everywhere, I see! That comes of being a public\r\nbenefactor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVilbert was an itinerant quack-doctor, well known to the rustic\r\npopulation, and absolutely unknown to anybody else, as he, indeed, took\r\ncare to be, to avoid inconvenient investigations. Cottagers formed his\r\nonly patients, and his Wessex-wide repute was among them alone. His\r\nposition was humbler and his field more obscure than those of the\r\nquacks with capital and an organized system of advertising. He was, in\r\nfact, a survival. The distances he traversed on foot were enormous, and\r\nextended nearly the whole length and breadth of Wessex. Jude had one\r\nday seen him selling a pot of coloured lard to an old woman as a\r\ncertain cure for a bad leg, the woman arranging to pay a guinea, in\r\ninstalments of a shilling a fortnight, for the precious salve, which,\r\naccording to the physician, could only be obtained from a particular\r\nanimal which grazed on Mount Sinai, and was to be captured only at\r\ngreat risk to life and limb. Jude, though he already had his doubts\r\nabout this gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s medicines, felt him to be unquestionably a\r\ntravelled personage, and one who might be a trustworthy source of\r\ninformation on matters not strictly professional.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI s\xe2\x80\x99pose you\xe2\x80\x99ve been to Christminster, Physician?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have\xe2\x80\x94many times,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the long thin man. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s one of my\r\ncentres.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful city for scholarship and religion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d say so, my boy, if you\xe2\x80\x99d seen it. Why, the very sons of the old\r\nwomen who do the washing of the colleges can talk in Latin\xe2\x80\x94not good\r\nLatin, that I admit, as a critic: dog-Latin\xe2\x80\x94cat-Latin, as we used to\r\ncall it in my undergraduate days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Greek?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s more for the men who are in training for bishops, that\r\nthey may be able to read the New Testament in the original.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to learn Latin and Greek myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lofty desire. You must get a grammar of each tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean to go to Christminster some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you do, you say that Physician Vilbert is the only proprietor\r\nof those celebrated pills that infallibly cure all disorders of the\r\nalimentary system, as well as asthma and shortness of breath. Two and\r\nthreepence a box\xe2\x80\x94specially licensed by the government stamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you get me the grammars if I promise to say it hereabout?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll sell you mine with pleasure\xe2\x80\x94those I used as a student.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thank you, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude gratefully, but in gasps, for the\r\namazing speed of the physician\xe2\x80\x99s walk kept him in a dog-trot which was\r\ngiving him a stitch in the side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99d better drop behind, my young man. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll do. I\xe2\x80\x99ll get you the grammars, and give you a first lesson, if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll remember, at every house in the village, to recommend Physician\r\nVilbert\xe2\x80\x99s golden ointment, life-drops, and female pills.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere will you be with the grammars?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be passing here this day fortnight at precisely this hour of\r\nfive-and-twenty minutes past seven. My movements are as truly timed as\r\nthose of the planets in their courses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere I\xe2\x80\x99ll be to meet you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith orders for my medicines?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Physician.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude then dropped behind, waited a few minutes to recover breath, and\r\nwent home with a consciousness of having struck a blow for\r\nChristminster.\r\n\r\nThrough the intervening fortnight he ran about and smiled outwardly at\r\nhis inward thoughts, as if they were people meeting and nodding to\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94smiled with that singularly beautiful irradiation which is seen to\r\nspread on young faces at the inception of some glorious idea, as if a\r\nsupernatural lamp were held inside their transparent natures, giving\r\nrise to the flattering fancy that heaven lies about them then.\r\n\r\nHe honestly performed his promise to the man of many cures, in whom he\r\nnow sincerely believed, walking miles hither and thither among the\r\nsurrounding hamlets as the Physician\xe2\x80\x99s agent in advance. On the evening\r\nappointed he stood motionless on the plateau, at the place where he had\r\nparted from Vilbert, and there awaited his approach. The road-physician\r\nwas fairly up to time; but, to the surprise of Jude on striking into\r\nhis pace, which the pedestrian did not diminish by a single unit of\r\nforce, the latter seemed hardly to recognize his young companion,\r\nthough with the lapse of the fortnight the evenings had grown light.\r\nJude thought it might perhaps be owing to his wearing another hat, and\r\nhe saluted the physician with dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my boy?\xe2\x80\x9d said the latter abstractedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou? who are you? Oh yes\xe2\x80\x94to be sure! Got any orders, lad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d And Jude told him the names and addresses of the cottagers who\r\nwere willing to test the virtues of the world-renowned pills and salve.\r\nThe quack mentally registered these with great care.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Latin and Greek grammars?\xe2\x80\x9d Jude\xe2\x80\x99s voice trembled with anxiety.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were to bring me yours, that you used before you took your\r\ndegree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, yes! Forgot all about it\xe2\x80\x94all! So many lives depending on my\r\nattention, you see, my man, that I can\xe2\x80\x99t give so much thought as I\r\nwould like to other things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude controlled himself sufficiently long to make sure of the truth;\r\nand he repeated, in a voice of dry misery, \xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t brought \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But you must get me some more orders from sick people, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbring the grammars next time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude dropped behind. He was an unsophisticated boy, but the gift of\r\nsudden insight which is sometimes vouchsafed to children showed him all\r\nat once what shoddy humanity the quack was made of. There was to be no\r\nintellectual light from this source. The leaves dropped from his\r\nimaginary crown of laurel; he turned to a gate, leant against it, and\r\ncried bitterly.\r\n\r\nThe disappointment was followed by an interval of blankness. He might,\r\nperhaps, have obtained grammars from Alfredston, but to do that\r\nrequired money, and a knowledge of what books to order; and though\r\nphysically comfortable, he was in such absolute dependence as to be\r\nwithout a farthing of his own.\r\n\r\nAt this date Mr. Phillotson sent for his pianoforte, and it gave Jude a\r\nlead. Why should he not write to the schoolmaster, and ask him to be so\r\nkind as to get him the grammars in Christminster? He might slip a\r\nletter inside the case of the instrument, and it would be sure to reach\r\nthe desired eyes. Why not ask him to send any old second-hand copies,\r\nwhich would have the charm of being mellowed by the university\r\natmosphere?\r\n\r\nTo tell his aunt of his intention would be to defeat it. It was\r\nnecessary to act alone.\r\n\r\nAfter a further consideration of a few days he did act, and on the day\r\nof the piano\xe2\x80\x99s departure, which happened to be his next birthday,\r\nclandestinely placed the letter inside the packing-case, directed to\r\nhis much-admired friend, being afraid to reveal the operation to his\r\naunt Drusilla, lest she should discover his motive, and compel him to\r\nabandon his scheme.\r\n\r\nThe piano was despatched, and Jude waited days and weeks, calling every\r\nmorning at the cottage post office before his great-aunt was stirring.\r\nAt last a packet did indeed arrive at the village, and he saw from the\r\nends of it that it contained two thin books. He took it away into a\r\nlonely place, and sat down on a felled elm to open it.\r\n\r\nEver since his first ecstasy or vision of Christminster and its\r\npossibilities, Jude had meditated much and curiously on the probable\r\nsort of process that was involved in turning the expressions of one\r\nlanguage into those of another. He concluded that a grammar of the\r\nrequired tongue would contain, primarily, a rule, prescription, or clue\r\nof the nature of a secret cipher, which, once known, would enable him,\r\nby merely applying it, to change at will all words of his own speech\r\ninto those of the foreign one. His childish idea was, in fact, a\r\npushing to the extremity of mathematical precision what is everywhere\r\nknown as Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s Law\xe2\x80\x94an aggrandizement of rough rules to ideal\r\ncompleteness. Thus he assumed that the words of the required language\r\nwere always to be found somewhere latent in the words of the given\r\nlanguage by those who had the art to uncover them, such art being\r\nfurnished by the books aforesaid.\r\n\r\nWhen, therefore, having noted that the packet bore the postmark of\r\nChristminster, he cut the string, opened the volumes, and turned to the\r\nLatin grammar, which chanced to come uppermost, he could scarcely\r\nbelieve his eyes.\r\n\r\nThe book was an old one\xe2\x80\x94thirty years old, soiled, scribbled wantonly\r\nover with a strange name in every variety of enmity to the letterpress,\r\nand marked at random with dates twenty years earlier than his own day.\r\nBut this was not the cause of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s amazement. He learnt for the first\r\ntime that there was no law of transmutation, as in his innocence he had\r\nsupposed (there was, in some degree, but the grammarian did not\r\nrecognize it), but that every word in both Latin and Greek was to be\r\nindividually committed to memory at the cost of years of plodding.\r\n\r\nJude flung down the books, lay backward along the broad trunk of the\r\nelm, and was an utterly miserable boy for the space of a quarter of an\r\nhour. As he had often done before, he pulled his hat over his face and\r\nwatched the sun peering insidiously at him through the interstices of\r\nthe straw. This was Latin and Greek, then, was it this grand delusion!\r\nThe charm he had supposed in store for him was really a labour like\r\nthat of Israel in Egypt.\r\n\r\nWhat brains they must have in Christminster and the great schools, he\r\npresently thought, to learn words one by one up to tens of thousands!\r\nThere were no brains in his head equal to this business; and as the\r\nlittle sun-rays continued to stream in through his hat at him, he\r\nwished he had never seen a book, that he might never see another, that\r\nhe had never been born.\r\n\r\nSomebody might have come along that way who would have asked him his\r\ntrouble, and might have cheered him by saying that his notions were\r\nfurther advanced than those of his grammarian. But nobody did come,\r\nbecause nobody does; and under the crushing recognition of his gigantic\r\nerror Jude continued to wish himself out of the world.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the three or four succeeding years a quaint and singular vehicle\r\nmight have been discerned moving along the lanes and by-roads near\r\nMarygreen, driven in a quaint and singular way.\r\n\r\nIn the course of a month or two after the receipt of the books Jude had\r\ngrown callous to the shabby trick played him by the dead languages. In\r\nfact, his disappointment at the nature of those tongues had, after a\r\nwhile, been the means of still further glorifying the erudition of\r\nChristminster. To acquire languages, departed or living in spite of\r\nsuch obstinacies as he now knew them inherently to possess, was a\r\nherculean performance which gradually led him on to a greater interest\r\nin it than in the presupposed patent process. The mountain-weight of\r\nmaterial under which the ideas lay in those dusty volumes called the\r\nclassics piqued him into a dogged, mouselike subtlety of attempt to\r\nmove it piecemeal.\r\n\r\nHe had endeavoured to make his presence tolerable to his crusty maiden\r\naunt by assisting her to the best of his ability, and the business of\r\nthe little cottage bakery had grown in consequence. An aged horse with\r\na hanging head had been purchased for eight pounds at a sale, a\r\ncreaking cart with a whity-brown tilt obtained for a few pounds more,\r\nand in this turn-out it became Jude\xe2\x80\x99s business thrice a week to carry\r\nloaves of bread to the villagers and solitary cotters immediately round\r\nMarygreen.\r\n\r\nThe singularity aforesaid lay, after all, less in the conveyance itself\r\nthan in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s manner of conducting it along its route. Its interior\r\nwas the scene of most of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s education by \xe2\x80\x9cprivate study.\xe2\x80\x9d As soon\r\nas the horse had learnt the road and the houses at which he was to\r\npause awhile, the boy, seated in front, would slip the reins over his\r\narm, ingeniously fix open, by means of a strap attached to the tilt,\r\nthe volume he was reading, spread the dictionary on his knees, and\r\nplunge into the simpler passages from Caesar, Virgil, or Horace, as the\r\ncase might be, in his purblind stumbling way, and with an expenditure\r\nof labour that would have made a tender-hearted pedagogue shed tears;\r\nyet somehow getting at the meaning of what he read, and divining rather\r\nthan beholding the spirit of the original, which often to his mind was\r\nsomething else than that which he was taught to look for.\r\n\r\nThe only copies he had been able to lay hands on were old Delphin\r\neditions, because they were superseded, and therefore cheap. But, bad\r\nfor idle schoolboys, it did so happen that they were passably good for\r\nhim. The hampered and lonely itinerant conscientiously covered up the\r\nmarginal readings, and used them merely on points of construction, as\r\nhe would have used a comrade or tutor who should have happened to be\r\npassing by. And though Jude may have had little chance of becoming a\r\nscholar by these rough and ready means, he was in the way of getting\r\ninto the groove he wished to follow.\r\n\r\nWhile he was busied with these ancient pages, which had already been\r\nthumbed by hands possibly in the grave, digging out the thoughts of\r\nthese minds so remote yet so near, the bony old horse pursued his\r\nrounds, and Jude would be aroused from the woes of Dido by the stoppage\r\nof his cart and the voice of some old woman crying, \xe2\x80\x9cTwo to-day, baker,\r\nand I return this stale one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was frequently met in the lanes by pedestrians and others without\r\nhis seeing them, and by degrees the people of the neighbourhood began\r\nto talk about his method of combining work and play (such they\r\nconsidered his reading to be), which, though probably convenient enough\r\nto himself, was not altogether a safe proceeding for other travellers\r\nalong the same roads. There were murmurs. Then a private resident of an\r\nadjoining place informed the local policeman that the baker\xe2\x80\x99s boy\r\nshould not be allowed to read while driving, and insisted that it was\r\nthe constable\xe2\x80\x99s duty to catch him in the act, and take him to the\r\npolice court at Alfredston, and get him fined for dangerous practices\r\non the highway. The policeman thereupon lay in wait for Jude, and one\r\nday accosted him and cautioned him.\r\n\r\nAs Jude had to get up at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning to heat the oven,\r\nand mix and set in the bread that he distributed later in the day, he\r\nwas obliged to go to bed at night immediately after laying the sponge;\r\nso that if he could not read his classics on the highways he could\r\nhardly study at all. The only thing to be done was, therefore, to keep\r\na sharp eye ahead and around him as well as he could in the\r\ncircumstances, and slip away his books as soon as anybody loomed in the\r\ndistance, the policeman in particular. To do that official justice, he\r\ndid not put himself much in the way of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s bread-cart, considering\r\nthat in such a lonely district the chief danger was to Jude himself,\r\nand often on seeing the white tilt over the hedges he would move in\r\nanother direction.\r\n\r\nOn a day when Fawley was getting quite advanced, being now about\r\nsixteen, and had been stumbling through the \xe2\x80\x9cCarmen S\xc3\xa6culare,\xe2\x80\x9d on his\r\nway home, he found himself to be passing over the high edge of the\r\nplateau by the Brown House. The light had changed, and it was the sense\r\nof this which had caused him to look up. The sun was going down, and\r\nthe full moon was rising simultaneously behind the woods in the\r\nopposite quarter. His mind had become so impregnated with the poem\r\nthat, in a moment of the same impulsive emotion which years before had\r\ncaused him to kneel on the ladder, he stopped the horse, alighted, and\r\nglancing round to see that nobody was in sight, knelt down on the\r\nroadside bank with open book. He turned first to the shiny goddess, who\r\nseemed to look so softly and critically at his doings, then to the\r\ndisappearing luminary on the other hand, as he began:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPh\xc5\x93be silvarumque potens Diana!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe horse stood still till he had finished the hymn, which Jude\r\nrepeated under the sway of a polytheistic fancy that he would never\r\nhave thought of humouring in broad daylight.\r\n\r\nReaching home, he mused over his curious superstition, innate or\r\nacquired, in doing this, and the strange forgetfulness which had led to\r\nsuch a lapse from common sense and custom in one who wished, next to\r\nbeing a scholar, to be a Christian divine. It had all come of reading\r\nheathen works exclusively. The more he thought of it the more convinced\r\nhe was of his inconsistency. He began to wonder whether he could be\r\nreading quite the right books for his object in life. Certainly there\r\nseemed little harmony between this pagan literature and the medi\xc3\xa6val\r\ncolleges at Christminster, that ecclesiastical romance in stone.\r\n\r\nUltimately he decided that in his sheer love of reading he had taken up\r\na wrong emotion for a Christian young man. He had dabbled in Clarke\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nHomer, but had never yet worked much at the New Testament in the Greek,\r\nthough he possessed a copy, obtained by post from a second-hand\r\nbookseller. He abandoned the now familiar Ionic for a new dialect, and\r\nfor a long time onward limited his reading almost entirely to the\r\nGospels and Epistles in Griesbach\xe2\x80\x99s text. Moreover, on going into\r\nAlfredston one day, he was introduced to patristic literature by\r\nfinding at the bookseller\xe2\x80\x99s some volumes of the Fathers which had been\r\nleft behind by an insolvent clergyman of the neighbourhood.\r\n\r\nAs another outcome of this change of groove he visited on Sundays all\r\nthe churches within a walk, and deciphered the Latin inscriptions on\r\nfifteenth-century brasses and tombs. On one of these pilgrimages he met\r\nwith a hunch-backed old woman of great intelligence, who read\r\neverything she could lay her hands on, and she told him more yet of the\r\nromantic charms of the city of light and lore. Thither he resolved as\r\nfirmly as ever to go.\r\n\r\nBut how live in that city? At present he had no income at all. He had\r\nno trade or calling of any dignity or stability whatever on which he\r\ncould subsist while carrying out an intellectual labour which might\r\nspread over many years.\r\n\r\nWhat was most required by citizens? Food, clothing, and shelter. An\r\nincome from any work in preparing the first would be too meagre; for\r\nmaking the second he felt a distaste; the preparation of the third\r\nrequisite he inclined to. They built in a city; therefore he would\r\nlearn to build. He thought of his unknown uncle, his cousin Susanna\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfather, an ecclesiastical worker in metal, and somehow medi\xc3\xa6val art in\r\nany material was a trade for which he had rather a fancy. He could not\r\ngo far wrong in following his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s footsteps, and engaging himself\r\nawhile with the carcases that contained the scholar souls.\r\n\r\nAs a preliminary he obtained some small blocks of freestone, metal not\r\nbeing available, and suspending his studies awhile, occupied his spare\r\nhalf-hours in copying the heads and capitals in his parish church.\r\n\r\nThere was a stone-mason of a humble kind in Alfredston, and as soon as\r\nhe had found a substitute for himself in his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s little business, he\r\noffered his services to this man for a trifling wage. Here Jude had the\r\nopportunity of learning at least the rudiments of freestone-working.\r\nSome time later he went to a church-builder in the same place, and\r\nunder the architect\xe2\x80\x99s direction became handy at restoring the\r\ndilapidated masonries of several village churches round about.\r\n\r\nNot forgetting that he was only following up this handicraft as a prop\r\nto lean on while he prepared those greater engines which he flattered\r\nhimself would be better fitted for him, he yet was interested in his\r\npursuit on its own account. He now had lodgings during the week in the\r\nlittle town, whence he returned to Marygreen village every Saturday\r\nevening. And thus he reached and passed his nineteenth year.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nAt this memorable date of his life he was, one Saturday, returning from\r\nAlfredston to Marygreen about three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the afternoon. It was\r\nfine, warm, and soft summer weather, and he walked with his tools at\r\nhis back, his little chisels clinking faintly against the larger ones\r\nin his basket. It being the end of the week he had left work early, and\r\nhad come out of the town by a round-about route which he did not\r\nusually frequent, having promised to call at a flour-mill near\r\nCresscombe to execute a commission for his aunt.\r\n\r\nHe was in an enthusiastic mood. He seemed to see his way to living\r\ncomfortably in Christminster in the course of a year or two, and\r\nknocking at the doors of one of those strongholds of learning of which\r\nhe had dreamed so much. He might, of course, have gone there now, in\r\nsome capacity or other, but he preferred to enter the city with a\r\nlittle more assurance as to means than he could be said to feel at\r\npresent. A warm self-content suffused him when he considered what he\r\nhad already done. Now and then as he went along he turned to face the\r\npeeps of country on either side of him. But he hardly saw them; the act\r\nwas an automatic repetition of what he had been accustomed to do when\r\nless occupied; and the one matter which really engaged him was the\r\nmental estimate of his progress thus far.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have acquired quite an average student\xe2\x80\x99s power to read the common\r\nancient classics, Latin in particular.\xe2\x80\x9d This was true, Jude possessing\r\na facility in that language which enabled him with great ease to\r\nhimself to beguile his lonely walks by imaginary conversations therein.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have read two books of the _Iliad_, besides being pretty familiar\r\nwith passages such as the speech of Ph\xc5\x93nix in the ninth book, the fight\r\nof Hector and Ajax in the fourteenth, the appearance of Achilles\r\nunarmed and his heavenly armour in the eighteenth, and the funeral\r\ngames in the twenty-third. I have also done some Hesiod, a little scrap\r\nof Thucydides, and a lot of the Greek Testament\xe2\x80\xa6 I wish there was only\r\none dialect all the same.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have done some mathematics, including the first six and the eleventh\r\nand twelfth books of Euclid; and algebra as far as simple equations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know something of the Fathers, and something of Roman and English\r\nhistory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese things are only a beginning. But I shall not make much farther\r\nadvance here, from the difficulty of getting books. Hence I must next\r\nconcentrate all my energies on settling in Christminster. Once there I\r\nshall so advance, with the assistance I shall there get, that my\r\npresent knowledge will appear to me but as childish ignorance. I must\r\nsave money, and I will; and one of those colleges shall open its doors\r\nto me\xe2\x80\x94shall welcome whom now it would spurn, if I wait twenty years for\r\nthe welcome.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be D.D. before I have done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he continued to dream, and thought he might become even a\r\nbishop by leading a pure, energetic, wise, Christian life. And what an\r\nexample he would set! If his income were \xc2\xa35000 a year, he would give\r\naway \xc2\xa34500 in one form and another, and live sumptuously (for him) on\r\nthe remainder. Well, on second thoughts, a bishop was absurd. He would\r\ndraw the line at an archdeacon. Perhaps a man could be as good and as\r\nlearned and as useful in the capacity of archdeacon as in that of\r\nbishop. Yet he thought of the bishop again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeanwhile I will read, as soon as I am settled in Christminster, the\r\nbooks I have not been able to get hold of here: Livy, Tacitus,\r\nHerodotus, \xc3\x86schylus, Sophocles, Aristophanes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha, ha! Hoity-toity!\xe2\x80\x9d The sounds were expressed in light voices on\r\nthe other side of the hedge, but he did not notice them. His thoughts\r\nwent on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94Euripides, Plato, Aristotle, Lucretius, Epictetus, Seneca, Antoninus.\r\nThen I must master other things: the Fathers thoroughly; Bede and\r\necclesiastical history generally; a smattering of Hebrew\xe2\x80\x94I only know\r\nthe letters as yet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoity-toity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94but I can work hard. I have staying power in abundance, thank God!\r\nand it is that which tells\xe2\x80\xa6 Yes, Christminster shall be my Alma Mater;\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll be her beloved son, in whom she shall be well pleased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn his deep concentration on these transactions of the future Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwalk had slackened, and he was now standing quite still, looking at the\r\nground as though the future were thrown thereon by a magic lantern. On\r\na sudden something smacked him sharply in the ear, and he became aware\r\nthat a soft cold substance had been flung at him, and had fallen at his\r\nfeet.\r\n\r\nA glance told him what it was\xe2\x80\x94a piece of flesh, the characteristic part\r\nof a barrow-pig, which the countrymen used for greasing their boots, as\r\nit was useless for any other purpose. Pigs were rather plentiful\r\nhereabout, being bred and fattened in large numbers in certain parts of\r\nNorth Wessex.\r\n\r\nOn the other side of the hedge was a stream, whence, as he now for the\r\nfirst time realized, had come the slight sounds of voices and laughter\r\nthat had mingled with his dreams. He mounted the bank and looked over\r\nthe fence. On the further side of the stream stood a small homestead,\r\nhaving a garden and pig-sties attached; in front of it, beside the\r\nbrook, three young women were kneeling, with buckets and platters\r\nbeside them containing heaps of pigs\xe2\x80\x99 chitterlings, which they were\r\nwashing in the running water. One or two pairs of eyes slyly glanced\r\nup, and perceiving that his attention had at last been attracted, and\r\nthat he was watching them, they braced themselves for inspection by\r\nputting their mouths demurely into shape and recommencing their rinsing\r\noperations with assiduity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude severely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _didn\xe2\x80\x99t_ throw it, I tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d asserted one girl to her neighbour,\r\nas if unconscious of the young man\xe2\x80\x99s presence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I,\xe2\x80\x9d the second answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anny, how can you!\xe2\x80\x9d said the third.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had thrown anything at all, it shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh! I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for him!\xe2\x80\x9d And they laughed and continued their\r\nwork, without looking up, still ostentatiously accusing each other.\r\n\r\nJude grew sarcastic as he wiped his face, and caught their remarks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t do it\xe2\x80\x94oh no!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the up-stream one of the three.\r\n\r\nShe whom he addressed was a fine dark-eyed girl, not exactly handsome,\r\nbut capable of passing as such at a little distance, despite some\r\ncoarseness of skin and fibre. She had a round and prominent bosom, full\r\nlips, perfect teeth, and the rich complexion of a Cochin hen\xe2\x80\x99s egg. She\r\nwas a complete and substantial female animal\xe2\x80\x94no more, no less; and Jude\r\nwas almost certain that to her was attributable the enterprise of\r\nattracting his attention from dreams of the humaner letters to what was\r\nsimmering in the minds around him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you\xe2\x80\x99ll never be told,\xe2\x80\x9d said she deedily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhoever did it was wasteful of other people\xe2\x80\x99s property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you want to speak to me, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes; if you like to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I clamber across, or will you come to the plank above here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPerhaps she foresaw an opportunity; for somehow or other the eyes of\r\nthe brown girl rested in his own when he had said the words, and there\r\nwas a momentary flash of intelligence, a dumb announcement of affinity\r\n_in posse_ between herself and him, which, so far as Jude Fawley was\r\nconcerned, had no sort of premeditation in it. She saw that he had\r\nsingled her out from the three, as a woman is singled out in such\r\ncases, for no reasoned purpose of further acquaintance, but in\r\ncommonplace obedience to conjunctive orders from headquarters,\r\nunconsciously received by unfortunate men when the last intention of\r\ntheir lives is to be occupied with the feminine.\r\n\r\nSpringing to her feet, she said: \xe2\x80\x9cBring back what is lying there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was now aware that no message on any matter connected with her\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s business had prompted her signal to him. He set down his\r\nbasket of tools, picked up the scrap of offal, beat a pathway for\r\nhimself with his stick, and got over the hedge. They walked in parallel\r\nlines, one on each bank of the stream, towards the small plank bridge.\r\nAs the girl drew nearer to it, she gave without Jude perceiving it, an\r\nadroit little suck to the interior of each of her cheeks in succession,\r\nby which curious and original man\xc5\x93uvre she brought as by magic upon its\r\nsmooth and rotund surface a perfect dimple, which she was able to\r\nretain there as long as she continued to smile. This production of\r\ndimples at will was a not unknown operation, which many attempted, but\r\nonly a few succeeded in accomplishing.\r\n\r\nThey met in the middle of the plank, and Jude, tossing back her\r\nmissile, seemed to expect her to explain why she had audaciously\r\nstopped him by this novel artillery instead of by hailing him.\r\n\r\nBut she, slyly looking in another direction, swayed herself backwards\r\nand forwards on her hand as it clutched the rail of the bridge; till,\r\nmoved by amatory curiosity, she turned her eyes critically upon him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t think _I_ would shy things at you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are doing this for my father, who naturally doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want anything\r\nthrown away. He makes that into dubbin.\xe2\x80\x9d She nodded towards the\r\nfragment on the grass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made either of the others throw it, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d Jude asked,\r\npolitely accepting her assertion, though he had very large doubts as to\r\nits truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpudence. Don\xe2\x80\x99t tell folk it was I, mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I? I don\xe2\x80\x99t know your name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, no. Shall I tell it to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArabella Donn. I\xe2\x80\x99m living here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must have known it if I had often come this way. But I mostly go\r\nstraight along the high-road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father is a pig-breeder, and these girls are helping me wash the\r\ninnerds for black-puddings and such like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey talked a little more and a little more, as they stood regarding\r\neach other and leaning against the hand-rail of the bridge.
