The\r\nunvoiced call of woman to man, which was uttered very distinctly by\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s personality, held Jude to the spot against his\r\nintention\xe2\x80\x94almost against his will, and in a way new to his experience.\r\nIt is scarcely an exaggeration to say that till this moment Jude had\r\nnever looked at a woman to consider her as such, but had vaguely\r\nregarded the sex as beings outside his life and purposes. He gazed from\r\nher eyes to her mouth, thence to her bosom, and to her full round naked\r\narms, wet, mottled with the chill of the water, and firm as marble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a nice-looking girl you are!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, though the words had\r\nnot been necessary to express his sense of her magnetism.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you should see me Sundays!\xe2\x80\x9d she said piquantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I could?\xe2\x80\x9d he answered\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for you to think on. There\xe2\x80\x99s nobody after me just now, though\r\nthere med be in a week or two.\xe2\x80\x9d She had spoken this without a smile,\r\nand the dimples disappeared.\r\n\r\nJude felt himself drifting strangely, but could not help it. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you\r\nlet me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time she had managed to get back one dimple by turning her face\r\naside for a moment and repeating the odd little sucking operation\r\nbefore mentioned, Jude being still unconscious of more than a general\r\nimpression of her appearance. \xe2\x80\x9cNext Sunday?\xe2\x80\x9d he hazarded. \xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow,\r\nthat is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I call?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe brightened with a little glow of triumph, swept him almost tenderly\r\nwith her eyes in turning, and retracing her steps down the brookside\r\ngrass rejoined her companions.\r\n\r\nJude Fawley shouldered his tool-basket and resumed his lonely way,\r\nfilled with an ardour at which he mentally stood at gaze. He had just\r\ninhaled a single breath from a new atmosphere, which had evidently been\r\nhanging round him everywhere he went, for he knew not how long, but had\r\nsomehow been divided from his actual breathing as by a sheet of glass.\r\nThe intentions as to reading, working, and learning, which he had so\r\nprecisely formulated only a few minutes earlier, were suffering a\r\ncurious collapse into a corner, he knew not how.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s only a bit of fun,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, faintly conscious\r\nthat to common sense there was something lacking, and still more\r\nobviously something redundant in the nature of this girl who had drawn\r\nhim to her which made it necessary that he should assert mere\r\nsportiveness on his part as his reason in seeking her\xe2\x80\x94something in her\r\nquite antipathetic to that side of him which had been occupied with\r\nliterary study and the magnificent Christminster dream. It had been no\r\nvestal who chose _that_ missile for opening her attack on him. He saw\r\nthis with his intellectual eye, just for a short fleeting while, as by\r\nthe light of a falling lamp one might momentarily see an inscription on\r\na wall before being enshrouded in darkness. And then this passing\r\ndiscriminative power was withdrawn, and Jude was lost to all conditions\r\nof things in the advent of a fresh and wild pleasure, that of having\r\nfound a new channel for emotional interest hitherto unsuspected, though\r\nit had lain close beside him. He was to meet this enkindling one of the\r\nother sex on the following Sunday.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the girl had joined her companions, and she silently resumed\r\nher flicking and sousing of the chitterlings in the pellucid stream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatched un, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d laconically asked the girl called Anny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I wish I had thrown something else than that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nregretfully murmured Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord! he\xe2\x80\x99s nobody, though you med think so. He used to drive old\r\nDrusilla Fawley\xe2\x80\x99s bread-cart out at Marygreen, till he \xe2\x80\x99prenticed\r\nhimself at Alfredston. Since then he\xe2\x80\x99s been very stuck up, and always\r\nreading. He wants to be a scholar, they say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what he is, or anything about \xe2\x80\x99n. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it,\r\nmy child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t ye! You needn\xe2\x80\x99t try to deceive us! What did you stay talking\r\nto him for, if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t want un? Whether you do or whether you don\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s as simple as a child. I could see it as you courted on the bridge,\r\nwhen he looked at \xe2\x80\x99ee as if he had never seen a woman before in his\r\nborn days. Well, he\xe2\x80\x99s to be had by any woman who can get him to care\r\nfor her a bit, if she likes to set herself to catch him the right way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next day Jude Fawley was pausing in his bedroom with the sloping\r\nceiling, looking at the books on the table, and then at the black mark\r\non the plaster above them, made by the smoke of his lamp in past\r\nmonths.\r\n\r\nIt was Sunday afternoon, four-and-twenty hours after his meeting with\r\nArabella Donn. During the whole bygone week he had been resolving to\r\nset this afternoon apart for a special purpose,\xe2\x80\x94the re-reading of his\r\nGreek Testament\xe2\x80\x94his new one, with better type than his old copy,\r\nfollowing Griesbach\xe2\x80\x99s text as amended by numerous correctors, and with\r\nvariorum readings in the margin. He was proud of the book, having\r\nobtained it by boldly writing to its London publisher, a thing he had\r\nnever done before.\r\n\r\nHe had anticipated much pleasure in this afternoon\xe2\x80\x99s reading, under the\r\nquiet roof of his great-aunt\xe2\x80\x99s house as formerly, where he now slept\r\nonly two nights a week. But a new thing, a great hitch, had happened\r\nyesterday in the gliding and noiseless current of his life, and he felt\r\nas a snake must feel who has sloughed off its winter skin, and cannot\r\nunderstand the brightness and sensitiveness of its new one.\r\n\r\nHe would not go out to meet her, after all. He sat down, opened the\r\nbook, and with his elbows firmly planted on the table, and his hands to\r\nhis temples, began at the beginning:\r\n\r\n\xce\x97 \xce\x9a\xce\x91\xce\x99\xce\x9d\xce\x97 \xce\x94\xce\x99\xce\x91\xce\x98\xce\x97\xce\x9a\xce\x97.\r\n\r\nHad he promised to call for her? Surely he had! She would wait indoors,\r\npoor girl, and waste all her afternoon on account of him. There was a\r\nsomething in her, too, which was very winning, apart from promises. He\r\nought not to break faith with her. Even though he had only Sundays and\r\nweek-day evenings for reading he could afford one afternoon, seeing\r\nthat other young men afforded so many. After to-day he would never\r\nprobably see her again. Indeed, it would be impossible, considering\r\nwhat his plans were.\r\n\r\nIn short, as if materially, a compelling arm of extraordinary muscular\r\npower seized hold of him\xe2\x80\x94something which had nothing in common with the\r\nspirits and influences that had moved him hitherto. This seemed to care\r\nlittle for his reason and his will, nothing for his so-called elevated\r\nintentions, and moved him along, as a violent schoolmaster a schoolboy\r\nhe has seized by the collar, in a direction which tended towards the\r\nembrace of a woman for whom he had no respect, and whose life had\r\nnothing in common with his own except locality.\r\n\r\n\xce\x97 \xce\x9a\xce\x91\xce\x99\xce\x9d\xce\x97 \xce\x94\xce\x99\xce\x91\xce\x98\xce\x97\xce\x9a\xce\x97 was no more heeded, and the predestinate Jude sprang up\r\nand across the room. Foreseeing such an event he had already arrayed\r\nhimself in his best clothes. In three minutes he was out of the house\r\nand descending by the path across the wide vacant hollow of corn-ground\r\nwhich lay between the village and the isolated house of Arabella in the\r\ndip beyond the upland.\r\n\r\nAs he walked he looked at his watch. He could be back in two hours,\r\neasily, and a good long time would still remain to him for reading\r\nafter tea.\r\n\r\nPassing the few unhealthy fir-trees and cottage where the path joined\r\nthe highway he hastened along, and struck away to the left, descending\r\nthe steep side of the country to the west of the Brown House. Here at\r\nthe base of the chalk formation he neared the brook that oozed from it,\r\nand followed the stream till he reached her dwelling. A smell of\r\npiggeries came from the back, and the grunting of the originators of\r\nthat smell. He entered the garden, and knocked at the door with the\r\nknob of his stick.\r\n\r\nSomebody had seen him through the window, for a male voice on the\r\ninside said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArabella! Here\xe2\x80\x99s your young man come coorting! Mizzle, my girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude winced at the words. Courting in such a businesslike aspect as it\r\nevidently wore to the speaker was the last thing he was thinking of. He\r\nwas going to walk with her, perhaps kiss her; but \xe2\x80\x9ccourting\xe2\x80\x9d was too\r\ncoolly purposeful to be anything but repugnant to his ideas. The door\r\nwas opened and he entered, just as Arabella came downstairs in radiant\r\nwalking attire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake a chair, Mr. What\xe2\x80\x99s-your-name?\xe2\x80\x9d said her father, an energetic,\r\nblack-whiskered man, in the same businesslike tones Jude had heard from\r\noutside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather go out at once, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered to Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll walk up to the Brown House and back, we can do\r\nit in half an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella looked so handsome amid her untidy surroundings that he felt\r\nglad he had come, and all the misgivings vanished that had hitherto\r\nhaunted him.\r\n\r\nFirst they clambered to the top of the great down, during which ascent\r\nhe had occasionally to take her hand to assist her. Then they bore off\r\nto the left along the crest into the ridgeway, which they followed till\r\nit intersected the high-road at the Brown House aforesaid, the spot of\r\nhis former fervid desires to behold Christminster. But he forgot them\r\nnow. He talked the commonest local twaddle to Arabella with greater\r\nzest than he would have felt in discussing all the philosophies with\r\nall the Dons in the recently adored university, and passed the spot\r\nwhere he had knelt to Diana and Ph\xc5\x93bus without remembering that there\r\nwere any such people in the mythology, or that the sun was anything\r\nelse than a useful lamp for illuminating Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s face. An\r\nindescribable lightness of heel served to lift him along; and Jude, the\r\nincipient scholar, prospective D.D., professor, bishop, or what not,\r\nfelt himself honoured and glorified by the condescension of this\r\nhandsome country wench in agreeing to take a walk with him in her\r\nSunday frock and ribbons.\r\n\r\nThey reached the Brown House barn\xe2\x80\x94the point at which he had planned to\r\nturn back. While looking over the vast northern landscape from this\r\nspot they were struck by the rising of a dense volume of smoke from the\r\nneighbourhood of the little town which lay beneath them at a distance\r\nof a couple of miles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a fire,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s run and see it\xe2\x80\x94do! It is not\r\nfar!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tenderness which had grown up in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s bosom left him no will to\r\nthwart her inclination now\xe2\x80\x94which pleased him in affording him excuse\r\nfor a longer time with her. They started off down the hill almost at a\r\ntrot; but on gaining level ground at the bottom, and walking a mile,\r\nthey found that the spot of the fire was much further off than it had\r\nseemed.\r\n\r\nHaving begun their journey, however, they pushed on; but it was not\r\ntill five o\xe2\x80\x99clock that they found themselves on the scene,\xe2\x80\x94the distance\r\nbeing altogether about half-a-dozen miles from Marygreen, and three\r\nfrom Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s. The conflagration had been got under by the time they\r\nreached it, and after a short inspection of the melancholy ruins they\r\nretraced their steps\xe2\x80\x94their course lying through the town of Alfredston.\r\n\r\nArabella said she would like some tea, and they entered an inn of an\r\ninferior class, and gave their order. As it was not for beer they had a\r\nlong time to wait. The maid-servant recognized Jude, and whispered her\r\nsurprise to her mistress in the background, that he, the student \xe2\x80\x9cwho\r\nkept hisself up so particular,\xe2\x80\x9d should have suddenly descended so low\r\nas to keep company with Arabella. The latter guessed what was being\r\nsaid, and laughed as she met the serious and tender gaze of her\r\nlover\xe2\x80\x94the low and triumphant laugh of a careless woman who sees she is\r\nwinning her game.\r\n\r\nThey sat and looked round the room, and at the picture of Samson and\r\nDelilah which hung on the wall, and at the circular beer-stains on the\r\ntable, and at the spittoons underfoot filled with sawdust. The whole\r\naspect of the scene had that depressing effect on Jude which few places\r\ncan produce like a tap-room on a Sunday evening when the setting sun is\r\nslanting in, and no liquor is going, and the unfortunate wayfarer finds\r\nhimself with no other haven of rest.\r\n\r\nIt began to grow dusk. They could not wait longer, really, for the tea,\r\nthey said. \xe2\x80\x9cYet what else can we do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a three-mile\r\nwalk for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we can have some beer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeer, oh yes. I had forgotten that. Somehow it seems odd to come to a\r\npublic-house for beer on a Sunday evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d Jude by this time wished he was out of such an\r\nuncongenial atmosphere; but he ordered the beer, which was promptly\r\nbrought.\r\n\r\nArabella tasted it. \xe2\x80\x9cUgh!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nJude tasted. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand\r\nbeer very much now, it is true. I like it well enough, but it is bad to\r\nread on, and I find coffee better. But this seems all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdulterated\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t touch it!\xe2\x80\x9d She mentioned three or four ingredients\r\nthat she detected in the liquor beyond malt and hops, much to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsurprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much you know!\xe2\x80\x9d he said good-humouredly.\r\n\r\nNevertheless she returned to the beer and drank her share, and they\r\nwent on their way. It was now nearly dark, and as soon as they had\r\nwithdrawn from the lights of the town they walked closer together, till\r\nthey touched each other. She wondered why he did not put his arm round\r\nher waist, but he did not; he merely said what to himself seemed a\r\nquite bold enough thing: \xe2\x80\x9cTake my arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took it, thoroughly, up to the shoulder. He felt the warmth of her\r\nbody against his, and putting his stick under his other arm held with\r\nhis right hand her right as it rested in its place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we are well together, dear, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d he observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said she; adding to herself: \xe2\x80\x9cRather mild!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow fast I have become!\xe2\x80\x9d he was thinking.\r\n\r\nThus they walked till they reached the foot of the upland, where they\r\ncould see the white highway ascending before them in the gloom. From\r\nthis point the only way of getting to Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s was by going up the\r\nincline, and dipping again into her valley on the right. Before they\r\nhad climbed far they were nearly run into by two men who had been\r\nwalking on the grass unseen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese lovers\xe2\x80\x94you find \xe2\x80\x99em out o\xe2\x80\x99 doors in all seasons and\r\nweathers\xe2\x80\x94lovers and homeless dogs only,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the men as they\r\nvanished down the hill.\r\n\r\nArabella tittered lightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we lovers?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you can tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor answer she inclined her head upon his shoulder. Jude took the hint,\r\nand encircling her waist with his arm, pulled her to him and kissed\r\nher.\r\n\r\nThey walked now no longer arm in arm but, as she had desired, clasped\r\ntogether. After all, what did it matter since it was dark, said Jude to\r\nhimself. When they were half-way up the long hill they paused as by\r\narrangement, and he kissed her again. They reached the top, and he\r\nkissed her once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can keep your arm there, if you would like to,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gently.\r\n\r\nHe did so, thinking how trusting she was.\r\n\r\nThus they slowly went towards her home. He had left his cottage at\r\nhalf-past three, intending to be sitting down again to the New\r\nTestament by half-past five. It was nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock when, with another\r\nembrace, he stood to deliver her up at her father\xe2\x80\x99s door.\r\n\r\nShe asked him to come in, if only for a minute, as it would seem so odd\r\notherwise, and as if she had been out alone in the dark. He gave way,\r\nand followed her in. Immediately that the door was opened he found, in\r\naddition to her parents, several neighbours sitting round. They all\r\nspoke in a congratulatory manner, and took him seriously as Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintended partner.\r\n\r\nThey did not belong to his set or circle, and he felt out of place and\r\nembarrassed. He had not meant this: a mere afternoon of pleasant\r\nwalking with Arabella, that was all he had meant. He did not stay\r\nlonger than to speak to her stepmother, a simple, quiet woman without\r\nfeatures or character; and bidding them all good night plunged with a\r\nsense of relief into the track over the down.\r\n\r\nBut that sense was only temporary: Arabella soon re-asserted her sway\r\nin his soul. He walked as if he felt himself to be another man from the\r\nJude of yesterday. What were his books to him? what were his\r\nintentions, hitherto adhered to so strictly, as to not wasting a single\r\nminute of time day by day? \xe2\x80\x9cWasting!\xe2\x80\x9d It depended on your point of view\r\nto define that: he was just living for the first time: not wasting\r\nlife. It was better to love a woman than to be a graduate, or a parson;\r\nay, or a pope!\r\n\r\nWhen he got back to the house his aunt had gone to bed, and a general\r\nconsciousness of his neglect seemed written on the face of all things\r\nconfronting him. He went upstairs without a light, and the dim interior\r\nof his room accosted him with sad inquiry. There lay his book open,\r\njust as he had left it, and the capital letters on the title-page\r\nregarded him with fixed reproach in the grey starlight, like the\r\nunclosed eyes of a dead man:\r\n\r\n\xce\x97 \xce\x9a\xce\x91\xce\x99\xce\x9d\xce\x97 \xce\x94\xce\x99\xce\x91\xce\x98\xce\x97\xce\x9a\xce\x97.\r\n\r\n\r\n*    *    *    *    *    *    *    *    *\r\n\r\nJude had to leave early next morning for his usual week of absence at\r\nlodgings; and it was with a sense of futility that he threw into his\r\nbasket upon his tools and other necessaries the unread book he had\r\nbrought with him.\r\n\r\nHe kept his impassioned doings a secret almost from himself. Arabella,\r\non the contrary, made them public among all her friends and\r\nacquaintances.\r\n\r\nRetracing by the light of dawn the road he had followed a few hours\r\nearlier under cover of darkness, with his sweetheart by his side, he\r\nreached the bottom of the hill, where he walked slowly, and stood\r\nstill. He was on the spot where he had given her the first kiss. As the\r\nsun had only just risen it was possible that nobody had passed there\r\nsince. Jude looked on the ground and sighed. He looked closely, and\r\ncould just discern in the damp dust the imprints of their feet as they\r\nhad stood locked in each other\xe2\x80\x99s arms. She was not there now, and \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nembroidery of imagination upon the stuff of nature\xe2\x80\x9d so depicted her\r\npast presence that a void was in his heart which nothing could fill. A\r\npollard willow stood close to the place, and that willow was different\r\nfrom all other willows in the world. Utter annihilation of the six days\r\nwhich must elapse before he could see her again as he had promised\r\nwould have been his intensest wish if he had had only the week to live.\r\n\r\nAn hour and a half later Arabella came along the same way with her two\r\ncompanions of the Saturday. She passed unheedingly the scene of the\r\nkiss, and the willow that marked it, though chattering freely on the\r\nsubject to the other two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did he tell \xe2\x80\x99ee next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d And she related almost word for word some of his\r\ntenderest speeches. If Jude had been behind the fence he would have\r\nfelt not a little surprised at learning how very few of his sayings and\r\ndoings on the previous evening were private.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got him to care for \xe2\x80\x99ee a bit, \xe2\x80\x99nation if you han\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nAnny judicially. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s well to be you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a few moments Arabella replied in a curiously low, hungry tone of\r\nlatent sensuousness: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got him to care for me: yes! But I want him\r\nto more than care for me; I want him to have me\xe2\x80\x94to marry me! I must\r\nhave him. I can\xe2\x80\x99t do without him. He\xe2\x80\x99s the sort of man I long for. I\r\nshall go mad if I can\xe2\x80\x99t give myself to him altogether! I felt I should\r\nwhen I first saw him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs he is a romancing, straightfor\xe2\x80\x99ard, honest chap, he\xe2\x80\x99s to be had,\r\nand as a husband, if you set about catching him in the right way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella remained thinking awhile. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat med be the right way?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh you don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sarah, the third girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn my word I don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x94No further, that is, than by plain courting, and\r\ntaking care he don\xe2\x80\x99t go too far!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe third girl looked at the second. \xe2\x80\x9cShe _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis clear she don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Anny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd having lived in a town, too, as one may say! Well, we can teach\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee som\xe2\x80\x99at then, as well as you us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And how do you mean\xe2\x80\x94a sure way to gain a man? Take me for an\r\ninnocent, and have done wi\xe2\x80\x99 it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA countryman that\xe2\x80\x99s honourable and serious-minded such as he; God\r\nforbid that I should say a sojer, or sailor, or commercial gent from\r\nthe towns, or any of them that be slippery with poor women! I\xe2\x80\x99d do no\r\nfriend that harm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, such as he, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s companions looked at each other, and turning up their eyes\r\nin drollery began smirking. Then one went up close to Arabella, and,\r\nalthough nobody was near, imparted some information in a low tone, the\r\nother observing curiously the effect upon Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the last-named slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI own I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think of that way! \xe2\x80\xa6\r\nBut suppose he _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ honourable? A woman had better not have tried\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing venture nothing have! Besides, you make sure that he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhonourable before you begin. You\xe2\x80\x99d be safe enough with yours. I wish I\r\nhad the chance! Lots of girls do it; or do you think they\xe2\x80\x99d get married\r\nat all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella pursued her way in silent thought. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try it!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwhispered; but not to them.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nOne week\xe2\x80\x99s end Jude was as usual walking out to his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s at Marygreen\r\nfrom his lodging in Alfredston, a walk which now had large attractions\r\nfor him quite other than his desire to see his aged and morose\r\nrelative. He diverged to the right before ascending the hill with the\r\nsingle purpose of gaining, on his way, a glimpse of Arabella that\r\nshould not come into the reckoning of regular appointments. Before\r\nquite reaching the homestead his alert eye perceived the top of her\r\nhead moving quickly hither and thither over the garden hedge. Entering\r\nthe gate he found that three young unfattened pigs had escaped from\r\ntheir sty by leaping clean over the top, and that she was endeavouring\r\nunassisted to drive them in through the door which she had set open.\r\nThe lines of her countenance changed from the rigidity of business to\r\nthe softness of love when she saw Jude, and she bent her eyes\r\nlanguishingly upon him. The animals took advantage of the pause by\r\ndoubling and bolting out of the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were only put in this morning!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, stimulated to pursue\r\nin spite of her lover\xe2\x80\x99s presence. \xe2\x80\x9cThey were drove from Spaddleholt\r\nFarm only yesterday, where Father bought \xe2\x80\x99em at a stiff price enough.\r\nThey are wanting to get home again, the stupid toads! Will you shut the\r\ngarden gate, dear, and help me to get \xe2\x80\x99em in. There are no men folk at\r\nhome, only Mother, and they\xe2\x80\x99ll be lost if we don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe set himself to assist, and dodged this way and that over the potato\r\nrows and the cabbages. Every now and then they ran together, when he\r\ncaught her for a moment and kissed her. The first pig was got back\r\npromptly; the second with some difficulty; the third a long-legged\r\ncreature, was more obstinate and agile. He plunged through a hole in\r\nthe garden hedge, and into the lane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be lost if I don\xe2\x80\x99t follow \xe2\x80\x99n!\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cCome along with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe rushed in full pursuit out of the garden, Jude alongside her,\r\nbarely contriving to keep the fugitive in sight. Occasionally they\r\nwould shout to some boy to stop the animal, but he always wriggled past\r\nand ran on as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me take your hand, darling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are getting out of\r\nbreath.\xe2\x80\x9d She gave him her now hot hand with apparent willingness, and\r\nthey trotted along together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis comes of driving \xe2\x80\x99em home,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cThey always know the\r\nway back if you do that. They ought to have been carted over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time the pig had reached an unfastened gate admitting to the\r\nopen down, across which he sped with all the agility his little legs\r\nafforded. As soon as the pursuers had entered and ascended to the top\r\nof the high ground it became apparent that they would have to run all\r\nthe way to the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s if they wished to get at him. From this summit\r\nhe could be seen as a minute speck, following an unerring line towards\r\nhis old home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no good!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be there long before we get\r\nthere. It don\xe2\x80\x99t matter now we know he\xe2\x80\x99s not lost or stolen on the way.\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99ll see it is ours, and send un back. Oh dear, how hot I be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout relinquishing her hold of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s hand she swerved aside and\r\nflung herself down on the sod under a stunted thorn, precipitately\r\npulling Jude on to his knees at the same time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I ask pardon\xe2\x80\x94I nearly threw you down, didn\xe2\x80\x99t I! But I am so\r\ntired!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe lay supine, and straight as an arrow, on the sloping sod of this\r\nhill-top, gazing up into the blue miles of sky, and still retaining her\r\nwarm hold of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s hand. He reclined on his elbow near her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve run all this way for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, her form heaving and\r\nfalling in quick pants, her face flushed, her full red lips parted, and\r\na fine dew of perspiration on her skin. \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94why don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak,\r\ndeary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m blown too. It was all up hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were in absolute solitude\xe2\x80\x94the most apparent of all solitudes, that\r\nof empty surrounding space. Nobody could be nearer than a mile to them\r\nwithout their seeing him. They were, in fact, on one of the summits of\r\nthe county, and the distant landscape around Christminster could be\r\ndiscerned from where they lay. But Jude did not think of that then.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I can see such a pretty thing up this tree,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nsort of a\xe2\x80\x94caterpillar, of the most loveliest green and yellow you ever\r\ncame across!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, sitting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t see him there\xe2\x80\x94you must come here,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\nHe bent nearer and put his head in front of hers. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t see it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, on the limb there where it branches off\xe2\x80\x94close to the moving\r\nleaf\xe2\x80\x94there!\xe2\x80\x9d She gently pulled him down beside her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see it,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, the back of his head against her cheek.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can, perhaps, standing up.\xe2\x80\x9d He stood accordingly, placing\r\nhimself in the direct line of her gaze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow stupid you are!\xe2\x80\x9d she said crossly, turning away her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to see it, dear: why should I?\xe2\x80\x9d he replied looking down\r\nupon her. \xe2\x80\x9cGet up, Abby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to let me kiss you. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been waiting to ever so long!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe rolled round her face, remained a moment looking deedily aslant at\r\nhim; then with a slight curl of the lip sprang to her feet, and\r\nexclaiming abruptly \xe2\x80\x9cI must mizzle!\xe2\x80\x9d walked off quickly homeward. Jude\r\nfollowed and rejoined her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust one!\xe2\x80\x9d he coaxed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShan\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe, surprised: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe kept her two lips resentfully together, and Jude followed her like\r\na pet lamb till she slackened her pace and walked beside him, talking\r\ncalmly on indifferent subjects, and always checking him if he tried to\r\ntake her hand or clasp her waist. Thus they descended to the precincts\r\nof her father\xe2\x80\x99s homestead, and Arabella went in, nodding good-bye to\r\nhim with a supercilious, affronted air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect I took too much liberty with her, somehow,\xe2\x80\x9d Jude said to\r\nhimself, as he withdrew with a sigh and went on to Marygreen.\r\n\r\nOn Sunday morning the interior of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s home was, as usual, the\r\nscene of a grand weekly cooking, the preparation of the special Sunday\r\ndinner. Her father was shaving before a little glass hung on the\r\nmullion of the window, and her mother and Arabella herself were\r\nshelling beans hard by. A neighbour passed on her way home from morning\r\nservice at the nearest church, and seeing Donn engaged at the window\r\nwith the razor, nodded and came in.\r\n\r\nShe at once spoke playfully to Arabella: \xe2\x80\x9cI zeed \xe2\x80\x99ee running with\r\n\xe2\x80\x99un\xe2\x80\x94hee-hee! I hope \xe2\x80\x99tis coming to something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella merely threw a look of consciousness into her face without\r\nraising her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s for Christminster, I hear, as soon as he can get there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you heard that lately\xe2\x80\x94quite lately?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Arabella with a\r\njealous, tigerish indrawing of breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no! But it has been known a long time that it is his plan. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\non\xe2\x80\x99y waiting here for an opening. Ah well: he must walk about with\r\nsomebody, I s\xe2\x80\x99pose. Young men don\xe2\x80\x99t mean much now-a-days. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a sip\r\nhere and a sip there with \xe2\x80\x99em. \xe2\x80\x99Twas different in my time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the gossip had departed Arabella said suddenly to her mother: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwant you and Father to go and inquire how the Edlins be, this evening\r\nafter tea. Or no\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s evening service at Fensworth\xe2\x80\x94you can walk to\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh? What\xe2\x80\x99s up to-night, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. Only I want the house to myself. He\xe2\x80\x99s shy; and I can\xe2\x80\x99t get un\r\nto come in when you are here. I shall let him slip through my fingers\r\nif I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind, much as I care for \xe2\x80\x99n!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is fine we med as well go, since you wish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon Arabella met and walked with Jude, who had now for\r\nweeks ceased to look into a book of Greek, Latin, or any other tongue.\r\nThey wandered up the slopes till they reached the green track along the\r\nridge, which they followed to the circular British earth-bank\r\nadjoining, Jude thinking of the great age of the trackway, and of the\r\ndrovers who had frequented it, probably before the Romans knew the\r\ncountry. Up from the level lands below them floated the chime of church\r\nbells. Presently they were reduced to one note, which quickened, and\r\nstopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll go back,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella, who had attended to the sounds.\r\n\r\nJude assented. So long as he was near her he minded little where he\r\nwas. When they arrived at her house he said lingeringly: \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nin. Why are you in such a hurry to go in to-night? It is not near\r\ndark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. She tried the handle of the door and found\r\nit locked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94they are gone to church,\xe2\x80\x9d she added. And searching behind the\r\nscraper she found the key and unlocked the door. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, you\xe2\x80\x99ll come in a\r\nmoment?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked lightly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be all alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude with alacrity, the case being unexpectedly\r\naltered.\r\n\r\nIndoors they went. Did he want any tea? No, it was too late: he would\r\nrather sit and talk to her. She took off her jacket and hat, and they\r\nsat down\xe2\x80\x94naturally enough close together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t touch me, please,\xe2\x80\x9d she said softly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am part egg-shell. Or\r\nperhaps I had better put it in a safe place.\xe2\x80\x9d She began unfastening the\r\ncollar of her gown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said her lover.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn egg\xe2\x80\x94a cochin\xe2\x80\x99s egg. I am hatching a very rare sort. I carry it\r\nabout everywhere with me, and it will get hatched in less than three\r\nweeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you carry it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust here.\xe2\x80\x9d She put her hand into her bosom and drew out the egg,\r\nwhich was wrapped in wool, outside it being a piece of pig\xe2\x80\x99s bladder,\r\nin case of accidents. Having exhibited it to him she put it back, \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nmind you don\xe2\x80\x99t come near me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get it broke, and have to\r\nbegin another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you do such a strange thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an old custom. I suppose it is natural for a woman to want to\r\nbring live things into the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very awkward for me just now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt serves you right. There\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all you can have of me\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had turned round her chair, and, reaching over the back of it,\r\npresented her cheek to him gingerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s very shabby of you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have catched me a minute ago when I had put the egg down!\r\nThere!\xe2\x80\x9d she said defiantly, \xe2\x80\x9cI am without it now!\xe2\x80\x9d She had quickly\r\nwithdrawn the egg a second time; but before he could quite reach her\r\nshe had put it back as quickly, laughing with the excitement of her\r\nstrategy. Then there was a little struggle, Jude making a plunge for it\r\nand capturing it triumphantly. Her face flushed; and becoming suddenly\r\nconscious he flushed also.\r\n\r\nThey looked at each other, panting; till he rose and said: \xe2\x80\x9cOne kiss,\r\nnow I can do it without damage to property; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she had jumped up too. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must find me first!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\n\r\nHer lover followed her as she withdrew. It was now dark inside the\r\nroom, and the window being small he could not discover for a long time\r\nwhat had become of her, till a laugh revealed her to have rushed up the\r\nstairs, whither Jude rushed at her heels.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was some two months later in the year, and the pair had met\r\nconstantly during the interval. Arabella seemed dissatisfied; she was\r\nalways imagining, and waiting, and wondering.\r\n\r\nOne day she met the itinerant Vilbert. She, like all the cottagers\r\nthereabout, knew the quack well, and she began telling him of her\r\nexperiences. Arabella had been gloomy, but before he left her she had\r\ngrown brighter. That evening she kept an appointment with Jude, who\r\nseemed sad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going away,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I ought to go. I think it\r\nwill be better both for you and for me. I wish some things had never\r\nbegun! I was much to blame, I know. But it is never too late to mend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella began to cry. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know it is not too late?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all very well to say! I haven\xe2\x80\x99t told you yet!\xe2\x80\x9d and she looked\r\ninto his face with streaming eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, turning pale. \xe2\x80\x9cNot\xe2\x80\xa6?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! And what shall I do if you desert me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Arabella\xe2\x80\x94how can you say that, my dear! You _know_ I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndesert you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have next to no wages as yet, you know; or perhaps I should have\r\nthought of this before\xe2\x80\xa6 But, of course if that\xe2\x80\x99s the case, we must\r\nmarry! What other thing do you think I could dream of doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought\xe2\x80\x94I thought, deary, perhaps you would go away all the more for\r\nthat, and leave me to face it alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou knew better! Of course I never dreamt six months ago, or even\r\nthree, of marrying. It is a complete smashing up of my plans\xe2\x80\x94I mean my\r\nplans before I knew you, my dear. But what are they, after all! Dreams\r\nabout books, and degrees, and impossible fellowships, and all that.\r\nCertainly we\xe2\x80\x99ll marry: we must!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat night he went out alone, and walked in the dark self-communing. He\r\nknew well, too well, in the secret centre of his brain, that Arabella\r\nwas not worth a great deal as a specimen of womankind. Yet, such being\r\nthe custom of the rural districts among honourable young men who had\r\ndrifted so far into intimacy with a woman as he unfortunately had done,\r\nhe was ready to abide by what he had said, and take the consequences.\r\nFor his own soothing he kept up a factitious belief in her. His idea of\r\nher was the thing of most consequence, not Arabella herself, he\r\nsometimes said laconically.\r\n\r\nThe banns were put in and published the very next Sunday. The people of\r\nthe parish all said what a simple fool young Fawley was. All his\r\nreading had only come to this, that he would have to sell his books to\r\nbuy saucepans. Those who guessed the probable state of affairs,\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s parents being among them, declared that it was the sort of\r\nconduct they would have expected of such an honest young man as Jude in\r\nreparation of the wrong he had done his innocent sweetheart. The parson\r\nwho married them seemed to think it satisfactory too. And so, standing\r\nbefore the aforesaid officiator, the two swore that at every other time\r\nof their lives till death took them, they would assuredly believe,\r\nfeel, and desire precisely as they had believed, felt, and desired\r\nduring the few preceding weeks. What was as remarkable as the\r\nundertaking itself was the fact that nobody seemed at all surprised at\r\nwhat they swore.\r\n\r\nFawley\xe2\x80\x99s aunt being a baker she made him a bride-cake, saying bitterly\r\nthat it was the last thing she could do for him, poor silly fellow; and\r\nthat it would have been far better if, instead of his living to trouble\r\nher, he had gone underground years before with his father and mother.\r\nOf this cake Arabella took some slices, wrapped them up in white\r\nnote-paper, and sent them to her companions in the pork-dressing\r\nbusiness, Anny and Sarah, labelling each packet \xe2\x80\x9c_In remembrance of\r\ngood advice_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prospects of the newly married couple were certainly not very\r\nbrilliant even to the most sanguine mind. He, a stone-mason\xe2\x80\x99s\r\napprentice, nineteen years of age, was working for half wages till he\r\nshould be out of his time. His wife was absolutely useless in a\r\ntown-lodging, where he at first had considered it would be necessary\r\nfor them to live. But the urgent need of adding to income in ever so\r\nlittle a degree caused him to take a lonely roadside cottage between\r\nthe Brown House and Marygreen, that he might have the profits of a\r\nvegetable garden, and utilize her past experiences by letting her keep\r\na pig. But it was not the sort of life he had bargained for, and it was\r\na long way to walk to and from Alfredston every day. Arabella, however,\r\nfelt that all these make-shifts were temporary; she had gained a\r\nhusband; that was the thing\xe2\x80\x94a husband with a lot of earning power in\r\nhim for buying her frocks and hats when he should begin to get\r\nfrightened a bit, and stick to his trade, and throw aside those stupid\r\nbooks for practical undertakings.\r\n\r\nSo to the cottage he took her on the evening of the marriage, giving up\r\nhis old room at his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94where so much of the hard labour at Greek\r\nand Latin had been carried on.\r\n\r\nA little chill overspread him at her first unrobing. A long tail of\r\nhair, which Arabella wore twisted up in an enormous knob at the back of\r\nher head, was deliberately unfastened, stroked out, and hung upon the\r\nlooking-glass which he had bought her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t your own?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a sudden distaste for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94it never is nowadays with the better class.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! Perhaps not in towns. But in the country it is supposed to\r\nbe different. Besides, you\xe2\x80\x99ve enough of your own, surely?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, enough as country notions go. But in town the men expect more,\r\nand when I was barmaid at Aldbrickham\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBarmaid at Aldbrickham?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not exactly barmaid\xe2\x80\x94I used to draw the drink at a public-house\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94just for a little time; that was all. Some people put me up to\r\ngetting this, and I bought it just for a fancy. The more you have the\r\nbetter in Aldbrickham, which is a finer town than all your\r\nChristminsters. Every lady of position wears false hair\xe2\x80\x94the barber\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nassistant told me so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude thought with a feeling of sickness that though this might be true\r\nto some extent, for all that he knew, many unsophisticated girls would\r\nand did go to towns and remain there for years without losing their\r\nsimplicity of life and embellishments. Others, alas, had an instinct\r\ntowards artificiality in their very blood, and became adepts in\r\ncounterfeiting at the first glimpse of it. However, perhaps there was\r\nno great sin in a woman adding to her hair, and he resolved to think no\r\nmore of it.\r\n\r\nA new-made wife can usually manage to excite interest for a few weeks,\r\neven though the prospects of the household ways and means are cloudy.\r\nThere is a certain piquancy about her situation, and her manner to her\r\nacquaintance at the sense of it, which carries off the gloom of facts,\r\nand renders even the humblest bride independent awhile of the real.\r\nMrs. Jude Fawley was walking in the streets of Alfredston one\r\nmarket-day with this quality in her carriage when she met Anny her\r\nformer friend, whom she had not seen since the wedding.\r\n\r\nAs usual they laughed before talking; the world seemed funny to them\r\nwithout saying it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it turned out a good plan, you see!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the girl to the wife.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it would with such as him. He\xe2\x80\x99s a dear good fellow, and you\r\nought to be proud of un.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Fawley quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when do you expect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSsh! Not at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Arabella, Arabella; you be a deep one! Mistaken! well, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclever\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s a real stroke of genius! It is a thing I never thought o\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 all my experience! I never thought beyond bringing about the real\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94not that one could sham it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you be too quick to cry sham! \xe2\x80\x99Twasn\xe2\x80\x99t sham. He\xe2\x80\x99ll shake down, bless\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee\xe2\x80\x94men always do. What can \xe2\x80\x99em do otherwise? Married is married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNevertheless it was with a little uneasiness that Arabella approached\r\nthe time when in the natural course of things she would have to reveal\r\nthat the alarm she had raised had been without foundation. The occasion\r\nwas one evening at bedtime, and they were in their chamber in the\r\nlonely cottage by the wayside to which Jude walked home from his work\r\nevery day. He had worked hard the whole twelve hours, and had retired\r\nto rest before his wife. When she came into the room he was between\r\nsleeping and waking, and was barely conscious of her undressing before\r\nthe little looking-glass as he lay.\r\n\r\nOne action of hers, however, brought him to full cognition. Her face\r\nbeing reflected towards him as she sat, he could perceive that she was\r\namusing herself by artificially producing in each cheek the dimple\r\nbefore alluded to, a curious accomplishment of which she was mistress,\r\neffecting it by a momentary suction. It seemed to him for the first\r\ntime that the dimples were far oftener absent from her face during his\r\nintercourse with her nowadays than they had been in the earlier weeks\r\nof their acquaintance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t do that, Arabella!\xe2\x80\x9d he said suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no harm in it,\r\nbut\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned and laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cLord, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were awake!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow countrified you are! That\xe2\x80\x99s nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you learn it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNowhere that I know of. I made a mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat bolt upright in bed and looked at her. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can that be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen fancy wrong things sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94! What\xe2\x80\x99s done can\xe2\x80\x99t be undone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no more to say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave the answer simply, and lay down; and there was silence between\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nWhen Jude awoke the next morning he seemed to see the world with a\r\ndifferent eye. As to the point in question he was compelled to accept\r\nher word; in the circumstances he could not have acted otherwise while\r\nordinary notions prevailed. But how came they to prevail?\r\n\r\nThere seemed to him, vaguely and dimly, something wrong in a social\r\nritual which made necessary a cancelling of well-formed schemes\r\ninvolving years of thought and labour, of foregoing a man\xe2\x80\x99s one\r\nopportunity of showing himself superior to the lower animals, and of\r\ncontributing his units of work to the general progress of his\r\ngeneration, because of a momentary surprise by a new and transitory\r\ninstinct which had nothing in it of the nature of vice, and could be\r\nonly at the most called weakness. He was inclined to inquire what he\r\nhad done, or she lost, for that matter, that he deserved to be caught\r\nin a gin which would cripple him, if not her also, for the rest of a\r\nlifetime? There was perhaps something fortunate in the fact that the\r\nimmediate reason of his marriage had proved to be non-existent. But the\r\nmarriage remained.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nThe time arrived for killing the pig which Jude and his wife had\r\nfattened in their sty during the autumn months, and the butchering was\r\ntimed to take place as soon as it was light in the morning, so that\r\nJude might get to Alfredston without losing more than a quarter of a\r\nday.\r\n\r\nThe night had seemed strangely silent. Jude looked out of the window\r\nlong before dawn, and perceived that the ground was covered with\r\nsnow\xe2\x80\x94snow rather deep for the season, it seemed, a few flakes still\r\nfalling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid the pig-killer won\xe2\x80\x99t be able to come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99ll come. You must get up and make the water hot, if you want\r\nChallow to scald him. Though I like singeing best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI like the way of my own county.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went downstairs, lit the fire under the copper, and began feeding it\r\nwith bean-stalks, all the time without a candle, the blaze flinging a\r\ncheerful shine into the room; though for him the sense of cheerfulness\r\nwas lessened by thoughts on the reason of that blaze\xe2\x80\x94to heat water to\r\nscald the bristles from the body of an animal that as yet lived, and\r\nwhose voice could be continually heard from a corner of the garden. At\r\nhalf-past six, the time of appointment with the butcher, the water\r\nboiled, and Jude\xe2\x80\x99s wife came downstairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Challow come?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey waited, and it grew lighter, with the dreary light of a snowy\r\ndawn. She went out, gazed along the road, and returning said, \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\ncoming. Drunk last night, I expect. The snow is not enough to hinder\r\nhim, surely!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we must put it off. It is only the water boiled for nothing. The\r\nsnow may be deep in the valley.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t be put off. There\xe2\x80\x99s no more victuals for the pig. He ate the\r\nlast mixing o\xe2\x80\x99 barleymeal yesterday morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday morning? What has he lived on since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94he has been starving?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. We always do it the last day or two, to save bother with the\r\ninnerds. What ignorance, not to know that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat accounts for his crying so. Poor creature!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94you must do the sticking\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s no help for it. I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you\r\nhow. Or I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it myself\xe2\x80\x94I think I could. Though as it is such a big\r\npig I had rather Challow had done it. However, his basket o\xe2\x80\x99 knives and\r\nthings have been already sent on here, and we can use \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you shan\xe2\x80\x99t do it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do it, since it must be\r\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went out to the sty, shovelled away the snow for the space of a\r\ncouple of yards or more, and placed the stool in front, with the knives\r\nand ropes at hand. A robin peered down at the preparations from the\r\nnearest tree, and, not liking the sinister look of the scene, flew\r\naway, though hungry. By this time Arabella had joined her husband, and\r\nJude, rope in hand, got into the sty, and noosed the affrighted animal,\r\nwho, beginning with a squeak of surprise, rose to repeated cries of\r\nrage. Arabella opened the sty-door, and together they hoisted the\r\nvictim on to the stool, legs upward, and while Jude held him Arabella\r\nbound him down, looping the cord over his legs to keep him from\r\nstruggling.\r\n\r\nThe animal\xe2\x80\x99s note changed its quality. It was not now rage, but the cry\r\nof despair; long-drawn, slow and hopeless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my soul I would sooner have gone without the pig than have had\r\nthis to do!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cA creature I have fed with my own hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be such a tender-hearted fool! There\xe2\x80\x99s the sticking-knife\xe2\x80\x94the\r\none with the point. Now whatever you do, don\xe2\x80\x99t stick un too deep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll stick him effectually, so as to make short work of it. That\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nchief thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cThe meat must be well bled, and to do that\r\nhe must die slow. We shall lose a shilling a score if the meat is red\r\nand bloody! Just touch the vein, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. I was brought up to it,\r\nand I know. Every good butcher keeps un bleeding long. He ought to be\r\neight or ten minutes dying, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shall not be half a minute if I can help it, however the meat may\r\nlook,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude determinedly. Scraping the bristles from the pig\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nupturned throat, as he had seen the butchers do, he slit the fat; then\r\nplunged in the knife with all his might.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Od damn it all!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthat ever I should say it! You\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nover-stuck un! And I telling you all the time\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo be quiet, Arabella, and have a little pity on the creature!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold up the pail to catch the blood, and don\xe2\x80\x99t talk!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever unworkmanlike the deed, it had been mercifully done. The blood\r\nflowed out in a torrent instead of in the trickling stream she had\r\ndesired. The dying animal\xe2\x80\x99s cry assumed its third and final tone, the\r\nshriek of agony; his glazing eyes riveting themselves on Arabella with\r\nthe eloquently keen reproach of a creature recognizing at last the\r\ntreachery of those who had seemed his only friends.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake un stop that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch a noise will bring somebody\r\nor other up here, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want people to know we are doing it\r\nourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d Picking up the knife from the ground whereon Jude had flung\r\nit, she slipped it into the gash, and slit the windpipe. The pig was\r\ninstantly silent, his dying breath coming through the hole.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s better,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a hateful business!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPigs must be killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe animal heaved in a final convulsion, and, despite the rope, kicked\r\nout with all his last strength. A tablespoonful of black clot came\r\nforth, the trickling of red blood having ceased for some seconds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it; now he\xe2\x80\x99ll go,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cArtful creatures\xe2\x80\x94they always keep\r\nback a drop like that as long as they can!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last plunge had come so unexpectedly as to make Jude stagger, and\r\nin recovering himself he kicked over the vessel in which the blood had\r\nbeen caught.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, thoroughly in a passion. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I can\xe2\x80\x99t make any\r\nblackpot. There\xe2\x80\x99s a waste, all through you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude put the pail upright, but only about a third of the whole steaming\r\nliquid was left in it, the main part being splashed over the snow, and\r\nforming a dismal, sordid, ugly spectacle\xe2\x80\x94to those who saw it as other\r\nthan an ordinary obtaining of meat. The lips and nostrils of the animal\r\nturned livid, then white, and the muscles of his limbs relaxed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God!\xe2\x80\x9d Jude said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s God got to do with such a messy job as a pig-killing, I should\r\nlike to know!\xe2\x80\x9d she said scornfully. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor folks must live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t scold you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly they became aware of a voice at hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done, young married volk! I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have carried it out much\r\nbetter myself, cuss me if I could!\xe2\x80\x9d The voice, which was husky, came\r\nfrom the garden-gate, and looking up from the scene of slaughter they\r\nsaw the burly form of Mr. Challow leaning over the gate, critically\r\nsurveying their performance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis well for \xe2\x80\x99ee to stand there and glane!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cOwing to\r\nyour being late the meat is blooded and half spoiled! \xe2\x80\x99Twon\xe2\x80\x99t fetch so\r\nmuch by a shilling a score!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChallow expressed his contrition. \xe2\x80\x9cYou should have waited a bit\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, shaking his head, \xe2\x80\x9cand not have done this\xe2\x80\x94in the delicate state,\r\ntoo, that you be in at present, ma\xe2\x80\x99am. \xe2\x80\x99Tis risking yourself too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t be concerned about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella, laughing. Jude\r\ntoo laughed, but there was a strong flavour of bitterness in his\r\namusement.\r\n\r\nChallow made up for his neglect of the killing by zeal in the scalding\r\nand scraping. Jude felt dissatisfied with himself as a man at what he\r\nhad done, though aware of his lack of common sense, and that the deed\r\nwould have amounted to the same thing if carried out by deputy. The\r\nwhite snow, stained with the blood of his fellow-mortal, wore an\r\nillogical look to him as a lover of justice, not to say a Christian;\r\nbut he could not see how the matter was to be mended. No doubt he was,\r\nas his wife had called him, a tender-hearted fool.\r\n\r\nHe did not like the road to Alfredston now. It stared him cynically in\r\nthe face. The wayside objects reminded him so much of his courtship of\r\nhis wife that, to keep them out of his eyes, he read whenever he could\r\nas he walked to and from his work. Yet he sometimes felt that by caring\r\nfor books he was not escaping common-place nor gaining rare ideas,\r\nevery working-man being of that taste now. When passing near the spot\r\nby the stream on which he had first made her acquaintance he one day\r\nheard voices just as he had done at that earlier time. One of the girls\r\nwho had been Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s companions was talking to a friend in a shed,\r\nhimself being the subject of discourse, possibly because they had seen\r\nhim in the distance. They were quite unaware that the shed-walls were\r\nso thin that he could hear their words as he passed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowsomever, \xe2\x80\x99twas I put her up to it! \xe2\x80\x98Nothing venture nothing have,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI said. If I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t she\xe2\x80\x99d no more have been his mis\xe2\x80\x99ess than I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis my belief she knew there was nothing the matter when she told him\r\nshe was\xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat had Arabella been put up to by this woman, so that he should make\r\nher his \xe2\x80\x9cmis\xe2\x80\x99ess,\xe2\x80\x9d otherwise wife? The suggestion was horridly\r\nunpleasant, and it rankled in his mind so much that instead of entering\r\nhis own cottage when he reached it he flung his basket inside the\r\ngarden-gate and passed on, determined to go and see his old aunt and\r\nget some supper there.\r\n\r\nThis made his arrival home rather late. Arabella however, was busy\r\nmelting down lard from fat of the deceased pig, for she had been out on\r\na jaunt all day, and so delayed her work. Dreading lest what he had\r\nheard should lead him to say something regrettable to her he spoke\r\nlittle. But Arabella was very talkative, and said among other things\r\nthat she wanted some money. Seeing the book sticking out of his pocket\r\nshe added that he ought to earn more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn apprentice\xe2\x80\x99s wages are not meant to be enough to keep a wife on, as\r\na rule, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have had one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Arabella! That\xe2\x80\x99s too bad, when you know how it came about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll declare afore Heaven that I thought what I told you was true.\r\nDoctor Vilbert thought so. It was a good job for you that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nso!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean before that time. I know\r\nit was not your fault; but those women friends of yours gave you bad\r\nadvice. If they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t, or you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t taken it, we should at this\r\nmoment have been free from a bond which, not to mince matters, galls\r\nboth of us devilishly. It may be very sad, but it is true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s been telling you about my friends? What advice? I insist upon\r\nyou telling me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99d rather not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you shall\xe2\x80\x94you ought to. It is mean of \xe2\x80\x99ee not to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x9d And he hinted gently what had been revealed to him. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t wish to dwell upon it. Let us say no more about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer defensive manner collapsed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, laughing\r\ncoldly. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery woman has a right to do such as that. The risk is hers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite deny it, Bella. She might if no lifelong penalty attached to\r\nit for the man, or, in his default, for herself; if the weakness of the\r\nmoment could end with the moment, or even with the year. But when\r\neffects stretch so far she should not go and do that which entraps a\r\nman if he is honest, or herself if he is otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat ought I to have done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGiven me time\xe2\x80\xa6 Why do you fuss yourself about melting down that pig\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfat to-night? Please put it away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I must do it to-morrow morning. It won\xe2\x80\x99t keep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\n\r\nNext morning, which was Sunday, she resumed operations about ten\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock; and the renewed work recalled the conversation which had\r\naccompanied it the night before, and put her back into the same\r\nintractable temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the story about me in Marygreen, is it\xe2\x80\x94that I entrapped \xe2\x80\x99ee?\r\nMuch of a catch you were, Lord send!\xe2\x80\x9d As she warmed she saw some of\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s dear ancient classics on a table where they ought not to have\r\nbeen laid. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t have them books here in the way!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried\r\npetulantly; and seizing them one by one she began throwing them upon\r\nthe floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave my books alone!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might have thrown them aside if\r\nyou had liked, but as to soiling them like that, it is disgusting!\xe2\x80\x9d In\r\nthe operation of making lard Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s hands had become smeared with\r\nthe hot grease, and her fingers consequently left very perceptible\r\nimprints on the book-covers. She continued deliberately to toss the\r\nbooks severally upon the floor, till Jude, incensed beyond bearing,\r\ncaught her by the arms to make her leave off. Somehow, in going so, he\r\nloosened the fastening of her hair, and it rolled about her ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me go!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromise to leave the books alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me go!\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromise!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a pause: \xe2\x80\x9cI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude relinquished his hold, and she crossed the room to the door, out\r\nof which she went with a set face, and into the highway. Here she began\r\nto saunter up and down, perversely pulling her hair into a worse\r\ndisorder than he had caused, and unfastening several buttons of her\r\ngown. It was a fine Sunday morning, dry, clear and frosty, and the\r\nbells of Alfredston Church could be heard on the breeze from the north.\r\nPeople were going along the road, dressed in their holiday clothes;\r\nthey were mainly lovers\xe2\x80\x94such pairs as Jude and Arabella had been when\r\nthey sported along the same track some months earlier. These\r\npedestrians turned to stare at the extraordinary spectacle she now\r\npresented, bonnetless, her dishevelled hair blowing in the wind, her\r\nbodice apart, her sleeves rolled above her elbows for her work, and her\r\nhands reeking with melted fat. One of the passers said in mock terror:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord deliver us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee how he\xe2\x80\x99s served me!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cMaking me work Sunday mornings\r\nwhen I ought to be going to my church, and tearing my hair off my head,\r\nand my gown off my back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was exasperated, and went out to drag her in by main force. Then\r\nhe suddenly lost his heat. Illuminated with the sense that all was over\r\nbetween them, and that it mattered not what she did, or he, her husband\r\nstood still, regarding her. Their lives were ruined, he thought; ruined\r\nby the fundamental error of their matrimonial union: that of having\r\nbased a permanent contract on a temporary feeling which had no\r\nnecessary connection with affinities that alone render a lifelong\r\ncomradeship tolerable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to ill-use me on principle, as your father ill-used your mother,\r\nand your father\xe2\x80\x99s sister ill-used her husband?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cAll you be\r\na queer lot as husbands and wives!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude fixed an arrested, surprised look on her. But she said no more,\r\nand continued her saunter till she was tired. He left the spot, and,\r\nafter wandering vaguely a little while, walked in the direction of\r\nMarygreen. Here he called upon his great-aunt, whose infirmities daily\r\nincreased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt\xe2\x80\x94did my father ill-use my mother, and my aunt her husband?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJude abruptly, sitting down by the fire.\r\n\r\nShe raised her ancient eyes under the rim of the by-gone bonnet that\r\nshe always wore. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s been telling you that?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard it spoken of, and want to know all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou med so well, I s\xe2\x80\x99pose; though your wife\xe2\x80\x94I reckon \xe2\x80\x99twas she\xe2\x80\x94must\r\nhave been a fool to open up that! There isn\xe2\x80\x99t much to know after all.\r\nYour father and mother couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get on together, and they parted. It\r\nwas coming home from Alfredston market, when you were a baby\xe2\x80\x94on the\r\nhill by the Brown House barn\xe2\x80\x94that they had their last difference, and\r\ntook leave of one another for the last time. Your mother soon\r\nafterwards died\xe2\x80\x94she drowned herself, in short, and your father went\r\naway with you to South Wessex, and never came here any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude recalled his father\xe2\x80\x99s silence about North Wessex and Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother, never speaking of either till his dying day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the same with your father\xe2\x80\x99s sister. Her husband offended her,\r\nand she so disliked living with him afterwards that she went away to\r\nLondon with her little maid. The Fawleys were not made for wedlock: it\r\nnever seemed to sit well upon us. There\xe2\x80\x99s sommat in our blood that\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t take kindly to the notion of being bound to do what we do readily\r\nenough if not bound. That\xe2\x80\x99s why you ought to have hearkened to me, and\r\nnot ha\xe2\x80\x99 married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did Father and Mother part\xe2\x80\x94by the Brown House, did you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little further on\xe2\x80\x94where the road to Fenworth branches off, and the\r\nhandpost stands. A gibbet once stood there not onconnected with our\r\nhistory. But let that be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the dusk of that evening Jude walked away from his old aunt\xe2\x80\x99s as if\r\nto go home. But as soon as he reached the open down he struck out upon\r\nit till he came to a large round pond. The frost continued, though it\r\nwas not particularly sharp, and the larger stars overhead came out slow\r\nand flickering. Jude put one foot on the edge of the ice, and then the\r\nother: it cracked under his weight; but this did not deter him. He\r\nploughed his way inward to the centre, the ice making sharp noises as\r\nhe went. When just about the middle he looked around him and gave a\r\njump. The cracking repeated itself; but he did not go down. He jumped\r\nagain, but the cracking had ceased. Jude went back to the edge, and\r\nstepped upon the ground.\r\n\r\nIt was curious, he thought. What was he reserved for? He supposed he\r\nwas not a sufficiently dignified person for suicide. Peaceful death\r\nabhorred him as a subject, and would not take him.\r\n\r\nWhat could he do of a lower kind than self-extermination; what was\r\nthere less noble, more in keeping with his present degraded position?\r\nHe could get drunk. Of course that was it; he had forgotten. Drinking\r\nwas the regular, stereotyped resource of the despairing worthless. He\r\nbegan to see now why some men boozed at inns. He struck down the hill\r\nnorthwards and came to an obscure public-house. On entering and sitting\r\ndown the sight of the picture of Samson and Delilah on the wall caused\r\nhim to recognize the place as that he had visited with Arabella on that\r\nfirst Sunday evening of their courtship. He called for liquor and drank\r\nbriskly for an hour or more.\r\n\r\nStaggering homeward late that night, with all his sense of depression\r\ngone, and his head fairly clear still, he began to laugh boisterously,\r\nand to wonder how Arabella would receive him in his new aspect. The\r\nhouse was in darkness when he entered, and in his stumbling state it\r\nwas some time before he could get a light. Then he found that, though\r\nthe marks of pig-dressing, of fats and scallops, were visible, the\r\nmaterials themselves had been taken away. A line written by his wife on\r\nthe inside of an old envelope was pinned to the cotton blower of the\r\nfireplace:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Have gone to my friends. Shall not return._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the next day he remained at home, and sent off the carcase of the\r\npig to Alfredston. He then cleaned up the premises, locked the door,\r\nput the key in a place she would know if she came back, and returned to\r\nhis masonry at Alfredston.\r\n\r\nAt night when he again plodded home he found she had not visited the\r\nhouse. The next day went in the same way, and the next. Then there came\r\na letter from her.\r\n\r\nThat she had gone tired of him she frankly admitted. He was such a slow\r\nold coach, and she did not care for the sort of life he led. There was\r\nno prospect of his ever bettering himself or her. She further went on\r\nto say that her parents had, as he knew, for some time considered the\r\nquestion of emigrating to Australia, the pig-jobbing business being a\r\npoor one nowadays. They had at last decided to go, and she proposed to\r\ngo with them, if he had no objection. A woman of her sort would have\r\nmore chance over there than in this stupid country.\r\n\r\nJude replied that he had not the least objection to her going. He\r\nthought it a wise course, since she wished to go, and one that might be\r\nto the advantage of both. He enclosed in the packet containing the\r\nletter the money that had been realized by the sale of the pig, with\r\nall he had besides, which was not much.\r\n\r\nFrom that day he heard no more of her except indirectly, though her\r\nfather and his household did not immediately leave, but waited till his\r\ngoods and other effects had been sold off. When Jude learnt that there\r\nwas to be an auction at the house of the Donns he packed his own\r\nhousehold goods into a waggon, and sent them to her at the aforesaid\r\nhomestead, that she might sell them with the rest, or as many of them\r\nas she should choose.\r\n\r\nHe then went into lodgings at Alfredston, and saw in a shopwindow the\r\nlittle handbill announcing the sale of his father-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s furniture.\r\nHe noted its date, which came and passed without Jude\xe2\x80\x99s going near the\r\nplace, or perceiving that the traffic out of Alfredston by the southern\r\nroad was materially increased by the auction. A few days later he\r\nentered a dingy broker\xe2\x80\x99s shop in the main street of the town, and amid\r\na heterogeneous collection of saucepans, a clothes-horse, rolling-pin,\r\nbrass candlestick, swing looking-glass, and other things at the back of\r\nthe shop, evidently just brought in from a sale, he perceived a framed\r\nphotograph, which turned out to be his own portrait.\r\n\r\nIt was one which he had had specially taken and framed by a local man\r\nin bird\xe2\x80\x99s-eye maple, as a present for Arabella, and had duly given her\r\non their wedding-day. On the back was still to be read, \xe2\x80\x9c_Jude to\r\nArabella_,\xe2\x80\x9d with the date. She must have thrown it in with the rest of\r\nher property at the auction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the broker, seeing him look at this and the other articles\r\nin the heap, and not perceiving that the portrait was of himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nis a small lot of stuff that was knocked down to me at a cottage sale\r\nout on the road to Marygreen. The frame is a very useful one, if you\r\ntake out the likeness. You shall have it for a shilling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe utter death of every tender sentiment in his wife, as brought home\r\nto him by this mute and undesigned evidence of her sale of his portrait\r\nand gift, was the conclusive little stroke required to demolish all\r\nsentiment in him. He paid the shilling, took the photograph away with\r\nhim, and burnt it, frame and all, when he reached his lodging.\r\n\r\nTwo or three days later he heard that Arabella and her parents had\r\ndeparted. He had sent a message offering to see her for a formal\r\nleave-taking, but she had said that it would be better otherwise, since\r\nshe was bent on going, which perhaps was true. On the evening following\r\ntheir emigration, when his day\xe2\x80\x99s work was done, he came out of doors\r\nafter supper, and strolled in the starlight along the too familiar road\r\ntowards the upland whereon had been experienced the chief emotions of\r\nhis life. It seemed to be his own again.\r\n\r\nHe could not realize himself. On the old track he seemed to be a boy\r\nstill, hardly a day older than when he had stood dreaming at the top of\r\nthat hill, inwardly fired for the first time with ardours for\r\nChristminster and scholarship. \xe2\x80\x9cYet I am a man,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a\r\nwife. More, I have arrived at the still riper stage of having disagreed\r\nwith her, disliked her, had a scuffle with her, and parted from her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe remembered then that he was standing not far from the spot at which\r\nthe parting between his father and his mother was said to have\r\noccurred.\r\n\r\nA little further on was the summit whence Christminster, or what he had\r\ntaken for that city, had seemed to be visible. A milestone, now as\r\nalways, stood at the roadside hard by. Jude drew near it, and felt\r\nrather than read the mileage to the city. He remembered that once on\r\nhis way home he had proudly cut with his keen new chisel an inscription\r\non the back of that milestone, embodying his aspirations. It had been\r\ndone in the first week of his apprenticeship, before he had been\r\ndiverted from his purposes by an unsuitable woman. He wondered if the\r\ninscription were legible still, and going to the back of the milestone\r\nbrushed away the nettles. By the light of a match he could still\r\ndiscern what he had cut so enthusiastically so long ago:\r\n\r\n[THITHER\xe2\x80\x94J. F.
