[with a pointing finger]]\r\n\r\nThe sight of it, unimpaired, within its screen of grass and nettles,\r\nlit in his soul a spark of the old fire. Surely his plan should be to\r\nmove onward through good and ill\xe2\x80\x94to avoid morbid sorrow even though he\r\ndid see uglinesses in the world? _Bene agere et l\xc3\xa6tari_\xe2\x80\x94to do good\r\ncheerfully\xe2\x80\x94which he had heard to be the philosophy of one Spinoza,\r\nmight be his own even now.\r\n\r\nHe might battle with his evil star, and follow out his original\r\nintention.\r\n\r\nBy moving to a spot a little way off he uncovered the horizon in a\r\nnorth-easterly direction. There actually rose the faint halo, a small\r\ndim nebulousness, hardly recognizable save by the eye of faith. It was\r\nenough for him. He would go to Christminster as soon as the term of his\r\napprenticeship expired.\r\n\r\nHe returned to his lodgings in a better mood, and said his prayers.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart Second AT CHRISTMINSTER\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cSave his own soul he hath no star.\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94SWINBURNE.\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cNotitiam primosque gradus vicinia fecit;\r\nTempore crevit amor.\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94OVID.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next noteworthy move in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s life was that in which he appeared\r\ngliding steadily onward through a dusky landscape of some three years\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlater leafage than had graced his courtship of Arabella, and the\r\ndisruption of his coarse conjugal life with her. He was walking towards\r\nChristminster City, at a point a mile or two to the south-west of it.\r\n\r\nHe had at last found himself clear of Marygreen and Alfredston: he was\r\nout of his apprenticeship, and with his tools at his back seemed to be\r\nin the way of making a new start\xe2\x80\x94the start to which, barring the\r\ninterruption involved in his intimacy and married experience with\r\nArabella, he had been looking forward for about ten years.\r\n\r\nJude would now have been described as a young man with a forcible,\r\nmeditative, and earnest rather than handsome cast of countenance. He\r\nwas of dark complexion, with dark harmonizing eyes, and he wore a\r\nclosely trimmed black beard of more advanced growth than is usual at\r\nhis age; this, with his great mass of black curly hair, was some\r\ntrouble to him in combing and washing out the stone-dust that settled\r\non it in the pursuit of his trade. His capabilities in the latter,\r\nhaving been acquired in the country, were of an all-round sort,\r\nincluding monumental stone-cutting, gothic free-stone work for the\r\nrestoration of churches, and carving of a general kind. In London he\r\nwould probably have become specialized and have made himself a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmoulding mason,\xe2\x80\x9d a \xe2\x80\x9cfoliage sculptor\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94perhaps a \xe2\x80\x9cstatuary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had that afternoon driven in a cart from Alfredston to the village\r\nnearest the city in this direction, and was now walking the remaining\r\nfour miles rather from choice than from necessity, having always\r\nfancied himself arriving thus.\r\n\r\nThe ultimate impulse to come had had a curious origin\xe2\x80\x94one more nearly\r\nrelated to the emotional side of him than to the intellectual, as is\r\noften the case with young men. One day while in lodgings at Alfredston\r\nhe had gone to Marygreen to see his old aunt, and had observed between\r\nthe brass candlesticks on her mantlepiece the photograph of a pretty\r\ngirlish face, in a broad hat with radiating folds under the brim like\r\nthe rays of a halo. He had asked who she was. His grand-aunt had\r\ngruffly replied that she was his cousin Sue Bridehead, of the inimical\r\nbranch of the family; and on further questioning the old woman had\r\nreplied that the girl lived in Christminster, though she did not know\r\nwhere, or what she was doing.\r\n\r\nHis aunt would not give him the photograph. But it haunted him; and\r\nultimately formed a quickening ingredient in his latent intent of\r\nfollowing his friend the school master thither.\r\n\r\nHe now paused at the top of a crooked and gentle declivity, and\r\nobtained his first near view of the city. Grey-stoned and dun-roofed,\r\nit stood within hail of the Wessex border, and almost with the tip of\r\none small toe within it, at the northernmost point of the crinkled line\r\nalong which the leisurely Thames strokes the fields of that ancient\r\nkingdom. The buildings now lay quiet in the sunset, a vane here and\r\nthere on their many spires and domes giving sparkle to a picture of\r\nsober secondary and tertiary hues.\r\n\r\nReaching the bottom he moved along the level way between pollard\r\nwillows growing indistinct in the twilight, and soon confronted the\r\noutmost lamps of the town\xe2\x80\x94some of those lamps which had sent into the\r\nsky the gleam and glory that caught his strained gaze in his days of\r\ndreaming, so many years ago. They winked their yellow eyes at him\r\ndubiously, and as if, though they had been awaiting him all these years\r\nin disappointment at his tarrying, they did not much want him now.\r\n\r\nHe was a species of Dick Whittington whose spirit was touched to finer\r\nissues than a mere material gain. He went along the outlying streets\r\nwith the cautious tread of an explorer. He saw nothing of the real city\r\nin the suburbs on this side. His first want being a lodging he\r\nscrutinized carefully such localities as seemed to offer on inexpensive\r\nterms the modest type of accommodation he demanded; and after inquiry\r\ntook a room in a suburb nicknamed \xe2\x80\x9cBeersheba,\xe2\x80\x9d though he did not know\r\nthis at the time. Here he installed himself, and having had some tea\r\nsallied forth.\r\n\r\nIt was a windy, whispering, moonless night. To guide himself he opened\r\nunder a lamp a map he had brought. The breeze ruffled and fluttered it,\r\nbut he could see enough to decide on the direction he should take to\r\nreach the heart of the place.\r\n\r\nAfter many turnings he came up to the first ancient medi\xc3\xa6val pile that\r\nhe had encountered. It was a college, as he could see by the gateway.\r\nHe entered it, walked round, and penetrated to dark corners which no\r\nlamplight reached. Close to this college was another; and a little\r\nfurther on another; and then he began to be encircled as it were with\r\nthe breath and sentiment of the venerable city. When he passed objects\r\nout of harmony with its general expression he allowed his eyes to slip\r\nover them as if he did not see them.\r\n\r\nA bell began clanging, and he listened till a hundred-and-one strokes\r\nhad sounded. He must have made a mistake, he thought: it was meant for\r\na hundred.\r\n\r\nWhen the gates were shut, and he could no longer get into the\r\nquadrangles, he rambled under the walls and doorways, feeling with his\r\nfingers the contours of their mouldings and carving. The minutes\r\npassed, fewer and fewer people were visible, and still he serpentined\r\namong the shadows, for had he not imagined these scenes through ten\r\nbygone years, and what mattered a night\xe2\x80\x99s rest for once? High against\r\nthe black sky the flash of a lamp would show crocketed pinnacles and\r\nindented battlements. Down obscure alleys, apparently never trodden now\r\nby the foot of man, and whose very existence seemed to be forgotten,\r\nthere would jut into the path porticoes, oriels, doorways of enriched\r\nand florid middle-age design, their extinct air being accentuated by\r\nthe rottenness of the stones. It seemed impossible that modern thought\r\ncould house itself in such decrepit and superseded chambers.\r\n\r\nKnowing not a human being here, Jude began to be impressed with the\r\nisolation of his own personality, as with a self-spectre, the sensation\r\nbeing that of one who walked but could not make himself seen or heard.\r\nHe drew his breath pensively, and, seeming thus almost his own ghost,\r\ngave his thoughts to the other ghostly presences with which the nooks\r\nwere haunted.\r\n\r\nDuring the interval of preparation for this venture, since his wife and\r\nfurniture\xe2\x80\x99s uncompromising disappearance into space, he had read and\r\nlearnt almost all that could be read and learnt by one in his position,\r\nof the worthies who had spent their youth within these reverend walls,\r\nand whose souls had haunted them in their maturer age. Some of them, by\r\nthe accidents of his reading, loomed out in his fancy\r\ndisproportionately large by comparison with the rest. The brushings of\r\nthe wind against the angles, buttresses, and door-jambs were as the\r\npassing of these only other inhabitants, the tappings of each ivy leaf\r\non its neighbour were as the mutterings of their mournful souls, the\r\nshadows as their thin shapes in nervous movement, making him comrades\r\nin his solitude. In the gloom it was as if he ran against them without\r\nfeeling their bodily frames.\r\n\r\nThe streets were now deserted, but on account of these things he could\r\nnot go in. There were poets abroad, of early date and of late, from the\r\nfriend and eulogist of Shakespeare down to him who has recently passed\r\ninto silence, and that musical one of the tribe who is still among us.\r\nSpeculative philosophers drew along, not always with wrinkled foreheads\r\nand hoary hair as in framed portraits, but pink-faced, slim, and active\r\nas in youth; modern divines sheeted in their surplices, among whom the\r\nmost real to Jude Fawley were the founders of the religious school\r\ncalled Tractarian; the well-known three, the enthusiast, the poet, and\r\nthe formularist, the echoes of whose teachings had influenced him even\r\nin his obscure home. A start of aversion appeared in his fancy to move\r\nthem at sight of those other sons of the place, the form in the\r\nfull-bottomed wig, statesman, rake, reasoner, and sceptic; the smoothly\r\nshaven historian so ironically civil to Christianity; with others of\r\nthe same incredulous temper, who knew each quad as well as the\r\nfaithful, and took equal freedom in haunting its cloisters.\r\n\r\nHe regarded the statesmen in their various types, men of firmer\r\nmovement and less dreamy air; the scholar, the speaker, the plodder;\r\nthe man whose mind grew with his growth in years, and the man whose\r\nmind contracted with the same.\r\n\r\nThe scientists and philologists followed on in his mind-sight in an odd\r\nimpossible combination, men of meditative faces, strained foreheads,\r\nand weak-eyed as bats with constant research; then official\r\ncharacters\xe2\x80\x94such men as governor-generals and lord-lieutenants, in whom\r\nhe took little interest; chief-justices and lord chancellors, silent\r\nthin-lipped figures of whom he knew barely the names. A keener regard\r\nattached to the prelates, by reason of his own former hopes. Of them he\r\nhad an ample band\xe2\x80\x94some men of heart, others rather men of head; he who\r\napologized for the Church in Latin; the saintly author of the Evening\r\nHymn; and near them the great itinerant preacher, hymn-writer, and\r\nzealot, shadowed like Jude by his matrimonial difficulties.\r\n\r\nJude found himself speaking out loud, holding conversations with them\r\nas it were, like an actor in a melodrama who apostrophizes the audience\r\non the other side of the footlights; till he suddenly ceased with a\r\nstart at his absurdity. Perhaps those incoherent words of the wanderer\r\nwere heard within the walls by some student or thinker over his lamp;\r\nand he may have raised his head, and wondered what voice it was, and\r\nwhat it betokened. Jude now perceived that, so far as solid flesh went,\r\nhe had the whole aged city to himself with the exception of a belated\r\ntownsman here and there, and that he seemed to be catching a cold.\r\n\r\nA voice reached him out of the shade; a real and local voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been a-settin\xe2\x80\x99 a long time on that plinth-stone, young man.\r\nWhat med you be up to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt came from a policeman who had been observing Jude without the latter\r\nobserving him.\r\n\r\nJude went home and to bed, after reading up a little about these men\r\nand their several messages to the world from a book or two that he had\r\nbrought with him concerning the sons of the university. As he drew\r\ntowards sleep various memorable words of theirs that he had just been\r\nconning seemed spoken by them in muttering utterances; some audible,\r\nsome unintelligible to him. One of the spectres (who afterwards mourned\r\nChristminster as \xe2\x80\x9cthe home of lost causes,\xe2\x80\x9d though Jude did not\r\nremember this) was now apostrophizing her thus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautiful city! so venerable, so lovely, so unravaged by the fierce\r\nintellectual life of our century, so serene! \xe2\x80\xa6 Her ineffable charm\r\nkeeps ever calling us to the true goal of all of us, to the ideal, to\r\nperfection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother voice was that of the Corn Law convert, whose phantom he had\r\njust seen in the quadrangle with a great bell. Jude thought his soul\r\nmight have been shaping the historic words of his master-speech:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir, I may be wrong, but my impression is that my duty towards a\r\ncountry threatened with famine requires that that which has been the\r\nordinary remedy under all similar circumstances should be resorted to\r\nnow, namely, that there should be free access to the food of man from\r\nwhatever quarter it may come\xe2\x80\xa6 Deprive me of office to-morrow, you can\r\nnever deprive me of the consciousness that I have exercised the powers\r\ncommitted to me from no corrupt or interested motives, from no desire\r\nto gratify ambition, for no personal gain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the sly author of the immortal Chapter on Christianity: \xe2\x80\x9cHow shall\r\nwe excuse the supine inattention of the Pagan and philosophic world, to\r\nthose evidences [miracles] which were presented by Omnipotence? \xe2\x80\xa6 The\r\nsages of Greece and Rome turned aside from the awful spectacle, and\r\nappeared unconscious of any alterations in the moral or physical\r\ngovernment of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the shade of the poet, the last of the optimists:\r\n\r\nHow the world is made for each of us!\r\n\r\n*    *    *    *    *    *    *    *    *\r\n\r\nAnd each of the Many helps to recruit\r\nThe life of the race by a general plan.\r\n\r\nThen one of the three enthusiasts he had seen just now, the author of\r\nthe _Apologia_:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy argument was \xe2\x80\xa6 that absolute certitude as to the truths of natural\r\ntheology was the result of an assemblage of concurring and converging\r\nprobabilities \xe2\x80\xa6 that probabilities which did not reach to logical\r\ncertainty might create a mental certitude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe second of them, no polemic, murmured quieter things:\r\n\r\nWhy should we faint, and fear to live alone,\r\nSince all alone, so Heaven has will\xe2\x80\x99d, we die?\r\n\r\nHe likewise heard some phrases spoken by the phantom with the short\r\nface, the genial Spectator:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I look upon the tombs of the great, every motion of envy dies in\r\nme; when I read the epitaphs of the beautiful, every inordinate desire\r\ngoes out; when I meet with the grief of parents upon a tombstone, my\r\nheart melts with compassion; when I see the tombs of the parents\r\nthemselves, I consider the vanity of grieving for those whom we must\r\nquickly follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd lastly a gentle-voiced prelate spoke, during whose meek, familiar\r\nrhyme, endeared to him from earliest childhood, Jude fell asleep:\r\n\r\nTeach me to live, that I may dread\r\nThe grave as little as my bed.\r\nTeach me to die\xe2\x80\xa6\r\n\r\nHe did not wake till morning. The ghostly past seemed to have gone, and\r\neverything spoke of to-day. He started up in bed, thinking he had\r\noverslept himself and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove\xe2\x80\x94I had quite forgotten my sweet-faced cousin, and that she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhere all the time! \xe2\x80\xa6 and my old schoolmaster, too.\xe2\x80\x9d His words about his\r\nschoolmaster had, perhaps, less zest in them than his words concerning\r\nhis cousin.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nNecessary meditations on the actual, including the mean\r\nbread-and-cheese question, dissipated the phantasmal for a while, and\r\ncompelled Jude to smother high thinkings under immediate needs. He had\r\nto get up, and seek for work, manual work; the only kind deemed by many\r\nof its professors to be work at all.\r\n\r\nPassing out into the streets on this errand he found that the colleges\r\nhad treacherously changed their sympathetic countenances: some were\r\npompous; some had put on the look of family vaults above ground;\r\nsomething barbaric loomed in the masonries of all. The spirits of the\r\ngreat men had disappeared.\r\n\r\nThe numberless architectural pages around him he read, naturally, less\r\nas an artist-critic of their forms than as an artizan and comrade of\r\nthe dead handicraftsmen whose muscles had actually executed those\r\nforms. He examined the mouldings, stroked them as one who knew their\r\nbeginning, said they were difficult or easy in the working, had taken\r\nlittle or much time, were trying to the arm, or convenient to the tool.\r\n\r\nWhat at night had been perfect and ideal was by day the more or less\r\ndefective real. Cruelties, insults, had, he perceived, been inflicted\r\non the aged erections. The condition of several moved him as he would\r\nhave been moved by maimed sentient beings. They were wounded, broken,\r\nsloughing off their outer shape in the deadly struggle against years,\r\nweather, and man.\r\n\r\nThe rottenness of these historical documents reminded him that he was\r\nnot, after all, hastening on to begin the morning practically as he had\r\nintended. He had come to work, and to live by work, and the morning had\r\nnearly gone. It was, in one sense, encouraging to think that in a place\r\nof crumbling stones there must be plenty for one of his trade to do in\r\nthe business of renovation. He asked his way to the workyard of the\r\nstone-mason whose name had been given him at Alfredston; and soon heard\r\nthe familiar sound of the rubbers and chisels.\r\n\r\nThe yard was a little centre of regeneration. Here, with keen edges and\r\nsmooth curves, were forms in the exact likeness of those he had seen\r\nabraded and time-eaten on the walls. These were the ideas in modern\r\nprose which the lichened colleges presented in old poetry. Even some of\r\nthose antiques might have been called prose when they were new. They\r\nhad done nothing but wait, and had become poetical. How easy to the\r\nsmallest building; how impossible to most men.\r\n\r\nHe asked for the foreman, and looked round among the new traceries,\r\nmullions, transoms, shafts, pinnacles, and battlements standing on the\r\nbankers half worked, or waiting to be removed. They were marked by\r\nprecision, mathematical straightness, smoothness, exactitude: there in\r\nthe old walls were the broken lines of the original idea; jagged\r\ncurves, disdain of precision, irregularity, disarray.\r\n\r\nFor a moment there fell on Jude a true illumination; that here in the\r\nstone yard was a centre of effort as worthy as that dignified by the\r\nname of scholarly study within the noblest of the colleges. But he lost\r\nit under stress of his old idea. He would accept any employment which\r\nmight be offered him on the strength of his late employer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrecommendation; but he would accept it as a provisional thing only.\r\nThis was his form of the modern vice of unrest.\r\n\r\nMoreover he perceived that at best only copying, patching and imitating\r\nwent on here; which he fancied to be owing to some temporary and local\r\ncause. He did not at that time see that medi\xc3\xa6valism was as dead as a\r\nfern-leaf in a lump of coal; that other developments were shaping in\r\nthe world around him, in which Gothic architecture and its associations\r\nhad no place. The deadly animosity of contemporary logic and vision\r\ntowards so much of what he held in reverence was not yet revealed to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nHaving failed to obtain work here as yet he went away, and thought\r\nagain of his cousin, whose presence somewhere at hand he seemed to feel\r\nin wavelets of interest, if not of emotion. How he wished he had that\r\npretty portrait of her! At last he wrote to his aunt to send it. She\r\ndid so, with a request, however, that he was not to bring disturbance\r\ninto the family by going to see the girl or her relations. Jude, a\r\nridiculously affectionate fellow, promised nothing, put the photograph\r\non the mantel-piece, kissed it\xe2\x80\x94he did not know why\xe2\x80\x94and felt more at\r\nhome. She seemed to look down and preside over his tea. It was\r\ncheering\xe2\x80\x94the one thing uniting him to the emotions of the living city.\r\n\r\nThere remained the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x94probably now a reverend parson. But he\r\ncould not possibly hunt up such a respectable man just yet; so raw and\r\nunpolished was his condition, so precarious were his fortunes. Thus he\r\nstill remained in loneliness. Although people moved round him he\r\nvirtually saw none. Not as yet having mingled with the active life of\r\nthe place it was largely non-existent to him. But the saints and\r\nprophets in the window-tracery, the paintings in the galleries, the\r\nstatues, the busts, the gargoyles, the corbel-heads\xe2\x80\x94these seemed to\r\nbreathe his atmosphere. Like all newcomers to a spot on which the past\r\nis deeply graven he heard that past announcing itself with an emphasis\r\naltogether unsuspected by, and even incredible to, the habitual\r\nresidents.\r\n\r\nFor many days he haunted the cloisters and quadrangles of the colleges\r\nat odd minutes in passing them, surprised by impish echoes of his own\r\nfootsteps, smart as the blows of a mallet. The Christminster\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csentiment,\xe2\x80\x9d as it had been called, ate further and further into him;\r\ntill he probably knew more about those buildings materially,\r\nartistically, and historically, than any one of their inmates.\r\n\r\nIt was not till now, when he found himself actually on the spot of his\r\nenthusiasm, that Jude perceived how far away from the object of that\r\nenthusiasm he really was. Only a wall divided him from those happy\r\nyoung contemporaries of his with whom he shared a common mental life;\r\nmen who had nothing to do from morning till night but to read, mark,\r\nlearn, and inwardly digest. Only a wall\xe2\x80\x94but what a wall!\r\n\r\nEvery day, every hour, as he went in search of labour, he saw them\r\ngoing and coming also, rubbed shoulders with them, heard their voices,\r\nmarked their movements. The conversation of some of the more thoughtful\r\namong them seemed oftentimes, owing to his long and persistent\r\npreparation for this place, to be peculiarly akin to his own thoughts.\r\nYet he was as far from them as if he had been at the antipodes. Of\r\ncourse he was. He was a young workman in a white blouse, and with\r\nstone-dust in the creases of his clothes; and in passing him they did\r\nnot even see him, or hear him, rather saw through him as through a pane\r\nof glass at their familiars beyond. Whatever they were to him, he to\r\nthem was not on the spot at all; and yet he had fancied he would be\r\nclose to their lives by coming there.\r\n\r\nBut the future lay ahead after all; and if he could only be so\r\nfortunate as to get into good employment he would put up with the\r\ninevitable. So he thanked God for his health and strength, and took\r\ncourage. For the present he was outside the gates of everything,\r\ncolleges included: perhaps some day he would be inside. Those palaces\r\nof light and leading; he might some day look down on the world through\r\ntheir panes.\r\n\r\nAt length he did receive a message from the stone-mason\xe2\x80\x99s yard\xe2\x80\x94that a\r\njob was waiting for him. It was his first encouragement, and he closed\r\nwith the offer promptly.\r\n\r\nHe was young and strong, or he never could have executed with such zest\r\nthe undertakings to which he now applied himself, since they involved\r\nreading most of the night after working all the day. First he bought a\r\nshaded lamp for four and six-pence, and obtained a good light. Then he\r\ngot pens, paper, and such other necessary books as he had been unable\r\nto obtain elsewhere. Then, to the consternation of his landlady, he\r\nshifted all the furniture of his room\xe2\x80\x94a single one for living and\r\nsleeping\xe2\x80\x94rigged up a curtain on a rope across the middle, to make a\r\ndouble chamber out of one, hung up a thick blind that nobody should\r\nknow how he was curtailing the hours of sleep, laid out his books, and\r\nsat down.\r\n\r\nHaving been deeply encumbered by marrying, getting a cottage, and\r\nbuying the furniture which had disappeared in the wake of his wife, he\r\nhad never been able to save any money since the time of those\r\ndisastrous ventures, and till his wages began to come in he was obliged\r\nto live in the narrowest way. After buying a book or two he could not\r\neven afford himself a fire; and when the nights reeked with the raw and\r\ncold air from the Meadows he sat over his lamp in a great-coat, hat,\r\nand woollen gloves.\r\n\r\nFrom his window he could perceive the spire of the cathedral, and the\r\nogee dome under which resounded the great bell of the city. The tall\r\ntower, tall belfry windows, and tall pinnacles of the college by the\r\nbridge he could also get a glimpse of by going to the staircase. These\r\nobjects he used as stimulants when his faith in the future was dim.\r\n\r\nLike enthusiasts in general he made no inquiries into details of\r\nprocedure. Picking up general notions from casual acquaintance, he\r\nnever dwelt upon them. For the present, he said to himself, the one\r\nthing necessary was to get ready by accumulating money and knowledge,\r\nand await whatever chances were afforded to such an one of becoming a\r\nson of the University. \xe2\x80\x9cFor wisdom is a defence, and money is a\r\ndefence; but the excellency of knowledge is, that wisdom giveth life to\r\nthem that have it.\xe2\x80\x9d His desire absorbed him, and left no part of him to\r\nweigh its practicability.\r\n\r\nAt this time he received a nervously anxious letter from his poor old\r\naunt, on the subject which had previously distressed her\xe2\x80\x94a fear that\r\nJude would not be strong-minded enough to keep away from his cousin Sue\r\nBridehead and her relations. Sue\xe2\x80\x99s father, his aunt believed, had gone\r\nback to London, but the girl remained at Christminster. To make her\r\nstill more objectionable, she was an artist or designer of some sort in\r\nwhat was called an ecclesiastical warehouse, which was a perfect\r\nseed-bed of idolatry, and she was no doubt abandoned to mummeries on\r\nthat account\xe2\x80\x94if not quite a Papist. (Miss Drusilla Fawley was of her\r\ndate, Evangelical.)\r\n\r\nAs Jude was rather on an intellectual track than a theological, this\r\nnews of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s probable opinions did not much influence him one way or\r\nthe other, but the clue to her whereabouts was decidedly interesting.\r\nWith an altogether singular pleasure he walked at his earliest spare\r\nminutes past the shops answering to his great-aunt\xe2\x80\x99s description; and\r\nbeheld in one of them a young girl sitting behind a desk, who was\r\nsuspiciously like the original of the portrait. He ventured to enter on\r\na trivial errand, and having made his purchase lingered on the scene.\r\nThe shop seemed to be kept entirely by women. It contained Anglican\r\nbooks, stationery, texts, and fancy goods: little plaster angels on\r\nbrackets, Gothic-framed pictures of saints, ebony crosses that were\r\nalmost crucifixes, prayer-books that were almost missals. He felt very\r\nshy of looking at the girl in the desk; she was so pretty that he could\r\nnot believe it possible that she should belong to him. Then she spoke\r\nto one of the two older women behind the counter; and he recognized in\r\nthe accents certain qualities of his own voice; softened and sweetened,\r\nbut his own. What was she doing? He stole a glance round. Before her\r\nlay a piece of zinc, cut to the shape of a scroll three or four feet\r\nlong, and coated with a dead-surface paint on one side. Hereon she was\r\ndesigning or illuminating, in characters of Church text, the single\r\nword\r\n\r\n[A L L E L U J A]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA sweet, saintly, Christian business, hers!\xe2\x80\x9d thought he.\r\n\r\nHer presence here was now fairly enough explained, her skill in work of\r\nthis sort having no doubt been acquired from her father\xe2\x80\x99s occupation as\r\nan ecclesiastical worker in metal. The lettering on which she was\r\nengaged was clearly intended to be fixed up in some chancel to assist\r\ndevotion.\r\n\r\nHe came out. It would have been easy to speak to her there and then,\r\nbut it seemed scarcely honourable towards his aunt to disregard her\r\nrequest so incontinently. She had used him roughly, but she had brought\r\nhim up: and the fact of her being powerless to control him lent a\r\npathetic force to a wish that would have been inoperative as an\r\nargument.\r\n\r\nSo Jude gave no sign. He would not call upon Sue just yet. He had other\r\nreasons against doing so when he had walked away. She seemed so dainty\r\nbeside himself in his rough working-jacket and dusty trousers that he\r\nfelt he was as yet unready to encounter her, as he had felt about Mr.\r\nPhillotson. And how possible it was that she had inherited the\r\nantipathies of her family, and would scorn him, as far as a Christian\r\ncould, particularly when he had told her that unpleasant part of his\r\nhistory which had resulted in his becoming enchained to one of her own\r\nsex whom she would certainly not admire.\r\n\r\nThus he kept watch over her, and liked to feel she was there. The\r\nconsciousness of her living presence stimulated him. But she remained\r\nmore or less an ideal character, about whose form he began to weave\r\ncurious and fantastic day-dreams.\r\n\r\nBetween two and three weeks afterwards Jude was engaged with some more\r\nmen, outside Crozier College in Old-time Street, in getting a block of\r\nworked freestone from a waggon across the pavement, before hoisting it\r\nto the parapet which they were repairing. Standing in position the head\r\nman said, \xe2\x80\x9cSpaik when he heave! He-ho!\xe2\x80\x9d And they heaved.\r\n\r\nAll of a sudden, as he lifted, his cousin stood close to his elbow,\r\npausing a moment on the bend of her foot till the obstructing object\r\nshould have been removed. She looked right into his face with liquid,\r\nuntranslatable eyes, that combined, or seemed to him to combine,\r\nkeenness with tenderness, and mystery with both, their expression, as\r\nwell as that of her lips, taking its life from some words just spoken\r\nto a companion, and being carried on into his face quite unconsciously.\r\nShe no more observed his presence than that of the dust-motes which his\r\nmanipulations raised into the sunbeams.\r\n\r\nHis closeness to her was so suggestive that he trembled, and turned his\r\nface away with a shy instinct to prevent her recognizing him, though as\r\nshe had never once seen him she could not possibly do so; and might\r\nvery well never have heard even his name. He could perceive that though\r\nshe was a country-girl at bottom, a latter girlhood of some years in\r\nLondon, and a womanhood here, had taken all rawness out of her.\r\n\r\nWhen she was gone he continued his work, reflecting on her. He had been\r\nso caught by her influence that he had taken no count of her general\r\nmould and build. He remembered now that she was not a large figure,\r\nthat she was light and slight, of the type dubbed elegant. That was\r\nabout all he had seen. There was nothing statuesque in her; all was\r\nnervous motion. She was mobile, living, yet a painter might not have\r\ncalled her handsome or beautiful. But the much that she was surprised\r\nhim. She was quite a long way removed from the rusticity that was his.\r\nHow could one of his cross-grained, unfortunate, almost accursed stock,\r\nhave contrived to reach this pitch of niceness? London had done it, he\r\nsupposed.\r\n\r\nFrom this moment the emotion which had been accumulating in his breast\r\nas the bottled-up effect of solitude and the poetized locality he dwelt\r\nin, insensibly began to precipitate itself on this half-visionary form;\r\nand he perceived that, whatever his obedient wish in a contrary\r\ndirection, he would soon be unable to resist the desire to make himself\r\nknown to her.\r\n\r\nHe affected to think of her quite in a family way, since there were\r\ncrushing reasons why he should not and could not think of her in any\r\nother.\r\n\r\nThe first reason was that he was married, and it would be wrong. The\r\nsecond was that they were cousins. It was not well for cousins to fall\r\nin love even when circumstances seemed to favour the passion. The\r\nthird: even were he free, in a family like his own where marriage\r\nusually meant a tragic sadness, marriage with a blood-relation would\r\nduplicate the adverse conditions, and a tragic sadness might be\r\nintensified to a tragic horror.\r\n\r\nTherefore, again, he would have to think of Sue with only a relation\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmutual interest in one belonging to him; regard her in a practical way\r\nas some one to be proud of; to talk and nod to; later on, to be invited\r\nto tea by, the emotion spent on her being rigorously that of a kinsman\r\nand well-wisher. So would she be to him a kindly star, an elevating\r\npower, a companion in Anglican worship, a tender friend.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nBut under the various deterrent influences Jude\xe2\x80\x99s instinct was to\r\napproach her timidly, and the next Sunday he went to the morning\r\nservice in the Cathedral church of Cardinal College to gain a further\r\nview of her, for he had found that she frequently attended there.\r\n\r\nShe did not come, and he awaited her in the afternoon, which was finer.\r\nHe knew that if she came at all she would approach the building along\r\nthe eastern side of the great green quadrangle from which it was\r\naccessible, and he stood in a corner while the bell was going. A few\r\nminutes before the hour for service she appeared as one of the figures\r\nwalking along under the college walls, and at sight of her he advanced\r\nup the side opposite, and followed her into the building, more than\r\never glad that he had not as yet revealed himself. To see her, and to\r\nbe himself unseen and unknown, was enough for him at present.\r\n\r\nHe lingered awhile in the vestibule, and the service was some way\r\nadvanced when he was put into a seat. It was a louring, mournful, still\r\nafternoon, when a religion of some sort seems a necessity to ordinary\r\npractical men, and not only a luxury of the emotional and leisured\r\nclasses. In the dim light and the baffling glare of the clerestory\r\nwindows he could discern the opposite worshippers indistinctly only,\r\nbut he saw that Sue was among them. He had not long discovered the\r\nexact seat that she occupied when the chanting of the 119th Psalm in\r\nwhich the choir was engaged reached its second part, _In quo corriget_,\r\nthe organ changing to a pathetic Gregorian tune as the singers gave\r\nforth:\r\n\r\nWherewithal shall a young man cleanse his way?\r\n\r\nIt was the very question that was engaging Jude\xe2\x80\x99s attention at this\r\nmoment. What a wicked worthless fellow he had been to give vent as he\r\nhad done to an animal passion for a woman, and allow it to lead to such\r\ndisastrous consequences; then to think of putting an end to himself;\r\nthen to go recklessly and get drunk. The great waves of pedal music\r\ntumbled round the choir, and, nursed on the supernatural as he had\r\nbeen, it is not wonderful that he could hardly believe that the psalm\r\nwas not specially set by some regardful Providence for this moment of\r\nhis first entry into the solemn building. And yet it was the ordinary\r\npsalm for the twenty-fourth evening of the month.\r\n\r\nThe girl for whom he was beginning to nourish an extraordinary\r\ntenderness was at this time ensphered by the same harmonies as those\r\nwhich floated into his ears; and the thought was a delight to him. She\r\nwas probably a frequenter of this place, and, steeped body and soul in\r\nchurch sentiment as she must be by occupation and habit, had, no doubt,\r\nmuch in common with him. To an impressionable and lonely young man the\r\nconsciousness of having at last found anchorage for his thoughts, which\r\npromised to supply both social and spiritual possibilities, was like\r\nthe dew of Hermon, and he remained throughout the service in a\r\nsustaining atmosphere of ecstasy.\r\n\r\nThough he was loth to suspect it, some people might have said to him\r\nthat the atmosphere blew as distinctly from Cyprus as from Galilee.\r\n\r\nJude waited till she had left her seat and passed under the screen\r\nbefore he himself moved. She did not look towards him, and by the time\r\nhe reached the door she was half-way down the broad path. Being dressed\r\nup in his Sunday suit he was inclined to follow her and reveal himself.\r\nBut he was not quite ready; and, alas, ought he to do so with the kind\r\nof feeling that was awakening in him?\r\n\r\nFor though it had seemed to have an ecclesiastical basis during the\r\nservice, and he had persuaded himself that such was the case, he could\r\nnot altogether be blind to the real nature of the magnetism. She was\r\nsuch a stranger that the kinship was affectation, and he said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t be! I, a man with a wife, must not know her!\xe2\x80\x9d Still Sue _was_ his\r\nown kin, and the fact of his having a wife, even though she was not in\r\nevidence in this hemisphere, might be a help in one sense. It would put\r\nall thought of a tender wish on his part out of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and make\r\nher intercourse with him free and fearless. It was with some heartache\r\nthat he saw how little he cared for the freedom and fearlessness that\r\nwould result in her from such knowledge.\r\n\r\n\r\nSome little time before the date of this service in the cathedral the\r\npretty, liquid-eyed, light-footed young woman, Sue Bridehead, had an\r\nafternoon\xe2\x80\x99s holiday, and leaving the ecclesiastical establishment in\r\nwhich she not only assisted but lodged, took a walk into the country\r\nwith a book in her hand. It was one of those cloudless days which\r\nsometimes occur in Wessex and elsewhere between days of cold and wet,\r\nas if intercalated by caprice of the weather-god. She went along for a\r\nmile or two until she came to much higher ground than that of the city\r\nshe had left behind her. The road passed between green fields, and\r\ncoming to a stile Sue paused there, to finish the page she was reading,\r\nand then looked back at the towers and domes and pinnacles new and old.\r\n\r\nOn the other side of the stile, in the footpath, she beheld a foreigner\r\nwith black hair and a sallow face, sitting on the grass beside a large\r\nsquare board whereon were fixed, as closely as they could stand, a\r\nnumber of plaster statuettes, some of them bronzed, which he was\r\nre-arranging before proceeding with them on his way. They were in the\r\nmain reduced copies of ancient marbles, and comprised divinities of a\r\nvery different character from those the girl was accustomed to see\r\nportrayed, among them being a Venus of standard pattern, a Diana, and,\r\nof the other sex, Apollo, Bacchus, and Mars. Though the figures were\r\nmany yards away from her the south-west sun brought them out so\r\nbrilliantly against the green herbage that she could discern their\r\ncontours with luminous distinctness; and being almost in a line between\r\nherself and the church towers of the city they awoke in her an oddly\r\nforeign and contrasting set of ideas by comparison. The man rose, and,\r\nseeing her, politely took off his cap, and cried, \xe2\x80\x9cI-i-i-mages!\xe2\x80\x9d in an\r\naccent that agreed with his appearance. In a moment he dexterously\r\nlifted upon his knee the great board with its assembled notabilities\r\ndivine and human, and raised it to the top of his head, bringing them\r\non to her and resting the board on the stile. First he offered her his\r\nsmaller wares\xe2\x80\x94the busts of kings and queens, then a minstrel, then a\r\nwinged Cupid. She shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much are these two?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, touching with her finger the Venus\r\nand the Apollo\xe2\x80\x94the largest figures on the tray.\r\n\r\nHe said she should have them for ten shillings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot afford that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. She offered considerably less, and to\r\nher surprise the image-man drew them from their wire stay and handed\r\nthem over the stile. She clasped them as treasures.\r\n\r\nWhen they were paid for, and the man had gone, she began to be\r\nconcerned as to what she should do with them. They seemed so very large\r\nnow that they were in her possession, and so very naked. Being of a\r\nnervous temperament she trembled at her enterprise. When she handled\r\nthem the white pipeclay came off on her gloves and jacket. After\r\ncarrying them along a little way openly an idea came to her, and,\r\npulling some huge burdock leaves, parsley, and other rank growths from\r\nthe hedge, she wrapped up her burden as well as she could in these, so\r\nthat what she carried appeared to be an enormous armful of green stuff\r\ngathered by a zealous lover of nature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anything is better than those everlasting church fallals!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. But she was still in a trembling state, and seemed almost to wish\r\nshe had not bought the figures.\r\n\r\nOccasionally peeping inside the leaves to see that Venus\xe2\x80\x99s arm was not\r\nbroken, she entered with her heathen load into the most Christian city\r\nin the country by an obscure street running parallel to the main one,\r\nand round a corner to the side door of the establishment to which she\r\nwas attached. Her purchases were taken straight up to her own chamber,\r\nand she at once attempted to lock them in a box that was her very own\r\nproperty; but finding them too cumbersome she wrapped them in large\r\nsheets of brown paper, and stood them on the floor in a corner.\r\n\r\nThe mistress of the house, Miss Fontover, was an elderly lady in\r\nspectacles, dressed almost like an abbess; a dab at Ritual, as become\r\none of her business, and a worshipper at the ceremonial church of St.\r\nSilas, in the suburb of Beersheba before-mentioned, which Jude also had\r\nbegun to attend. She was the daughter of a clergyman in reduced\r\ncircumstances, and at his death, which had occurred several years\r\nbefore this date, she boldly avoided penury by taking over a little\r\nshop of church requisites and developing it to its present creditable\r\nproportions. She wore a cross and beads round her neck as her only\r\nornament, and knew the Christian Year by heart.\r\n\r\nShe now came to call Sue to tea, and, finding that the girl did not\r\nrespond for a moment, entered the room just as the other was hastily\r\nputting a string round each parcel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething you have been buying, Miss Bridehead?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, regarding\r\nthe enwrapped objects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94just something to ornament my room,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I should have thought I had put enough here already,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFontover, looking round at the Gothic-framed prints of saints, the\r\nChurch-text scrolls, and other articles which, having become too stale\r\nto sell, had been used to furnish this obscure chamber. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\r\nHow bulky!\xe2\x80\x9d She tore a little hole, about as big as a wafer, in the\r\nbrown paper, and tried to peep in. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, statuary? Two figures? Where\r\ndid you get them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I bought them of a travelling man who sells casts\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo saints?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat ones?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSt. Peter and St.\xe2\x80\x94St. Mary Magdalen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94now come down to tea, and go and finish that organ-text, if\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s light enough afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese little obstacles to the indulgence of what had been the merest\r\npassing fancy created in Sue a great zest for unpacking her objects and\r\nlooking at them; and at bedtime, when she was sure of being\r\nundisturbed, she unrobed the divinities in comfort. Placing the pair of\r\nfigures on the chest of drawers, a candle on each side of them, she\r\nwithdrew to the bed, flung herself down thereon, and began reading a\r\nbook she had taken from her box, which Miss Fontover knew nothing of.\r\nIt was a volume of Gibbon, and she read the chapter dealing with the\r\nreign of Julian the Apostate. Occasionally she looked up at the\r\nstatuettes, which appeared strange and out of place, there happening to\r\nbe a Calvary print hanging between them, and, as if the scene suggested\r\nthe action, she at length jumped up and withdrew another book from her\r\nbox\xe2\x80\x94a volume of verse\xe2\x80\x94and turned to the familiar poem\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nThou hast conquered, O pale Galilean:\r\nThe world has grown grey from thy breath!\r\n\r\nwhich she read to the end. Presently she put out the candles,\r\nundressed, and finally extinguished her own light.\r\n\r\nShe was of an age which usually sleeps soundly, yet to-night she kept\r\nwaking up, and every time she opened her eyes there was enough diffused\r\nlight from the street to show her the white plaster figures, standing\r\non the chest of drawers in odd contrast to their environment of text\r\nand martyr, and the Gothic-framed Crucifix-picture that was only\r\ndiscernible now as a Latin cross, the figure thereon being obscured by\r\nthe shades.\r\n\r\nOn one of these occasions the church clocks struck some small hour. It\r\nfell upon the ears of another person who sat bending over his books at\r\na not very distant spot in the same city. Being Saturday night the\r\nmorrow was one on which Jude had not set his alarm-clock to call him at\r\nhis usually early time, and hence he had stayed up, as was his custom,\r\ntwo or three hours later than he could afford to do on any other day of\r\nthe week. Just then he was earnestly reading from his Griesbach\xe2\x80\x99s text.\r\nAt the very time that Sue was tossing and staring at her figures, the\r\npoliceman and belated citizens passing along under his window might\r\nhave heard, if they had stood still, strange syllables mumbled with\r\nfervour within\xe2\x80\x94words that had for Jude an indescribable enchantment:\r\ninexplicable sounds something like these:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_All hemin heis Theos ho Pater, ex hou ta panta, kai hemeis eis\r\nauton:_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTill the sounds rolled with reverent loudness, as a book was heard to\r\nclose:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Kai heis Kurios Iesous Christos, di hou ta panta kai hemeis di\r\nautou!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\n\r\nHe was a handy man at his trade, an all-round man, as artizans in\r\ncountry-towns are apt to be. In London the man who carves the boss or\r\nknob of leafage declines to cut the fragment of moulding which merges\r\nin that leafage, as if it were a degradation to do the second half of\r\none whole. When there was not much Gothic moulding for Jude to run, or\r\nmuch window-tracery on the bankers, he would go out lettering monuments\r\nor tombstones, and take a pleasure in the change of handiwork.\r\n\r\nThe next time that he saw her was when he was on a ladder executing a\r\njob of this sort inside one of the churches. There was a short morning\r\nservice, and when the parson entered Jude came down from his ladder,\r\nand sat with the half-dozen people forming the congregation, till the\r\nprayer should be ended, and he could resume his tapping. He did not\r\nobserve till the service was half over that one of the women was Sue,\r\nwho had perforce accompanied the elderly Miss Fontover thither.\r\n\r\nJude sat watching her pretty shoulders, her easy, curiously nonchalant\r\nrisings and sittings, and her perfunctory genuflexions, and thought\r\nwhat a help such an Anglican would have been to him in happier\r\ncircumstances. It was not so much his anxiety to get on with his work\r\nthat made him go up to it immediately the worshipers began to take\r\ntheir leave: it was that he dared not, in this holy spot, confront the\r\nwoman who was beginning to influence him in such an indescribable\r\nmanner. Those three enormous reasons why he must not attempt intimate\r\nacquaintance with Sue Bridehead, now that his interest in her had shown\r\nitself to be unmistakably of a sexual kind, loomed as stubbornly as\r\never. But it was also obvious that man could not live by work alone;\r\nthat the particular man Jude, at any rate, wanted something to love.\r\nSome men would have rushed incontinently to her, snatched the pleasure\r\nof easy friendship which she could hardly refuse, and have left the\r\nrest to chance. Not so Jude\xe2\x80\x94at first.\r\n\r\nBut as the days, and still more particularly the lonely evenings,\r\ndragged along, he found himself, to his moral consternation, to be\r\nthinking more of her instead of thinking less of her, and experiencing\r\na fearful bliss in doing what was erratic, informal, and unexpected.\r\nSurrounded by her influence all day, walking past the spots she\r\nfrequented, he was always thinking of her, and was obliged to own to\r\nhimself that his conscience was likely to be the loser in this battle.\r\n\r\nTo be sure she was almost an ideality to him still. Perhaps to know her\r\nwould be to cure himself of this unexpected and unauthorized passion. A\r\nvoice whispered that, though he desired to know her, he did not desire\r\nto be cured.\r\n\r\nThere was not the least doubt that from his own orthodox point of view\r\nthe situation was growing immoral. For Sue to be the loved one of a man\r\nwho was licensed by the laws of his country to love Arabella and none\r\nother unto his life\xe2\x80\x99s end, was a pretty bad second beginning when the\r\nman was bent on such a course as Jude purposed. This conviction was so\r\nreal with him that one day when, as was frequent, he was at work in a\r\nneighbouring village church alone, he felt it to be his duty to pray\r\nagainst his weakness. But much as he wished to be an exemplar in these\r\nthings he could not get on. It was quite impossible, he found, to ask\r\nto be delivered from temptation when your heart\xe2\x80\x99s desire was to be\r\ntempted unto seventy times seven. So he excused himself. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter all,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not altogether an _erotolepsy_ that is the matter with\r\nme, as at that first time. I can see that she is exceptionally bright;\r\nand it is partly a wish for intellectual sympathy, and a craving for\r\nloving-kindness in my solitude.\xe2\x80\x9d Thus he went on adoring her, fearing\r\nto realize that it was human perversity. For whatever Sue\xe2\x80\x99s virtues,\r\ntalents, or ecclesiastical saturation, it was certain that those items\r\nwere not at all the cause of his affection for her.\r\n\r\nOn an afternoon at this time a young girl entered the stone-mason\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyard with some hesitation, and, lifting her skirts to avoid draggling\r\nthem in the white dust, crossed towards the office.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a nice girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the men known as Uncle Joe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is she?\xe2\x80\x9d asked another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen her about here and there. Why, yes, she\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ndaughter of that clever chap Bridehead who did all the wrought ironwork\r\nat St. Silas\xe2\x80\x99 ten years ago, and went away to London afterwards. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what he\xe2\x80\x99s doing now\xe2\x80\x94not much I fancy\xe2\x80\x94as she\xe2\x80\x99s come back\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the young woman had knocked at the office door and asked if\r\nMr. Jude Fawley was at work in the yard. It so happened that Jude had\r\ngone out somewhere or other that afternoon, which information she\r\nreceived with a look of disappointment, and went away immediately. When\r\nJude returned they told him, and described her, whereupon he exclaimed,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s my cousin Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked along the street after her, but she was out of sight. He had\r\nno longer any thought of a conscientious avoidance of her, and resolved\r\nto call upon her that very evening. And when he reached his lodging he\r\nfound a note from her\xe2\x80\x94a first note\xe2\x80\x94one of those documents which, simple\r\nand commonplace in themselves, are seen retrospectively to have been\r\npregnant with impassioned consequences. The very unconsciousness of a\r\nlooming drama which is shown in such innocent first epistles from women\r\nto men, or _vice versa_, makes them, when such a drama follows, and\r\nthey are read over by the purple or lurid light of it, all the more\r\nimpressive, solemn, and in cases, terrible.\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s was of the most artless and natural kind. She addressed him as\r\nher dear cousin Jude; said she had only just learnt by the merest\r\naccident that he was living in Christminster, and reproached him with\r\nnot letting her know. They might have had such nice times together, she\r\nsaid, for she was thrown much upon herself, and had hardly any\r\ncongenial friend. But now there was every probability of her soon going\r\naway, so that the chance of companionship would be lost perhaps for\r\never.\r\n\r\nA cold sweat overspread Jude at the news that she was going away. That\r\nwas a contingency he had never thought of, and it spurred him to write\r\nall the more quickly to her. He would meet her that very evening, he\r\nsaid, one hour from the time of writing, at the cross in the pavement\r\nwhich marked the spot of the Martyrdoms.\r\n\r\nWhen he had despatched the note by a boy he regretted that in his hurry\r\nhe should have suggested to her to meet him out of doors, when he might\r\nhave said he would call upon her. It was, in fact, the country custom\r\nto meet thus, and nothing else had occurred to him. Arabella had been\r\nmet in the same way, unfortunately, and it might not seem respectable\r\nto a dear girl like Sue. However, it could not be helped now, and he\r\nmoved towards the point a few minutes before the hour, under the\r\nglimmer of the newly lighted lamps.\r\n\r\nThe broad street was silent, and almost deserted, although it was not\r\nlate. He saw a figure on the other side, which turned out to be hers,\r\nand they both converged towards the crossmark at the same moment.\r\nBefore either had reached it she called out to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to meet you just there, for the first time in my life!\r\nCome further on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice, though positive and silvery, had been tremulous. They walked\r\non in parallel lines, and, waiting her pleasure, Jude watched till she\r\nshowed signs of closing in, when he did likewise, the place being where\r\nthe carriers\xe2\x80\x99 carts stood in the daytime, though there was none on the\r\nspot then.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry that I asked you to meet me, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t call,\xe2\x80\x9d began Jude\r\nwith the bashfulness of a lover. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought it would save time if\r\nwe were going to walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with the freedom of a friend. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nreally no place to ask anybody in to. What I meant was that the place\r\nyou chose was so horrid\xe2\x80\x94I suppose I ought not to say horrid\xe2\x80\x94I mean\r\ngloomy and inauspicious in its associations\xe2\x80\xa6 But isn\xe2\x80\x99t it funny to\r\nbegin like this, when I don\xe2\x80\x99t know you yet?\xe2\x80\x9d She looked him up and down\r\ncuriously, though Jude did not look much at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to know me more than I know you,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I have seen you now and then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you knew who I was, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t speak? And now I am going away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. That\xe2\x80\x99s unfortunate. I have hardly any other friend. I have,\r\nindeed, one very old friend here somewhere, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like to\r\ncall on him just yet. I wonder if you know anything of him\xe2\x80\x94Mr.\r\nPhillotson? A parson somewhere about the county I think he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I only know of one Mr. Phillotson. He lives a little way out in the\r\ncountry, at Lumsdon. He\xe2\x80\x99s a village schoolmaster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I wonder if he\xe2\x80\x99s the same. Surely it is impossible! Only a\r\nschoolmaster still! Do you know his Christian name\xe2\x80\x94is it Richard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94it is; I\xe2\x80\x99ve directed books to him, though I\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s countenance fell, for how could he succeed in an enterprise\r\nwherein the great Phillotson had failed? He would have had a day of\r\ndespair if the news had not arrived during his sweet Sue\xe2\x80\x99s presence,\r\nbut even at this moment he had visions of how Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s failure in\r\nthe grand university scheme would depress him when she had gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs we are going to take a walk, suppose we go and call upon him?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJude suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe agreed, and they went along up a hill, and through some prettily\r\nwooded country. Presently the embattled tower and square turret of the\r\nchurch rose into the sky, and then the school-house. They inquired of a\r\nperson in the street if Mr. Phillotson was likely to be at home, and\r\nwere informed that he was always at home. A knock brought him to the\r\nschool-house door, with a candle in his hand and a look of inquiry on\r\nhis face, which had grown thin and careworn since Jude last set eyes on\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nThat after all these years the meeting with Mr. Phillotson should be of\r\nthis homely complexion destroyed at one stroke the halo which had\r\nsurrounded the school-master\xe2\x80\x99s figure in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s imagination ever since\r\ntheir parting. It created in him at the same time a sympathy with\r\nPhillotson as an obviously much chastened and disappointed man. Jude\r\ntold him his name, and said he had come to see him as an old friend who\r\nhad been kind to him in his youthful days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember you in the least,\xe2\x80\x9d said the school-master\r\nthoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cYou were one of my pupils, you say? Yes, no doubt; but\r\nthey number so many thousands by this time of my life, and have\r\nnaturally changed so much, that I remember very few except the quite\r\nrecent ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was out at Marygreen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, wishing he had not come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I was there a short time. And is this an old pupil, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s my cousin\xe2\x80\xa6 I wrote to you for some grammars, if you\r\nrecollect, and you sent them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94yes!\xe2\x80\x94I do dimly recall that incident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was very kind of you to do it. And it was you who first started me\r\non that course. On the morning you left Marygreen, when your goods were\r\non the waggon, you wished me good-bye, and said your scheme was to be a\r\nuniversity man and enter the Church\xe2\x80\x94that a degree was the necessary\r\nhall-mark of one who wanted to do anything as a theologian or teacher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember I thought all that privately; but I wonder I did not keep\r\nmy own counsel. The idea was given up years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never forgotten it. It was that which brought me to this part\r\nof the country, and out here to see you to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd your cousin, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey entered the parlour of the school-house, where there was a lamp\r\nwith a paper shade, which threw the light down on three or four books.\r\nPhillotson took it off, so that they could see each other better, and\r\nthe rays fell on the nervous little face and vivacious dark eyes and\r\nhair of Sue, on the earnest features of her cousin, and on the\r\nschoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s own maturer face and figure, showing him to be a spare\r\nand thoughtful personage of five-and-forty, with a thin-lipped,\r\nsomewhat refined mouth, a slightly stooping habit, and a black frock\r\ncoat, which from continued frictions shone a little at the\r\nshoulder-blades, the middle of the back, and the elbows.\r\n\r\nThe old friendship was imperceptibly renewed, the schoolmaster speaking\r\nof his experiences, and the cousins of theirs. He told them that he\r\nstill thought of the Church sometimes, and that though he could not\r\nenter it as he had intended to do in former years he might enter it as\r\na licentiate. Meanwhile, he said, he was comfortable in his present\r\nposition, though he was in want of a pupil-teacher.\r\n\r\nThey did not stay to supper, Sue having to be indoors before it grew\r\nlate, and the road was retraced to Christminster. Though they had\r\ntalked of nothing more than general subjects, Jude was surprised to\r\nfind what a revelation of woman his cousin was to him. She was so\r\nvibrant that everything she did seemed to have its source in feeling.\r\nAn exciting thought would make her walk ahead so fast that he could\r\nhardly keep up with her; and her sensitiveness on some points was such\r\nthat it might have been misread as vanity. It was with heart-sickness\r\nhe perceived that, while her sentiments towards him were those of the\r\nfrankest friendliness only, he loved her more than before becoming\r\nacquainted with her; and the gloom of the walk home lay not in the\r\nnight overhead, but in the thought of her departure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy must you leave Christminster?\xe2\x80\x9d he said regretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can you\r\ndo otherwise than cling to a city in whose history such men as Newman,\r\nPusey, Ward, Keble, loom so large!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94they do. Though how large do they loom in the history of the\r\nworld? \xe2\x80\xa6 What a funny reason for caring to stay! I should never have\r\nthought of it!\xe2\x80\x9d She laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I must go,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fontover, one of the partners\r\nwhom I serve, is offended with me, and I with her; and it is best to\r\ngo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did that happen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe broke some statuary of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh? Wilfully?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. She found it in my room, and though it was my property she threw\r\nit on the floor and stamped on it, because it was not according to her\r\ntaste, and ground the arms and the head of one of the figures all to\r\nbits with her heel\xe2\x80\x94a horrid thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo Catholic-Apostolic for her, I suppose? No doubt she called them\r\npopish images and talked of the invocation of saints.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\xa6 No, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t do that. She saw the matter quite differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Then I am surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It was for quite some other reason that she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like my\r\npatron-saints. So I was led to retort upon her; and the end of it was\r\nthat I resolved not to stay, but to get into an occupation in which I\r\nshall be more independent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you try teaching again? You once did, I heard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of resuming it; for I was getting on as an\r\nart-designer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ let me ask Mr. Phillotson to let you try your hand in his school?\r\nIf you like it, and go to a training college, and become a first-class\r\ncertificated mistress, you get twice as large an income as any designer\r\nor church artist, and twice as much freedom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94ask him. Now I must go in. Good-bye, dear Jude! I am so glad we\r\nhave met at last. We needn\xe2\x80\x99t quarrel because our parents did, need we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude did not like to let her see quite how much he agreed with her, and\r\nwent his way to the remote street in which he had his lodging.\r\n\r\nTo keep Sue Bridehead near him was now a desire which operated without\r\nregard of consequences, and the next evening he again set out for\r\nLumsdon, fearing to trust to the persuasive effects of a note only. The\r\nschool-master was unprepared for such a proposal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I rather wanted was a second year\xe2\x80\x99s transfer, as it is called,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course your cousin would do, personally; but she has had\r\nno experience. Oh\xe2\x80\x94she has, has she? Does she really think of adopting\r\nteaching as a profession?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude said she was disposed to do so, he thought, and his ingenious\r\narguments on her natural fitness for assisting Mr. Phillotson, of which\r\nJude knew nothing whatever, so influenced the schoolmaster that he said\r\nhe would engage her, assuring Jude as a friend that unless his cousin\r\nreally meant to follow on in the same course, and regarded this step as\r\nthe first stage of an apprenticeship, of which her training in a normal\r\nschool would be the second stage, her time would be wasted quite, the\r\nsalary being merely nominal.\r\n\r\nThe day after this visit Phillotson received a letter from Jude,\r\ncontaining the information that he had again consulted his cousin, who\r\ntook more and more warmly to the idea of tuition; and that she had\r\nagreed to come. It did not occur for a moment to the schoolmaster and\r\nrecluse that Jude\xe2\x80\x99s ardour in promoting the arrangement arose from any\r\nother feelings towards Sue than the instinct of co-operation common\r\namong members of the same family.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe schoolmaster sat in his homely dwelling attached to the school,\r\nboth being modern erections; and he looked across the way at the old\r\nhouse in which his teacher Sue had a lodging. The arrangement had been\r\nconcluded very quickly. A pupil-teacher who was to have been\r\ntransferred to Mr. Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s school had failed him, and Sue had been\r\ntaken as stop-gap. All such provisional arrangements as these could\r\nonly last till the next annual visit of H.M. Inspector, whose approval\r\nwas necessary to make them permanent. Having taught for some two years\r\nin London, though she had abandoned that vocation of late, Miss\r\nBridehead was not exactly a novice, and Phillotson thought there would\r\nbe no difficulty in retaining her services, which he already wished to\r\ndo, though she had only been with him three or four weeks. He had found\r\nher quite as bright as Jude had described her; and what\r\nmaster-tradesman does not wish to keep an apprentice who saves him half\r\nhis labour?\r\n\r\nIt was a little over half-past eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning and he was\r\nwaiting to see her cross the road to the school, when he would follow.\r\nAt twenty minutes to nine she did cross, a light hat tossed on her\r\nhead; and he watched her as a curiosity. A new emanation, which had\r\nnothing to do with her skill as a teacher, seemed to surround her this\r\nmorning. He went to the school also, and Sue remained governing her\r\nclass at the other end of the room, all day under his eye. She\r\ncertainly was an excellent teacher.\r\n\r\nIt was part of his duty to give her private lessons in the evening, and\r\nsome article in the Code made it necessary that a respectable, elderly\r\nwoman should be present at these lessons when the teacher and the\r\ntaught were of different sexes. Richard Phillotson thought of the\r\nabsurdity of the regulation in this case, when he was old enough to be\r\nthe girl\xe2\x80\x99s father; but he faithfully acted up to it; and sat down with\r\nher in a room where Mrs. Hawes, the widow at whose house Sue lodged,\r\noccupied herself with sewing. The regulation was, indeed, not easy to\r\nevade, for there was no other sitting-room in the dwelling.\r\n\r\nSometimes as she figured\xe2\x80\x94it was arithmetic that they were working\r\nat\xe2\x80\x94she would involuntarily glance up with a little inquiring smile at\r\nhim, as if she assumed that, being the master, he must perceive all\r\nthat was passing in her brain, as right or wrong. Phillotson was not\r\nreally thinking of the arithmetic at all, but of her, in a novel way\r\nwhich somehow seemed strange to him as preceptor. Perhaps she knew that\r\nhe was thinking of her thus.\r\n\r\nFor a few weeks their work had gone on with a monotony which in itself\r\nwas a delight to him. Then it happened that the children were to be\r\ntaken to Christminster to see an itinerant exhibition, in the shape of\r\na model of Jerusalem, to which schools were admitted at a penny a head\r\nin the interests of education. They marched along the road two and two,\r\nshe beside her class with her simple cotton sunshade, her little thumb\r\ncocked up against its stem; and Phillotson behind in his long dangling\r\ncoat, handling his walking-stick genteelly, in the musing mood which\r\nhad come over him since her arrival. The afternoon was one of sun and\r\ndust, and when they entered the exhibition room few people were present\r\nbut themselves. The model of the ancient city stood in the middle of\r\nthe apartment, and the proprietor, with a fine religious philanthropy\r\nwritten on his features, walked round it with a pointer in his hand,\r\nshowing the young people the various quarters and places known to them\r\nby name from reading their Bibles; Mount Moriah, the Valley of\r\nJehoshaphat, the City of Zion, the walls and the gates, outside one of\r\nwhich there was a large mound like a tumulus, and on the mound a little\r\nwhite cross. The spot, he said, was Calvary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue to the schoolmaster, as she stood with him a little\r\nin the background, \xe2\x80\x9cthat this model, elaborate as it is, is a very\r\nimaginary production. How does anybody know that Jerusalem was like\r\nthis in the time of Christ? I am sure this man doesn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is made after the best conjectural maps, based on actual visits to\r\nthe city as it now exists.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy we have had enough of Jerusalem,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cconsidering we\r\nare not descended from the Jews. There was nothing first-rate about the\r\nplace, or people, after all\xe2\x80\x94as there was about Athens, Rome,\r\nAlexandria, and other old cities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my dear girl, consider what it is to us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was silent, for she was easily repressed; and then perceived behind\r\nthe group of children clustered round the model a young man in a white\r\nflannel jacket, his form being bent so low in his intent inspection of\r\nthe Valley of Jehoshaphat that his face was almost hidden from view by\r\nthe Mount of Olives. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at your cousin Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the\r\nschoolmaster. \xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t think we have had enough of Jerusalem!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see him!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried in her quick, light voice. \xe2\x80\x9cJude\xe2\x80\x94how\r\nseriously you are going into it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude started up from his reverie, and saw her. \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with\r\na glad flush of embarrassment. \xe2\x80\x9cThese are your school-children, of\r\ncourse! I saw that schools were admitted in the afternoons, and thought\r\nyou might come; but I got so deeply interested that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t remember\r\nwhere I was. How it carries one back, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it! I could examine it\r\nfor hours, but I have only a few minutes, unfortunately; for I am in\r\nthe middle of a job out here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour cousin is so terribly clever that she criticizes it\r\nunmercifully,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson, with good-humoured satire. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is\r\nquite sceptical as to its correctness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Mr. Phillotson, I am not\xe2\x80\x94altogether! I hate to be what is called a\r\nclever girl\xe2\x80\x94there are too many of that sort now!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Sue\r\nsensitively. \xe2\x80\x9cI only meant\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I meant\xe2\x80\x94except that it was\r\nwhat you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ know your meaning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude ardently (although he did not). \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI think you are quite right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a good Jude\xe2\x80\x94I know _you_ believe in me!\xe2\x80\x9d She impulsively seized\r\nhis hand, and leaving a reproachful look on the schoolmaster turned\r\naway to Jude, her voice revealing a tremor which she herself felt to be\r\nabsurdly uncalled for by sarcasm so gentle. She had not the least\r\nconception how the hearts of the twain went out to her at this\r\nmomentary revelation of feeling, and what a complication she was\r\nbuilding up thereby in the futures of both.\r\n\r\nThe model wore too much of an educational aspect for the children not\r\nto tire of it soon, and a little later in the afternoon they were all\r\nmarched back to Lumsdon, Jude returning to his work. He watched the\r\njuvenile flock in their clean frocks and pinafores, filing down the\r\nstreet towards the country beside Phillotson and Sue, and a sad,\r\ndissatisfied sense of being out of the scheme of the latters\xe2\x80\x99 lives had\r\npossession of him. Phillotson had invited him to walk out and see them\r\non Friday evening, when there would be no lessons to give to Sue, and\r\nJude had eagerly promised to avail himself of the opportunity.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the scholars and teachers moved homewards, and the next day,\r\non looking on the blackboard in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s class, Phillotson was surprised\r\nto find upon it, skilfully drawn in chalk, a perspective view of\r\nJerusalem, with every building shown in its place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you took no interest in the model, and hardly looked at it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly did,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I remembered that much of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is more than I had remembered myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer Majesty\xe2\x80\x99s school-inspector was at that time paying\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csurprise-visits\xe2\x80\x9d in this neighbourhood to test the teaching unawares;\r\nand two days later, in the middle of the morning lessons, the latch of\r\nthe door was softly lifted, and in walked my gentleman, the king of\r\nterrors\xe2\x80\x94to pupil-teachers.\r\n\r\nTo Mr. Phillotson the surprise was not great; like the lady in the\r\nstory, he had been played that trick too many times to be unprepared.\r\nBut Sue\xe2\x80\x99s class was at the further end of the room, and her back was\r\ntowards the entrance; the inspector therefore came and stood behind her\r\nand watched her teaching some half-minute before she became aware of\r\nhis presence. She turned, and realized that an oft-dreaded moment had\r\ncome. The effect upon her timidity was such that she uttered a cry of\r\nfright. Phillotson, with a strange instinct of solicitude quite beyond\r\nhis control, was at her side just in time to prevent her falling from\r\nfaintness. She soon recovered herself, and laughed; but when the\r\ninspector had gone there was a reaction, and she was so white that\r\nPhillotson took her into his room, and gave her some brandy to bring\r\nher round. She found him holding her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to have told me,\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped petulantly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat one of the\r\ninspector\xe2\x80\x99s surprise-visits was imminent! Oh, what shall I do! Now\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll write and tell the managers that I am no good, and I shall be\r\ndisgraced for ever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t do that, my dear little girl. You are the best teacher ever I\r\nhad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked so gently at her that she was moved, and regretted that she\r\nhad upbraided him. When she was better she went home.\r\n\r\nJude in the meantime had been waiting impatiently for Friday. On both\r\nWednesday and Thursday he had been so much under the influence of his\r\ndesire to see her that he walked after dark some distance along the\r\nroad in the direction of the village, and, on returning to his room to\r\nread, found himself quite unable to concentrate his mind on the page.\r\nOn Friday, as soon as he had got himself up as he thought Sue would\r\nlike to see him, and made a hasty tea, he set out, notwithstanding that\r\nthe evening was wet. The trees overhead deepened the gloom of the hour,\r\nand they dripped sadly upon him, impressing him with\r\nforebodings\xe2\x80\x94illogical forebodings; for though he knew that he loved her\r\nhe also knew that he could not be more to her than he was.\r\n\r\nOn turning the corner and entering the village the first sight that\r\ngreeted his eyes was that of two figures under one umbrella coming out\r\nof the vicarage gate. He was too far back for them to notice him, but\r\nhe knew in a moment that they were Sue and Phillotson. The latter was\r\nholding the umbrella over her head, and they had evidently been paying\r\na visit to the vicar\xe2\x80\x94probably on some business connected with the\r\nschool work. And as they walked along the wet and deserted lane Jude\r\nsaw Phillotson place his arm round the girl\xe2\x80\x99s waist; whereupon she\r\ngently removed it; but he replaced it; and she let it remain, looking\r\nquickly round her with an air of misgiving. She did not look absolutely\r\nbehind her, and therefore did not see Jude, who sank into the hedge\r\nlike one struck with a blight. There he remained hidden till they had\r\nreached Sue\xe2\x80\x99s cottage and she had passed in, Phillotson going on to the\r\nschool hard by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99s too old for her\xe2\x80\x94too old!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude in all the terrible\r\nsickness of hopeless, handicapped love.\r\n\r\nHe could not interfere.
