Was he not Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s? He was unable to go on\r\nfurther, and retraced his steps towards Christminster. Every tread of\r\nhis feet seemed to say to him that he must on no account stand in the\r\nschoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s way with Sue. Phillotson was perhaps twenty years her\r\nsenior, but many a happy marriage had been made in such conditions of\r\nage. The ironical clinch to his sorrow was given by the thought that\r\nthe intimacy between his cousin and the schoolmaster had been brought\r\nabout entirely by himself.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s old and embittered aunt lay unwell at Marygreen, and on the\r\nfollowing Sunday he went to see her\xe2\x80\x94a visit which was the result of a\r\nvictorious struggle against his inclination to turn aside to the\r\nvillage of Lumsdon and obtain a miserable interview with his cousin, in\r\nwhich the word nearest his heart could not be spoken, and the sight\r\nwhich had tortured him could not be revealed.\r\n\r\nHis aunt was now unable to leave her bed, and a great part of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshort day was occupied in making arrangements for her comfort. The\r\nlittle bakery business had been sold to a neighbour, and with the\r\nproceeds of this and her savings she was comfortably supplied with\r\nnecessaries and more, a widow of the same village living with her and\r\nministering to her wants. It was not till the time had nearly come for\r\nhim to leave that he obtained a quiet talk with her, and his words\r\ntended insensibly towards his cousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas Sue born here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was\xe2\x80\x94in this room. They were living here at that time. What made\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee ask that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I wanted to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve been seeing her!\xe2\x80\x9d said the harsh old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did I\r\ntell \xe2\x80\x99ee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94that I was not to see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you gossiped with her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen don\xe2\x80\x99t keep it up. She was brought up by her father to hate her\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s family; and she\xe2\x80\x99ll look with no favour upon a working chap\r\nlike you\xe2\x80\x94a townish girl as she\xe2\x80\x99s become by now. I never cared much\r\nabout her. A pert little thing, that\xe2\x80\x99s what she was too often, with her\r\ntight-strained nerves. Many\xe2\x80\x99s the time I\xe2\x80\x99ve smacked her for her\r\nimpertinence. Why, one day when she was walking into the pond with her\r\nshoes and stockings off, and her petticoats pulled above her knees,\r\nafore I could cry out for shame, she said: \xe2\x80\x98Move on, Aunty! This is no\r\nsight for modest eyes!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was a little child then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was twelve if a day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94of course. But now she\xe2\x80\x99s older she\xe2\x80\x99s of a thoughtful, quivering,\r\ntender nature, and as sensitive as\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude!\xe2\x80\x9d cried his aunt, springing up in bed. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you be a fool about\r\nher!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, of course not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour marrying that woman Arabella was about as bad a thing as a man\r\ncould possibly do for himself by trying hard. But she\xe2\x80\x99s gone to the\r\nother side of the world, and med never trouble you again. And there\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe a worse thing if you, tied and bound as you be, should have a fancy\r\nfor Sue. If your cousin is civil to you, take her civility for what it\r\nis worth. But anything more than a relation\xe2\x80\x99s good wishes it is stark\r\nmadness for \xe2\x80\x99ee to give her. If she\xe2\x80\x99s townish and wanton it med bring\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee to ruin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say anything against her, Aunt! Don\xe2\x80\x99t, please!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA relief was afforded to him by the entry of the companion and nurse of\r\nhis aunt, who must have been listening to the conversation, for she\r\nbegan a commentary on past years, introducing Sue Bridehead as a\r\ncharacter in her recollections. She described what an odd little maid\r\nSue had been when a pupil at the village school across the green\r\nopposite, before her father went to London\xe2\x80\x94how, when the vicar arranged\r\nreadings and recitations, she appeared on the platform, the smallest of\r\nthem all, \xe2\x80\x9cin her little white frock, and shoes, and pink sash\xe2\x80\x9d; how\r\nshe recited \xe2\x80\x9cExcelsior,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a sound of revelry by night,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Raven\xe2\x80\x9d; how during the delivery she would knit her little brows\r\nand glare round tragically, and say to the empty air, as if some real\r\ncreature stood there\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGhastly, grim, and ancient Raven,\r\n    wandering from the Nightly shore,\r\nTell me what thy lordly name is\r\n    on the Night\xe2\x80\x99s Plutonian shore!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99d bring up the nasty carrion bird that clear,\xe2\x80\x9d corroborated the\r\nsick woman reluctantly, \xe2\x80\x9cas she stood there in her little sash and\r\nthings, that you could see un a\xe2\x80\x99most before your very eyes. You too,\r\nJude, had the same trick as a child of seeming to see things in the\r\nair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe neighbour told also of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s accomplishments in other kinds:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was not exactly a tomboy, you know; but she could do things that\r\nonly boys do, as a rule. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen her hit in and steer down the long\r\nslide on yonder pond, with her little curls blowing, one of a file of\r\ntwenty moving along against the sky like shapes painted on glass, and\r\nup the back slide without stopping. All boys except herself; and then\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d cheer her, and then she\xe2\x80\x99d say, \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be saucy, boys,\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nsuddenly run indoors. They\xe2\x80\x99d try to coax her out again. But \xe2\x80\x99a wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese retrospective visions of Sue only made Jude the more miserable\r\nthat he was unable to woo her, and he left the cottage of his aunt that\r\nday with a heavy heart. He would fain have glanced into the school to\r\nsee the room in which Sue\xe2\x80\x99s little figure had so glorified itself; but\r\nhe checked his desire and went on.\r\n\r\nIt being Sunday evening some villagers who had known him during his\r\nresidence here were standing in a group in their best clothes. Jude was\r\nstartled by a salute from one of them:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe\xe2\x80\x99ve got there right enough, then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude showed that he did not understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, to the seat of l\xe2\x80\x99arning\xe2\x80\x94the \xe2\x80\x98City of Light\xe2\x80\x99 you used to talk to\r\nus about as a little boy! Is it all you expected of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; more!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was there once for an hour I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see much in it for my part;\r\nauld crumbling buildings, half church, half almshouse, and not much\r\ngoing on at that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are wrong, John; there is more going on than meets the eye of a\r\nman walking through the streets. It is a unique centre of thought and\r\nreligion\xe2\x80\x94the intellectual and spiritual granary of this country. All\r\nthat silence and absence of goings-on is the stillness of infinite\r\nmotion\xe2\x80\x94the sleep of the spinning-top, to borrow the simile of a\r\nwell-known writer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, it med be all that, or it med not. As I say, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nnothing of it the hour or two I was there; so I went in and had a pot\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 beer, and a penny loaf, and a ha\xe2\x80\x99porth o\xe2\x80\x99 cheese, and waited till it\r\nwas time to come along home. You\xe2\x80\x99ve j\xe2\x80\x99ined a college by this time, I\r\nsuppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI am almost as far off that as ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude slapped his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust what we thought! Such places be not for such as you\xe2\x80\x94only for them\r\nwith plenty o\xe2\x80\x99 money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, with some bitterness. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are for\r\nsuch ones!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill, the remark was sufficient to withdraw Jude\xe2\x80\x99s attention from the\r\nimaginative world he had lately inhabited, in which an abstract figure,\r\nmore or less himself, was steeping his mind in a sublimation of the\r\narts and sciences, and making his calling and election sure to a seat\r\nin the paradise of the learned. He was set regarding his prospects in a\r\ncold northern light. He had lately felt that he could not quite satisfy\r\nhimself in his Greek\xe2\x80\x94in the Greek of the dramatists particularly. So\r\nfatigued was he sometimes after his day\xe2\x80\x99s work that he could not\r\nmaintain the critical attention necessary for thorough application. He\r\nfelt that he wanted a coach\xe2\x80\x94a friend at his elbow to tell him in a\r\nmoment what sometimes would occupy him a weary month in extracting from\r\nunanticipative, clumsy books.\r\n\r\nIt was decidedly necessary to consider facts a little more closely than\r\nhe had done of late. What was the good, after all, of using up his\r\nspare hours in a vague labour called \xe2\x80\x9cprivate study\xe2\x80\x9d without giving an\r\noutlook on practicabilities?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have thought of this before,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he journeyed\r\nback. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been better never to have embarked in the scheme\r\nat all than to do it without seeing clearly where I am going, or what I\r\nam aiming at\xe2\x80\xa6 This hovering outside the walls of the colleges, as if\r\nexpecting some arm to be stretched out from them to lift me inside,\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t do! I must get special information.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next week accordingly he sought it. What at first seemed an\r\nopportunity occurred one afternoon when he saw an elderly gentleman,\r\nwho had been pointed out as the head of a particular college, walking\r\nin the public path of a parklike enclosure near the spot at which Jude\r\nchanced to be sitting. The gentleman came nearer, and Jude looked\r\nanxiously at his face. It seemed benign, considerate, yet rather\r\nreserved. On second thoughts Jude felt that he could not go up and\r\naddress him; but he was sufficiently influenced by the incident to\r\nthink what a wise thing it would be for him to state his difficulties\r\nby letter to some of the best and most judicious of these old masters,\r\nand obtain their advice.\r\n\r\nDuring the next week or two he accordingly placed himself in such\r\npositions about the city as would afford him glimpses of several of the\r\nmost distinguished among the provosts, wardens, and other heads of\r\nhouses; and from those he ultimately selected five whose physiognomies\r\nseemed to say to him that they were appreciative and far-seeing men. To\r\nthese five he addressed letters, briefly stating his difficulties, and\r\nasking their opinion on his stranded situation.\r\n\r\nWhen the letters were posted Jude mentally began to criticize them; he\r\nwished they had not been sent. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is just one of those intrusive,\r\nvulgar, pushing, applications which are so common in these days,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nthought. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I know better than address utter strangers in\r\nsuch a way? I may be an impostor, an idle scamp, a man with a bad\r\ncharacter, for all that they know to the contrary\xe2\x80\xa6 Perhaps that\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nI am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNevertheless, he found himself clinging to the hope of some reply as to\r\nhis one last chance of redemption. He waited day after day, saying that\r\nit was perfectly absurd to expect, yet expecting. While he waited he\r\nwas suddenly stirred by news about Phillotson. Phillotson was giving up\r\nthe school near Christminster, for a larger one further south, in\r\nMid-Wessex. What this meant; how it would affect his cousin; whether,\r\nas seemed possible, it was a practical move of the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntowards a larger income, in view of a provision for two instead of one,\r\nhe would not allow himself to say. And the tender relations between\r\nPhillotson and the young girl of whom Jude was passionately enamoured\r\neffectually made it repugnant to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s tastes to apply to Phillotson\r\nfor advice on his own scheme.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the academic dignitaries to whom Jude had written vouchsafed\r\nno answer, and the young man was thus thrown back entirely on himself,\r\nas formerly, with the added gloom of a weakened hope. By indirect\r\ninquiries he soon perceived clearly what he had long uneasily\r\nsuspected, that to qualify himself for certain open scholarships and\r\nexhibitions was the only brilliant course. But to do this a good deal\r\nof coaching would be necessary, and much natural ability. It was next\r\nto impossible that a man reading on his own system, however widely and\r\nthoroughly, even over the prolonged period of ten years, should be able\r\nto compete with those who had passed their lives under trained teachers\r\nand had worked to ordained lines.\r\n\r\nThe other course, that of buying himself in, so to speak, seemed the\r\nonly one really open to men like him, the difficulty being simply of a\r\nmaterial kind. With the help of his information he began to reckon the\r\nextent of this material obstacle, and ascertained, to his dismay, that,\r\nat the rate at which, with the best of fortune, he would be able to\r\nsave money, fifteen years must elapse before he could be in a position\r\nto forward testimonials to the head of a college and advance to a\r\nmatriculation examination. The undertaking was hopeless.\r\n\r\nHe saw what a curious and cunning glamour the neighbourhood of the\r\nplace had exercised over him. To get there and live there, to move\r\namong the churches and halls and become imbued with the _genius loci_,\r\nhad seemed to his dreaming youth, as the spot shaped its charms to him\r\nfrom its halo on the horizon, the obvious and ideal thing to do. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\r\nme only get there,\xe2\x80\x9d he had said with the fatuousness of Crusoe over his\r\nbig boat, \xe2\x80\x9cand the rest is but a matter of time and energy.\xe2\x80\x9d It would\r\nhave been far better for him in every way if he had never come within\r\nsight and sound of the delusive precincts, had gone to some busy\r\ncommercial town with the sole object of making money by his wits, and\r\nthence surveyed his plan in true perspective. Well, all that was clear\r\nto him amounted to this, that the whole scheme had burst up, like an\r\niridescent soap-bubble, under the touch of a reasoned inquiry. He\r\nlooked back at himself along the vista of his past years, and his\r\nthought was akin to Heine\xe2\x80\x99s:\r\n\r\nAbove the youth\xe2\x80\x99s inspired and flashing eyes\r\nI see the motley mocking fool\xe2\x80\x99s-cap rise!\r\n\r\nFortunately he had not been allowed to bring his disappointment into\r\nhis dear Sue\xe2\x80\x99s life by involving her in this collapse. And the painful\r\ndetails of his awakening to a sense of his limitations should now be\r\nspared her as far as possible. After all, she had only known a little\r\npart of the miserable struggle in which he had been engaged thus\r\nunequipped, poor, and unforeseeing.\r\n\r\nHe always remembered the appearance of the afternoon on which he awoke\r\nfrom his dream. Not quite knowing what to do with himself, he went up\r\nto an octagonal chamber in the lantern of a singularly built theatre\r\nthat was set amidst this quaint and singular city. It had windows all\r\nround, from which an outlook over the whole town and its edifices could\r\nbe gained. Jude\xe2\x80\x99s eyes swept all the views in succession, meditatively,\r\nmournfully, yet sturdily. Those buildings and their associations and\r\nprivileges were not for him. From the looming roof of the great\r\nlibrary, into which he hardly ever had time to enter, his gaze\r\ntravelled on to the varied spires, halls, gables, streets, chapels,\r\ngardens, quadrangles, which composed the ensemble of this unrivalled\r\npanorama. He saw that his destiny lay not with these, but among the\r\nmanual toilers in the shabby purlieu which he himself occupied,\r\nunrecognized as part of the city at all by its visitors and\r\npanegyrists, yet without whose denizens the hard readers could not read\r\nnor the high thinkers live.\r\n\r\nHe looked over the town into the country beyond, to the trees which\r\nscreened her whose presence had at first been the support of his heart,\r\nand whose loss was now a maddening torture. But for this blow he might\r\nhave borne with his fate. With Sue as companion he could have renounced\r\nhis ambitions with a smile. Without her it was inevitable that the\r\nreaction from the long strain to which he had subjected himself should\r\naffect him disastrously. Phillotson had no doubt passed through a\r\nsimilar intellectual disappointment to that which now enveloped him.\r\nBut the schoolmaster had been since blest with the consolation of sweet\r\nSue, while for him there was no consoler.\r\n\r\nDescending to the streets, he went listlessly along till he arrived at\r\nan inn, and entered it. Here he drank several glasses of beer in rapid\r\nsuccession, and when he came out it was night. By the light of the\r\nflickering lamps he rambled home to supper, and had not long been\r\nsitting at table when his landlady brought up a letter that had just\r\narrived for him. She laid it down as if impressed with a sense of its\r\npossible importance, and on looking at it Jude perceived that it bore\r\nthe embossed stamp of one of the colleges whose heads he had addressed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_One_\xe2\x80\x94at last!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude.\r\n\r\nThe communication was brief, and not exactly what he had expected;\r\nthough it really was from the master in person. It ran thus:\r\n\r\nBIBLIOLL COLLEGE.\r\n\r\n\r\nSIR,\xe2\x80\x94I have read your letter with interest; and, judging from your\r\ndescription of yourself as a working-man, I venture to think that you\r\nwill have a much better chance of success in life by remaining in your\r\nown sphere and sticking to your trade than by adopting any other\r\ncourse. That, therefore, is what I advise you to do. Yours faithfully,\r\n\r\n\r\nT. TETUPHENAY.\r\n\r\n\r\nTo Mr. J. FAWLEY, Stone-mason.\r\n\r\nThis terribly sensible advice exasperated Jude. He had known all that\r\nbefore. He knew it was true. Yet it seemed a hard slap after ten years\r\nof labour, and its effect upon him just now was to make him rise\r\nrecklessly from the table, and, instead of reading as usual, to go\r\ndownstairs and into the street. He stood at a bar and tossed off two or\r\nthree glasses, then unconsciously sauntered along till he came to a\r\nspot called The Fourways in the middle of the city, gazing abstractedly\r\nat the groups of people like one in a trance, till, coming to himself,\r\nhe began talking to the policeman fixed there.\r\n\r\nThat officer yawned, stretched out his elbows, elevated himself an inch\r\nand a half on the balls of his toes, smiled, and looking humorously at\r\nJude, said, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve had a wet, young man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I\xe2\x80\x99ve only begun,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied cynically.\r\n\r\nWhatever his wetness, his brains were dry enough. He only heard in part\r\nthe policeman\xe2\x80\x99s further remarks, having fallen into thought on what\r\nstruggling people like himself had stood at that crossway, whom nobody\r\never thought of now. It had more history than the oldest college in the\r\ncity. It was literally teeming, stratified, with the shades of human\r\ngroups, who had met there for tragedy, comedy, farce; real enactments\r\nof the intensest kind. At Fourways men had stood and talked of\r\nNapoleon, the loss of America, the execution of King Charles, the\r\nburning of the Martyrs, the Crusades, the Norman Conquest, possibly of\r\nthe arrival of Caesar. Here the two sexes had met for loving, hating,\r\ncoupling, parting; had waited, had suffered, for each other; had\r\ntriumphed over each other; cursed each other in jealousy, blessed each\r\nother in forgiveness.\r\n\r\nHe began to see that the town life was a book of humanity infinitely\r\nmore palpitating, varied, and compendious than the gown life. These\r\nstruggling men and women before him were the reality of Christminster,\r\nthough they knew little of Christ or Minster. That was one of the\r\nhumours of things. The floating population of students and teachers,\r\nwho did know both in a way, were not Christminster in a local sense at\r\nall.\r\n\r\nHe looked at his watch, and, in pursuit of this idea, he went on till\r\nhe came to a public hall, where a promenade concert was in progress.\r\nJude entered, and found the room full of shop youths and girls,\r\nsoldiers, apprentices, boys of eleven smoking cigarettes, and light\r\nwomen of the more respectable and amateur class. He had tapped the real\r\nChristminster life. A band was playing, and the crowd walked about and\r\njostled each other, and every now and then a man got upon a platform\r\nand sang a comic song.\r\n\r\nThe spirit of Sue seemed to hover round him and prevent his flirting\r\nand drinking with the frolicsome girls who made advances\xe2\x80\x94wistful to\r\ngain a little joy. At ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock he came away, choosing a circuitous\r\nroute homeward to pass the gates of the college whose head had just\r\nsent him the note.\r\n\r\nThe gates were shut, and, by an impulse, he took from his pocket the\r\nlump of chalk which as a workman he usually carried there, and wrote\r\nalong the wall:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I have understanding as well as you; I am not inferior to you: yea,\r\nwho knoweth not such things as these?_\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Job xii. 3.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe stroke of scorn relieved his mind, and the next morning he laughed\r\nat his self-conceit. But the laugh was not a healthy one. He re-read\r\nthe letter from the master, and the wisdom in its lines, which had at\r\nfirst exasperated him, chilled and depressed him now. He saw himself as\r\na fool indeed.\r\n\r\nDeprived of the objects of both intellect and emotion, he could not\r\nproceed to his work. Whenever he felt reconciled to his fate as a\r\nstudent, there came to disturb his calm his hopeless relations with\r\nSue. That the one affined soul he had ever met was lost to him through\r\nhis marriage returned upon him with cruel persistency, till, unable to\r\nbear it longer, he again rushed for distraction to the real\r\nChristminster life. He now sought it out in an obscure and low-ceiled\r\ntavern up a court which was well known to certain worthies of the\r\nplace, and in brighter times would have interested him simply by its\r\nquaintness. Here he sat more or less all the day, convinced that he was\r\nat bottom a vicious character, of whom it was hopeless to expect\r\nanything.\r\n\r\nIn the evening the frequenters of the house dropped in one by one, Jude\r\nstill retaining his seat in the corner, though his money was all spent,\r\nand he had not eaten anything the whole day except a biscuit. He\r\nsurveyed his gathering companions with all the equanimity and\r\nphilosophy of a man who has been drinking long and slowly, and made\r\nfriends with several: to wit, Tinker Taylor, a decayed\r\nchurch-ironmonger who appeared to have been of a religious turn in\r\nearlier years, but was somewhat blasphemous now; also a red-nosed\r\nauctioneer; also two Gothic masons like himself, called Uncle Jim and\r\nUncle Joe. There were present, too, some clerks, and a gown- and\r\nsurplice-maker\xe2\x80\x99s assistant; two ladies who sported moral characters of\r\nvarious depths of shade, according to their company, nicknamed \xe2\x80\x9cBower\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 Bliss\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cFreckles\xe2\x80\x9d; some horsey men \xe2\x80\x9cin the know\xe2\x80\x9d of betting\r\ncircles; a travelling actor from the theatre, and two devil-may-care\r\nyoung men who proved to be gownless undergraduates; they had slipped in\r\nby stealth to meet a man about bull-pups, and stayed to drink and smoke\r\nshort pipes with the racing gents aforesaid, looking at their watches\r\nevery now and then.\r\n\r\nThe conversation waxed general. Christminster society was criticized,\r\nthe dons, magistrates, and other people in authority being sincerely\r\npitied for their shortcomings, while opinions on how they ought to\r\nconduct themselves and their affairs to be properly respected, were\r\nexchanged in a large-minded and disinterested manner.\r\n\r\nJude Fawley, with the self-conceit, effrontery, and _aplomb_ of a\r\nstrong-brained fellow in liquor, threw in his remarks somewhat\r\nperemptorily; and his aims having been what they were for so many\r\nyears, everything the others said turned upon his tongue, by a sort of\r\nmechanical craze, to the subject of scholarship and study, the extent\r\nof his own learning being dwelt upon with an insistence that would have\r\nappeared pitiable to himself in his sane hours.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care a damn,\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying, \xe2\x80\x9cfor any provost, warden,\r\nprincipal, fellow, or cursed master of arts in the university! What I\r\nknow is that I\xe2\x80\x99d lick \xe2\x80\x99em on their own ground if they\xe2\x80\x99d give me a\r\nchance, and show \xe2\x80\x99em a few things they are not up to yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear!\xe2\x80\x9d said the undergraduates from the corner, where they were\r\ntalking privately about the pups.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou always was fond o\xe2\x80\x99 books, I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tinker Taylor, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t doubt what you state. Now with me \xe2\x80\x99twas different. I always saw\r\nthere was more to be learnt outside a book than in; and I took my steps\r\naccordingly, or I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been the man I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou aim at the Church, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Joe. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you are such a\r\nscholar as to pitch yer hopes so high as that, why not give us a\r\nspecimen of your scholarship? Canst say the Creed in Latin, man? That\r\nwas how they once put it to a chap down in my country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude haughtily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot he! Like his conceit!\xe2\x80\x9d screamed one of the ladies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust you shut up, Bower o\xe2\x80\x99 Bliss!\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the undergraduates.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSilence!\xe2\x80\x9d He drank off the spirits in his tumbler, rapped with it on\r\nthe counter, and announced, \xe2\x80\x9cThe gentleman in the corner is going to\r\nrehearse the Articles of his Belief, in the Latin tongue, for the\r\nedification of the company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94have a try!\xe2\x80\x9d said the surplice-maker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Joe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he can!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tinker Taylor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll swear I can!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, come now, stand me a small Scotch\r\ncold, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it straight off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fair offer,\xe2\x80\x9d said the undergraduate, throwing down the money\r\nfor the whisky.\r\n\r\nThe barmaid concocted the mixture with the bearing of a person\r\ncompelled to live amongst animals of an inferior species, and the glass\r\nwas handed across to Jude, who, having drunk the contents, stood up and\r\nbegan rhetorically, without hesitation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Credo in unum Deum, Patrem omnipotentem, Factorem coeli et terrae,\r\nvisibilium omnium et invisibilium._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! Excellent Latin!\xe2\x80\x9d cried one of the undergraduates, who, however,\r\nhad not the slightest conception of a single word.\r\n\r\nA silence reigned among the rest in the bar, and the maid stood still,\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s voice echoing sonorously into the inner parlour, where the\r\nlandlord was dozing, and bringing him out to see what was going on.\r\nJude had declaimed steadily ahead, and was continuing:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Crucifixus etiam pro nobis: sub Pontio Pilato passus, et sepultus\r\nest. Et resurrexit tertia die, secundum Scripturas._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the Nicene,\xe2\x80\x9d sneered the second undergraduate. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we wanted\r\nthe Apostles\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t say so! And every fool knows, except you, that the Nicene\r\nis the most historic creed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet un go on, let un go on!\xe2\x80\x9d said the auctioneer.\r\n\r\nBut Jude\xe2\x80\x99s mind seemed to grow confused soon, and he could not get on.\r\nHe put his hand to his forehead, and his face assumed an expression of\r\npain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him another glass\xe2\x80\x94then he\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch up and get through it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nTinker Taylor.\r\n\r\nSomebody threw down threepence, the glass was handed, Jude stretched\r\nout his arm for it without looking, and having swallowed the liquor,\r\nwent on in a moment in a revived voice, raising it as he neared the end\r\nwith the manner of a priest leading a congregation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Et in Spiritum Sanctum, Dominum et vivificantem, qui ex Patre\r\nFilioque procedit. Qui cum Patre et Filio simul adoratur et\r\nconglorificatur. Qui locutus est per prophetas._\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Et unam Catholicam et Apostolicam Ecclesiam. Confiteor unum Baptisma\r\nin remissionem peccatorum. Et exspecto Resurrectionem mortuorum. Et\r\nvitam venturi saeculi. Amen._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done!\xe2\x80\x9d said several, enjoying the last word, as being the first\r\nand only one they had recognized.\r\n\r\nThen Jude seemed to shake the fumes from his brain, as he stared round\r\nupon them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou pack of fools!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWhich one of you knows whether I have\r\nsaid it or no? It might have been the Ratcatcher\xe2\x80\x99s Daughter in double\r\nDutch for all that your besotted heads can tell! See what I have\r\nbrought myself to\xe2\x80\x94the crew I have come among!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe landlord, who had already had his license endorsed for harbouring\r\nqueer characters, feared a riot, and came outside the counter; but\r\nJude, in his sudden flash of reason, had turned in disgust and left the\r\nscene, the door slamming with a dull thud behind him.\r\n\r\nHe hastened down the lane and round into the straight broad street,\r\nwhich he followed till it merged in the highway, and all sound of his\r\nlate companions had been left behind. Onward he still went, under the\r\ninfluence of a childlike yearning for the one being in the world to\r\nwhom it seemed possible to fly\xe2\x80\x94an unreasoning desire, whose ill\r\njudgement was not apparent to him now. In the course of an hour, when\r\nit was between ten and eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock, he entered the village of\r\nLumsdon, and reaching the cottage, saw that a light was burning in a\r\ndownstairs room, which he assumed, rightly as it happened, to be hers.\r\n\r\nJude stepped close to the wall, and tapped with his finger on the pane,\r\nsaying impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cSue, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe must have recognized his voice, for the light disappeared from the\r\napartment, and in a second or two the door was unlocked and opened, and\r\nSue appeared with a candle in her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Jude? Yes, it is! My dear, dear cousin, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help coming, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, sinking down upon the\r\ndoorstep. \xe2\x80\x9cI am so wicked, Sue\xe2\x80\x94my heart is nearly broken, and I could\r\nnot bear my life as it was! So I have been drinking, and blaspheming,\r\nor next door to it, and saying holy things in disreputable\r\nquarters\xe2\x80\x94repeating in idle bravado words which ought never to be\r\nuttered but reverently! Oh, do anything with me, Sue\xe2\x80\x94kill me\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare! Only don\xe2\x80\x99t hate me and despise me like all the rest of the\r\nworld!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are ill, poor dear! No, I won\xe2\x80\x99t despise you; of course I won\xe2\x80\x99t!\r\nCome in and rest, and let me see what I can do for you. Now lean on me,\r\nand don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d With one hand holding the candle and the other\r\nsupporting him, she led him indoors, and placed him in the only easy\r\nchair the meagrely furnished house afforded, stretching his feet upon\r\nanother, and pulling off his boots. Jude, now getting towards his sober\r\nsenses, could only say, \xe2\x80\x9cDear, dear Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d in a voice broken by grief\r\nand contrition.\r\n\r\nShe asked him if he wanted anything to eat, but he shook his head. Then\r\ntelling him to go to sleep, and that she would come down early in the\r\nmorning and get him some breakfast, she bade him good-night and\r\nascended the stairs.\r\n\r\nAlmost immediately he fell into a heavy slumber, and did not wake till\r\ndawn. At first he did not know where he was, but by degrees his\r\nsituation cleared to him, and he beheld it in all the ghastliness of a\r\nright mind. She knew the worst of him\xe2\x80\x94the very worst. How could he face\r\nher now? She would soon be coming down to see about breakfast, as she\r\nhad said, and there would he be in all his shame confronting her. He\r\ncould not bear the thought, and softly drawing on his boots, and taking\r\nhis hat from the nail on which she had hung it, he slipped noiselessly\r\nout of the house.\r\n\r\nHis fixed idea was to get away to some obscure spot and hide, and\r\nperhaps pray; and the only spot which occurred to him was Marygreen. He\r\ncalled at his lodging in Christminster, where he found awaiting him a\r\nnote of dismissal from his employer; and having packed up he turned his\r\nback upon the city that had been such a thorn in his side, and struck\r\nsouthward into Wessex. He had no money left in his pocket, his small\r\nsavings, deposited at one of the banks in Christminster, having\r\nfortunately been left untouched. To get to Marygreen, therefore, his\r\nonly course was walking; and the distance being nearly twenty miles, he\r\nhad ample time to complete on the way the sobering process begun in\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nAt some hour of the evening he reached Alfredston. Here he pawned his\r\nwaistcoat, and having gone out of the town a mile or two, slept under a\r\nrick that night. At dawn he rose, shook off the hayseeds and stems from\r\nhis clothes, and started again, breasting the long white road up the\r\nhill to the downs, which had been visible to him a long way off, and\r\npassing the milestone at the top, whereon he had carved his hopes years\r\nago.\r\n\r\nHe reached the ancient hamlet while the people were at breakfast. Weary\r\nand mud-bespattered, but quite possessed of his ordinary clearness of\r\nbrain, he sat down by the well, thinking as he did so what a poor\r\nChrist he made. Seeing a trough of water near he bathed his face, and\r\nwent on to the cottage of his great-aunt, whom he found breakfasting in\r\nbed, attended by the woman who lived with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94out o\xe2\x80\x99 work?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his relative, regarding him through eyes\r\nsunken deep, under lids heavy as pot-covers, no other cause for his\r\ntumbled appearance suggesting itself to one whose whole life had been a\r\nstruggle with material things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude heavily. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I must have a little rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRefreshed by some breakfast, he went up to his old room and lay down in\r\nhis shirt-sleeves, after the manner of the artizan. He fell asleep for\r\na short while, and when he awoke it was as if he had awakened in hell.\r\nIt _was_ hell\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe hell of conscious failure,\xe2\x80\x9d both in ambition and in\r\nlove. He thought of that previous abyss into which he had fallen before\r\nleaving this part of the country; the deepest deep he had supposed it\r\nthen; but it was not so deep as this. That had been the breaking in of\r\nthe outer bulwarks of his hope: this was of his second line.\r\n\r\nIf he had been a woman he must have screamed under the nervous tension\r\nwhich he was now undergoing. But that relief being denied to his\r\nvirility, he clenched his teeth in misery, bringing lines about his\r\nmouth like those in the Laoco\xc3\xb6n, and corrugations between his brows.\r\n\r\nA mournful wind blew through the trees, and sounded in the chimney like\r\nthe pedal notes of an organ. Each ivy leaf overgrowing the wall of the\r\nchurchless church-yard hard by, now abandoned, pecked its neighbour\r\nsmartly, and the vane on the new Victorian-Gothic church in the new\r\nspot had already begun to creak. Yet apparently it was not always the\r\noutdoor wind that made the deep murmurs; it was a voice. He guessed its\r\norigin in a moment or two; the curate was praying with his aunt in the\r\nadjoining room. He remembered her speaking of him. Presently the sounds\r\nceased, and a step seemed to cross the landing. Jude sat up, and\r\nshouted \xe2\x80\x9cHoi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe step made for his door, which was open, and a man looked in. It was\r\na young clergyman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are Mr. Highridge,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cMy aunt has mentioned you\r\nmore than once. Well, here I am, just come home; a fellow gone to the\r\nbad; though I had the best intentions in the world at one time. Now I\r\nam melancholy mad, what with drinking and one thing and another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSlowly Jude unfolded to the curate his late plans and movements, by an\r\nunconscious bias dwelling less upon the intellectual and ambitious side\r\nof his dream, and more upon the theological, though this had, up till\r\nnow, been merely a portion of the general plan of advancement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I know I have been a fool, and that folly is with me,\xe2\x80\x9d added Jude\r\nin conclusion. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I don\xe2\x80\x99t regret the collapse of my university hopes\r\none jot. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t begin again if I were sure to succeed. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nfor social success any more at all. But I do feel I should like to do\r\nsome good thing; and I bitterly regret the Church, and the loss of my\r\nchance of being her ordained minister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe curate, who was a new man to this neighbourhood, had grown deeply\r\ninterested, and at last he said: \xe2\x80\x9cIf you feel a real call to the\r\nministry, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t say from your conversation that you do not, for\r\nit is that of a thoughtful and educated man, you might enter the Church\r\nas a licentiate. Only you must make up your mind to avoid strong\r\ndrink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could avoid that easily enough, if I had any kind of hope to support\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart Third AT MELCHESTER\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cFor there was no other girl, O bridegroom, like her!\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\xe2\x80\x94SAPPHO (H. T. Wharton).\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was a new idea\xe2\x80\x94the ecclesiastical and altruistic life as distinct\r\nfrom the intellectual and emulative life. A man could preach and do\r\ngood to his fellow-creatures without taking double-firsts in the\r\nschools of Christminster, or having anything but ordinary knowledge.\r\nThe old fancy which had led on to the culminating vision of the\r\nbishopric had not been an ethical or theological enthusiasm at all, but\r\na mundane ambition masquerading in a surplice. He feared that his whole\r\nscheme had degenerated to, even though it might not have originated in,\r\na social unrest which had no foundation in the nobler instincts; which\r\nwas purely an artificial product of civilization. There were thousands\r\nof young men on the same self-seeking track at the present moment. The\r\nsensual hind who ate, drank, and lived carelessly with his wife through\r\nthe days of his vanity was a more likable being than he.\r\n\r\nBut to enter the Church in such an unscholarly way that he could not in\r\nany probability rise to a higher grade through all his career than that\r\nof the humble curate wearing his life out in an obscure village or city\r\nslum\xe2\x80\x94that might have a touch of goodness and greatness in it; that\r\nmight be true religion, and a purgatorial course worthy of being\r\nfollowed by a remorseful man.\r\n\r\nThe favourable light in which this new thought showed itself by\r\ncontrast with his foregone intentions cheered Jude, as he sat there,\r\nshabby and lonely; and it may be said to have given, during the next\r\nfew days, the _coup de gr\xc3\xa2ce_ to his intellectual career\xe2\x80\x94a career which\r\nhad extended over the greater part of a dozen years. He did nothing,\r\nhowever, for some long stagnant time to advance his new desire,\r\noccupying himself with little local jobs in putting up and lettering\r\nheadstones about the neighbouring villages, and submitting to be\r\nregarded as a social failure, a returned purchase, by the half-dozen or\r\nso of farmers and other country-people who condescended to nod to him.\r\n\r\nThe human interest of the new intention\xe2\x80\x94and a human interest is\r\nindispensable to the most spiritual and self-sacrificing\xe2\x80\x94was created by\r\na letter from Sue, bearing a fresh postmark. She evidently wrote with\r\nanxiety, and told very little about her own doings, more than that she\r\nhad passed some sort of examination for a Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Scholarship, and was\r\ngoing to enter a training college at Melchester to complete herself for\r\nthe vocation she had chosen, partly by his influence. There was a\r\ntheological college at Melchester; Melchester was a quiet and soothing\r\nplace, almost entirely ecclesiastical in its tone; a spot where worldly\r\nlearning and intellectual smartness had no establishment; where the\r\naltruistic feeling that he did possess would perhaps be more highly\r\nestimated than a brilliancy which he did not.\r\n\r\nAs it would be necessary that he should continue for a time to work at\r\nhis trade while reading up Divinity, which he had neglected at\r\nChristminster for the ordinary classical grind, what better course for\r\nhim than to get employment at the further city, and pursue this plan of\r\nreading? That his excessive human interest in the new place was\r\nentirely of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s making, while at the same time Sue was to be regarded\r\neven less than formerly as proper to create it, had an ethical\r\ncontradictoriness to which he was not blind. But that much he conceded\r\nto human frailty, and hoped to learn to love her only as a friend and\r\nkinswoman.\r\n\r\nHe considered that he might so mark out his coming years as to begin\r\nhis ministry at the age of thirty\xe2\x80\x94an age which much attracted him as\r\nbeing that of his exemplar when he first began to teach in Galilee.\r\nThis would allow him plenty of time for deliberate study, and for\r\nacquiring capital by his trade to help his aftercourse of keeping the\r\nnecessary terms at a theological college.\r\n\r\n\r\nChristmas had come and passed, and Sue had gone to the Melchester\r\nNormal School. The time was just the worst in the year for Jude to get\r\ninto new employment, and he had written suggesting to her that he\r\nshould postpone his arrival for a month or so, till the days had\r\nlengthened. She had acquiesced so readily that he wished he had not\r\nproposed it\xe2\x80\x94she evidently did not much care about him, though she had\r\nnever once reproached him for his strange conduct in coming to her that\r\nnight, and his silent disappearance. Neither had she ever said a word\r\nabout her relations with Mr. Phillotson.\r\n\r\nSuddenly, however, quite a passionate letter arrived from Sue. She was\r\nquite lonely and miserable, she told him. She hated the place she was\r\nin; it was worse than the ecclesiastical designer\xe2\x80\x99s; worse than\r\nanywhere. She felt utterly friendless; could he come\r\nimmediately?\xe2\x80\x94though when he did come she would only be able to see him\r\nat limited times, the rules of the establishment she found herself in\r\nbeing strict to a degree. It was Mr. Phillotson who had advised her to\r\ncome there, and she wished she had never listened to him.\r\n\r\nPhillotson\xe2\x80\x99s suit was not exactly prospering, evidently; and Jude felt\r\nunreasonably glad. He packed up his things and went to Melchester with\r\na lighter heart than he had known for months.\r\n\r\nThis being the turning over a new leaf he duly looked about for a\r\ntemperance hotel, and found a little establishment of that description\r\nin the street leading from the station. When he had had something to\r\neat he walked out into the dull winter light over the town bridge, and\r\nturned the corner towards the Close. The day was foggy, and standing\r\nunder the walls of the most graceful architectural pile in England he\r\npaused and looked up. The lofty building was visible as far as the\r\nroofridge; above, the dwindling spire rose more and more remotely, till\r\nits apex was quite lost in the mist drifting across it.\r\n\r\nThe lamps now began to be lighted, and turning to the west front he\r\nwalked round. He took it as a good omen that numerous blocks of stone\r\nwere lying about, which signified that the cathedral was undergoing\r\nrestoration or repair to a considerable extent. It seemed to him, full\r\nof the superstitions of his beliefs, that this was an exercise of\r\nforethought on the part of a ruling Power, that he might find plenty to\r\ndo in the art he practised while waiting for a call to higher labours.\r\n\r\nThen a wave of warmth came over him as he thought how near he now stood\r\nto the bright-eyed vivacious girl with the broad forehead and pile of\r\ndark hair above it; the girl with the kindling glance, daringly soft at\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94something like that of the girls he had seen in engravings from\r\npaintings of the Spanish school. She was here\xe2\x80\x94actually in this Close\xe2\x80\x94in\r\none of the houses confronting this very west fa\xc3\xa7ade.\r\n\r\nHe went down the broad gravel path towards the building. It was an\r\nancient edifice of the fifteenth century, once a palace, now a\r\ntraining-school, with mullioned and transomed windows, and a courtyard\r\nin front shut in from the road by a wall. Jude opened the gate and went\r\nup to the door through which, on inquiring for his cousin, he was\r\ngingerly admitted to a waiting-room, and in a few minutes she came.\r\n\r\nThough she had been here such a short while, she was not as he had seen\r\nher last. All her bounding manner was gone; her curves of motion had\r\nbecome subdued lines. The screens and subtleties of convention had\r\nlikewise disappeared. Yet neither was she quite the woman who had\r\nwritten the letter that summoned him. That had plainly been dashed off\r\nin an impulse which second thoughts had somewhat regretted; thoughts\r\nthat were possibly of his recent self-disgrace. Jude was quite overcome\r\nwith emotion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94think me a demoralized wretch\xe2\x80\x94for coming to you as I was\xe2\x80\x94and\r\ngoing so shamefully, Sue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have tried not to! You said enough to let me know what had\r\ncaused it. I hope I shall never have any doubt of your worthiness, my\r\npoor Jude! And I am glad you have come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe wore a murrey-coloured gown with a little lace collar. It was made\r\nquite plain, and hung about her slight figure with clinging\r\ngracefulness. Her hair, which formerly she had worn according to the\r\ncustom of the day was now twisted up tightly, and she had altogether\r\nthe air of a woman clipped and pruned by severe discipline, an\r\nunder-brightness shining through from the depths which that discipline\r\nhad not yet been able to reach.\r\n\r\nShe had come forward prettily, but Jude felt that she had hardly\r\nexpected him to kiss her, as he was burning to do, under other colours\r\nthan those of cousinship. He could not perceive the least sign that Sue\r\nregarded him as a lover, or ever would do so, now that she knew the\r\nworst of him, even if he had the right to behave as one; and this\r\nhelped on his growing resolve to tell her of his matrimonial\r\nentanglement, which he had put off doing from time to time in sheer\r\ndread of losing the bliss of her company.\r\n\r\nSue came out into the town with him, and they walked and talked with\r\ntongues centred only on the passing moments. Jude said he would like to\r\nbuy her a little present of some sort, and then she confessed, with\r\nsomething of shame, that she was dreadfully hungry. They were kept on\r\nvery short allowances in the college, and a dinner, tea, and supper all\r\nin one was the present she most desired in the world. Jude thereupon\r\ntook her to an inn and ordered whatever the house afforded, which was\r\nnot much. The place, however, gave them a delightful opportunity for a\r\n_t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_, nobody else being in the room, and they talked freely.\r\n\r\nShe told him about the school as it was at that date, and the rough\r\nliving, and the mixed character of her fellow-students, gathered\r\ntogether from all parts of the diocese, and how she had to get up and\r\nwork by gas-light in the early morning, with all the bitterness of a\r\nyoung person to whom restraint was new. To all this he listened; but it\r\nwas not what he wanted especially to know\xe2\x80\x94her relations with\r\nPhillotson. That was what she did not tell. When they had sat and\r\neaten, Jude impulsively placed his hand upon hers; she looked up and\r\nsmiled, and took his quite freely into her own little soft one,\r\ndividing his fingers and coolly examining them, as if they were the\r\nfingers of a glove she was purchasing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour hands are rather rough, Jude, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. So would yours be if they held a mallet and chisel all day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t dislike it, you know. I think it is noble to see a man\xe2\x80\x99s hands\r\nsubdued to what he works in\xe2\x80\xa6 Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m rather glad I came to this\r\ntraining-school, after all. See how independent I shall be after the\r\ntwo years\xe2\x80\x99 training! I shall pass pretty high, I expect, and Mr.\r\nPhillotson will use his influence to get me a big school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had touched the subject at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI had a suspicion, a fear,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJude, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he\xe2\x80\x94cared about you rather warmly, and perhaps wanted to\r\nmarry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow don\xe2\x80\x99t be such a silly boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has said something about it, I expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he had, what would it matter? An old man like him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, Sue; he\xe2\x80\x99s not so very old. And I know what I saw him doing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot kissing me\xe2\x80\x94that I\xe2\x80\x99m certain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But putting his arm round your waist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94I remember. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know he was going to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are wriggling out if it, Sue, and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t quite kind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer ever-sensitive lip began to quiver, and her eye to blink, at\r\nsomething this reproof was deciding her to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you\xe2\x80\x99ll be angry if I tell you everything, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said soothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have no real right to\r\nask you, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, with the perverseness that was part of\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is what I have done: I have promised\xe2\x80\x94I have promised\xe2\x80\x94that I\r\nwill marry him when I come out of the training-school two years hence,\r\nand have got my certificate; his plan being that we shall then take a\r\nlarge double school in a great town\xe2\x80\x94he the boys\xe2\x80\x99 and I the girls\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nmarried school-teachers often do, and make a good income between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Sue! \xe2\x80\xa6 But of course it is right\xe2\x80\x94you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe glanced at her and their eyes met, the reproach in his own belying\r\nhis words. Then he drew his hand quite away from hers, and turned his\r\nface in estrangement from her to the window. Sue regarded him passively\r\nwithout moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you would be angry!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with an air of no emotion\r\nwhatever. \xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94I am wrong, I suppose! I ought not to have let you\r\ncome to see me! We had better not meet again; and we\xe2\x80\x99ll only correspond\r\nat long intervals, on purely business matters!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was just the one thing he would not be able to bear, as she\r\nprobably knew, and it brought him round at once. \xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, we will,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cYour being engaged can make no difference to me\r\nwhatever. I have a perfect right to see you when I want to; and I\r\nshall!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen don\xe2\x80\x99t let us talk of it any more. It is quite spoiling our\r\nevening together. What does it matter about what one is going to do two\r\nyears hence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was something of a riddle to him, and he let the subject drift\r\naway. \xe2\x80\x9cShall we go and sit in the cathedral?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, when their meal\r\nwas finished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCathedral? Yes. Though I think I\xe2\x80\x99d rather sit in the railway station,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe answered, a remnant of vexation still in her voice. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ncentre of the town life now. The cathedral has had its day!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow modern you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo would you be if you had lived so much in the Middle Ages as I have\r\ndone these last few years! The cathedral was a very good place four or\r\nfive centuries ago; but it is played out now\xe2\x80\xa6 I am not modern, either.\r\nI am more ancient than medi\xc3\xa6valism, if you only knew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude looked distressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t say any more of that!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly you don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nhow bad I am, from your point of view, or you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think so much of\r\nme, or care whether I was engaged or not. Now there\xe2\x80\x99s just time for us\r\nto walk round the Close, then I must go in, or I shall be locked out\r\nfor the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took her to the gate and they parted. Jude had a conviction that his\r\nunhappy visit to her on that sad night had precipitated this marriage\r\nengagement, and it did anything but add to his happiness. Her reproach\r\nhad taken that shape, then, and not the shape of words. However, next\r\nday he set about seeking employment, which it was not so easy to get as\r\nat Christminster, there being, as a rule, less stone-cutting in\r\nprogress in this quiet city, and hands being mostly permanent. But he\r\nedged himself in by degrees. His first work was some carving at the\r\ncemetery on the hill; and ultimately he became engaged on the labour he\r\nmost desired\xe2\x80\x94the cathedral repairs, which were very extensive, the\r\nwhole interior stonework having been overhauled, to be largely replaced\r\nby new. It might be a labour of years to get it all done, and he had\r\nconfidence enough in his own skill with the mallet and chisel to feel\r\nthat it would be a matter of choice with himself how long he would\r\nstay.\r\n\r\nThe lodgings he took near the Close Gate would not have disgraced a\r\ncurate, the rent representing a higher percentage on his wages than\r\nmechanics of any sort usually care to pay. His combined bed and\r\nsitting-room was furnished with framed photographs of the rectories and\r\ndeaneries at which his landlady had lived as trusted servant in her\r\ntime, and the parlour downstairs bore a clock on the mantelpiece\r\ninscribed to the effect that it was presented to the same\r\nserious-minded woman by her fellow-servants on the occasion of her\r\nmarriage. Jude added to the furniture of his room by unpacking\r\nphotographs of the ecclesiastical carvings and monuments that he had\r\nexecuted with his own hands; and he was deemed a satisfactory\r\nacquisition as tenant of the vacant apartment.\r\n\r\nHe found an ample supply of theological books in the city book-shops,\r\nand with these his studies were recommenced in a different spirit and\r\ndirection from his former course. As a relaxation from the Fathers, and\r\nsuch stock works as Paley and Butler, he read Newman, Pusey, and many\r\nother modern lights. He hired a harmonium, set it up in his lodging,\r\nand practised chants thereon, single and double.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow is our grand day, you know. Where shall we go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have leave from three till nine. Wherever we can get to and come\r\nback from in that time. Not ruins, Jude\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94Wardour Castle. And then we can do Fonthill if we like\xe2\x80\x94all in the\r\nsame afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWardour is Gothic ruins\xe2\x80\x94and I hate Gothic!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Quite otherwise. It is a classic building\xe2\x80\x94Corinthian, I think;\r\nwith a lot of pictures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94that will do. I like the sound of Corinthian. We\xe2\x80\x99ll go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir conversation had run thus some few weeks later, and next morning\r\nthey prepared to start. Every detail of the outing was a facet\r\nreflecting a sparkle to Jude, and he did not venture to meditate on the\r\nlife of inconsistency he was leading. His Sue\xe2\x80\x99s conduct was one lovely\r\nconundrum to him; he could say no more.\r\n\r\nThere duly came the charm of calling at the college door for her; her\r\nemergence in a nunlike simplicity of costume that was rather enforced\r\nthan desired; the traipsing along to the station, the porters\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cB\xe2\x80\x99your\r\nleave!,\xe2\x80\x9d the screaming of the trains\xe2\x80\x94everything formed the basis of a\r\nbeautiful crystallization. Nobody stared at Sue, because she was so\r\nplainly dressed, which comforted Jude in the thought that only himself\r\nknew the charms those habiliments subdued. A matter of ten pounds spent\r\nin a drapery-shop, which had no connection with her real life or her\r\nreal self, would have set all Melchester staring. The guard of the\r\ntrain thought they were lovers, and put them into a compartment all by\r\nthemselves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a good intention wasted!\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\nJude did not respond. He thought the remark unnecessarily cruel, and\r\npartly untrue.\r\n\r\nThey reached the park and castle and wandered through the\r\npicture-galleries, Jude stopping by preference in front of the\r\ndevotional pictures by Del Sarto, Guido Reni, Spagnoletto,\r\nSassoferrato, Carlo Dolci, and others. Sue paused patiently beside him,\r\nand stole critical looks into his face as, regarding the Virgins, Holy\r\nFamilies, and Saints, it grew reverent and abstracted. When she had\r\nthoroughly estimated him at this, she would move on and wait for him\r\nbefore a Lely or Reynolds. It was evident that her cousin deeply\r\ninterested her, as one might be interested in a man puzzling out his\r\nway along a labyrinth from which one had one\xe2\x80\x99s self escaped.\r\n\r\nWhen they came out a long time still remained to them and Jude proposed\r\nthat as soon as they had had something to eat they should walk across\r\nthe high country to the north of their present position, and intercept\r\nthe train of another railway leading back to Melchester, at a station\r\nabout seven miles off. Sue, who was inclined for any adventure that\r\nwould intensify the sense of her day\xe2\x80\x99s freedom, readily agreed; and\r\naway they went, leaving the adjoining station behind them.\r\n\r\nIt was indeed open country, wide and high. They talked and bounded on,\r\nJude cutting from a little covert a long walking-stick for Sue as tall\r\nas herself, with a great crook, which made her look like a shepherdess.\r\nAbout half-way on their journey they crossed a main road running due\r\neast and west\xe2\x80\x94the old road from London to Land\xe2\x80\x99s End. They paused, and\r\nlooked up and down it for a moment, and remarked upon the desolation\r\nwhich had come over this once lively thoroughfare, while the wind\r\ndipped to earth and scooped straws and hay-stems from the ground.\r\n\r\nThey crossed the road and passed on, but during the next half-mile Sue\r\nseemed to grow tired, and Jude began to be distressed for her. They had\r\nwalked a good distance altogether, and if they could not reach the\r\nother station it would be rather awkward. For a long time there was no\r\ncottage visible on the wide expanse of down and turnip-land; but\r\npresently they came to a sheepfold, and next to the shepherd, pitching\r\nhurdles. He told them that the only house near was his mother\xe2\x80\x99s and\r\nhis, pointing to a little dip ahead from which a faint blue smoke\r\narose, and recommended them to go on and rest there.\r\n\r\nThis they did, and entered the house, admitted by an old woman without\r\na single tooth, to whom they were as civil as strangers can be when\r\ntheir only chance of rest and shelter lies in the favour of the\r\nhouseholder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice little cottage,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know about the niceness. I shall have to thatch it soon,\r\nand where the thatch is to come from I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell, for straw do get\r\nthat dear, that \xe2\x80\x99twill soon be cheaper to cover your house wi\xe2\x80\x99 chainey\r\nplates than thatch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sat resting, and the shepherd came in. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ee mind I,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nwith a deprecating wave of the hand; \xe2\x80\x9cbide here as long as ye will. But\r\nmid you be thinking o\xe2\x80\x99 getting back to Melchester to-night by train?\r\nBecause you\xe2\x80\x99ll never do it in this world, since you don\xe2\x80\x99t know the lie\r\nof the country. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind going with ye some o\xe2\x80\x99 the ways, but even\r\nthen the train mid be gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey started up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can bide here, you know, over the night\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99em, Mother? The\r\nplace is welcome to ye. \xe2\x80\x99Tis hard lying, rather, but volk may do\r\nworse.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to Jude and asked privately: \xe2\x80\x9cBe you a married\r\ncouple?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHsh\xe2\x80\x94no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I meant nothing ba\xe2\x80\x99dy\xe2\x80\x94not I! Well then, she can go into Mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom, and you and I can lie in the outer chimmer after they\xe2\x80\x99ve gone\r\nthrough. I can call ye soon enough to catch the first train back.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ve lost this one now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn consideration they decided to close with this offer, and drew up and\r\nshared with the shepherd and his mother the boiled bacon and greens for\r\nsupper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI rather like this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, while their entertainers were clearing\r\naway the dishes. \xe2\x80\x9cOutside all laws except gravitation and germination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou only think you like it; you don\xe2\x80\x99t: you are quite a product of\r\ncivilization,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, a recollection of her engagement reviving his\r\nsoreness a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I am not, Jude. I like reading and all that, but I crave to get\r\nback to the life of my infancy and its freedom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember it so well? You seem to me to have nothing\r\nunconventional at all about you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, haven\xe2\x80\x99t I! You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s inside me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Ishmaelite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn urban miss is what you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked severe disagreement, and turned away.\r\n\r\nThe shepherd aroused them the next morning, as he had said. It was\r\nbright and clear, and the four miles to the train were accomplished\r\npleasantly. When they had reached Melchester, and walked to the Close,\r\nand the gables of the old building in which she was again to be immured\r\nrose before Sue\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, she looked a little scared. \xe2\x80\x9cI expect I shall\r\ncatch it!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\nThey rang the great bell and waited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I bought something for you, which I had nearly forgotten,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid quickly, searching her pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a new little photograph of\r\nme. Would you like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Would_ I!\xe2\x80\x9d He took it gladly, and the porter came. There seemed to be\r\nan ominous glance on his face when he opened the gate. She passed in,\r\nlooking back at Jude, and waving her hand.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe seventy young women, of ages varying in the main from nineteen to\r\none-and-twenty, though several were older, who at this date filled the\r\nspecies of nunnery known as the Training-School at Melchester, formed a\r\nvery mixed community, which included the daughters of mechanics,\r\ncurates, surgeons, shopkeepers, farmers, dairy-men, soldiers, sailors,\r\nand villagers. They sat in the large school-room of the establishment\r\non the evening previously described, and word was passed round that Sue\r\nBridehead had not come in at closing-time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe went out with her young man,\xe2\x80\x9d said a second-year\xe2\x80\x99s student, who\r\nknew about young men. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Miss Traceley saw her at the station with\r\nhim. She\xe2\x80\x99ll have it hot when she does come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said he was her cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d observed a youthful new girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat excuse has been made a little too often in this school to be\r\neffectual in saving our souls,\xe2\x80\x9d said the head girl of the year, drily.\r\n\r\nThe fact was that, only twelve months before, there had occurred a\r\nlamentable seduction of one of the pupils who had made the same\r\nstatement in order to gain meetings with her lover. The affair had\r\ncreated a scandal, and the management had consequently been rough on\r\ncousins ever since.\r\n\r\nAt nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock the names were called, Sue\xe2\x80\x99s being pronounced three\r\ntimes sonorously by Miss Traceley without eliciting an answer.\r\n\r\nAt a quarter past nine the seventy stood up to sing the \xe2\x80\x9cEvening Hymn,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand then knelt down to prayers. After prayers they went in to supper,\r\nand every girl\xe2\x80\x99s thought was, Where is Sue Bridehead? Some of the\r\nstudents, who had seen Jude from the window, felt that they would not\r\nmind risking her punishment for the pleasure of being kissed by such a\r\nkindly-faced young man. Hardly one among them believed in the\r\ncousinship.\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later they all lay in their cubicles, their tender\r\nfeminine faces upturned to the flaring gas-jets which at intervals\r\nstretched down the long dormitories, every face bearing the legend \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nWeaker\xe2\x80\x9d upon it, as the penalty of the sex wherein they were moulded,\r\nwhich by no possible exertion of their willing hearts and abilities\r\ncould be made strong while the inexorable laws of nature remain what\r\nthey are. They formed a pretty, suggestive, pathetic sight, of whose\r\npathos and beauty they were themselves unconscious, and would not\r\ndiscover till, amid the storms and strains of after-years, with their\r\ninjustice, loneliness, child-bearing, and bereavement, their minds\r\nwould revert to this experience as to something which had been allowed\r\nto slip past them insufficiently regarded.\r\n\r\nOne of the mistresses came in to turn out the lights, and before doing\r\nso gave a final glance at Sue\xe2\x80\x99s cot, which remained empty, and at her\r\nlittle dressing-table at the foot, which, like all the rest, was\r\nornamented with various girlish trifles, framed photographs being not\r\nthe least conspicuous among them. Sue\xe2\x80\x99s table had a moderate show, two\r\nmen in their filigree and velvet frames standing together beside her\r\nlooking-glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are these men\xe2\x80\x94did she ever say?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the mistress. \xe2\x80\x9cStrictly\r\nspeaking, relations\xe2\x80\x99 portraits only are allowed on these tables, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x94the middle-aged man,\xe2\x80\x9d said a student in the next bed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cis the\r\nschoolmaster she served under\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Phillotson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the other\xe2\x80\x94this undergraduate in cap and gown\xe2\x80\x94who is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a friend, or was. She has never told his name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it either of these two who came for her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure \xe2\x80\x99twas not the undergraduate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite. He was a young man with a black beard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lights were promptly extinguished, and till they fell asleep the\r\ngirls indulged in conjectures about Sue, and wondered what games she\r\nhad carried on in London and at Christminster before she came here,\r\nsome of the more restless ones getting out of bed and looking from the\r\nmullioned windows at the vast west front of the cathedral opposite, and\r\nthe spire rising behind it.\r\n\r\nWhen they awoke the next morning they glanced into Sue\xe2\x80\x99s nook, to find\r\nit still without a tenant. After the early lessons by gas-light, in\r\nhalf-toilet, and when they had come up to dress for breakfast, the bell\r\nof the entrance gate was heard to ring loudly. The mistress of the\r\ndormitory went away, and presently came back to say that the\r\nprincipal\xe2\x80\x99s orders were that nobody was to speak to Bridehead without\r\npermission.\r\n\r\nWhen, accordingly, Sue came into the dormitory to hastily tidy herself,\r\nlooking flushed and tired, she went to her cubicle in silence, none of\r\nthem coming out to greet her or to make inquiry. When they had gone\r\ndownstairs they found that she did not follow them into the dining-hall\r\nto breakfast, and they then learnt that she had been severely\r\nreprimanded, and ordered to a solitary room for a week, there to be\r\nconfined, and take her meals, and do all her reading.\r\n\r\nAt this the seventy murmured, the sentence being, they thought, too\r\nsevere. A round robin was prepared and sent in to the principal, asking\r\nfor a remission of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s punishment. No notice was taken. Towards\r\nevening, when the geography mistress began dictating her subject, the\r\ngirls in the class sat with folded arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that you are not going to work?\xe2\x80\x9d said the mistress at last.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may as well tell you that it has been ascertained that the young man\r\nBridehead stayed out with was not her cousin, for the very good reason\r\nthat she has no such relative. We have written to Christminster to\r\nascertain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are willing to take her word,\xe2\x80\x9d said the head girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis young man was discharged from his work at Christminster for\r\ndrunkenness and blasphemy in public-houses, and he has come here to\r\nlive, entirely to be near her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, they remained stolid and motionless, and the mistress left the\r\nroom to inquire from her superiors what was to be done.\r\n\r\nPresently, towards dusk, the pupils, as they sat, heard exclamations\r\nfrom the first-year\xe2\x80\x99s girls in an adjoining classroom, and one rushed\r\nin to say that Sue Bridehead had got out of the back window of the room\r\nin which she had been confined, escaped in the dark across the lawn,\r\nand disappeared. How she had managed to get out of the garden nobody\r\ncould tell, as it was bounded by the river at the bottom, and the side\r\ndoor was locked.\r\n\r\nThey went and looked at the empty room, the casement between the middle\r\nmullions of which stood open. The lawn was again searched with a\r\nlantern, every bush and shrub being examined, but she was nowhere\r\nhidden. Then the porter of the front gate was interrogated, and on\r\nreflection he said that he remembered hearing a sort of splashing in\r\nthe stream at the back, but he had taken no notice, thinking some ducks\r\nhad come down the river from above.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must have walked through the river!\xe2\x80\x9d said a mistress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr drownded herself,\xe2\x80\x9d said the porter.\r\n\r\nThe mind of the matron was horrified\xe2\x80\x94not so much at the possible death\r\nof Sue as at the possible half-column detailing that event in all the\r\nnewspapers, which, added to the scandal of the year before, would give\r\nthe college an unenviable notoriety for many months to come.\r\n\r\nMore lanterns were procured, and the river examined; and then, at last,\r\non the opposite shore, which was open to the fields, some little\r\nboot-tracks were discerned in the mud, which left no doubt that the too\r\nexcitable girl had waded through a depth of water reaching nearly to\r\nher shoulders\xe2\x80\x94for this was the chief river of the county, and was\r\nmentioned in all the geography books with respect. As Sue had not\r\nbrought disgrace upon the school by drowning herself, the matron began\r\nto speak superciliously of her, and to express gladness that she was\r\ngone.\r\n\r\nOn the self-same evening Jude sat in his lodgings by the Close Gate.\r\nOften at this hour after dusk he would enter the silent Close, and\r\nstand opposite the house that contained Sue, and watch the shadows of\r\nthe girls\xe2\x80\x99 heads passing to and fro upon the blinds, and wish he had\r\nnothing else to do but to sit reading and learning all day what many of\r\nthe thoughtless inmates despised. But to-night, having finished tea and\r\nbrushed himself up, he was deep in the perusal of the Twenty-ninth\r\nVolume of Pusey\xe2\x80\x99s Library of the Fathers, a set of books which he had\r\npurchased of a second-hand dealer at a price that seemed to him to be\r\none of miraculous cheapness for that invaluable work. He fancied he\r\nheard something rattle lightly against his window; then he heard it\r\nagain. Certainly somebody had thrown gravel. He rose and gently lifted\r\nthe sash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude!\xe2\x80\x9d (from below).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94it is! Can I come up without being seen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen don\xe2\x80\x99t come down. Shut the window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude waited, knowing that she could enter easily enough, the front door\r\nbeing opened merely by a knob which anybody could turn, as in most old\r\ncountry towns. He palpitated at the thought that she had fled to him in\r\nher trouble as he had fled to her in his. What counterparts they were!\r\nHe unlatched the door of his room, heard a stealthy rustle on the dark\r\nstairs, and in a moment she appeared in the light of his lamp. He went\r\nup to seize her hand, and found she was clammy as a marine deity, and\r\nthat her clothes clung to her like the robes upon the figures in the\r\nParthenon frieze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m so cold!\xe2\x80\x9d she said through her chattering teeth. \xe2\x80\x9cCan I come by\r\nyour fire, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe crossed to his little grate and very little fire, but as the water\r\ndripped from her as she moved, the idea of drying herself was absurd.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever have you done, darling?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, with alarm, the tender\r\nepithet slipping out unawares.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalked through the largest river in the county\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99ve done!\r\nThey locked me up for being out with you; and it seemed so unjust that\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear it, so I got out of the window and escaped across the\r\nstream!\xe2\x80\x9d She had begun the explanation in her usual slightly\r\nindependent tones, but before she had finished the thin pink lips\r\ntrembled, and she could hardly refrain from crying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must take off all your things! And let me\r\nsee\xe2\x80\x94you must borrow some from the landlady. I\xe2\x80\x99ll ask her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! Don\xe2\x80\x99t let her know, for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake! It is close here.\xe2\x80\x9d In fact, everything was\r\nclose and handy in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s single chamber, because there was not room\r\nfor it to be otherwise. He opened a drawer, took out his best dark\r\nsuit, and giving the garments a shake, said, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, how long shall I\r\ngive you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude left the room and went into the street, where he walked up and\r\ndown. A clock struck half-past seven, and he returned. Sitting in his\r\nonly arm-chair he saw a slim and fragile being masquerading as himself\r\non a Sunday, so pathetic in her defencelessness that his heart felt big\r\nwith the sense of it. On two other chairs before the fire were her wet\r\ngarments. She blushed as he sat down beside her, but only for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose, Jude, it is odd that you should see me like this and all my\r\nthings hanging there? Yet what nonsense! They are only a woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclothes\xe2\x80\x94sexless cloth and linen\xe2\x80\xa6 I wish I didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel so ill and sick!\r\nWill you dry my clothes now? Please do, Jude, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll get a lodging by\r\nand by. It is not late yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you shan\xe2\x80\x99t, if you are ill. You must stay here.
