I wish I could get warm.\xe2\x80\x9d Jude\r\nput on her his great-coat in addition, and then ran out to the nearest\r\npublic-house, whence he returned with a little bottle in his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s six of best brandy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cNow you drink it, dear; all of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t out of the bottle, can I?\xe2\x80\x9d Jude fetched the glass from the\r\ndressing-table, and administered the spirit in some water. She gasped a\r\nlittle, but gulped it down, and lay back in the armchair.\r\n\r\nShe then began to relate circumstantially her experiences since they\r\nhad parted; but in the middle of her story her voice faltered, her head\r\nnodded, and she ceased. She was in a sound sleep. Jude, dying of\r\nanxiety lest she should have caught a chill which might permanently\r\ninjure her, was glad to hear the regular breathing. He softly went\r\nnearer to her, and observed that a warm flush now rosed her hitherto\r\nblue cheeks, and felt that her hanging hand was no longer cold. Then he\r\nstood with his back to the fire regarding her, and saw in her almost a\r\ndivinity.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s reverie was interrupted by the creak of footsteps ascending the\r\nstairs.\r\n\r\nHe whisked Sue\xe2\x80\x99s clothing from the chair where it was drying, thrust it\r\nunder the bed, and sat down to his book. Somebody knocked and opened\r\nthe door immediately. It was the landlady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know whether you was in or not, Mr. Fawley. His landlady brought up the supper,\r\nhowever, on this occasion, and he took it from her at the door.\r\n\r\nWhen she had descended he set the teapot on the hob, and drew out Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclothes anew; but they were far from dry. A thick woollen gown, he\r\nfound, held a deal of water. So he hung them up again, and enlarged his\r\nfire and mused as the steam from the garments went up the chimney.\r\n\r\nSuddenly she said, \xe2\x80\x9cJude!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Until she says by a look \xe2\x80\x98Come on\xe2\x80\x99 he is always\r\nafraid to, and if you never say it, or look it, he never comes.\r\nHowever, what I was going to say is that when I was eighteen I formed a\r\nfriendly intimacy with an undergraduate at Christminster, and he taught\r\nme a great deal, and lent me books which I should never have got hold\r\nof otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs your friendship broken off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. Some of the\r\nmost passionately erotic poets have been the most self-contained in\r\ntheir daily lives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you told Mr. Phillotson about this university scholar friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94long ago. In the year eighteen hundred and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a sarcasm in that which is rather unpleasant to me, Sue. Now\r\nwill you do what I want you to? Now will you concentrate your attention on\r\nany book of these you like, and sit with your back to me, and leave me\r\nto my custom? You are sure you won\xe2\x80\x99t join me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll look at you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Don\xe2\x80\x99t tease, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll do just as you bid me, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t vex you, Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nreplied, in the tone of a child who was going to be good for ever\r\nafter, turning her back upon him accordingly. A small Bible other than\r\nthe one he was using lay near her, and during his retreat she took it\r\nup, and turned over the leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said brightly, when he had finished and come back to her;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwill you let me make you a _new_ New Testament, like the one I made\r\nfor myself at Christminster?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. How was that made?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI altered my old one by cutting up all the Epistles and Gospels into\r\nseparate _brochures_, and rearranging them in chronological order as\r\nwritten, beginning the book with Thessalonians, following on with the\r\nEpistles, and putting the Gospels much further on. Then I had the\r\nvolume rebound. My university friend Mr.\xe2\x80\x94but never mind his name, poor\r\nboy\xe2\x80\x94said it was an excellent idea. I know that reading it afterwards\r\nmade it twice as interesting as before, and twice as understandable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, with a sense of sacrilege.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what a literary enormity this is,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as she glanced into\r\nthe pages of Solomon\xe2\x80\x99s Song. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean the synopsis at the head of each\r\nchapter, explaining away the real nature of that rhapsody. You needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe alarmed: nobody claims inspiration for the chapter headings. Indeed,\r\nmany divines treat them with contempt. It seems the drollest thing to\r\nthink of the four-and-twenty elders, or bishops, or whatever number\r\nthey were, sitting with long faces and writing down such stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude looked pained. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are quite Voltairean!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed? Then I won\xe2\x80\x99t say any more, except that people have no right to\r\nfalsify the Bible! I _hate_ such hum-bug as could attempt to plaster\r\nover with ecclesiastical abstractions such ecstatic, natural, human\r\nlove as lies in that great and passionate song!\xe2\x80\x9d Her speech had grown\r\nspirited, and almost petulant at his rebuke, and her eyes moist. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\n_wish_ I had a friend here to support me; but nobody is ever on my\r\nside!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my dear Sue, my very dear Sue, I am not against you!\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\ntaking her hand, and surprised at her introducing personal feeling into\r\nmere argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes you are, yes you are!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, turning away her face that he\r\nmight not see her brimming eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are on the side of the people in\r\nthe training-school\xe2\x80\x94at least you seem almost to be! What I insist on\r\nis, that to explain such verses as this: \xe2\x80\x98Whither is thy beloved gone,\r\nO thou fairest among women?\xe2\x80\x99 by the note: \xe2\x80\x98_The Church professeth her\r\nfaith_,\xe2\x80\x99 is supremely ridiculous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, let it be! You make such a personal matter of everything! I\r\nam\xe2\x80\x94only too inclined just now to apply the words profanely. You know\r\n_you_ are fairest among women to me, come to that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are not to say it now!\xe2\x80\x9d Sue replied, her voice changing to its\r\nsoftest note of severity. Then their eyes met, and they shook hands\r\nlike cronies in a tavern, and Jude saw the absurdity of quarrelling on\r\nsuch a hypothetical subject, and she the silliness of crying about what\r\nwas written in an old book like the Bible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t disturb your convictions\xe2\x80\x94I really won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on\r\nsoothingly, for now he was rather more ruffled than she. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I did\r\nwant and long to ennoble some man to high aims; and when I saw you, and\r\nknew you wanted to be my comrade, I\xe2\x80\x94shall I confess it?\xe2\x80\x94thought that\r\nman might be you. But you take so much tradition on trust that I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear; I suppose one must take some things on trust. Life isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlong enough to work out everything in Euclid problems before you\r\nbelieve it. I take Christianity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps you might take something worse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I might. Perhaps I have done so!\xe2\x80\x9d He thought of Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t ask what, because we are going to be _very_ nice with each\r\nother, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we, and never, never, vex each other any more?\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nlooked up trustfully, and her voice seemed trying to nestle in his\r\nbreast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall always care for you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I for you. Because you are single-hearted, and forgiving to your\r\nfaulty and tiresome little Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked away, for that epicene tenderness of hers was too harrowing.\r\nWas it that which had broken the heart of the poor leader-writer; and\r\nwas he to be the next one? \xe2\x80\xa6 But Sue was so dear! \xe2\x80\xa6 If he could only\r\nget over the sense of her sex, as she seemed to be able to do so easily\r\nof his, what a comrade she would make; for their difference of opinion\r\non conjectural subjects only drew them closer together on matters of\r\ndaily human experience. She was nearer to him than any other woman he\r\nhad ever met, and he could scarcely believe that time, creed, or\r\nabsence, would ever divide him from her.\r\n\r\nBut his grief at her incredulities returned. They sat on till she fell\r\nasleep again, and he nodded in his chair likewise. Whenever he aroused\r\nhimself he turned her things, and made up the fire anew. About six\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock he awoke completely, and lighting a candle, found that her\r\nclothes were dry. Her chair being a far more comfortable one than his\r\nshe still slept on inside his great-coat, looking warm as a new bun and\r\nboyish as a Ganymede. Placing the garments by her and touching her on\r\nthe shoulder he went downstairs, and washed himself by starlight in the\r\nyard.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen he returned she was dressed as usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow could I get out without anybody seeing me?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cThe town\r\nis not yet astir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have had no breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want any! I fear I ought not to have run away from that\r\nschool! Things seem so different in the cold light of morning, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthey? What Mr. Phillotson will say I don\xe2\x80\x99t know! It was quite by his\r\nwish that I went there. He is the only man in the world for whom I have\r\nany respect or fear. I hope he\xe2\x80\x99ll forgive me; but he\xe2\x80\x99ll scold me\r\ndreadfully, I expect!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go to him and explain\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, you shan\xe2\x80\x99t. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for him! He may think what he likes\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nshall do just as I choose!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you just this moment said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if I did, I shall do as I like for all him! I have thought of\r\nwhat I shall do\xe2\x80\x94go to the sister of one of my fellow-students in the\r\ntraining-school, who has asked me to visit her. She has a school near\r\nShaston, about eighteen miles from here\xe2\x80\x94and I shall stay there till\r\nthis has blown over, and I get back to the training-school again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the last moment he persuaded her to let him make her a cup of\r\ncoffee, in a portable apparatus he kept in his room for use on rising\r\nto go to his work every day before the household was astir.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow a dew-bit to eat with it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cand off we go. You can have a\r\nregular breakfast when you get there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went quietly out of the house, Jude accompanying her to the\r\nstation. As they departed along the street a head was thrust out of an\r\nupper window of his lodging and quickly withdrawn. Sue still seemed\r\nsorry for her rashness, and to wish she had not rebelled; telling him\r\nat parting that she would let him know as soon as she got re-admitted\r\nto the training-school. They stood rather miserably together on the\r\nplatform; and it was apparent that he wanted to say more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to tell you something\xe2\x80\x94two things,\xe2\x80\x9d he said hurriedly as the\r\ntrain came up. \xe2\x80\x9cOne is a warm one, the other a cold one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI know one of them. And you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t love me. You are to like me\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s face became so full of complicated glooms that hers was agitated\r\nin sympathy as she bade him adieu through the carriage window. And then\r\nthe train moved on, and waving her pretty hand to him she vanished\r\naway.\r\n\r\nMelchester was a dismal place enough for Jude that Sunday of her\r\ndeparture, and the Close so hateful that he did not go once to the\r\ncathedral services. The next morning there came a letter from her,\r\nwhich, with her usual promptitude, she had written directly she had\r\nreached her friend\xe2\x80\x99s house. She told him of her safe arrival and\r\ncomfortable quarters, and then added:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nWhat I really write about, dear Jude, is something I said to you at\r\nparting. You had been so very good and kind to me that when you were\r\nout of sight I felt what a cruel and ungrateful woman I was to say it,\r\nand it has reproached me ever since. _If you want to love me, Jude, you\r\nmay_: I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind at all; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll never say again that you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t!\r\n    Now I won\xe2\x80\x99t write any more about that. You do forgive your\r\n    thoughtless friend for her cruelty? and won\xe2\x80\x99t make her miserable by\r\n    saying you don\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x94Ever,\r\n\r\n\r\nSUE.\r\n\r\nIt would be superfluous to say what his answer was; and how he thought\r\nwhat he would have done had he been free, which should have rendered a\r\nlong residence with a female friend quite unnecessary for Sue. He felt\r\nhe might have been pretty sure of his own victory if it had come to a\r\nconflict between Phillotson and himself for the possession of her.\r\n\r\nYet Jude was in danger of attaching more meaning to Sue\xe2\x80\x99s impulsive\r\nnote than it really was intended to bear.\r\n\r\nAfter the lapse of a few days he found himself hoping that she would\r\nwrite again. But he received no further communication; and in the\r\nintensity of his solicitude he sent another note, suggesting that he\r\nshould pay her a visit some Sunday, the distance being under eighteen\r\nmiles.\r\n\r\nHe expected a reply on the second morning after despatching his\r\nmissive; but none came. The third morning arrived; the postman did not\r\nstop. This was Saturday, and in a feverish state of anxiety about her\r\nhe sent off three brief lines stating that he was coming the following\r\nday, for he felt sure something had happened.\r\n\r\nHis first and natural thought had been that she was ill from her\r\nimmersion; but it soon occurred to him that somebody would have written\r\nfor her in such a case. Conjectures were put an end to by his arrival\r\nat the village school-house near Shaston on the bright morning of\r\nSunday, between eleven and twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when the parish was as\r\nvacant as a desert, most of the inhabitants having gathered inside the\r\nchurch, whence their voices could occasionally be heard in unison.\r\n\r\nA little girl opened the door. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Bridehead is up-stairs,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you please walk up to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she ill?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude hastily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a little\xe2\x80\x94not very.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude entered and ascended. On reaching the landing a voice told him\r\nwhich way to turn\xe2\x80\x94the voice of Sue calling his name. He passed the\r\ndoorway, and found her lying in a little bed in a room a dozen feet\r\nsquare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, sitting down beside her and taking her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nis this! You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t write?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t that!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI did catch a bad cold\xe2\x80\x94but I could\r\nhave written. Only I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x94frightening me like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that was what I was afraid of! But I had decided not to write to\r\nyou any more. They won\xe2\x80\x99t have me back at the school\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t write. Not the fact, but the reason!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey not only won\xe2\x80\x99t have me, but they gave me a parting piece of\r\nadvice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not answer directly. \xe2\x80\x9cI vowed I never would tell you, Jude\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nis so vulgar and distressing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it about us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94somebody has sent them baseless reports about us, and they say\r\nyou and I ought to marry as soon as possible, for the sake of my\r\nreputation! \xe2\x80\xa6 There\xe2\x80\x94now I have told you, and I wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, poor Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think of you like that means! It did just _occur_ to me to\r\nregard you in the way they think I do, but I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t begun to. I _have_\r\nrecognized that the cousinship was merely nominal, since we met as\r\ntotal strangers. But my marrying you, dear Jude\xe2\x80\x94why, of course, if I\r\nhad reckoned upon marrying you I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have come to you so often!\r\nAnd I never supposed you thought of such a thing as marrying me till\r\nthe other evening; when I began to fancy you did love me a little.\r\nPerhaps I ought not to have been so intimate with you. It is all my\r\nfault. Everything is my fault always!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe speech seemed a little forced and unreal, and they regarded each\r\nother with a mutual distress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was so blind at first!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t see what you felt at\r\nall. Oh, you have been unkind to me\xe2\x80\x94you have\xe2\x80\x94to look upon me as a\r\nsweetheart without saying a word, and leaving me to discover it myself!\r\nYour attitude to me has become known; and naturally they think we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen doing wrong! I\xe2\x80\x99ll never trust you again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said simply; \xe2\x80\x9cI am to blame\xe2\x80\x94more than you think. I was\r\nquite aware that you did not suspect till within the last meeting or\r\ntwo what I was feeling about you. I admit that our meeting as strangers\r\nprevented a sense of relationship, and that it was a sort of subterfuge\r\nto avail myself of it. But don\xe2\x80\x99t you think I deserve a little\r\nconsideration for concealing my wrong, very wrong, sentiments, since I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help having them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned her eyes doubtfully towards him, and then looked away as if\r\nafraid she might forgive him.\r\n\r\nBy every law of nature and sex a kiss was the only rejoinder that\r\nfitted the mood and the moment, under the suasion of which Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nundemonstrative regard of him might not inconceivably have changed its\r\ntemperature. Some men would have cast scruples to the winds, and\r\nventured it, oblivious both of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s declaration of her neutral\r\nfeelings, and of the pair of autographs in the vestry chest of\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s parish church. Jude did not. He had, in fact, come in part\r\nto tell his own fatal story. It was upon his lips; yet at the hour of\r\nthis distress he could not disclose it. He preferred to dwell upon the\r\nrecognized barriers between them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course\xe2\x80\x94I know you don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94care about me in any particular way,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsorrowed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ought not, and you are right. You belong to\xe2\x80\x94Mr.\r\nPhillotson. I suppose he has been to see you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said shortly, her face changing a little. \xe2\x80\x9cThough I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nask him to come. You are glad, of course, that he has been! But I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t come any more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very perplexing to her lover that she should be piqued at his\r\nhonest acquiescence in his rival, if Jude\xe2\x80\x99s feelings of love were\r\ndeprecated by her. He went on to something else.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis will blow over, dear Sue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe training-school\r\nauthorities are not all the world. You can get to be a student in some\r\nother, no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll ask Mr. Phillotson,\xe2\x80\x9d she said decisively.\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s kind hostess now returned from church, and there was no more\r\nintimate conversation. Jude left in the afternoon, hopelessly unhappy.\r\nBut he had seen her, and sat with her. Such intercourse as that would\r\nhave to content him for the remainder of his life. The lesson of\r\nrenunciation it was necessary and proper that he, as a parish priest,\r\nshould learn.\r\n\r\nBut the next morning when he awoke he felt rather vexed with her, and\r\ndecided that she was rather unreasonable, not to say capricious. Then,\r\nin illustration of what he had begun to discern as one of her redeeming\r\ncharacteristics there came promptly a note, which she must have written\r\nalmost immediately he had gone from her:\r\n\r\nForgive me for my petulance yesterday! I was horrid to you; I know it,\r\nand I feel perfectly miserable at my horridness. It was so dear of you\r\nnot to be angry! Jude, please still keep me as your friend and\r\nassociate, with all my faults. I\xe2\x80\x99ll try not to be like it again.\r\n    I am coming to Melchester on Saturday, to get my things away from\r\n    the T. S., &c. I could walk with you for half an hour, if you would\r\n    like?\xe2\x80\x94Your repentant\r\n\r\n\r\nSUE.\r\n\r\nJude forgave her straightway, and asked her to call for him at the\r\ncathedral works when she came.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nMeanwhile a middle-aged man was dreaming a dream of great beauty\r\nconcerning the writer of the above letter. He was Richard Phillotson,\r\nwho had recently removed from the mixed village school at Lumsdon near\r\nChristminster, to undertake a large boys\xe2\x80\x99 school in his native town of\r\nShaston, which stood on a hill sixty miles to the south-west as the\r\ncrow flies.\r\n\r\nA glance at the place and its accessories was almost enough to reveal\r\nthat the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s plans and dreams so long indulged in had been\r\nabandoned for some new dream with which neither the Church nor\r\nliterature had much in common. Essentially an unpractical man, he was\r\nnow bent on making and saving money for a practical purpose\xe2\x80\x94that of\r\nkeeping a wife, who, if she chose, might conduct one of the girls\xe2\x80\x99\r\nschools adjoining his own; for which purpose he had advised her to go\r\ninto training, since she would not marry him offhand.\r\n\r\nAbout the time that Jude was removing from Marygreen to Melchester, and\r\nentering on adventures at the latter place with Sue, the schoolmaster\r\nwas settling down in the new school-house at Shaston. All the furniture\r\nbeing fixed, the books shelved, and the nails driven, he had begun to\r\nsit in his parlour during the dark winter nights and re-attempt some of\r\nhis old studies\xe2\x80\x94one branch of which had included Roman-Britannic\r\nantiquities\xe2\x80\x94an unremunerative labour for a national school-master but a\r\nsubject, that, after his abandonment of the university scheme, had\r\ninterested him as being a comparatively unworked mine; practicable to\r\nthose who, like himself, had lived in lonely spots where these remains\r\nwere abundant, and were seen to compel inferences in startling contrast\r\nto accepted views on the civilization of that time.\r\n\r\nA resumption of this investigation was the outward and apparent hobby\r\nof Phillotson at present\xe2\x80\x94his ostensible reason for going alone into\r\nfields where causeways, dykes, and tumuli abounded, or shutting himself\r\nup in his house with a few urns, tiles, and mosaics he had collected,\r\ninstead of calling round upon his new neighbours, who for their part\r\nhad showed themselves willing enough to be friendly with him. But it\r\nwas not the real, or the whole, reason, after all. Thus on a particular\r\nevening in the month, when it had grown quite late\xe2\x80\x94to near midnight,\r\nindeed\xe2\x80\x94and the light of his lamp, shining from his window at a salient\r\nangle of the hill-top town over infinite miles of valley westward,\r\nannounced as by words a place and person given over to study, he was\r\nnot exactly studying.\r\n\r\nThe interior of the room\xe2\x80\x94the books, the furniture, the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nloose coat, his attitude at the table, even the flickering of the fire,\r\nbespoke the same dignified tale of undistracted research\xe2\x80\x94more than\r\ncreditable to a man who had had no advantages beyond those of his own\r\nmaking. And yet the tale, true enough till latterly, was not true now.\r\nWhat he was regarding was not history. They were historic notes,\r\nwritten in a bold womanly hand at his dictation some months before, and\r\nit was the clerical rendering of word after word that absorbed him.\r\n\r\nHe presently took from a drawer a carefully tied bundle of letters,\r\nfew, very few, as correspondence counts nowadays. Each was in its\r\nenvelope just as it had arrived, and the handwriting was of the same\r\nwomanly character as the historic notes. He unfolded them one by one\r\nand read them musingly. At first sight there seemed in these small\r\ndocuments to be absolutely nothing to muse over. They were\r\nstraightforward, frank letters, signed \xe2\x80\x9cSue B\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d; just such ones as\r\nwould be written during short absences, with no other thought than\r\ntheir speedy destruction, and chiefly concerning books in reading and\r\nother experiences of a training school, forgotten doubtless by the\r\nwriter with the passing of the day of their inditing. In one of\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94quite a recent note\xe2\x80\x94the young woman said that she had received his\r\nconsiderate letter, and that it was honourable and generous of him to\r\nsay he would not come to see her oftener than she desired (the school\r\nbeing such an awkward place for callers, and because of her strong wish\r\nthat her engagement to him should not be known, which it would\r\ninfallibly be if he visited her often). Over these phrases the\r\nschool-master pored. What precise shade of satisfaction was to be\r\ngathered from a woman\xe2\x80\x99s gratitude that the man who loved her had not\r\nbeen often to see her? The problem occupied him, distracted him.\r\n\r\nHe opened another drawer, and found therein an envelope, from which he\r\ndrew a photograph of Sue as a child, long before he had known her,\r\nstanding under trellis-work with a little basket in her hand. There was\r\nanother of her as a young woman, her dark eyes and hair making a very\r\ndistinct and attractive picture of her, which just disclosed, too, the\r\nthoughtfulness that lay behind her lighter moods. It was a duplicate of\r\nthe one she had given Jude, and would have given to any man. Phillotson\r\nbrought it half-way to his lips, but withdrew it in doubt at her\r\nperplexing phrases: ultimately kissing the dead pasteboard with all the\r\npassionateness, and more than all the devotion, of a young man of\r\neighteen.\r\n\r\nThe schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s was an unhealthy-looking, old-fashioned face,\r\nrendered more old-fashioned by his style of shaving. A certain\r\ngentlemanliness had been imparted to it by nature, suggesting an\r\ninherent wish to do rightly by all. His speech was a little slow, but\r\nhis tones were sincere enough to make his hesitation no defect. His\r\ngreying hair was curly, and radiated from a point in the middle of his\r\ncrown. There were four lines across his forehead, and he only wore\r\nspectacles when reading at night. It was almost certainly a\r\nrenunciation forced upon him by his academic purpose, rather than a\r\ndistaste for women, which had hitherto kept him from closing with one\r\nof the sex in matrimony.\r\n\r\nSuch silent proceedings as those of this evening were repeated many and\r\noft times when he was not under the eye of the boys, whose quick and\r\npenetrating regard would frequently become almost intolerable to the\r\nself-conscious master in his present anxious care for Sue, making him,\r\nin the grey hours of morning, dread to meet anew the gimlet glances,\r\nlest they should read what the dream within him was.\r\n\r\nHe had honourably acquiesced in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s announced wish that he was not\r\noften to visit her at the training school; but at length, his patience\r\nbeing sorely tried, he set out one Saturday afternoon to pay her an\r\nunexpected call. There the news of her departure\xe2\x80\x94expulsion as it might\r\nalmost have been considered\xe2\x80\x94was flashed upon him without warning or\r\nmitigation as he stood at the door expecting in a few minutes to behold\r\nher face; and when he turned away he could hardly see the road before\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nSue had, in fact, never written a line to her suitor on the subject,\r\nalthough it was fourteen days old. A short reflection told him that\r\nthis proved nothing, a natural delicacy being as ample a reason for\r\nsilence as any degree of blameworthiness.\r\n\r\nThey had informed him at the school where she was living, and having no\r\nimmediate anxiety about her comfort, his thoughts took the direction of\r\na burning indignation against the training school committee. In his\r\nbewilderment Phillotson entered the adjacent cathedral, just now in a\r\ndirely dismantled state by reason of the repairs. He sat down on a\r\nblock of freestone, regardless of the dusty imprint it made on his\r\nbreeches; and his listless eyes following the movements of the workmen\r\nhe presently became aware that the reputed culprit, Sue\xe2\x80\x99s lover Jude,\r\nwas one amongst them.\r\n\r\nJude had never spoken to his former hero since the meeting by the model\r\nof Jerusalem. Having inadvertently witnessed Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s tentative\r\ncourtship of Sue in the lane there had grown up in the younger man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind a curious dislike to think of the elder, to meet him, to\r\ncommunicate in any way with him; and since Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s success in\r\nobtaining at least her promise had become known to Jude, he had frankly\r\nrecognized that he did not wish to see or hear of his senior any more,\r\nlearn anything of his pursuits, or even imagine again what excellencies\r\nmight appertain to his character. On this very day of the\r\nschoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s visit Jude was expecting Sue, as she had promised; and\r\nwhen therefore he saw the schoolmaster in the nave of the building,\r\nsaw, moreover, that he was coming to speak to him, he felt no little\r\nembarrassment; which Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s own embarrassment prevented his\r\nobserving.\r\n\r\nJude joined him, and they both withdrew from the other workmen to the\r\nspot where Phillotson had been sitting. Jude offered him a piece of\r\nsackcloth for a cushion, and told him it was dangerous to sit on the\r\nbare block.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson abstractedly, as he reseated himself, his\r\neyes resting on the ground as if he were trying to remember where he\r\nwas. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t keep you long. It was merely that I have heard that you\r\nhave seen my little friend Sue recently. It occurred to me to speak to\r\nyou on that account. I merely want to ask\xe2\x80\x94about her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I know what!\xe2\x80\x9d Jude hurriedly said. \xe2\x80\x9cAbout her escaping from\r\nthe training school, and her coming to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Jude for a moment felt an unprincipled and fiendish wish to\r\nannihilate his rival at all cost. By the exercise of that treachery\r\nwhich love for the same woman renders possible to men the most\r\nhonourable in every other relation of life, he could send off\r\nPhillotson in agony and defeat by saying that the scandal was true, and\r\nthat Sue had irretrievably committed herself with him. But his action\r\ndid not respond for a moment to his animal instinct; and what he said\r\nwas, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad of your kindness in coming to talk plainly to me about\r\nit. You know what they say?\xe2\x80\x94that I ought to marry her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I wish with all my soul I could!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson trembled, and his naturally pale face acquired a corpselike\r\nsharpness in its lines. \xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea that it was of this nature! God\r\nforbid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude aghast. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you understood? I mean that were\r\nI in a position to marry her, or someone, and settle down, instead of\r\nliving in lodgings here and there, I should be glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat he had really meant was simply that he loved her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94since this painful matter has been opened up\xe2\x80\x94what really\r\nhappened?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Phillotson, with the firmness of a man who felt that a\r\nsharp smart now was better than a long agony of suspense hereafter.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCases arise, and this is one, when even ungenerous questions must be\r\nput to make false assumptions impossible, and to kill scandal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude explained readily; giving the whole series of adventures,\r\nincluding the night at the shepherd\xe2\x80\x99s, her wet arrival at his lodging,\r\nher indisposition from her immersion, their vigil of discussion, and\r\nhis seeing her off next morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson at the conclusion, \xe2\x80\x9cI take it as your final\r\nword, and I know I can believe you, that the suspicion which led to her\r\nrustication is an absolutely baseless one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude solemnly. \xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely. So help me God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe schoolmaster rose. Each of the twain felt that the interview could\r\nnot comfortably merge in a friendly discussion of their recent\r\nexperiences, after the manner of friends; and when Jude had taken him\r\nround, and shown him some features of the renovation which the old\r\ncathedral was undergoing, Phillotson bade the young man good-day and\r\nwent away.\r\n\r\nThis visit took place about eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning; but no Sue\r\nappeared. When Jude went to his dinner at one he saw his beloved ahead\r\nof him in the street leading up from the North Gate, walking as if no\r\nway looking for him. Speedily overtaking her he remarked that he had\r\nasked her to come to him at the cathedral, and she had promised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been to get my things from the college,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nobservation which he was expected to take as an answer, though it was\r\nnot one. Finding her to be in this evasive mood he felt inclined to\r\ngive her the information so long withheld.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not seen Mr. Phillotson to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d he ventured to inquire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not. But I am not going to be cross-examined about him; and if\r\nyou ask anything more I won\xe2\x80\x99t answer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very odd that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped, regarding her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you are often not so nice in your real presence as you are in\r\nyour letters!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it really seem so to you?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, smiling with quick\r\ncuriosity. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s strange; but I feel just the same about you,\r\nJude. When you are gone away I seem such a coldhearted\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she knew his sentiment towards her Jude saw that they were getting\r\nupon dangerous ground. It was now, he thought, that he must speak as an\r\nhonest man.\r\n\r\nBut he did not speak, and she continued: \xe2\x80\x9cIt was that which made me\r\nwrite and say\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind your loving me\xe2\x80\x94if you wanted to, much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe exultation he might have felt at what that implied, or seemed to\r\nimply, was nullified by his intention, and he rested rigid till he\r\nbegan: \xe2\x80\x9cI have never told you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes you have,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, I have never told you my history\xe2\x80\x94all of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I guess it. I know nearly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude looked up. Could she possibly know of that morning performance of\r\nhis with Arabella; which in a few months had ceased to be a marriage\r\nmore completely than by death? He saw that she did not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t quite tell you here in the street,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on with a gloomy\r\ntongue. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you had better not come to my lodgings. Let us go in\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe building by which they stood was the market-house; it was the only\r\nplace available; and they entered, the market being over, and the\r\nstalls and areas empty. He would have preferred a more congenial spot,\r\nbut, as usually happens, in place of a romantic field or solemn aisle\r\nfor his tale, it was told while they walked up and down over a floor\r\nlittered with rotten cabbage-leaves, and amid all the usual squalors of\r\ndecayed vegetable matter and unsaleable refuse. He began and finished\r\nhis brief narrative, which merely led up to the information that he had\r\nmarried a wife some years earlier, and that his wife was living still.\r\nAlmost before her countenance had time to change she hurried out the\r\nwords,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. It seemed so cruel to tell it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo yourself, Jude. So it was better to be cruel to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear darling!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude passionately. He tried to take her hand,\r\nbut she withdrew it. Their old relations of confidence seemed suddenly\r\nto have ended, and the antagonisms of sex to sex were left without any\r\ncounter-poising predilections. She was his comrade, friend, unconscious\r\nsweetheart no longer; and her eyes regarded him in estranged silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was ashamed of the episode in my life which brought about the\r\nmarriage,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t explain it precisely now. I could\r\nhave done it if you had taken it differently!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how can I?\xe2\x80\x9d she burst out. \xe2\x80\x9cHere I have been saying, or writing,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94that you might love me, or something of the sort!\xe2\x80\x94just out of\r\ncharity\xe2\x80\x94and all the time\xe2\x80\x94oh, it is perfectly damnable how things are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, stamping her foot in a nervous quiver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou take me wrong, Sue! I never thought you cared for me at all, till\r\nquite lately; so I felt it did not matter! Do you care for me, Sue?\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nknow how I mean?\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t like \xe2\x80\x98out of charity\xe2\x80\x99 at all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a question which in the circumstances Sue did not choose to\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose she\xe2\x80\x94your wife\xe2\x80\x94is\xe2\x80\x94a very pretty woman, even if she\xe2\x80\x99s wicked?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s pretty enough, as far as that goes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrettier than I am, no doubt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not the least alike. And I have never seen her for years\xe2\x80\xa6 But\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s sure to come back\xe2\x80\x94they always do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow strange of you to stay apart from her like this!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, her\r\ntrembling lip and lumpy throat belying her irony. \xe2\x80\x9cYou, such a\r\nreligious man. How will the demi-gods in your Pantheon\xe2\x80\x94I mean those\r\nlegendary persons you call saints\xe2\x80\x94intercede for you after this? Now if\r\nI had done such a thing it would have been different, and not\r\nremarkable, for I at least don\xe2\x80\x99t regard marriage as a sacrament. Your\r\ntheories are not so advanced as your practice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue, you are terribly cutting when you like to be\xe2\x80\x94a perfect Voltaire!\r\nBut you must treat me as you will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she saw how wretched he was she softened, and trying to blink away\r\nher sympathetic tears said with all the winning reproachfulness of a\r\nheart-hurt woman: \xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94you should have told me before you gave me that\r\nidea that you wanted to be allowed to love me! I had no feeling before\r\nthat moment at the railway-station, except\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d For once Sue was as\r\nmiserable as he, in her attempts to keep herself free from emotion, and\r\nher less than half-success.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry, dear!\xe2\x80\x9d he implored.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am\xe2\x80\x94not crying\xe2\x80\x94because I meant to\xe2\x80\x94love you; but because of your want\r\nof\xe2\x80\x94confidence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were quite screened from the market-square without, and he could\r\nnot help putting out his arm towards her waist. His momentary desire\r\nwas the means of her rallying. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, drawing back\r\nstringently, and wiping her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course not! It would be hypocrisy\r\nto pretend that it would be meant as from my cousin; and it can\xe2\x80\x99t be in\r\nany other way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey moved on a dozen paces, and she showed herself recovered. It was\r\ndistracting to Jude, and his heart would have ached less had she\r\nappeared anyhow but as she did appear; essentially large-minded and\r\ngenerous on reflection, despite a previous exercise of those narrow\r\nwomanly humours on impulse that were necessary to give her sex.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you for what you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nshould I be so foolish? I do blame you a little bit for not telling me\r\nbefore. But, after all, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. We should have had to keep\r\napart, you see, even if this had not been in your life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, Sue! This is the only obstacle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forget that I must have loved you, and wanted to be your wife,\r\neven if there had been no obstacle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, with a gentle\r\nseriousness which did not reveal her mind. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then we are cousins,\r\nand it is bad for cousins to marry. And\xe2\x80\x94I am engaged to somebody else.\r\nAs to our going on together as we were going, in a sort of friendly\r\nway, the people round us would have made it unable to continue. Their\r\nviews of the relations of man and woman are limited, as is proved by\r\ntheir expelling me from the school. Their philosophy only recognizes\r\nrelations based on animal desire. The wide field of strong attachment\r\nwhere desire plays, at least, only a secondary part, is ignored by\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94the part of\xe2\x80\x94who is it?\xe2\x80\x94Venus Urania.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer being able to talk learnedly showed that she was mistress of\r\nherself again; and before they parted she had almost regained her\r\nvivacious glance, her reciprocity of tone, her gay manner, and her\r\nsecond-thought attitude of critical largeness towards others of her age\r\nand sex.\r\n\r\nHe could speak more freely now. \xe2\x80\x9cThere were several reasons against my\r\ntelling you rashly. One was what I have said; another, that it was\r\nalways impressed upon me that I ought not to marry\xe2\x80\x94that I belonged to\r\nan odd and peculiar family\xe2\x80\x94the wrong breed for marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94who used to say that to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy great-aunt. She said it always ended badly with us Fawleys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s strange. My father used to say the same to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stood possessed by the same thought, ugly enough, even as an\r\nassumption: that a union between them, had such been possible, would\r\nhave meant a terrible intensification of unfitness\xe2\x80\x94two bitters in one\r\ndish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but there can\xe2\x80\x99t be anything in it!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with nervous\r\nlightness. \xe2\x80\x9cOur family have been unlucky of late years in choosing\r\nmates\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then they pretended to persuade themselves that all that had\r\nhappened was of no consequence, and that they could still be cousins\r\nand friends and warm correspondents, and have happy genial times when\r\nthey met, even if they met less frequently than before. Their parting\r\nwas in good friendship, and yet Jude\xe2\x80\x99s last look into her eyes was\r\ntinged with inquiry, for he felt that he did not even now quite know\r\nher mind.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nTidings from Sue a day or two after passed across Jude like a withering\r\nblast.\r\n\r\nBefore reading the letter he was led to suspect that its contents were\r\nof a somewhat serious kind by catching sight of the signature\xe2\x80\x94which was\r\nin her full name, never used in her correspondence with him since her\r\nfirst note:\r\n\r\nMY DEAR JUDE,\xe2\x80\x94I have something to tell you which perhaps you will not\r\nbe surprised to hear, though certainly it may strike you as being\r\naccelerated (as the railway companies say of their trains). Mr.\r\nPhillotson and I are to be married quite soon\xe2\x80\x94in three or four weeks.\r\nWe had intended, as you know, to wait till I had gone through my course\r\nof training and obtained my certificate, so as to assist him, if\r\nnecessary, in the teaching. But he generously says he does not see any\r\nobject in waiting, now I am not at the training school. It is so good\r\nof him, because the awkwardness of my situation has really come about\r\nby my fault in getting expelled.\r\n    Wish me joy. Remember I say you are to, and you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n    refuse!\xe2\x80\x94Your affectionate cousin,\r\n\r\n\r\nSUSANNA FLORENCE MARY BRIDEHEAD.\r\n\r\nJude staggered under the news; could eat no breakfast; and kept on\r\ndrinking tea because his mouth was so dry. Then presently he went back\r\nto his work and laughed the usual bitter laugh of a man so confronted.\r\nEverything seemed turning to satire. And yet, what could the poor girl\r\ndo? he asked himself, and felt worse than shedding tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Susanna Florence Mary!\xe2\x80\x9d he said as he worked. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nmarriage means!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCould it be possible that his announcement of his own marriage had\r\npricked her on to this, just as his visit to her when in liquor may\r\nhave pricked her on to her engagement? To be sure, there seemed to\r\nexist these other and sufficient reasons, practical and social, for her\r\ndecision; but Sue was not a very practical or calculating person; and\r\nhe was compelled to think that a pique at having his secret sprung upon\r\nher had moved her to give way to Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s probable representations,\r\nthat the best course to prove how unfounded were the suspicions of the\r\nschool authorities would be to marry him off-hand, as in fulfilment of\r\nan ordinary engagement. Sue had, in fact, been placed in an awkward\r\ncorner. Poor Sue!\r\n\r\nHe determined to play the Spartan; to make the best of it, and support\r\nher; but he could not write the requested good wishes for a day or two.\r\nMeanwhile there came another note from his impatient little dear:\r\n\r\nJude, will you give me away? I have nobody else who could do it so\r\nconveniently as you, being the only married relation I have here on the\r\nspot, even if my father were friendly enough to be willing, which he\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t. I hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t think it a trouble? I have been looking at the\r\nmarriage service in the prayer-book, and it seems to me very\r\nhumiliating that a giver-away should be required at all. According to\r\nthe ceremony as there printed, my bridegroom chooses me of his own will\r\nand pleasure; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t choose him. Somebody _gives_ me to him, like\r\na she-ass or she-goat, or any other domestic animal. Bless your exalted\r\nviews of woman, O churchman! But I forget: I am no longer privileged to\r\ntease you.\xe2\x80\x94Ever,\r\n\r\n\r\nSUSANNA FLORENCE MARY BRIDEHEAD.\r\n\r\nJude screwed himself up to heroic key; and replied:\r\n\r\nMY DEAR SUE,\xe2\x80\x94Of course I wish you joy! And also of course I will give\r\nyou away. What I suggest is that, as you have no house of your own, you\r\ndo not marry from your school friend\xe2\x80\x99s, but from mine. It would be more\r\nproper, I think, since I am, as you say, the person nearest related to\r\nyou in this part of the world.\r\n    I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you sign your letter in such a new and terribly\r\n    formal way? Surely you care a bit about me still!\xe2\x80\x94Ever your\r\n    affectionate,\r\n\r\n\r\nJUDE.\r\n\r\nWhat had jarred on him even more than the signature was a little sting\r\nhe had been silent on\xe2\x80\x94the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cmarried relation\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94What an idiot it\r\nmade him seem as her lover! If Sue had written that in satire, he could\r\nhardly forgive her; if in suffering\xe2\x80\x94ah, that was another thing!\r\n\r\nHis offer of his lodging must have commended itself to Phillotson at\r\nany rate, for the schoolmaster sent him a line of warm thanks,\r\naccepting the convenience. Sue also thanked him. Jude immediately moved\r\ninto more commodious quarters, as much to escape the espionage of the\r\nsuspicious landlady who had been one cause of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s unpleasant\r\nexperience as for the sake of room.\r\n\r\nThen Sue wrote to tell him the day fixed for the wedding; and Jude\r\ndecided, after inquiry, that she should come into residence on the\r\nfollowing Saturday, which would allow of a ten days\xe2\x80\x99 stay in the city\r\nprior to the ceremony, sufficiently representing a nominal residence of\r\nfifteen.\r\n\r\nShe arrived by the ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock train on the day aforesaid, Jude not\r\ngoing to meet her at the station, by her special request, that he\r\nshould not lose a morning\xe2\x80\x99s work and pay, she said (if this were her\r\ntrue reason). But so well by this time did he know Sue that the\r\nremembrance of their mutual sensitiveness at emotional crises might, he\r\nthought, have weighed with her in this. When he came home to dinner she\r\nhad taken possession of her apartment.\r\n\r\nShe lived in the same house with him, but on a different floor, and\r\nthey saw each other little, an occasional supper being the only meal\r\nthey took together, when Sue\xe2\x80\x99s manner was something like that of a\r\nscared child. What she felt he did not know; their conversation was\r\nmechanical, though she did not look pale or ill. Phillotson came\r\nfrequently, but mostly when Jude was absent. On the morning of the\r\nwedding, when Jude had given himself a holiday, Sue and her cousin had\r\nbreakfast together for the first and last time during this curious\r\ninterval; in his room\xe2\x80\x94the parlour\xe2\x80\x94which he had hired for the period of\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s residence. Seeing, as women do, how helpless he was in making the\r\nplace comfortable, she bustled about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d she said suddenly.\r\n\r\nHe was leaning with his elbows on the table and his chin on his hands,\r\nlooking into a futurity which seemed to be sketched out on the\r\ntablecloth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are \xe2\x80\x98father\xe2\x80\x99, you know. That\xe2\x80\x99s what they call the man who gives\r\nyou away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude could have said \xe2\x80\x9cPhillotson\xe2\x80\x99s age entitles him to be called that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBut he would not annoy her by such a cheap retort.\r\n\r\nShe talked incessantly, as if she dreaded his indulgence in reflection,\r\nand before the meal was over both he and she wished they had not put\r\nsuch confidence in their new view of things, and had taken breakfast\r\napart. What oppressed Jude was the thought that, having done a wrong\r\nthing of this sort himself, he was aiding and abetting the woman he\r\nloved in doing a like wrong thing, instead of imploring and warning her\r\nagainst it. It was on his tongue to say, \xe2\x80\x9cYou have quite made up your\r\nmind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast they went out on an errand together moved by a mutual\r\nthought that it was the last opportunity they would have of indulging\r\nin unceremonious companionship. By the irony of fate, and the curious\r\ntrick in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s nature of tempting Providence at critical times, she\r\ntook his arm as they walked through the muddy street\xe2\x80\x94a thing she had\r\nnever done before in her life\xe2\x80\x94and on turning the corner they found\r\nthemselves close to a grey perpendicular church with a low-pitched\r\nroof\xe2\x80\x94the church of St. Thomas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the church,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere I am going to be married?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed with curiosity. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I should like to go in and\r\nsee what the spot is like where I am so soon to kneel and do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cShe does not realize what marriage means!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe passively acquiesced in her wish to go in, and they entered by the\r\nwestern door. The only person inside the gloomy building was a\r\ncharwoman cleaning. Sue still held Jude\xe2\x80\x99s arm, almost as if she loved\r\nhim. Cruelly sweet, indeed, she had been to him that morning; but his\r\nthoughts of a penance in store for her were tempered by an ache:\r\n\r\n                                        \xe2\x80\xa6 I can find no way\r\nHow a blow should fall, such as falls on men,\r\nNor prove too much for your womanhood!\r\n\r\nThey strolled undemonstratively up the nave towards the altar railing,\r\nwhich they stood against in silence, turning then and walking down the\r\nnave again, her hand still on his arm, precisely like a couple just\r\nmarried. The too suggestive incident, entirely of her making, nearly\r\nbroke down Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like to do things like this,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in the delicate voice of an\r\nepicure in emotions, which left no doubt that she spoke the truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you do!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are interesting, because they have probably never been done\r\nbefore. I shall walk down the church like this with my husband in about\r\ntwo hours, shan\xe2\x80\x99t I!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt you will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it like this when you were married?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God, Sue\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t be so awfully merciless! \xe2\x80\xa6 There, dear one, I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94you are vexed!\xe2\x80\x9d she said regretfully, as she blinked away an access\r\nof eye moisture. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I promised never to vex you! \xe2\x80\xa6 I suppose I ought\r\nnot to have asked you to bring me in here. Oh, I oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t! I see it\r\nnow. My curiosity to hunt up a new sensation always leads me into these\r\nscrapes. Forgive me! \xe2\x80\xa6 You will, won\xe2\x80\x99t you, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe appeal was so remorseful that Jude\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were even wetter than\r\nhers as he pressed her hand for Yes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll hurry away, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t do it any more!\xe2\x80\x9d she continued\r\nhumbly; and they came out of the building, Sue intending to go on to\r\nthe station to meet Phillotson. But the first person they encountered\r\non entering the main street was the schoolmaster himself, whose train\r\nhad arrived sooner than Sue expected. There was nothing really to demur\r\nto in her leaning on Jude\xe2\x80\x99s arm; but she withdrew her hand, and Jude\r\nthought that Phillotson had looked surprised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have been doing such a funny thing!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, smiling candidly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve been to the church, rehearsing as it were. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t we, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson curiously.\r\n\r\nJude inwardly deplored what he thought to be unnecessary frankness; but\r\nshe had gone too far not to explain all, which she accordingly did,\r\ntelling him how they had marched up to the altar.\r\n\r\nSeeing how puzzled Phillotson seemed, Jude said as cheerfully as he\r\ncould, \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to buy her another little present. Will you both\r\ncome to the shop with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go on to the house with him\xe2\x80\x9d; and requesting her\r\nlover not to be a long time she departed with the schoolmaster.\r\n\r\nJude soon joined them at his rooms, and shortly after they prepared for\r\nthe ceremony. Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s hair was brushed to a painful extent, and\r\nhis shirt collar appeared stiffer than it had been for the previous\r\ntwenty years. Beyond this he looked dignified and thoughtful, and\r\naltogether a man of whom it was not unsafe to predict that he would\r\nmake a kind and considerate husband. That he adored Sue was obvious;\r\nand she could almost be seen to feel that she was undeserving his\r\nadoration.\r\n\r\nAlthough the distance was so short he had hired a fly from the Red\r\nLion, and six or seven women and children had gathered by the door when\r\nthey came out. The schoolmaster and Sue were unknown, though Jude was\r\ngetting to be recognized as a citizen; and the couple were judged to be\r\nsome relations of his from a distance, nobody supposing Sue to have\r\nbeen a recent pupil at the training school.\r\n\r\nIn the carriage Jude took from his pocket his extra little\r\nwedding-present, which turned out to be two or three yards of white\r\ntulle, which he threw over her bonnet and all, as a veil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks so odd over a bonnet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take the bonnet off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94let it stay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson. And she obeyed.\r\n\r\nWhen they had passed up the church and were standing in their places\r\nJude found that the antecedent visit had certainly taken off the edge\r\nof this performance, but by the time they were half-way on with the\r\nservice he wished from his heart that he had not undertaken the\r\nbusiness of giving her away. How could Sue have had the temerity to ask\r\nhim to do it\xe2\x80\x94a cruelty possibly to herself as well as to him? Women\r\nwere different from men in such matters. Was it that they were, instead\r\nof more sensitive, as reputed, more callous, and less romantic; or were\r\nthey more heroic? Or was Sue simply so perverse that she wilfully gave\r\nherself and him pain for the odd and mournful luxury of practising\r\nlong-suffering in her own person, and of being touched with tender pity\r\nfor him at having made him practise it? He could perceive that her face\r\nwas nervously set, and when they reached the trying ordeal of Jude\r\ngiving her to Phillotson she could hardly command herself; rather,\r\nhowever, as it seemed, from her knowledge of what her cousin must feel,\r\nwhom she need not have had there at all, than from self-consideration.\r\nPossibly she would go on inflicting such pains again and again, and\r\ngrieving for the sufferer again and again, in all her colossal\r\ninconsistency.\r\n\r\nPhillotson seemed not to notice, to be surrounded by a mist which\r\nprevented his seeing the emotions of others. As soon as they had signed\r\ntheir names and come away, and the suspense was over, Jude felt\r\nrelieved.\r\n\r\nThe meal at his lodging was a very simple affair, and at two o\xe2\x80\x99clock\r\nthey went off. In crossing the pavement to the fly she looked back; and\r\nthere was a frightened light in her eyes. Could it be that Sue had\r\nacted with such unusual foolishness as to plunge into she knew not what\r\nfor the sake of asserting her independence of him, of retaliating on\r\nhim for his secrecy? Perhaps Sue was thus venturesome with men because\r\nshe was childishly ignorant of that side of their natures which wore\r\nout women\xe2\x80\x99s hearts and lives.\r\n\r\nWhen her foot was on the carriage-step she turned round, saying that\r\nshe had forgotten something. Jude and the landlady offered to get it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, running back. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is my handkerchief. I know where I\r\nleft it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude followed her back. She had found it, and came holding it in her\r\nhand. She looked into his eyes with her own tearful ones, and her lips\r\nsuddenly parted as if she were going to avow something. But she went\r\non; and whatever she had meant to say remained unspoken.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nJude wondered if she had really left her handkerchief behind; or\r\nwhether it were that she had miserably wished to tell him of a love\r\nthat at the last moment she could not bring herself to express.\r\n\r\nHe could not stay in his silent lodging when they were gone, and\r\nfearing that he might be tempted to drown his misery in alcohol he went\r\nupstairs, changed his dark clothes for his white, his thin boots for\r\nhis thick, and proceeded to his customary work for the afternoon.\r\n\r\nBut in the cathedral he seemed to hear a voice behind him, and to be\r\npossessed with an idea that she would come back. She could not possibly\r\ngo home with Phillotson, he fancied. The feeling grew and stirred. The\r\nmoment that the clock struck the last of his working hours he threw\r\ndown his tools and rushed homeward. \xe2\x80\x9cHas anybody been for me?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked.\r\n\r\nNobody had been there.\r\n\r\nAs he could claim the downstairs sitting-room till twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock that\r\nnight he sat in it all the evening; and even when the clock had struck\r\neleven, and the family had retired, he could not shake off the feeling\r\nthat she would come back and sleep in the little room adjoining his own\r\nin which she had slept so many previous days. Her actions were always\r\nunpredictable: why should she not come? Gladly would he have compounded\r\nfor the denial of her as a sweetheart and wife by having her live thus\r\nas a fellow-lodger and friend, even on the most distant terms. His\r\nsupper still remained spread, and going to the front door, and softly\r\nsetting it open, he returned to the room and sat as watchers sit on\r\nOld-Midsummer eves, expecting the phantom of the Beloved. But she did\r\nnot come.\r\n\r\nHaving indulged in this wild hope he went upstairs, and looked out of\r\nthe window, and pictured her through the evening journey to London,\r\nwhither she and Phillotson had gone for their holiday; their rattling\r\nalong through the damp night to their hotel, under the same sky of\r\nribbed cloud as that he beheld, through which the moon showed its\r\nposition rather than its shape, and one or two of the larger stars made\r\nthemselves visible as faint nebul\xc3\xa6 only. It was a new beginning of\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s history. He projected his mind into the future, and saw her with\r\nchildren more or less in her own likeness around her. But the\r\nconsolation of regarding them as a continuation of her identity was\r\ndenied to him, as to all such dreamers, by the wilfulness of Nature in\r\nnot allowing issue from one parent alone. Every desired renewal of an\r\nexistence is debased by being half alloy. \xe2\x80\x9cIf at the estrangement or\r\ndeath of my lost love, I could go and see her child\xe2\x80\x94hers solely\xe2\x80\x94there\r\nwould be comfort in it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. And then he again uneasily saw, as\r\nhe had latterly seen with more and more frequency, the scorn of Nature\r\nfor man\xe2\x80\x99s finer emotions, and her lack of interest in his aspirations.\r\n\r\nThe oppressive strength of his affection for Sue showed itself on the\r\nmorrow and following days yet more clearly. He could no longer endure\r\nthe light of the Melchester lamps; the sunshine was as drab paint, and\r\nthe blue sky as zinc. Then he received news that his old aunt was\r\ndangerously ill at Marygreen, which intelligence almost coincided with\r\na letter from his former employer at Christminster, who offered him\r\npermanent work of a good class if he would come back. The letters were\r\nalmost a relief to him. He started to visit Aunt Drusilla, and resolved\r\nto go onward to Christminster to see what worth there might be in the\r\nbuilder\xe2\x80\x99s offer.\r\n\r\nJude found his aunt even worse than the communication from the Widow\r\nEdlin had led him to expect. There was every possibility of her\r\nlingering on for weeks or months, though little likelihood. He wrote to\r\nSue informing her of the state of her aunt, and suggesting that she\r\nmight like to see her aged relative alive. He would meet her at\r\nAlfredston Road, the following evening, Monday, on his way back from\r\nChristminster, if she could come by the up-train which crossed his\r\ndown-train at that station. Next morning, according, he went on to\r\nChristminster, intending to return to Alfredston soon enough to keep\r\nthe suggested appointment with Sue.\r\n\r\nThe city of learning wore an estranged look, and he had lost all\r\nfeeling for its associations. Yet as the sun made vivid lights and\r\nshades of the mullioned architecture of the fa\xc3\xa7ades, and drew patterns\r\nof the crinkled battlements on the young turf of the quadrangles, Jude\r\nthought he had never seen the place look more beautiful. He came to the\r\nstreet in which he had first beheld Sue. The chair she had occupied\r\nwhen, leaning over her ecclesiastical scrolls, a hog-hair brush in her\r\nhand, her girlish figure had arrested the gaze of his inquiring eyes,\r\nstood precisely in its former spot, empty. It was as if she were dead,\r\nand nobody had been found capable of succeeding her in that artistic\r\npursuit. Hers was now the city phantom, while those of the intellectual\r\nand devotional worthies who had once moved him to emotion were no\r\nlonger able to assert their presence there.\r\n\r\nHowever, here he was; and in fulfilment of his intention he went on to\r\nhis former lodging in \xe2\x80\x9cBeersheba,\xe2\x80\x9d near the ritualistic church of St.\r\nSilas. The old landlady who opened the door seemed glad to see him\r\nagain, and bringing some lunch informed him that the builder who had\r\nemployed him had called to inquire his address.\r\n\r\nJude went on to the stone-yard where he had worked. But the old sheds\r\nand bankers were distasteful to him; he felt it impossible to engage\r\nhimself to return and stay in this place of vanished dreams. He longed\r\nfor the hour of the homeward train to Alfredston, where he might\r\nprobably meet Sue.\r\n\r\nThen, for one ghastly half-hour of depression caused by these scenes,\r\nthere returned upon him that feeling which had been his undoing more\r\nthan once\xe2\x80\x94that he was not worth the trouble of being taken care of\r\neither by himself or others; and during this half-hour he met Tinker\r\nTaylor, the bankrupt ecclesiastical ironmonger, at Fourways, who\r\nproposed that they should adjourn to a bar and drink together. They\r\nwalked along the street till they stood before one of the great\r\npalpitating centres of Christminster life, the inn wherein he formerly\r\nhad responded to the challenge to rehearse the Creed in Latin\xe2\x80\x94now a\r\npopular tavern with a spacious and inviting entrance, which gave\r\nadmittance to a bar that had been entirely renovated and refitted in\r\nmodern style since Jude\xe2\x80\x99s residence here.\r\n\r\nTinker Taylor drank off his glass and departed, saying it was too\r\nstylish a place now for him to feel at home in unless he was drunker\r\nthan he had money to be just then. Jude was longer finishing his, and\r\nstood abstractedly silent in the, for the minute, almost empty place.\r\nThe bar had been gutted and newly arranged throughout, mahogany\r\nfixtures having taken the place of the old painted ones, while at the\r\nback of the standing-space there were stuffed sofa-benches. The room\r\nwas divided into compartments in the approved manner, between which\r\nwere screens of ground glass in mahogany framing, to prevent topers in\r\none compartment being put to the blush by the recognitions of those in\r\nthe next. On the inside of the counter two barmaids leant over the\r\nwhite-handled beer-engines, and the row of little silvered taps inside,\r\ndripping into a pewter trough.\r\n\r\nFeeling tired, and having nothing more to do till the train left, Jude\r\nsat down on one of the sofas. At the back of the barmaids rose\r\nbevel-edged mirrors, with glass shelves running along their front, on\r\nwhich stood precious liquids that Jude did not know the name of, in\r\nbottles of topaz, sapphire, ruby and amethyst. The moment was enlivened\r\nby the entrance of some customers into the next compartment, and the\r\nstarting of the mechanical tell-tale of monies received, which emitted\r\na ting-ting every time a coin was put in.\r\n\r\nThe barmaid attending to this compartment was invisible to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndirect glance, though a reflection of her back in the glass behind her\r\nwas occasionally caught by his eyes. He had only observed this\r\nlistlessly, when she turned her face for a moment to the glass to set\r\nher hair tidy. Then he was amazed to discover that the face was\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nIf she had come on to his compartment she would have seen him. But she\r\ndid not, this being presided over by the maiden on the other side. Abby\r\nwas in a black gown, with white linen cuffs and a broad white collar,\r\nand her figure, more developed than formerly, was accentuated by a\r\nbunch of daffodils that she wore on her left bosom. In the compartment\r\nshe served stood an electro-plated fountain of water over a\r\nspirit-lamp, whose blue flame sent a steam from the top, all this being\r\nvisible to him only in the mirror behind her; which also reflected the\r\nfaces of the men she was attending to\xe2\x80\x94one of them a handsome,\r\ndissipated young fellow, possibly an undergraduate, who had been\r\nrelating to her an experience of some humorous sort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Cockman, now! How can you tell such a tale to me in my\r\ninnocence!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Cockman, what do you use to make your\r\nmoustache curl so beautiful?\xe2\x80\x9d As the young man was clean shaven, the\r\nretort provoked a laugh at his expense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have a cura\xc3\xa7ao; and a light, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe served the liqueur from one of the lovely bottles and striking a\r\nmatch held it to his cigarette with ministering archness while he\r\nwhiffed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, have you heard from your husband lately, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a sound,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI left him in Australia; and I suppose he\xe2\x80\x99s there still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s eyes grew rounder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you part from him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you ask questions, and you won\xe2\x80\x99t hear lies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome then, give me my change, which you\xe2\x80\x99ve been keeping from me for\r\nthe last quarter of an hour; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll romantically vanish up the street\r\nof this picturesque city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe handed the change over the counter, in taking which he caught her\r\nfingers and held them. There was a slight struggle and titter, and he\r\nbade her good-bye and left.\r\n\r\nJude had looked on with the eye of a dazed philosopher. It was\r\nextraordinary how far removed from his life Arabella now seemed to be.\r\nHe could not realize their nominal closeness. And, this being the case,\r\nin his present frame of mind he was indifferent to the fact that\r\nArabella was his wife indeed.\r\n\r\nThe compartment that she served emptied itself of visitors, and after a\r\nbrief thought he entered it, and went forward to the counter. Arabella\r\ndid not recognize him for a moment. Then their glances met. She\r\nstarted; till a humorous impudence sparkled in her eyes, and she spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m blest! I thought you were underground years ago!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never heard anything of you, or I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I should have come\r\nhere. But never mind! What shall I treat you to this afternoon? A\r\nScotch and soda? Come, anything that the house will afford, for old\r\nacquaintance\xe2\x80\x99 sake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, Arabella,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude without a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nanything more than I\xe2\x80\x99ve had.\xe2\x80\x9d The fact was that her unexpected presence\r\nthere had destroyed at a stroke his momentary taste for strong liquor\r\nas completely as if it had whisked him back to his milk-fed infancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pity, now you could get it for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long have you been here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout six weeks. I returned from Sydney three months ago. I always\r\nliked this business, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder you came to this place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as I say, I thought you were gone to glory, and being in London\r\nI saw the situation in an advertisement. Nobody was likely to know me\r\nhere, even if I had minded, for I was never in Christminster in my\r\ngrowing up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you return from Australia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I had my reasons\xe2\x80\xa6 Then you are not a don yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot even a reverend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor so much as a rather reverend dissenting gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am as I was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue\xe2\x80\x94you look so.\xe2\x80\x9d She idly allowed her fingers to rest on the pull of\r\nthe beer-engine as she inspected him critically. He observed that her\r\nhands were smaller and whiter than when he had lived with her, and that\r\non the hand which pulled the engine she wore an ornamental ring set\r\nwith what seemed to be real sapphires\xe2\x80\x94which they were, indeed, and were\r\nmuch admired as such by the young men who frequented the bar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you pass as having a living husband,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I thought it might be awkward if I called myself a widow, as I\r\nshould have liked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. I am known here a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean on that account\xe2\x80\x94for as I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect you. It\r\nwas for other reasons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to go into them,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied evasively. \xe2\x80\x9cI make a very\r\ngood living, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I want your company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere a chappie with no chin, and a moustache like a lady\xe2\x80\x99s eyebrow,\r\ncame and asked for a curiously compounded drink, and Arabella was\r\nobliged to go and attend to him. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t talk here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nstepping back a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you wait till nine? Say yes, and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe a fool. I can get off duty two hours sooner than usual, if I ask. I\r\nam not living in the house at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe reflected and said gloomily, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll come back. I suppose we\xe2\x80\x99d better\r\narrange something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bother arranging! I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to arrange anything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I must know a thing or two; and, as you say, we can\xe2\x80\x99t talk here.\r\nVery well; I\xe2\x80\x99ll call for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDepositing his unemptied glass he went out and walked up and down the\r\nstreet. Here was a rude flounce into the pellucid sentimentality of his\r\nsad attachment to Sue. Though Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s word was absolutely\r\nuntrustworthy, he thought there might be some truth in her implication\r\nthat she had not wished to disturb him, and had really supposed him\r\ndead. However, there was only one thing now to be done, and that was to\r\nplay a straightforward part, the law being the law, and the woman\r\nbetween whom and himself there was no more unity than between east and\r\nwest, being in the eye of the Church one person with him.\r\n\r\nHaving to meet Arabella here, it was impossible to meet Sue at\r\nAlfredston as he had promised. At every thought of this a pang had gone\r\nthrough him; but the conjuncture could not be helped. Arabella was\r\nperhaps an intended intervention to punish him for his unauthorized\r\nlove. Passing the evening, therefore, in a desultory waiting about the\r\ntown wherein he avoided the precincts of every cloister and hall,\r\nbecause he could not bear to behold them, he repaired to the tavern bar\r\nwhile the hundred and one strokes were resounding from the Great Bell\r\nof Cardinal College, a coincidence which seemed to him gratuitous\r\nirony. The inn was now brilliantly lighted up, and the scene was\r\naltogether more brisk and gay. The faces of the barmaidens had risen in\r\ncolour, each having a pink flush on her cheek; their manners were still\r\nmore vivacious than before\xe2\x80\x94more abandoned, more excited, more sensuous,\r\nand they expressed their sentiments and desires less euphemistically,\r\nlaughing in a lackadaisical tone, without reserve.\r\n\r\nThe bar had been crowded with men of all sorts during the previous\r\nhour, and he had heard from without the hubbub of their voices; but the\r\ncustomers were fewer at last.
