He nodded to Arabella, and told her that\r\nshe would find him outside the door when she came away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must have something with me first,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with great good\r\nhumour. \xe2\x80\x9cJust an early night-cap: I always do. Then you can go out and\r\nwait a minute, as it is best we should not be seen going together.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\ndrew a couple of liqueur glasses of brandy; and though she had\r\nevidently, from her countenance, already taken in enough alcohol either\r\nby drinking or, more probably, from the atmosphere she had breathed for\r\nso many hours, she finished hers quickly. He also drank his, and went\r\noutside the house.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes she came, in a thick jacket and a hat with a black\r\nfeather. \xe2\x80\x9cI live quite near,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, taking his arm, \xe2\x80\x9cand can let\r\nmyself in by a latch-key at any time. What arrangement do you want to\r\ncome to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94none in particular,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, thoroughly sick and tired, his\r\nthoughts again reverting to Alfredston, and the train he did not go by;\r\nthe probable disappointment of Sue that he was not there when she\r\narrived, and the missed pleasure of her company on the long and lonely\r\nclimb by starlight up the hills to Marygreen. \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have gone\r\nback really! My aunt is on her deathbed, I fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go over with you to-morrow morning. I think I could get a day\r\noff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was something particularly uncongenial in the idea of Arabella,\r\nwho had no more sympathy than a tigress with his relations or him,\r\ncoming to the bedside of his dying aunt, and meeting Sue. Yet he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, if you\xe2\x80\x99d like to, you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that we\xe2\x80\x99ll consider\xe2\x80\xa6 Now, until we have come to some agreement\r\nit is awkward our being together here\xe2\x80\x94where you are known, and I am\r\ngetting known, though without any suspicion that I have anything to do\r\nwith you. As we are going towards the station, suppose we take the\r\nnine-forty train to Aldbrickham? We shall be there in little more than\r\nhalf an hour, and nobody will know us for one night, and we shall be\r\nquite free to act as we choose till we have made up our minds whether\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll make anything public or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen wait till I get two or three things. This is my lodging.\r\nSometimes when late I sleep at the hotel where I am engaged, so nobody\r\nwill think anything of my staying out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe speedily returned, and they went on to the railway, and made the\r\nhalf-hour\xe2\x80\x99s journey to Aldbrickham, where they entered a third-rate inn\r\nnear the station in time for a late supper.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the morrow between nine and half-past they were journeying back to\r\nChristminster, the only two occupants of a compartment in a third-class\r\nrailway-carriage. Having, like Jude, made rather a hasty toilet to\r\ncatch the train, Arabella looked a little frowsy, and her face was very\r\nfar from possessing the animation which had characterized it at the bar\r\nthe night before. When they came out of the station she found that she\r\nstill had half an hour to spare before she was due at the bar. They\r\nwalked in silence a little way out of the town in the direction of\r\nAlfredston. Jude looked up the far highway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh \xe2\x80\xa6 poor feeble me!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the very road by which I came into Christminster years ago\r\nfull of plans!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, whatever the road is I think my time is nearly up, as I have to\r\nbe in the bar by eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock. And as I said, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t ask for the\r\nday to go with you to see your aunt. So perhaps we had better part\r\nhere. I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner not walk up Chief Street with you, since we\xe2\x80\x99ve come to\r\nno conclusion at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. But you said when we were getting up this morning that you\r\nhad something you wished to tell me before I left?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I had\xe2\x80\x94two things\xe2\x80\x94one in particular. But you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t promise to\r\nkeep it a secret. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you now if you promise? As an honest woman\r\nI wish you to know it\xe2\x80\xa6 It was what I began telling you in the\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x94about that gentleman who managed the Sydney hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d Arabella\r\nspoke somewhat hurriedly for her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll keep it close?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94I promise!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\nreveal your secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever I met him out for a walk, he used to say that he was much\r\ntaken with my looks, and he kept pressing me to marry him. I never\r\nthought of coming back to England again; and being out there in\r\nAustralia, with no home of my own after leaving my father, I at last\r\nagreed, and did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94marry him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRegularly\xe2\x80\x94legally\xe2\x80\x94in church?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And lived with him till shortly before I left. It was stupid, I\r\nknow; but I did! There, now I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you. Don\xe2\x80\x99t round upon me! He\r\ntalks of coming back to England, poor old chap. But if he does, he\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be likely to find me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude stood pale and fixed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy the devil didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me last, night!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\xa6 Won\xe2\x80\x99t you make it up with me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo in talking of \xe2\x80\x98your husband\xe2\x80\x99 to the bar gentlemen you meant him, of\r\ncourse\xe2\x80\x94not me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course\xe2\x80\xa6 Come, don\xe2\x80\x99t fuss about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing more to say!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing at all to\r\nsay about the\xe2\x80\x94crime\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ve confessed to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrime! Pooh. They don\xe2\x80\x99t think much of such as that over there! Lots of\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em do it\xe2\x80\xa6 Well, if you take it like that I shall go back to him! He\r\nwas very fond of me, and we lived honourable enough, and as respectable\r\nas any married couple in the colony! How did I know where you were?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t go blaming you. I could say a good deal; but perhaps it would\r\nbe misplaced. What do you wish me to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. There was one thing more I wanted to tell you; but I fancy\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ve seen enough of one another for the present! I shall think over\r\nwhat you said about your circumstances, and let you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus they parted. Jude watched her disappear in the direction of the\r\nhotel, and entered the railway station close by. Finding that it wanted\r\nthree-quarters of an hour of the time at which he could get a train\r\nback to Alfredston, he strolled mechanically into the city as far as to\r\nthe Fourways, where he stood as he had so often stood before, and\r\nsurveyed Chief Street stretching ahead, with its college after college,\r\nin picturesqueness unrivalled except by such Continental vistas as the\r\nStreet of Palaces in Genoa; the lines of the buildings being as\r\ndistinct in the morning air as in an architectural drawing. But Jude\r\nwas far from seeing or criticizing these things; they were hidden by an\r\nindescribable consciousness of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s midnight contiguity, a sense\r\nof degradation at his revived experiences with her, of her appearance\r\nas she lay asleep at dawn, which set upon his motionless face a look as\r\nof one accurst. If he could only have felt resentment towards her he\r\nwould have been less unhappy; but he pitied while he contemned her.\r\n\r\nJude turned and retraced his steps. Drawing again towards the station\r\nhe started at hearing his name pronounced\xe2\x80\x94less at the name than at the\r\nvoice. To his great surprise no other than Sue stood like a vision\r\nbefore him\xe2\x80\x94her look bodeful and anxious as in a dream, her little mouth\r\nnervous, and her strained eyes speaking reproachful inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Jude\xe2\x80\x94I am so glad\xe2\x80\x94to meet you like this!\xe2\x80\x9d she said in quick,\r\nuneven accents not far from a sob. Then she flushed as she observed his\r\nthought that they had not met since her marriage.\r\n\r\nThey looked away from each other to hide their emotion, took each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s hand without further speech, and went on together awhile, till\r\nshe glanced at him with furtive solicitude. \xe2\x80\x9cI arrived at Alfredston\r\nstation last night, as you asked me to, and there was nobody to meet\r\nme! But I reached Marygreen alone, and they told me Aunt was a trifle\r\nbetter. I sat up with her, and as you did not come all night I was\r\nfrightened about you\xe2\x80\x94I thought that perhaps, when you found yourself\r\nback in the old city, you were upset at\xe2\x80\x94at thinking I was\xe2\x80\x94married, and\r\nnot there as I used to be; and that you had nobody to speak to; so you\r\nhad tried to drown your gloom\xe2\x80\x94as you did at that former time when you\r\nwere disappointed about entering as a student, and had forgotten your\r\npromise to me that you never would again. And this, I thought, was why\r\nyou hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come to meet me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you came to hunt me up, and deliver me, like a good angel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I would come by the morning train and try to find you\xe2\x80\x94in\r\ncase\xe2\x80\x94in case\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did think of my promise to you, dear, continually! I shall never\r\nbreak out again as I did, I am sure. I may have been doing nothing\r\nbetter, but I was not doing that\xe2\x80\x94I loathe the thought of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad your staying had nothing to do with that. But,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nthe faintest pout entering into her tone, \xe2\x80\x9cyou didn\xe2\x80\x99t come back last\r\nnight and meet me, as you engaged to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I am sorry to say. I had an appointment at nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock\xe2\x80\x94too\r\nlate for me to catch the train that would have met yours, or to get\r\nhome at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLooking at his loved one as she appeared to him now, in his tender\r\nthought the sweetest and most disinterested comrade that he had ever\r\nhad, living largely in vivid imaginings, so ethereal a creature that\r\nher spirit could be seen trembling through her limbs, he felt heartily\r\nashamed of his earthliness in spending the hours he had spent in\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s company. There was something rude and immoral in thrusting\r\nthese recent facts of his life upon the mind of one who, to him, was so\r\nuncarnate as to seem at times impossible as a human wife to any average\r\nman. And yet she was Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s. How she had become such, how she\r\nlived as such, passed his comprehension as he regarded her to-day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll go back with me?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a train just now. I wonder\r\nhow my aunt is by this time\xe2\x80\xa6 And so, Sue, you really came on my account\r\nall this way! At what an early time you must have started, poor thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Sitting up watching alone made me all nerves for you, and instead\r\nof going to bed when it got light I started. And now you won\xe2\x80\x99t frighten\r\nme like this again about your morals for nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was not so sure that she had been frightened about his morals for\r\nnothing. He released her hand till they had entered the train,\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nseemed the same carriage he had lately got out of with another\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nthey sat down side by side, Sue between him and the window. He regarded\r\nthe delicate lines of her profile, and the small, tight, applelike\r\nconvexities of her bodice, so different from Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s amplitudes.\r\nThough she knew he was looking at her she did not turn to him, but kept\r\nher eyes forward, as if afraid that by meeting his own some troublous\r\ndiscussion would be initiated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue\xe2\x80\x94you are married now, you know, like me; and yet we have been in\r\nsuch a hurry that we have not said a word about it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no necessity,\xe2\x80\x9d she quickly returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh well\xe2\x80\x94perhaps not\xe2\x80\xa6 But I wish\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t talk about _me_\xe2\x80\x94I wish you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she entreated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\ndistresses me, rather. Forgive my saying it! \xe2\x80\xa6 Where did you stay last\r\nnight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had asked the question in perfect innocence, to change the topic.\r\nHe knew that, and said merely, \xe2\x80\x9cAt an inn,\xe2\x80\x9d though it would have been a\r\nrelief to tell her of his meeting with an unexpected one. But the\r\nlatter\xe2\x80\x99s final announcement of her marriage in Australia bewildered him\r\nlest what he might say should do his ignorant wife an injury.\r\n\r\nTheir talk proceeded but awkwardly till they reached Alfredston. That\r\nSue was not as she had been, but was labelled \xe2\x80\x9cPhillotson,\xe2\x80\x9d paralyzed\r\nJude whenever he wanted to commune with her as an individual. Yet she\r\nseemed unaltered\xe2\x80\x94he could not say why. There remained the five-mile\r\nextra journey into the country, which it was just as easy to walk as to\r\ndrive, the greater part of it being uphill. Jude had never before in\r\nhis life gone that road with Sue, though he had with another. It was\r\nnow as if he carried a bright light which temporarily banished the\r\nshady associations of the earlier time.\r\n\r\nSue talked; but Jude noticed that she still kept the conversation from\r\nherself. At length he inquired if her husband were well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is obliged to be in the school all the day, or\r\nhe would have come with me. He is so good and kind that to accompany me\r\nhe would have dismissed the school for once, even against his\r\nprinciples\xe2\x80\x94for he is strongly opposed to giving casual holidays\xe2\x80\x94only I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let him. I felt it would be better to come alone. Aunt\r\nDrusilla, I knew, was so very eccentric; and his being almost a\r\nstranger to her now would have made it irksome to both. Since it turns\r\nout that she is hardly conscious I am glad I did not ask him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude had walked moodily while this praise of Phillotson was being\r\nexpressed. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Phillotson obliges you in everything, as he ought,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to be a happy wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd of course I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBride, I might almost have said, as yet. It is not so many weeks since\r\nI gave you to him, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know! I know!\xe2\x80\x9d There was something in her face which belied her\r\nlate assuring words, so strictly proper and so lifelessly spoken that\r\nthey might have been taken from a list of model speeches in \xe2\x80\x9cThe Wife\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nGuide to Conduct.\xe2\x80\x9d Jude knew the quality of every vibration in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvoice, could read every symptom of her mental condition; and he was\r\nconvinced that she was unhappy, although she had not been a month\r\nmarried. But her rushing away thus from home, to see the last of a\r\nrelative whom she had hardly known in her life, proved nothing; for Sue\r\nnaturally did such things as those.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you have my good wishes now as always, Mrs. Phillotson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe reproached him by a glance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you are not Mrs. Phillotson,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are dear, free\r\nSue Bridehead, only you don\xe2\x80\x99t know it! Wifedom has not yet squashed up\r\nand digested you in its vast maw as an atom which has no further\r\nindividuality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue put on a look of being offended, till she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cNor has\r\nhusbandom you, so far as I can see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it has!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, shaking his head sadly.\r\n\r\nWhen they reached the lone cottage under the firs, between the Brown\r\nHouse and Marygreen, in which Jude and Arabella had lived and\r\nquarrelled, he turned to look at it. A squalid family lived there now.\r\nHe could not help saying to Sue: \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the house my wife and I\r\noccupied the whole of the time we lived together. I brought her home to\r\nthat house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at it. \xe2\x80\x9cThat to you was what the school-house at Shaston is\r\nto me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but I was not very happy there as you are in yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe closed her lips in retortive silence, and they walked some way till\r\nshe glanced at him to see how he was taking it. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I may have\r\nexaggerated your happiness\xe2\x80\x94one never knows,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued blandly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think that, Jude, for a moment, even though you may have said it\r\nto sting me! He\xe2\x80\x99s as good to me as a man can be, and gives me perfect\r\nliberty\xe2\x80\x94which elderly husbands don\xe2\x80\x99t do in general\xe2\x80\xa6 If you think I am\r\nnot happy because he\xe2\x80\x99s too old for me, you are wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think anything against him\xe2\x80\x94to you dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you won\xe2\x80\x99t say things to distress me, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said no more, but he knew that, from some cause or other, in taking\r\nPhillotson as a husband, Sue felt that she had done what she ought not\r\nto have done.\r\n\r\nThey plunged into the concave field on the other side of which rose the\r\nvillage\xe2\x80\x94the field wherein Jude had received a thrashing from the farmer\r\nmany years earlier. On ascending to the village and approaching the\r\nhouse they found Mrs. Edlin standing at the door, who at sight of them\r\nlifted her hands deprecatingly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s downstairs, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll believe\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the widow. \xe2\x80\x9cOut o\xe2\x80\x99 bed she got, and nothing could turn her.\r\nWhat will come o\xe2\x80\x99t I do not know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn entering, there indeed by the fireplace sat the old woman, wrapped\r\nin blankets, and turning upon them a countenance like that of\r\nSebastiano\xe2\x80\x99s Lazarus. They must have looked their amazement, for she\r\nsaid in a hollow voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94sceered ye, have I! I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to bide up there no longer, to\r\nplease nobody! \xe2\x80\x99Tis more than flesh and blood can bear, to be ordered\r\nto do this and that by a feller that don\xe2\x80\x99t know half as well as you do\r\nyourself! \xe2\x80\xa6 Ah\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll rue this marrying as well as he!\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\r\nturning to Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cAll our family do\xe2\x80\x94and nearly all everybody else\xe2\x80\x99s. You\r\nshould have done as I did, you simpleton! And Phillotson the\r\nschoolmaster, of all men! What made \xe2\x80\x99ee marry him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes most women marry, Aunt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! You mean to say you loved the man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t meant to say anything definite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo ye love un?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me, Aunt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can mind the man very well. A very civil, honourable liver; but\r\nLord!\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to wownd your feelings, but\xe2\x80\x94there be certain men\r\nhere and there that no woman of any niceness can stomach. I should have\r\nsaid he was one. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say so _now_, since you must ha\xe2\x80\x99 known better\r\nthan I\xe2\x80\x94but that\xe2\x80\x99s what I _should_ have said!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue jumped up and went out. Jude followed her, and found her in the\r\nouthouse, crying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry, dear!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude in distress. \xe2\x80\x9cShe means well, but is very\r\ncrusty and queer now, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94it isn\xe2\x80\x99t that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, trying to dry her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\nher roughness one bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is that what she says is\xe2\x80\x94is true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t like him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that!\xe2\x80\x9d she said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cThat I ought\xe2\x80\x94perhaps I ought\r\nnot to have married!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wondered if she had really been going to say that at first. They\r\nwent back, and the subject was smoothed over, and her aunt took rather\r\nkindly to Sue, telling her that not many young women newly married\r\nwould have come so far to see a sick old crone like her. In the\r\nafternoon Sue prepared to depart, Jude hiring a neighbour to drive her\r\nto Alfredston.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go with you to the station, if you\xe2\x80\x99d like?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nShe would not let him. The man came round with the trap, and Jude\r\nhelped her into it, perhaps with unnecessary attention, for she looked\r\nat him prohibitively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose\xe2\x80\x94I may come to see you some day, when I am back again at\r\nMelchester?\xe2\x80\x9d he half-crossly observed.\r\n\r\nShe bent down and said softly: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear\xe2\x80\x94you are not to come yet. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think you are in a good mood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d She waved her hand and was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s right! I won\xe2\x80\x99t go!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\nHe passed the evening and following days in mortifying by every\r\npossible means his wish to see her, nearly starving himself in attempts\r\nto extinguish by fasting his passionate tendency to love her. He read\r\nsermons on discipline, and hunted up passages in Church history that\r\ntreated of the Ascetics of the second century. Before he had returned\r\nfrom Marygreen to Melchester there arrived a letter from Arabella. The\r\nsight of it revived a stronger feeling of self-condemnation for his\r\nbrief return to her society than for his attachment to Sue.\r\n\r\nThe letter, he perceived, bore a London postmark instead of the\r\nChristminster one. Arabella informed him that a few days after their\r\nparting in the morning at Christminster, she had been surprised by an\r\naffectionate letter from her Australian husband, formerly manager of\r\nthe hotel in Sydney. He had come to England on purpose to find her; and\r\nhad taken a free, fully-licensed public, in Lambeth, where he wished\r\nher to join him in conducting the business, which was likely to be a\r\nvery thriving one, the house being situated in an excellent, densely\r\npopulated, gin-drinking neighbourhood, and already doing a trade of\r\n\xc2\xa3200 a month, which could be easily doubled.\r\n\r\nAs he had said that he loved her very much still, and implored her to\r\ntell him where she was, and as they had only parted in a slight tiff,\r\nand as her engagement in Christminster was only temporary, she had just\r\ngone to join him as he urged. She could not help feeling that she\r\nbelonged to him more than to Jude, since she had properly married him,\r\nand had lived with him much longer than with her first husband. In thus\r\nwishing Jude good-bye she bore him no ill-will, and trusted he would\r\nnot turn upon her, a weak woman, and inform against her, and bring her\r\nto ruin now that she had a chance of improving her circumstances and\r\nleading a genteel life.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nJude returned to Melchester, which had the questionable recommendation\r\nof being only a dozen and a half miles from his Sue\xe2\x80\x99s now permanent\r\nresidence. At first he felt that this nearness was a distinct reason\r\nfor not going southward at all; but Christminster was too sad a place\r\nto bear, while the proximity of Shaston to Melchester might afford him\r\nthe glory of worsting the Enemy in a close engagement, such as was\r\ndeliberately sought by the priests and virgins of the early Church,\r\nwho, disdaining an ignominious flight from temptation, became even\r\nchamber-partners with impunity. Jude did not pause to remember that, in\r\nthe laconic words of the historian, \xe2\x80\x9cinsulted Nature sometimes\r\nvindicated her rights\xe2\x80\x9d in such circumstances.\r\n\r\nHe now returned with feverish desperation to his study for the\r\npriesthood\xe2\x80\x94in the recognition that the single-mindedness of his aims,\r\nand his fidelity to the cause, had been more than questionable of late.\r\nHis passion for Sue troubled his soul; yet his lawful abandonment to\r\nthe society of Arabella for twelve hours seemed instinctively a worse\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94even though she had not told him of her Sydney husband till\r\nafterwards. He had, he verily believed, overcome all tendency to fly to\r\nliquor\xe2\x80\x94which, indeed, he had never done from taste, but merely as an\r\nescape from intolerable misery of mind. Yet he perceived with\r\ndespondency that, taken all round, he was a man of too many passions to\r\nmake a good clergyman; the utmost he could hope for was that in a life\r\nof constant internal warfare between flesh and spirit the former might\r\nnot always be victorious.\r\n\r\nAs a hobby, auxiliary to his readings in Divinity, he developed his\r\nslight skill in church-music and thorough-bass, till he could join in\r\npart-singing from notation with some accuracy. A mile or two from\r\nMelchester there was a restored village church, to which Jude had\r\noriginally gone to fix the new columns and capitals. By this means he\r\nhad become acquainted with the organist, and the ultimate result was\r\nthat he joined the choir as a bass voice.\r\n\r\nHe walked out to this parish twice every Sunday, and sometimes in the\r\nweek. One evening about Easter the choir met for practice, and a new\r\nhymn which Jude had heard of as being by a Wessex composer was to be\r\ntried and prepared for the following week. It turned out to be a\r\nstrangely emotional composition. As they all sang it over and over\r\nagain its harmonies grew upon Jude, and moved him exceedingly.\r\n\r\nWhen they had finished he went round to the organist to make inquiries.\r\nThe score was in manuscript, the name of the composer being at the\r\nhead, together with the title of the hymn: \xe2\x80\x9cThe Foot of the Cross.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the organist. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a local man. He is a professional\r\nmusician at Kennetbridge\xe2\x80\x94between here and Christminster. The vicar\r\nknows him. He was brought up and educated in Christminster traditions,\r\nwhich accounts for the quality of the piece. I think he plays in the\r\nlarge church there, and has a surpliced choir. He comes to Melchester\r\nsometimes, and once tried to get the cathedral organ when the post was\r\nvacant. The hymn is getting about everywhere this Easter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he walked humming the air on his way home, Jude fell to musing on\r\nits composer, and the reasons why he composed it. What a man of\r\nsympathies he must be! Perplexed and harassed as he himself was about\r\nSue and Arabella, and troubled as was his conscience by the\r\ncomplication of his position, how he would like to know that man! \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nof all men would understand my difficulties,\xe2\x80\x9d said the impulsive Jude.\r\nIf there were any person in the world to choose as a confidant, this\r\ncomposer would be the one, for he must have suffered, and throbbed, and\r\nyearned.\r\n\r\nIn brief, ill as he could afford the time and money for the journey,\r\nFawley resolved, like the child that he was, to go to Kennetbridge the\r\nvery next Sunday. He duly started, early in the morning, for it was\r\nonly by a series of crooked railways that he could get to the town.\r\nAbout mid-day he reached it, and crossing the bridge into the quaint\r\nold borough he inquired for the house of the composer.\r\n\r\nThey told him it was a red brick building some little way further on.\r\nAlso that the gentleman himself had just passed along the street not\r\nfive minutes before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich way?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude with alacrity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStraight along homeward from church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude hastened on, and soon had the pleasure of observing a man in a\r\nblack coat and a black slouched felt hat no considerable distance\r\nahead. Stretching out his legs yet more widely, he stalked after. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nhungry soul in pursuit of a full soul!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI must speak to that\r\nman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could not, however, overtake the musician before he had entered his\r\nown house, and then arose the question if this were an expedient time\r\nto call. Whether or not he decided to do so there and then, now that he\r\nhad got here, the distance home being too great for him to wait till\r\nlate in the afternoon. This man of soul would understand scant\r\nceremony, and might be quite a perfect adviser in a case in which an\r\nearthly and illegitimate passion had cunningly obtained entrance into\r\nhis heart through the opening afforded for religion.\r\n\r\nJude accordingly rang the bell, and was admitted.\r\n\r\nThe musician came to him in a moment, and being respectably dressed,\r\ngood-looking, and frank in manner, Jude obtained a favourable\r\nreception. He was nevertheless conscious that there would be a certain\r\nawkwardness in explaining his errand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been singing in the choir of a little church near Melchester,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we have this week practised \xe2\x80\x98The Foot of the Cross,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwhich I understand, sir, that you composed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did\xe2\x80\x94a year or so ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94like it. I think it supremely beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh well\xe2\x80\x94other people have said so too. Yes, there\xe2\x80\x99s money in it, if I\r\ncould only see about getting it published. I have other compositions to\r\ngo with it, too; I wish I could bring them out; for I haven\xe2\x80\x99t made a\r\nfive-pound note out of any of them yet. These publishing people\xe2\x80\x94they\r\nwant the copyright of an obscure composer\xe2\x80\x99s work, such as mine is, for\r\nalmost less than I should have to pay a person for making a fair\r\nmanuscript copy of the score. The one you speak of I have lent to\r\nvarious friends about here and Melchester, and so it has got to be sung\r\na little. But music is a poor staff to lean on\xe2\x80\x94I am giving it up\r\nentirely. You must go into trade if you want to make money nowadays.\r\nThe wine business is what I am thinking of. This is my forthcoming\r\nlist\xe2\x80\x94it is not issued yet\xe2\x80\x94but you can take one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe handed Jude an advertisement list of several pages in booklet shape,\r\nornamentally margined with a red line, in which were set forth the\r\nvarious clarets, champagnes, ports, sherries, and other wines with\r\nwhich he purposed to initiate his new venture. It took Jude more than\r\nby surprise that the man with the soul was thus and thus; and he felt\r\nthat he could not open up his confidences.\r\n\r\nThey talked a little longer, but constrainedly, for when the musician\r\nfound that Jude was a poor man his manner changed from what it had been\r\nwhile Jude\xe2\x80\x99s appearance and address deceived him as to his position and\r\npursuits. Jude stammered out something about his feelings in wishing to\r\ncongratulate the author on such an exalted composition, and took an\r\nembarrassed leave.\r\n\r\nAll the way home by the slow Sunday train, sitting in the fireless\r\nwaiting-rooms on this cold spring day, he was depressed enough at his\r\nsimplicity in taking such a journey. But no sooner did he reach his\r\nMelchester lodging than he found awaiting him a letter which had\r\narrived that morning a few minutes after he had left the house. It was\r\na contrite little note from Sue, in which she said, with sweet\r\nhumility, that she felt she had been horrid in telling him he was not\r\nto come to see her, that she despised herself for having been so\r\nconventional; and that he was to be sure to come by the\r\neleven-forty-five train that very Sunday, and have dinner with them at\r\nhalf-past one.\r\n\r\nJude almost tore his hair at having missed this letter till it was too\r\nlate to act upon its contents; but he had chastened himself\r\nconsiderably of late, and at last his chimerical expedition to\r\nKennetbridge really did seem to have been another special intervention\r\nof Providence to keep him away from temptation. But a growing\r\nimpatience of faith, which he had noticed in himself more than once of\r\nlate, made him pass over in ridicule the idea that God sent people on\r\nfools\xe2\x80\x99 errands. He longed to see her; he was angry at having missed\r\nher: and he wrote instantly, telling her what had happened, and saying\r\nhe had not enough patience to wait till the following Sunday, but would\r\ncome any day in the week that she liked to name.\r\n\r\nSince he wrote a little over-ardently, Sue, as her manner was, delayed\r\nher reply till Thursday before Good Friday, when she said he might come\r\nthat afternoon if he wished, this being the earliest day on which she\r\ncould welcome him, for she was now assistant-teacher in her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nschool. Jude therefore got leave from the cathedral works at the\r\ntrifling expense of a stoppage of pay, and went.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart Fourth AT SHASTON\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cWhoso prefers either Matrimony or other Ordinance before the Good of\r\nMan and the plain Exigence of Charity, let him profess Papist, or\r\nProtestant, or what he will, he is no better than a Pharisee.\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94J.\r\nMILTON.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nShaston, the ancient British Palladour,\r\n\r\nFrom whose foundation first such strange reports arise,\r\n\r\n(as Drayton sang it), was, and is, in itself the city of a dream. Vague\r\nimaginings of its castle, its three mints, its magnificent apsidal\r\nabbey, the chief glory of South Wessex, its twelve churches, its\r\nshrines, chantries, hospitals, its gabled freestone mansions\xe2\x80\x94all now\r\nruthlessly swept away\xe2\x80\x94throw the visitor, even against his will, into a\r\npensive melancholy, which the stimulating atmosphere and limitless\r\nlandscape around him can scarcely dispel. The spot was the burial-place\r\nof a king and a queen, of abbots and abbesses, saints and bishops,\r\nknights and squires. The bones of King Edward \xe2\x80\x9cthe Martyr,\xe2\x80\x9d carefully\r\nremoved hither for holy preservation, brought Shaston a renown which\r\nmade it the resort of pilgrims from every part of Europe, and enabled\r\nit to maintain a reputation extending far beyond English shores. To\r\nthis fair creation of the great Middle-Age the Dissolution was, as\r\nhistorians tell us, the death-knell. With the destruction of the\r\nenormous abbey the whole place collapsed in a general ruin: the\r\nMartyr\xe2\x80\x99s bones met with the fate of the sacred pile that held them, and\r\nnot a stone is now left to tell where they lie.\r\n\r\nThe natural picturesqueness and singularity of the town still remain;\r\nbut strange to say these qualities, which were noted by many writers in\r\nages when scenic beauty is said to have been unappreciated, are passed\r\nover in this, and one of the queerest and quaintest spots in England\r\nstands virtually unvisited to-day.\r\n\r\nIt has a unique position on the summit of a steep and imposing scarp,\r\nrising on the north, south, and west sides of the borough out of the\r\ndeep alluvial Vale of Blackmoor, the view from the Castle Green over\r\nthree counties of verdant pasture\xe2\x80\x94South, Mid, and Nether Wessex\xe2\x80\x94being\r\nas sudden a surprise to the unexpectant traveller\xe2\x80\x99s eyes as the\r\nmedicinal air is to his lungs. Impossible to a railway, it can best be\r\nreached on foot, next best by light vehicles; and it is hardly\r\naccessible to these but by a sort of isthmus on the north-east, that\r\nconnects it with the high chalk table-land on that side.\r\n\r\nSuch is, and such was, the now world-forgotten Shaston or Palladour.\r\nIts situation rendered water the great want of the town; and within\r\nliving memory, horses, donkeys and men may have been seen toiling up\r\nthe winding ways to the top of the height, laden with tubs and barrels\r\nfilled from the wells beneath the mountain, and hawkers retailing their\r\ncontents at the price of a halfpenny a bucketful.\r\n\r\nThis difficulty in the water supply, together with two other odd facts,\r\nnamely, that the chief graveyard slopes up as steeply as a roof behind\r\nthe church, and that in former times the town passed through a curious\r\nperiod of corruption, conventual and domestic, gave rise to the saying\r\nthat Shaston was remarkable for three consolations to man, such as the\r\nworld afforded not elsewhere. It was a place where the churchyard lay\r\nnearer heaven than the church steeple, where beer was more plentiful\r\nthan water, and where there were more wanton women than honest wives\r\nand maids. It is also said that after the Middle Ages the inhabitants\r\nwere too poor to pay their priests, and hence were compelled to pull\r\ndown their churches, and refrain altogether from the public worship of\r\nGod; a necessity which they bemoaned over their cups in the settles of\r\ntheir inns on Sunday afternoons. In those days the Shastonians were\r\napparently not without a sense of humour.\r\n\r\nThere was another peculiarity\xe2\x80\x94this a modern one\xe2\x80\x94which Shaston appeared\r\nto owe to its site. It was the resting-place and headquarters of the\r\nproprietors of wandering vans, shows, shooting-galleries, and other\r\nitinerant concerns, whose business lay largely at fairs and markets. As\r\nstrange wild birds are seen assembled on some lofty promontory,\r\nmeditatively pausing for longer flights, or to return by the course\r\nthey followed thither, so here, in this cliff-town, stood in stultified\r\nsilence the yellow and green caravans bearing names not local, as if\r\nsurprised by a change in the landscape so violent as to hinder their\r\nfurther progress; and here they usually remained all the winter till\r\nthey turned to seek again their old tracks in the following spring.\r\n\r\nIt was to this breezy and whimsical spot that Jude ascended from the\r\nnearest station for the first time in his life about four o\xe2\x80\x99clock one\r\nafternoon, and entering on the summit of the peak after a toilsome\r\nclimb, passed the first houses of the aerial town; and drew towards the\r\nschool-house. The hour was too early; the pupils were still in school,\r\nhumming small, like a swarm of gnats; and he withdrew a few steps along\r\nAbbey Walk, whence he regarded the spot which fate had made the home of\r\nall he loved best in the world. In front of the schools, which were\r\nextensive and stone-built, grew two enormous beeches with smooth\r\nmouse-coloured trunks, as such trees will only grow on chalk uplands.\r\nWithin the mullioned and transomed windows he could see the black,\r\nbrown, and flaxen crowns of the scholars over the sills, and to pass\r\nthe time away he walked down to the level terrace where the abbey\r\ngardens once had spread, his heart throbbing in spite of him.\r\n\r\nUnwilling to enter till the children were dismissed he remained here\r\ntill young voices could be heard in the open air, and girls in white\r\npinafores over red and blue frocks appeared dancing along the paths\r\nwhich the abbess, prioress, subprioress, and fifty nuns had demurely\r\npaced three centuries earlier. Retracing his steps he found that he had\r\nwaited too long, and that Sue had gone out into the town at the heels\r\nof the last scholar, Mr. Phillotson having been absent all the\r\nafternoon at a teachers\xe2\x80\x99 meeting at Shottsford.\r\n\r\nJude went into the empty schoolroom and sat down, the girl who was\r\nsweeping the floor having informed him that Mrs. Phillotson would be\r\nback again in a few minutes. A piano stood near\xe2\x80\x94actually the old piano\r\nthat Phillotson had possessed at Marygreen\xe2\x80\x94and though the dark\r\nafternoon almost prevented him seeing the notes Jude touched them in\r\nhis humble way, and could not help modulating into the hymn which had\r\nso affected him in the previous week.\r\n\r\nA figure moved behind him, and thinking it was still the girl with the\r\nbroom Jude took no notice, till the person came close and laid her\r\nfingers lightly upon his bass hand. The imposed hand was a little one\r\nhe seemed to know, and he turned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t stop,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cI like it. I learnt it before I left\r\nMelchester. They used to play it in the training school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t strum before you! Play it for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh well\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue sat down, and her rendering of the piece, though not remarkable,\r\nseemed divine as compared with his own. She, like him, was evidently\r\ntouched\xe2\x80\x94to her own surprise\xe2\x80\x94by the recalled air; and when she had\r\nfinished, and he moved his hand towards hers, it met his own half-way.\r\nJude grasped it\xe2\x80\x94just as he had done before her marriage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is odd,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a voice quite changed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I should care\r\nabout that air; because\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not that sort\xe2\x80\x94quite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot easily moved?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite mean that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but you _are_ one of that sort, for you are just like me at\r\nheart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut not at head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe played on and suddenly turned round; and by an unpremeditated\r\ninstinct each clasped the other\xe2\x80\x99s hand again.\r\n\r\nShe uttered a forced little laugh as she relinquished his quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nfunny!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what we both did that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose because we are both alike, as I said before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in our thoughts! Perhaps a little in our feelings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they rule thoughts\xe2\x80\xa6 Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it enough to make one blaspheme that the\r\ncomposer of that hymn is one of the most commonplace men I ever met!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went to see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you goose\xe2\x80\x94to do just what I should have done! Why did you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we are not alike,\xe2\x80\x9d he said drily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll have some tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cShall we have it here instead of\r\nin my house? It is no trouble to get the kettle and things brought in.\r\nWe don\xe2\x80\x99t live at the school you know, but in that ancient dwelling\r\nacross the way called Old-Grove Place. It is so antique and dismal that\r\nit depresses me dreadfully. Such houses are very well to visit, but not\r\nto live in\xe2\x80\x94I feel crushed into the earth by the weight of so many\r\nprevious lives there spent. In a new place like these schools there is\r\nonly your own life to support. Sit down, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell Ada to bring the\r\ntea-things across.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe waited in the light of the stove, the door of which she flung open\r\nbefore going out, and when she returned, followed by the maiden with\r\ntea, they sat down by the same light, assisted by the blue rays of a\r\nspirit-lamp under the brass kettle on the stand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is one of your wedding-presents to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, signifying the\r\nlatter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\nThe kettle of his gift sang with some satire in its note, to his mind;\r\nand to change the subject he said, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know of any good readable\r\nedition of the uncanonical books of the New Testament? You don\xe2\x80\x99t read\r\nthem in the school I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear no!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99twould alarm the neighbourhood\xe2\x80\xa6 Yes, there is one. I am\r\nnot familiar with it now, though I was interested in it when my former\r\nfriend was alive. Cowper\xe2\x80\x99s _Apocryphal Gospels_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat sounds like what I want.\xe2\x80\x9d His thoughts, however reverted with a\r\ntwinge to the \xe2\x80\x9cformer friend\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94by whom she meant, as he knew, the\r\nuniversity comrade of her earlier days. He wondered if she talked of\r\nhim to Phillotson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gospel of Nicodemus is very nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on to keep him from\r\nhis jealous thoughts, which she read clearly, as she always did. Indeed\r\nwhen they talked on an indifferent subject, as now, there was ever a\r\nsecond silent conversation passing between their emotions, so perfect\r\nwas the reciprocity between them. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite like the genuine\r\narticle. All cut up into verses, too; so that it is like one of the\r\nother evangelists read in a dream, when things are the same, yet not\r\nthe same. But, Jude, do you take an interest in those questions still?\r\nAre you getting up _Apologetica_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I am reading Divinity harder than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe regarded him curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you look at me like that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94why do you want to know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you can tell me anything I may be ignorant of in that\r\nsubject. You must have learnt a lot of everything from your dear dead\r\nfriend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe won\xe2\x80\x99t get on to that now!\xe2\x80\x9d she coaxed. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you be carving out at\r\nthat church again next week, where you learnt the pretty hymn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be very nice. Shall I come and see you there? It is in this\r\ndirection, and I could come any afternoon by train for half an hour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Don\xe2\x80\x99t come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94aren\xe2\x80\x99t we going to be friends, then, any longer, as we used to\r\nbe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know that. I thought you were always going to be kind to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I done, then? I am sure I thought we two\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The _tremolo_ in\r\nher voice caused her to break off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue, I sometimes think you are a flirt,\xe2\x80\x9d said he abruptly.\r\n\r\nThere was a momentary pause, till she suddenly jumped up; and to his\r\nsurprise he saw by the kettle-flame that her face was flushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t talk to you any longer, Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, the tragic contralto\r\nnote having come back as of old. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is getting too dark to stay\r\ntogether like this, after playing morbid Good Friday tunes that make\r\none feel what one shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t! \xe2\x80\xa6 We mustn\xe2\x80\x99t sit and talk in this way any\r\nmore. Yes\xe2\x80\x94you must go away, for you mistake me! I am very much the\r\nreverse of what you say so cruelly\xe2\x80\x94Oh, Jude, it _was_ cruel to say\r\nthat! Yet I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you the truth\xe2\x80\x94I should shock you by letting you\r\nknow how I give way to my impulses, and how much I feel that I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been provided with attractiveness unless it were meant\r\nto be exercised! Some women\xe2\x80\x99s love of being loved is insatiable; and\r\nso, often, is their love of loving; and in the last case they may find\r\nthat they can\xe2\x80\x99t give it continuously to the chamber-officer appointed\r\nby the bishop\xe2\x80\x99s licence to receive it. But you are so straightforward,\r\nJude, that you can\xe2\x80\x99t understand me! \xe2\x80\xa6 Now you must go. I am sorry my\r\nhusband is not at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI perceive I have said that in mere convention! Honestly I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nI am sorry. It does not matter, either way, sad to say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs they had overdone the grasp of hands some time sooner, she touched\r\nhis fingers but lightly when he went out now. He had hardly gone from\r\nthe door when, with a dissatisfied look, she jumped on a form and\r\nopened the iron casement of a window beneath which he was passing in\r\nthe path without. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen do you leave here to catch your train, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked.\r\n\r\nHe looked up in some surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cThe coach that runs to meet it goes in\r\nthree-quarters of an hour or so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do with yourself for the time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94wander about, I suppose. Perhaps I shall go and sit in the old\r\nchurch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does seem hard of me to pack you off so! You have thought enough of\r\nchurches, Heaven knows, without going into one in the dark. Stay\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere you are. I can talk to you better like this than when you were\r\ninside\xe2\x80\xa6 It was so kind and tender of you to give up half a day\xe2\x80\x99s work\r\nto come to see me! \xe2\x80\xa6 You are Joseph the dreamer of dreams, dear Jude.\r\nAnd a tragic Don Quixote. And sometimes you are St. Stephen, who, while\r\nthey were stoning him, could see Heaven opened. Oh, my poor friend and\r\ncomrade, you\xe2\x80\x99ll suffer yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow that the high window-sill was between them, so that he could not\r\nget at her, she seemed not to mind indulging in a frankness she had\r\nfeared at close quarters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, still in the tone of one brimful\r\nof feeling, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the social moulds civilization fits us into have no\r\nmore relation to our actual shapes than the conventional shapes of the\r\nconstellations have to the real star-patterns. I am called Mrs. Richard\r\nPhillotson, living a calm wedded life with my counterpart of that name.\r\nBut I am not really Mrs. Richard Phillotson, but a woman tossed about,\r\nall alone, with aberrant passions, and unaccountable antipathies\xe2\x80\xa6 Now\r\nyou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t wait longer, or you will lose the coach. Come and see me\r\nagain. You must come to the house then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen shall it be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow week. Good-bye\xe2\x80\x94good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d She stretched out her hand and\r\nstroked his forehead pitifully\xe2\x80\x94just once. Jude said good-bye, and went\r\naway into the darkness.\r\n\r\nPassing along Bimport Street he thought he heard the wheels of the\r\ncoach departing, and, truly enough, when he reached the Duke\xe2\x80\x99s Arms in\r\nthe Market Place the coach had gone. It was impossible for him to get\r\nto the station on foot in time for this train, and he settled himself\r\nperforce to wait for the next\xe2\x80\x94the last to Melchester that night.\r\n\r\nHe wandered about awhile, obtained something to eat; and then, having\r\nanother half-hour on his hands, his feet involuntarily took him through\r\nthe venerable graveyard of Trinity Church, with its avenues of limes,\r\nin the direction of the schools again. They were entirely in darkness.\r\nShe had said she lived over the way at Old-Grove Place, a house which\r\nhe soon discovered from her description of its antiquity.\r\n\r\nA glimmering candlelight shone from a front window, the shutters being\r\nyet unclosed. He could see the interior clearly\xe2\x80\x94the floor sinking a\r\ncouple of steps below the road without, which had become raised during\r\nthe centuries since the house was built. Sue, evidently just come in,\r\nwas standing with her hat on in this front parlour or sitting-room,\r\nwhose walls were lined with wainscoting of panelled oak reaching from\r\nfloor to ceiling, the latter being crossed by huge moulded beams only a\r\nlittle way above her head. The mantelpiece was of the same heavy\r\ndescription, carved with Jacobean pilasters and scroll-work. The\r\ncenturies did, indeed, ponderously overhang a young wife who passed her\r\ntime here.\r\n\r\nShe had opened a rosewood work-box, and was looking at a photograph.\r\nHaving contemplated it a little while she pressed it against her bosom,\r\nand put it again in its place.\r\n\r\nThen becoming aware that she had not obscured the windows she came\r\nforward to do so, candle in hand. It was too dark for her to see Jude\r\nwithout, but he could see her face distinctly, and there was an\r\nunmistakable tearfulness about the dark, long-lashed eyes.\r\n\r\nShe closed the shutters, and Jude turned away to pursue his solitary\r\njourney home. \xe2\x80\x9cWhose photograph was she looking at?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He had\r\nonce given her his; but she had others, he knew. Yet it was his,\r\nsurely?\r\n\r\nHe knew he should go to see her again, according to her invitation.\r\nThose earnest men he read of, the saints, whom Sue, with gentle\r\nirreverence, called his demi-gods, would have shunned such encounters\r\nif they doubted their own strength. But he could not. He might fast and\r\npray during the whole interval, but the human was more powerful in him\r\nthan the Divine.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nHowever, if God disposed not, woman did. The next morning but one\r\nbrought him this note from her:\r\n\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t come next week. On your own account don\xe2\x80\x99t! We were too free,\r\nunder the influence of that morbid hymn and the twilight. Think no more\r\nthan you can help of\r\n\r\n\r\nSUSANNA FLORENCE MARY.\r\n\r\nThe disappointment was keen. He knew her mood, the look of her face,\r\nwhen she subscribed herself at length thus. But, whatever her mood, he\r\ncould not say she was wrong in her view. He replied:\r\n\r\nI acquiesce. You are right. It is a lesson in renunciation which I\r\nsuppose I ought to learn at this season.\r\n\r\n\r\nJUDE.\r\n\r\nHe despatched the note on Easter Eve, and there seemed a finality in\r\ntheir decisions. But other forces and laws than theirs were in\r\noperation. On Easter Monday morning he received a message from the\r\nWidow Edlin, whom he had directed to telegraph if anything serious\r\nhappened:\r\n\r\nYour aunt is sinking. Come at once.\r\n\r\nHe threw down his tools and went. Three and a half hours later he was\r\ncrossing the downs about Marygreen, and presently plunged into the\r\nconcave field across which the short cut was made to the village. As he\r\nascended on the other side a labouring man, who had been watching his\r\napproach from a gate across the path, moved uneasily, and prepared to\r\nspeak. \xe2\x80\x9cI can see in his face that she is dead,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Aunt\r\nDrusilla!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was as he had supposed, and Mrs. Edlin had sent out the man to break\r\nthe news to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have knowed \xe2\x80\x99ee. She lay like a doll wi\xe2\x80\x99 glass eyes; so\r\nit didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter that you wasn\xe2\x80\x99t here,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nJude went on to the house, and in the afternoon, when everything was\r\ndone, and the layers-out had finished their beer, and gone, he sat down\r\nalone in the silent place. It was absolutely necessary to communicate\r\nwith Sue, though two or three days earlier they had agreed to mutual\r\nseverance. He wrote in the briefest terms:\r\n\r\nAunt Drusilla is dead, having been taken almost suddenly. The funeral\r\nis on Friday afternoon.\r\n\r\nHe remained in and about Marygreen through the intervening days, went\r\nout on Friday morning to see that the grave was finished, and wondered\r\nif Sue would come. She had not written, and that seemed to signify\r\nrather that she would come than that she would not. Having timed her by\r\nher only possible train, he locked the door about mid-day, and crossed\r\nthe hollow field to the verge of the upland by the Brown House, where\r\nhe stood and looked over the vast prospect northwards, and over the\r\nnearer landscape in which Alfredston stood. Two miles behind it a jet\r\nof white steam was travelling from the left to the right of the\r\npicture.\r\n\r\nThere was a long time to wait, even now, till he would know if she had\r\narrived. He did wait, however, and at last a small hired vehicle pulled\r\nup at the bottom of the hill, and a person alighted, the conveyance\r\ngoing back, while the passenger began ascending the hill. He knew her;\r\nand she looked so slender to-day that it seemed as if she might be\r\ncrushed in the intensity of a too passionate embrace\xe2\x80\x94such as it was not\r\nfor him to give. Two-thirds of the way up her head suddenly took a\r\nsolicitous poise, and he knew that she had at that moment recognized\r\nhim. Her face soon began a pensive smile, which lasted till, having\r\ndescended a little way, he met her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought,\xe2\x80\x9d she began with nervous quickness, \xe2\x80\x9cthat it would be so sad\r\nto let you attend the funeral alone! And so\xe2\x80\x94at the last moment\xe2\x80\x94I came.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear faithful Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Jude.\r\n\r\nWith the elusiveness of her curious double nature, however, Sue did not\r\nstand still for any further greeting, though it wanted some time to the\r\nburial. A pathos so unusually compounded as that which attached to this\r\nhour was unlikely to repeat itself for years, if ever, and Jude would\r\nhave paused, and meditated, and conversed. But Sue either saw it not at\r\nall, or, seeing it more than he, would not allow herself to feel it.\r\n\r\nThe sad and simple ceremony was soon over, their progress to the church\r\nbeing almost at a trot, the bustling undertaker having a more important\r\nfuneral an hour later, three miles off. Drusilla was put into the new\r\nground, quite away from her ancestors. Sue and Jude had gone side by\r\nside to the grave, and now sat down to tea in the familiar house; their\r\nlives united at least in this last attention to the dead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was opposed to marriage, from first to last, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nSue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Particularly for members of our family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer eyes met his, and remained on him awhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are rather a sad family, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said we made bad husbands and wives. Certainly we make unhappy\r\nones. At all events, I do, for one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue was silent. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it wrong, Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a tentative tremor,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfor a husband or wife to tell a third person that they are unhappy in\r\ntheir marriage? If a marriage ceremony is a religious thing, it is\r\npossibly wrong; but if it is only a sordid contract, based on material\r\nconvenience in householding, rating, and taxing, and the inheritance of\r\nland and money by children, making it necessary that the male parent\r\nshould be known\xe2\x80\x94which it seems to be\xe2\x80\x94why surely a person may say, even\r\nproclaim upon the housetops, that it hurts and grieves him or her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have said so, anyhow, to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently she went on: \xe2\x80\x9cAre there many couples, do you think, where one\r\ndislikes the other for no definite fault?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose. If either cares for another person, for instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut even apart from that? Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t the woman, for example, be very\r\nbad-natured if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to live with her husband; merely\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94her\r\nvoice undulated, and he guessed things\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmerely because she had a\r\npersonal feeling against it\xe2\x80\x94a physical objection\xe2\x80\x94a fastidiousness, or\r\nwhatever it may be called\xe2\x80\x94although she might respect and be grateful to\r\nhim? But that\r\nis much like saying that the amputation of a limb is no affliction,\r\nsince a person gets comfortably accustomed to the use of a wooden leg\r\nor arm in the course of time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude could hardly speak, but he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought there was something\r\nwrong, Sue! Oh, I thought there was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is not as you think!\xe2\x80\x94there is nothing wrong except my own\r\nwickedness, I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99d call it\xe2\x80\x94a repugnance on my part, for a\r\nreason I cannot disclose, and what would not be admitted as one by the\r\nworld in general! \xe2\x80\xa6 What tortures me so much is the necessity of being\r\nresponsive to this man whenever he wishes, good as he is morally!\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndreadful contract to feel in a particular way in a matter whose essence\r\nis its voluntariness! \xe2\x80\xa6 I wish he would beat me, or be faithless to me,\r\nor do some open thing that I could talk about as a justification for\r\nfeeling as I do! That\xe2\x80\x99s why he didn\xe2\x80\x99t come to\r\nthe funeral\xe2\x80\xa6 Oh, I am very miserable\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do! \xe2\x80\xa6 Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome near me, Jude, because you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he had jumped up and put his face against hers\xe2\x80\x94or rather against\r\nher ear, her face being inaccessible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you not to, Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you did\xe2\x80\x94I only wish to\xe2\x80\x94console you! Jude did not follow her. Twenty minutes later he saw her\r\ncross the village green towards Mrs. Edlin\xe2\x80\x99s, and soon she sent a\r\nlittle girl to fetch her bag, and tell him she was too tired to see him\r\nagain that night.\r\n\r\nIn the lonely room of his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s house, Jude sat watching the cottage\r\nof the Widow Edlin as it disappeared behind the night shade. He knew\r\nthat Sue was sitting within its walls equally lonely and disheartened;\r\nand again questioned his devotional motto that all was for the best.\r\n\r\nHe retired to rest early, but his sleep was fitful from the sense that\r\nSue was so near at hand. At some time near two o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when he was\r\nbeginning to sleep more soundly, he was aroused by a shrill squeak that\r\nhad been familiar enough to him when he lived regularly at Marygreen.\r\nIt was the cry of a rabbit caught in a gin. As was the little\r\ncreature\xe2\x80\x99s habit, it did not soon repeat its cry; and probably would\r\nnot do so more than once or twice; but would remain bearing its torture\r\ntill the morrow when the trapper would come and knock it on the head.\r\n\r\nHe who in his childhood had saved the lives of the earthworms now began\r\nto picture the agonies of the rabbit from its lacerated leg. If it were\r\na \xe2\x80\x9cbad catch\xe2\x80\x9d by the hind-leg, the animal would tug during the ensuing\r\nsix hours till the iron teeth of the trap had stripped the leg-bone of\r\nits flesh, when, should a weak-springed instrument enable it to escape,\r\nit would die in the fields from the mortification of the limb. If it\r\nwere a \xe2\x80\x9cgood catch,\xe2\x80\x9d namely, by the fore-leg, the bone would be broken\r\nand the limb nearly torn in two in attempts at an impossible escape.\r\n\r\nAlmost half an hour passed, and the rabbit repeated its cry. Jude could\r\nrest no longer till he had put it out of its pain, so dressing himself\r\nquickly he descended, and by the light of the moon went across the\r\ngreen in the direction of the sound. He reached the hedge bordering the\r\nwidow\xe2\x80\x99s garden, when he stood still. The faint click of the trap as\r\ndragged about by the writhing animal guided him now, and reaching the\r\nspot he struck the rabbit on the back of the neck with the side of his\r\npalm, and it stretched itself out dead.\r\n\r\nHe was turning away when he saw a woman looking out of the open\r\ncasement at a window on the ground floor of the adjacent cottage.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude!\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice timidly\xe2\x80\x94Sue\xe2\x80\x99s voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is you\xe2\x80\x94is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t been able to sleep at all, and then I heard the rabbit, and\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help thinking of what it suffered, till I felt I must come\r\ndown and kill it! But I am so glad you got there first\xe2\x80\xa6 They ought not\r\nto be allowed to set these steel traps, ought they!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude had reached the window, which was quite a low one, so that she was\r\nvisible down to her waist.
