I wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nnow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t wish it, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat may have _been_ my view; but my\r\ndoctrines and I begin to part company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it\xe2\x80\x94I knew it! And that\xe2\x80\x99s why I vowed I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t disturb your\r\nbelief. But\xe2\x80\x94I am _so glad_ to see you!\xe2\x80\x94and, oh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to see\r\nyou again, now the last tie between us, Aunt Drusilla, is dead!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude seized her hand and kissed it. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a stronger one left!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never care about my doctrines or my religion any more! Let\r\nthem go! Let me help you, even if I do love you, and even if you\xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say it!\xe2\x80\x94I know what you mean; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t admit so much as that.\r\nThere! Guess what you like, but don\xe2\x80\x99t press me to answer questions!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you were happy, whatever I may be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ be! So few could enter into my feeling\xe2\x80\x94they would say \xe2\x80\x99twas\r\nmy fanciful fastidiousness, or something of that sort, and condemn me\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nIt is none of the natural tragedies of love that\xe2\x80\x99s love\xe2\x80\x99s usual tragedy\r\nin civilized life, but a tragedy artificially manufactured for people\r\nwho in a natural state would find relief in parting! \xe2\x80\xa6 It would have\r\nbeen wrong, perhaps, for me to tell my distress to you, if I had been\r\nable to tell it to anybody else. But I have nobody. And I _must_ tell\r\nsomebody! Jude, before I married him I had never thought out fully what\r\nmarriage meant, even though I knew. It was idiotic of me\xe2\x80\x94there is no\r\nexcuse. I was old enough, and I thought I was very experienced. So I\r\nrushed on, when I had got into that training school scrape, with all\r\nthe cock-sureness of the fool that I was! \xe2\x80\xa6 I am certain one ought to\r\nbe allowed to undo what one had done so ignorantly! I daresay it\r\nhappens to lots of women, only they submit, and I kick\xe2\x80\xa6 When people of\r\na later age look back upon the barbarous customs and superstitions of\r\nthe times that we have the unhappiness to live in, what _will_ they\r\nsay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very bitter, darling Sue! How I wish\xe2\x80\x94I wish\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must go in now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a moment of impulse she bent over the sill, and laid her face upon\r\nhis hair, weeping, and then imprinting a scarcely perceptible little\r\nkiss upon the top of his head, withdrawing quickly, so that he could\r\nnot put his arms round her, as otherwise he unquestionably would have\r\ndone. She shut the casement, and he returned to his cottage.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s distressful confession recurred to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s mind all the night as\r\nbeing a sorrow indeed.\r\n\r\nThe morning after, when it was time for her to go, the neighbours saw\r\nher companion and herself disappearing on foot down the hill path which\r\nled into the lonely road to Alfredston. An hour passed before he\r\nreturned along the same route, and in his face there was a look of\r\nexaltation not unmixed with recklessness. An incident had occurred.\r\n\r\nThey had stood parting in the silent highway, and their tense and\r\npassionate moods had led to bewildered inquiries of each other on how\r\nfar their intimacy ought to go; till they had almost quarrelled, and\r\nshe said tearfully that it was hardly proper of him as a parson in\r\nembryo to think of such a thing as kissing her even in farewell as he\r\nnow wished to do. Then she had conceded that the fact of the kiss would\r\nbe nothing: all would depend upon the spirit of it. If given in the\r\nspirit of a cousin and a friend she saw no objection: if in the spirit\r\nof a lover she could not permit it. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you swear that it will not be\r\nin that spirit?\xe2\x80\x9d she had said.\r\n\r\nNo: he would not. And then they had turned from each other in\r\nestrangement, and gone their several ways, till at a distance of twenty\r\nor thirty yards both had looked round simultaneously. That look behind\r\nwas fatal to the reserve hitherto more or less maintained. They had\r\nquickly run back, and met, and embracing most unpremeditatedly, kissed\r\nclose and long. When they parted for good it was with flushed cheeks on\r\nher side, and a beating heart on his.\r\n\r\nThe kiss was a turning-point in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s career. Back again in the\r\ncottage, and left to reflection, he saw one thing: that though his kiss\r\nof that aerial being had seemed the purest moment of his faultful life,\r\nas long as he nourished this unlicensed tenderness it was glaringly\r\ninconsistent for him to pursue the idea of becoming the soldier and\r\nservant of a religion in which sexual love was regarded as at its best\r\na frailty, and at its worst damnation. What Sue had said in warmth was\r\nreally the cold truth. When to defend his affection tooth and nail, to\r\npersist with headlong force in impassioned attentions to her, was all\r\nhe thought of, he was condemned _ipso facto_ as a professor of the\r\naccepted school of morals. He was as unfit, obviously, by nature, as he\r\nhad been by social position, to fill the part of a propounder of\r\naccredited dogma.\r\n\r\nStrange that his first aspiration\xe2\x80\x94towards academical proficiency\xe2\x80\x94had\r\nbeen checked by a woman, and that his second aspiration\xe2\x80\x94towards\r\napostleship\xe2\x80\x94had also been checked by a woman. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthe women are to blame; or is it the artificial system of things, under\r\nwhich the normal sex-impulses are turned into devilish domestic gins\r\nand springs to noose and hold back those who want to progress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt had been his standing desire to become a prophet, however humble, to\r\nhis struggling fellow-creatures, without any thought of personal gain.\r\nYet with a wife living away from him with another husband, and himself\r\nin love erratically, the loved one\xe2\x80\x99s revolt against her state being\r\npossibly on his account, he had sunk to be barely respectable according\r\nto regulation views.\r\n\r\nIt was not for him to consider further: he had only to confront the\r\nobvious, which was that he had made himself quite an impostor as a\r\nlaw-abiding religious teacher.\r\n\r\nAt dusk that evening he went into the garden and dug a shallow hole, to\r\nwhich he brought out all the theological and ethical works that he\r\npossessed, and had stored here. He knew that, in this country of true\r\nbelievers, most of them were not saleable at a much higher price than\r\nwaste-paper value, and preferred to get rid of them in his own way,\r\neven if he should sacrifice a little money to the sentiment of thus\r\ndestroying them. Lighting some loose pamphlets to begin with, he cut\r\nthe volumes into pieces as well as he could, and with a three-pronged\r\nfork shook them over the flames. They kindled, and lighted up the back\r\nof the house, the pigsty, and his own face, till they were more or less\r\nconsumed.\r\n\r\nThough he was almost a stranger here now, passing cottagers talked to\r\nhim over the garden hedge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBurning up your awld aunt\xe2\x80\x99s rubbidge, I suppose? Ay; a lot gets heaped\r\nup in nooks and corners when you\xe2\x80\x99ve lived eighty years in one house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was nearly one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning before the leaves, covers, and\r\nbinding of Jeremy Taylor, Butler, Doddridge, Paley, Pusey, Newman and\r\nthe rest had gone to ashes, but the night was quiet, and as he turned\r\nand turned the paper shreds with the fork, the sense of being no longer\r\na hypocrite to himself afforded his mind a relief which gave him calm.\r\nHe might go on believing as before, but he professed nothing, and no\r\nlonger owned and exhibited engines of faith which, as their proprietor,\r\nhe might naturally be supposed to exercise on himself first of all. In\r\nhis passion for Sue he could not stand as an ordinary sinner, and not\r\nas a whited sepulchre.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Sue, after parting from him earlier in the day, had gone\r\nalong to the station, with tears in her eyes for having run back and\r\nlet him kiss her. Jude ought not to have pretended that he was not a\r\nlover, and made her give way to an impulse to act unconventionally, if\r\nnot wrongly. She was inclined to call it the latter; for Sue\xe2\x80\x99s logic\r\nwas extraordinarily compounded, and seemed to maintain that before a\r\nthing was done it might be right to do, but that being done it became\r\nwrong; or, in other words, that things which were right in theory were\r\nwrong in practice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been too weak, I think!\xe2\x80\x9d she jerked out as she pranced on,\r\nshaking down tear-drops now and then. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was burning, like a\r\nlover\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94oh, it was! And I won\xe2\x80\x99t write to him any more, or at least for\r\na long time, to impress him with my dignity! And I hope it will hurt\r\nhim very much\xe2\x80\x94expecting a letter to-morrow morning, and the next, and\r\nthe next, and no letter coming. He\xe2\x80\x99ll suffer then with suspense\xe2\x80\x94won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhe, that\xe2\x80\x99s all!\xe2\x80\x94and I am very glad of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Tears of pity for Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\napproaching sufferings at her hands mingled with those which had surged\r\nup in pity for herself.\r\n\r\nThen the slim little wife of a husband whose person was disagreeable to\r\nher, the ethereal, fine-nerved, sensitive girl, quite unfitted by\r\ntemperament and instinct to fulfil the conditions of the matrimonial\r\nrelation with Phillotson, possibly with scarce any man, walked fitfully\r\nalong, and panted, and brought weariness into her eyes by gazing and\r\nworrying hopelessly.\r\n\r\nPhillotson met her at the arrival station, and, seeing that she was\r\ntroubled, thought it must be owing to the depressing effect of her\r\naunt\xe2\x80\x99s death and funeral. He began telling her of his day\xe2\x80\x99s doings, and\r\nhow his friend Gillingham, a neighbouring schoolmaster whom he had not\r\nseen for years, had called upon him. While ascending to the town,\r\nseated on the top of the omnibus beside him, she said suddenly and with\r\nan air of self-chastisement, regarding the white road and its bordering\r\nbushes of hazel:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichard\xe2\x80\x94I let Mr. Fawley hold my hand a long while. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhether you think it wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe, waking apparently from thoughts of far different mould, said\r\nvaguely, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, did you? What did you do that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. He wanted to, and I let him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it pleased him. I should think it was hardly a novelty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey lapsed into silence. Had this been a case in the court of an\r\nomniscient judge, he might have entered on his notes the curious fact\r\nthat Sue had placed the minor for the major indiscretion, and had not\r\nsaid a word about the kiss.\r\n\r\nAfter tea that evening Phillotson sat balancing the school registers.\r\nShe remained in an unusually silent, tense, and restless condition, and\r\nat last, saying she was tired, went to bed early. When Phillotson\r\narrived upstairs, weary with the drudgery of the attendance-numbers, it\r\nwas a quarter to twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Entering their chamber, which by day\r\ncommanded a view of some thirty or forty miles over the Vale of\r\nBlackmoor, and even into Outer Wessex, he went to the window, and,\r\npressing his face against the pane, gazed with hard-breathing fixity\r\ninto the mysterious darkness which now covered the far-reaching scene.\r\nHe was musing, \xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, without turning his head,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat I must get the committee to change the school-stationer. All the\r\ncopybooks are sent wrong this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no reply. Thinking Sue was dozing he went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there must be a rearrangement of that ventilator in the\r\nclass-room. The wind blows down upon my head unmercifully and gives me\r\nthe ear-ache.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the silence seemed more absolute than ordinarily he turned round.\r\nThe heavy, gloomy oak wainscot, which extended over the walls upstairs\r\nand down in the dilapidated \xe2\x80\x9cOld-Grove Place,\xe2\x80\x9d and the massive\r\nchimney-piece reaching to the ceiling, stood in odd contrast to the new\r\nand shining brass bedstead, and the new suite of birch furniture that\r\nhe had bought for her, the two styles seeming to nod to each other\r\nacross three centuries upon the shaking floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoo!\xe2\x80\x9d he said (this being the way in which he pronounced her name).\r\n\r\nShe was not in the bed, though she had apparently been there\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nclothes on her side being flung back. Thinking she might have forgotten\r\nsome kitchen detail and gone downstairs for a moment to see to it, he\r\npulled off his coat and idled quietly enough for a few minutes, when,\r\nfinding she did not come, he went out upon the landing, candle in hand,\r\nand said again \xe2\x80\x9cSoo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d came back to him in her voice, from the distant kitchen quarter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing down there at midnight\xe2\x80\x94tiring yourself out for\r\nnothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sleepy; I am reading; and there is a larger fire here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went to bed. Some time in the night he awoke. She was not there,\r\neven now. Lighting a candle he hastily stepped out upon the landing,\r\nand again called her name.\r\n\r\nShe answered \xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d as before, but the tones were small and confined,\r\nand whence they came he could not at first understand. Under the\r\nstaircase was a large clothes-closet, without a window; they seemed to\r\ncome from it. The door was shut, but there was no lock or other\r\nfastening. Phillotson, alarmed, went towards it, wondering if she had\r\nsuddenly become deranged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing in there?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to disturb you I came here, as it was so late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s no bed, is there? And no ventilation! Why, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nsuffocated if you stay all night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I think not. Don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble about me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Phillotson seized the knob and pulled at the door. She had\r\nfastened it inside with a piece of string, which broke at his pull.\r\nThere being no bedstead she had flung down some rugs and made a little\r\nnest for herself in the very cramped quarters the closet afforded.\r\n\r\nWhen he looked in upon her she sprang out of her lair, great-eyed and\r\ntrembling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought not to have pulled open the door!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried excitedly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nis not becoming in you! Oh, will you go away; please will you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked so pitiful and pleading in her white nightgown against the\r\nshadowy lumber-hole that he was quite worried. She continued to beseech\r\nhim not to disturb her.\r\n\r\nHe said: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been kind to you, and given you every liberty; and it is\r\nmonstrous that you should feel in this way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, weeping. \xe2\x80\x9cI know that! It is wrong and wicked of me, I\r\nsuppose! I am very sorry. But it is not I altogether that am to blame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is then? Am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know! The universe, I suppose\xe2\x80\x94things in general, because\r\nthey are so horrid and cruel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is no use talking like that. Making a man\xe2\x80\x99s house so unseemly\r\nat this time o\xe2\x80\x99 night! Eliza will hear if we don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d (He meant the\r\nservant.) \xe2\x80\x9cJust think if either of the parsons in this town was to see\r\nus now! I hate such eccentricities, Sue. There\xe2\x80\x99s no order or regularity\r\nin your sentiments! \xe2\x80\xa6 But I won\xe2\x80\x99t intrude on you further; only I would\r\nadvise you not to shut the door too tight, or I shall find you stifled\r\nto-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn rising the next morning he immediately looked into the closet, but\r\nSue had already gone downstairs. There was a little nest where she had\r\nlain, and spiders\xe2\x80\x99 webs hung overhead. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat must a woman\xe2\x80\x99s aversion be\r\nwhen it is stronger than her fear of spiders!\xe2\x80\x9d he said bitterly.\r\n\r\nHe found her sitting at the breakfast-table, and the meal began almost\r\nin silence, the burghers walking past upon the pavement\xe2\x80\x94or rather\r\nroadway, pavements being scarce here\xe2\x80\x94which was two or three feet above\r\nthe level of the parlour floor. They nodded down to the happy couple\r\ntheir morning greetings, as they went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichard,\xe2\x80\x9d she said all at once; \xe2\x80\x9cwould you mind my living away from\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAway from me? Why, that\xe2\x80\x99s what you were doing when I married you. What\r\nthen was the meaning of marrying at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like me any the better for telling you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t object to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I thought I could do nothing else. You had got my promise a\r\nlong time before that, remember. Then, as time went on, I regretted I\r\nhad promised you, and was trying to see an honourable way to break it\r\noff. But as I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I became rather reckless and careless about the\r\nconventions. Then you know what scandals were spread, and how I was\r\nturned out of the training school you had taken such time and trouble\r\nto prepare me for and get me into; and this frightened me and it seemed\r\nthen that the one thing I could do would be to let the engagement\r\nstand. Of course I, of all people, ought not to have cared what was\r\nsaid, for it was just what I fancied I never did care for. But I was a\r\ncoward\xe2\x80\x94as so many women are\xe2\x80\x94and my theoretic unconventionality broke\r\ndown. If that had not entered into the case it would have been better\r\nto have hurt your feelings once for all then, than to marry you and\r\nhurt them all my life after\xe2\x80\xa6 And you were so generous in never giving\r\ncredit for a moment to the rumour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am bound in honesty to tell you that I weighed its probability and\r\ninquired of your cousin about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with pained surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t doubt you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you inquired!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took his word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer eyes had filled. \xe2\x80\x9c_He_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have inquired!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t answered me. Will you let me go away? I know how irregular it\r\nis of me to ask it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is irregular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do ask it! Domestic laws should be made according to\r\ntemperaments, which should be classified. If people are at all peculiar\r\nin character they have to suffer from the very rules that produce\r\ncomfort in others! \xe2\x80\xa6 Will you let me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we married\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the use of thinking of laws and ordinances,\xe2\x80\x9d she burst out,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cif they make you miserable when you know you are committing no sin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are committing a sin in not liking me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _do_ like you! But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t reflect it would be\xe2\x80\x94that it would be so\r\nmuch more than that\xe2\x80\xa6 For a man and woman to live on intimate terms when\r\none feels as I do is adultery, in any circumstances, however legal.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve said it! \xe2\x80\xa6 Will you let me, Richard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou distress me, Susanna, by such importunity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy can\xe2\x80\x99t we agree to free each other? We made the compact, and surely\r\nwe can cancel it\xe2\x80\x94not legally of course; but we can morally, especially\r\nas no new interests, in the shape of children, have arisen to be looked\r\nafter. Then we might be friends, and meet without pain to either. Oh\r\nRichard, be my friend and have pity! We shall both be dead in a few\r\nyears, and then what will it matter to anybody that you relieved me\r\nfrom constraint for a little while? I daresay you think me eccentric,\r\nor super-sensitive, or something absurd. Well\xe2\x80\x94why should I suffer for\r\nwhat I was born to be, if it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt other people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it does\xe2\x80\x94it hurts _me_! And you vowed to love me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s it! I am in the wrong. I always am! It is as culpable to\r\nbind yourself to love always as to believe a creed always, and as silly\r\nas to vow always to like a particular food or drink!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you mean, by living away from me, living by yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you insisted, yes. But I meant living with Jude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs his wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson writhed.\r\n\r\nSue continued: \xe2\x80\x9cShe, or he, \xe2\x80\x98who lets the world, or his own portion of\r\nit, choose his plan of life for him, has no need of any other faculty\r\nthan the apelike one of imitation.\xe2\x80\x99 J. S. Mill\xe2\x80\x99s words, those are. I\r\nhave been reading it up. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t you act upon them? I wish to,\r\nalways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I care about J. S. Mill!\xe2\x80\x9d moaned he. \xe2\x80\x9cI only want to lead a\r\nquiet life! Do you mind my saying that I have guessed what never once\r\noccurred to me before our marriage\xe2\x80\x94that you were in love, and are in\r\nlove, with Jude Fawley!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may go on guessing that I am, since you have begun. But do you\r\nsuppose that if I had been I should have asked you to let me go and\r\nlive with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ringing of the school bell saved Phillotson from the necessity of\r\nreplying at present to what apparently did not strike him as being such\r\na convincing _argumentum ad verecundiam_ as she, in her loss of courage\r\nat the last moment, meant it to appear. She was beginning to be so\r\npuzzling and unstateable that he was ready to throw in with her other\r\nlittle peculiarities the extremest request which a wife could make.\r\n\r\nThey proceeded to the schools that morning as usual, Sue entering the\r\nclass-room, where he could see the back of her head through the glass\r\npartition whenever he turned his eyes that way. As he went on giving\r\nand hearing lessons his forehead and eyebrows twitched from\r\nconcentrated agitation of thought, till at length he tore a scrap from\r\na sheet of scribbling paper and wrote:\r\n\r\nYour request prevents my attending to work at all. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I\r\nam doing! Was it seriously made?\r\n\r\nHe folded the piece of paper very small, and gave it to a little boy to\r\ntake to Sue. The child toddled off into the class-room. Phillotson saw\r\nhis wife turn and take the note, and the bend of her pretty head as she\r\nread it, her lips slightly crisped, to prevent undue expression under\r\nfire of so many young eyes. He could not see her hands, but she changed\r\nher position, and soon the child returned, bringing nothing in reply.\r\nIn a few minutes, however, one of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s class appeared, with a little\r\nnote similar to his own. These words only were pencilled therein:\r\n\r\nI am sincerely sorry to say that it was seriously made.\r\n\r\nPhillotson looked more disturbed than before, and the meeting-place of\r\nhis brows twitched again. In ten minutes he called up the child he had\r\njust sent to her, and dispatched another missive:\r\n\r\nGod knows I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to thwart you in any reasonable way. My whole\r\nthought is to make you comfortable and happy. But I cannot agree to\r\nsuch a preposterous notion as your going to live with your lover. You\r\nwould lose everybody\xe2\x80\x99s respect and regard; and so should I!\r\n\r\nAfter an interval a similar part was enacted in the class-room, and an\r\nanswer came:\r\n\r\nI know you mean my good. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be respectable! To produce\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuman development in its richest diversity\xe2\x80\x9d (to quote your Humboldt)\r\nis to my mind far above respectability. No doubt my tastes are low\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nyour view\xe2\x80\x94hopelessly low! If you won\xe2\x80\x99t let me go to him, will you grant\r\nme this one request\xe2\x80\x94allow me to live in your house in a separate way?\r\n\r\nTo this he returned no answer.\r\n\r\nShe wrote again:\r\n\r\nI know what you think. But cannot you have pity on me? I beg you to; I\r\nimplore you to be merciful! I would not ask if I were not almost\r\ncompelled by what I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear! No poor woman has ever wished more than\r\nI that Eve had not fallen, so that (as the primitive Christians\r\nbelieved) some harmless mode of vegetation might have peopled Paradise.\r\nBut I won\xe2\x80\x99t trifle! Be kind to me\xe2\x80\x94even though I have not been kind to\r\nyou! I will go away, go abroad, anywhere, and never trouble you.\r\n\r\nNearly an hour passed, and then he returned an answer:\r\n\r\nI do not wish to pain you. How well you _know_ I don\xe2\x80\x99t! Give me a\r\nlittle time. I am disposed to agree to your last request.\r\n\r\nOne line from her:\r\n\r\nThank you from my heart, Richard. I do not deserve your kindness.\r\n\r\nAll day Phillotson bent a dazed regard upon her through the glazed\r\npartition; and he felt as lonely as when he had not known her.\r\n\r\nBut he was as good as his word, and consented to her living apart in\r\nthe house. At first, when they met at meals, she had seemed more\r\ncomposed under the new arrangement; but the irksomeness of their\r\nposition worked on her temperament, and the fibres of her nature seemed\r\nstrained like harp-strings. She talked vaguely and indiscriminately to\r\nprevent his talking pertinently.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\n\r\nPhillotson was sitting up late, as was often his custom, trying to get\r\ntogether the materials for his long-neglected hobby of Roman\r\nantiquities. For the first time since reviving the subject he felt a\r\nreturn of his old interest in it. He forgot time and place, and when he\r\nremembered himself and ascended to rest it was nearly two o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nHis preoccupation was such that, though he now slept on the other side\r\nof the house, he mechanically went to the room that he and his wife had\r\noccupied when he first became a tenant of Old-Grove Place, which since\r\nhis differences with Sue had been hers exclusively. He entered, and\r\nunconsciously began to undress.\r\n\r\nThere was a cry from the bed, and a quick movement. Before the\r\nschoolmaster had realized where he was he perceived Sue starting up\r\nhalf-awake, staring wildly, and springing out upon the floor on the\r\nside away from him, which was towards the window. This was somewhat\r\nhidden by the canopy of the bedstead, and in a moment he heard her\r\nflinging up the sash. Before he had thought that she meant to do more\r\nthan get air she had mounted upon the sill and leapt out. She\r\ndisappeared in the darkness, and he heard her fall below.\r\n\r\nPhillotson, horrified, ran downstairs, striking himself sharply against\r\nthe newel in his haste. Opening the heavy door he ascended the two or\r\nthree steps to the level of the ground, and there on the gravel before\r\nhim lay a white heap. Phillotson seized it in his arms, and bringing\r\nSue into the hall seated her on a chair, where he gazed at her by the\r\nflapping light of the candle which he had set down in the draught on\r\nthe bottom stair.\r\n\r\nShe had certainly not broken her neck. She looked at him with eyes that\r\nseemed not to take him in; and though not particularly large in general\r\nthey appeared so now. She pressed her side and rubbed her arm, as if\r\nconscious of pain; then stood up, averting her face, in evident\r\ndistress at his gaze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God\xe2\x80\x94you are not killed! Though it\xe2\x80\x99s not for want of trying\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nmuch hurt I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer fall, in fact, had not been a serious one, probably owing to the\r\nlowness of the old rooms and to the high level of the ground without.\r\nBeyond a scraped elbow and a blow in the side she had apparently\r\nincurred little harm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was asleep, I think!\xe2\x80\x9d she began, her pale face still turned away\r\nfrom him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd something frightened me\xe2\x80\x94a terrible dream\xe2\x80\x94I thought I saw\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The actual circumstances seemed to come back to her, and she was\r\nsilent.\r\n\r\nHer cloak was hanging at the back of the door, and the wretched\r\nPhillotson flung it round her. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I help you upstairs?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\ndrearily; for the significance of all this sickened him of himself and\r\nof everything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo thank you, Richard. I am very little hurt. I can walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to lock your door,\xe2\x80\x9d he mechanically said, as if lecturing in\r\nschool. \xe2\x80\x9cThen no one could intrude even by accident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have tried\xe2\x80\x94it won\xe2\x80\x99t lock. All the doors are out of order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe aspect of things was not improved by her admission. She ascended\r\nthe staircase slowly, the waving light of the candle shining on her.\r\nPhillotson did not approach her, or attempt to ascend himself till he\r\nheard her enter her room. Then he fastened up the front door, and\r\nreturning, sat down on the lower stairs, holding the newel with one\r\nhand, and bowing his face into the other. Thus he remained for a long\r\nlong time\xe2\x80\x94a pitiable object enough to one who had seen him; till,\r\nraising his head and sighing a sigh which seemed to say that the\r\nbusiness of his life must be carried on, whether he had a wife or no,\r\nhe took the candle and went upstairs to his lonely room on the other\r\nside of the landing.\r\n\r\nNo further incident touching the matter between them occurred till the\r\nfollowing evening, when, immediately school was over, Phillotson walked\r\nout of Shaston, saying he required no tea, and not informing Sue where\r\nhe was going. He descended from the town level by a steep road in a\r\nnorth-westerly direction, and continued to move downwards till the soil\r\nchanged from its white dryness to a tough brown clay. He was now on the\r\nlow alluvial beds\r\n\r\nWhere Duncliffe is the traveller\xe2\x80\x99s mark,\r\nAnd cloty Stour\xe2\x80\x99s a-rolling dark.\r\n\r\nMore than once he looked back in the increasing obscurity of evening.\r\nAgainst the sky was Shaston, dimly visible\r\n\r\nOn the grey-topp\xe2\x80\x99d height\r\nOf Paladore, as pale day wore\r\nAway\xe2\x80\xa6[1]\r\n\r\n\r\n [1] William Barnes.\r\n\r\nThe new-lit lights from its windows burnt with a steady shine as if\r\nwatching him, one of which windows was his own. Above it he could just\r\ndiscern the pinnacled tower of Trinity Church. The air down here,\r\ntempered by the thick damp bed of tenacious clay, was not as it had\r\nbeen above, but soft and relaxing, so that when he had walked a mile or\r\ntwo he was obliged to wipe his face with his handkerchief.\r\n\r\nLeaving Duncliffe Hill on the left he proceeded without hesitation\r\nthrough the shade, as a man goes on, night or day, in a district over\r\nwhich he has played as a boy. He had walked altogether about four and a\r\nhalf miles\r\n\r\nWhere Stour receives her strength,\r\nFrom six cleere fountains fed,[2]\r\n\r\n\r\n [2] Drayton.\r\n\r\nwhen he crossed a tributary of the Stour, and reached Leddenton\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle town of three or four thousand inhabitants\xe2\x80\x94where he went on to\r\nthe boys\xe2\x80\x99 school, and knocked at the door of the master\xe2\x80\x99s residence.\r\n\r\nA boy pupil-teacher opened it, and to Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s inquiry if Mr.\r\nGillingham was at home, replied that he was, going at once off to his\r\nown house, and leaving Phillotson to find his way in as he could. He\r\ndiscovered his friend putting away some books from which he had been\r\ngiving evening lessons. The light of the paraffin lamp fell on\r\nPhillotson\xe2\x80\x99s face\xe2\x80\x94pale and wretched by contrast with his friend\xe2\x80\x99s, who\r\nhad a cool, practical look. They had been schoolmates in boyhood, and\r\nfellow-students at Wintoncester Training College, many years before\r\nthis time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad to see you, Dick! But you don\xe2\x80\x99t look well! Nothing the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson advanced without replying, and Gillingham closed the\r\ncupboard and pulled up beside his visitor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy you haven\xe2\x80\x99t been here\xe2\x80\x94let me see\xe2\x80\x94since you were married? I called,\r\nyou know, but you were out; and upon my word it is such a climb after\r\ndark that I have been waiting till the days are longer before lumpering\r\nup again. I am glad you didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait, however.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThough well-trained and even proficient masters, they occasionally used\r\na dialect-word of their boyhood to each other in private.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come, George, to explain to you my reasons for taking a step that\r\nI am about to take, so that you, at least, will understand my motives\r\nif other people question them anywhen\xe2\x80\x94as they may, indeed certainly\r\nwill\xe2\x80\xa6 But anything is better than the present condition of things. God\r\nforbid that you should ever have such an experience as mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down. You don\xe2\x80\x99t mean\xe2\x80\x94anything wrong between you and Mrs.\r\nPhillotson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do\xe2\x80\xa6 My wretched state is that I\xe2\x80\x99ve a wife I love who not only does\r\nnot love me, but\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94 Well, I won\xe2\x80\x99t say. I know her feeling! I should\r\nprefer hatred from her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSsh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the sad part of it is that she is not so much to blame as I. She\r\nwas a pupil-teacher under me, as you know, and I took advantage of her\r\ninexperience, and toled her out for walks, and got her to agree to a\r\nlong engagement before she well knew her own mind. Afterwards she saw\r\nsomebody else, but she blindly fulfilled her engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLoving the other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; with a curious tender solicitude seemingly; though her exact\r\nfeeling for him is a riddle to me\xe2\x80\x94and to him too, I think\xe2\x80\x94possibly to\r\nherself. She is one of the oddest creatures I ever met. However, I have\r\nbeen struck with these two facts; the extraordinary sympathy, or\r\nsimilarity, between the pair. He is her cousin, which perhaps accounts\r\nfor some of it. They seem to be one person split in two! And with her\r\nunconquerable aversion to myself as a husband, even though she may like\r\nme as a friend, \xe2\x80\x99tis too much to bear longer. She has conscientiously\r\nstruggled against it, but to no purpose. I cannot bear it\xe2\x80\x94I cannot! I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t answer her arguments\xe2\x80\x94she has read ten times as much as I. Her\r\nintellect sparkles like diamonds, while mine smoulders like brown\r\npaper\xe2\x80\xa6 She\xe2\x80\x99s one too many for me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll get over it, good-now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! It is\xe2\x80\x94but I won\xe2\x80\x99t go into it\xe2\x80\x94there are reasons why she never\r\nwill. At last she calmly and firmly asked if she might leave me and go\r\nto him. The climax came last night, when, owing to my entering her room\r\nby accident, she jumped out of window\xe2\x80\x94so strong was her dread of me!\r\nShe pretended it was a dream, but that was to soothe me. Now when a\r\nwoman jumps out of window without caring whether she breaks her neck or\r\nno, she\xe2\x80\x99s not to be mistaken; and this being the case I have come to a\r\nconclusion: that it is wrong to so torture a fellow-creature any\r\nlonger; and I won\xe2\x80\x99t be the inhuman wretch to do it, cost what it may!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll let her go? And with her lover?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhom with is her matter. I shall let her go; with him certainly, if\r\nshe wishes. I know I may be wrong\xe2\x80\x94I know I can\xe2\x80\x99t logically, or\r\nreligiously, defend my concession to such a wish of hers, or harmonize\r\nit with the doctrines I was brought up in. Only I know one thing:\r\nsomething within me tells me I am doing wrong in refusing her. I, like\r\nother men, profess to hold that if a husband gets such a so-called\r\npreposterous request from his wife, the only course that can possibly\r\nbe regarded as right and proper and honourable in him is to refuse it,\r\nand put her virtuously under lock and key, and murder her lover\r\nperhaps. But is that essentially right, and proper, and honourable, or\r\nis it contemptibly mean and selfish? I don\xe2\x80\x99t profess to decide. I\r\nsimply am going to act by instinct, and let principles take care of\r\nthemselves. If a person who has blindly walked into a quagmire cries\r\nfor help, I am inclined to give it, if possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94you see, there\xe2\x80\x99s the question of neighbours and society\xe2\x80\x94what will\r\nhappen if everybody\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am not going to be a philosopher any longer! I only see what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nunder my eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with your instinct, Dick!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gillingham gravely.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am quite amazed, to tell the truth, that such a sedate, plodding\r\nfellow as you should have entertained such a craze for a moment. You\r\nsaid when I called that she was puzzling and peculiar: I think you\r\nare!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever stood before a woman whom you know to be intrinsically a\r\ngood woman, while she has pleaded for release\xe2\x80\x94been the man she has\r\nknelt to and implored indulgence of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thankful to say I haven\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you are in a position to give an opinion. I have\r\nbeen that man, and it makes all the difference in the world, if one has\r\nany manliness or chivalry in him. I had not the remotest idea\xe2\x80\x94living\r\napart from women as I have done for so many years\xe2\x80\x94that merely taking a\r\nwoman to church and putting a ring upon her finger could by any\r\npossibility involve one in such a daily, continuous tragedy as that now\r\nshared by her and me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I could admit some excuse for letting her leave you, provided\r\nshe kept to herself. But to go attended by a cavalier\xe2\x80\x94that makes a\r\ndifference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit. Suppose, as I believe, she would rather endure her present\r\nmisery than be made to promise to keep apart from him? All that is a\r\nquestion for herself. It is not the same thing at all as the treachery\r\nof living on with a husband and playing him false\xe2\x80\xa6 However, she has not\r\ndistinctly implied living with him as wife, though I think she means\r\nto... And, to the best of my understanding, it is not an ignoble,\r\nmerely animal, feeling between the two: that is the worst of it;\r\nbecause it makes me think their affection will be enduring. I did not\r\nmean to confess to you that in the first jealous weeks of my marriage,\r\nbefore I had come to my right mind, I hid myself in the school one\r\nevening when they were together there, and I heard what they said. I am\r\nashamed of it now, though I suppose I was only exercising a legal\r\nright. I found from their manner that an extraordinary affinity, or\r\nsympathy, entered into their attachment, which somehow took away all\r\nflavour of grossness. Their supreme desire is to be together\xe2\x80\x94to share\r\neach other\xe2\x80\x99s emotions, and fancies, and dreams.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlatonic!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell no. Shelleyan would be nearer to it. They remind me of\xe2\x80\x94what are\r\ntheir names\xe2\x80\x94Laon and Cythna. Also of Paul and Virginia a little. The\r\nmore I reflect, the more _entirely_ I am on their side!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if people did as you want to do, there\xe2\x80\x99d be a general domestic\r\ndisintegration. The family would no longer be the social unit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I am all abroad, I suppose!\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cI was never a\r\nvery bright reasoner, you remember. \xe2\x80\xa6 And yet, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why the\r\nwoman and the children should not be the unit without the man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Lord Harry!\xe2\x80\x94Matriarchy! \xe2\x80\xa6 Does _she_ say all this too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no. She little thinks I have out-Sued Sue in this\xe2\x80\x94all in the last\r\ntwelve hours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will upset all received opinion hereabout. Good God\xe2\x80\x94what will\r\nShaston say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t say that it won\xe2\x80\x99t. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know! \xe2\x80\xa6 As I say, I am\r\nonly a feeler, not a reasoner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gillingham, \xe2\x80\x9clet us take it quietly, and have something to\r\ndrink over it.\xe2\x80\x9d He went under the stairs, and produced a bottle of\r\ncider-wine, of which they drank a rummer each. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you are rafted,\r\nand not yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cDo go back and make up your mind to\r\nput up with a few whims. But keep her. I hear on all sides that she\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ncharming young thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes! That\xe2\x80\x99s the bitterness of it! Well, I won\xe2\x80\x99t stay. I have a long\r\nwalk before me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham accompanied his friend a mile on his way, and at parting\r\nexpressed his hope that this consultation, singular as its subject was,\r\nwould be the renewal of their old comradeship. \xe2\x80\x9cStick to her!\xe2\x80\x9d were his\r\nlast words, flung into the darkness after Phillotson; from which his\r\nfriend answered \xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut when Phillotson was alone under the clouds of night, and no sound\r\nwas audible but that of the purling tributaries of the Stour, he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Gillingham, my friend, you had no stronger arguments against it\r\nthan those!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think she ought to be smacked, and brought to her senses\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nI think!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Gillingham, as he walked back alone.\r\n\r\nThe next morning came, and at breakfast Phillotson told Sue:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may go\xe2\x80\x94with whom you will. I absolutely and unconditionally\r\nagree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving once come to this conclusion it seemed to Phillotson more and\r\nmore indubitably the true one. His mild serenity at the sense that he\r\nwas doing his duty by a woman who was at his mercy almost overpowered\r\nhis grief at relinquishing her.\r\n\r\nSome days passed, and the evening of their last meal together had\r\ncome\xe2\x80\x94a cloudy evening with wind\xe2\x80\x94which indeed was very seldom absent in\r\nthis elevated place. How permanently it was imprinted upon his vision;\r\nthat look of her as she glided into the parlour to tea; a slim flexible\r\nfigure; a face, strained from its roundness, and marked by the pallors\r\nof restless days and nights, suggesting tragic possibilities quite at\r\nvariance with her times of buoyancy; a trying of this morsel and that,\r\nand an inability to eat either. Her nervous manner, begotten of a fear\r\nlest he should be injured by her course, might have been interpreted by\r\na stranger as displeasure that Phillotson intruded his presence on her\r\nfor the few brief minutes that remained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better have a slice of ham or an egg, or something with your\r\ntea? You can\xe2\x80\x99t travel on a mouthful of bread and butter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took the slice he helped her to; and they discussed as they sat\r\ntrivial questions of housekeeping, such as where he would find the key\r\nof this or that cupboard, what little bills were paid, and what not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a bachelor by nature, as you know, Sue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a heroic\r\nattempt to put her at her ease. \xe2\x80\x9cSo that being without a wife will not\r\nreally be irksome to me, as it might be to other men who have had one a\r\nlittle while. I have, too, this grand hobby in my head of writing \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nRoman Antiquities of Wessex,\xe2\x80\x99 which will occupy all my spare hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you will send me some of the manuscript to copy at any time, as you\r\nused to, I will do it with so much pleasure!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with amenable\r\ngentleness. \xe2\x80\x9cI should much like to be some help to you still\xe2\x80\x94as\r\na\xe2\x80\x94f-f-friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson mused, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I think we ought to be really\r\nseparate, if we are to be at all. I wish you\r\nwould take three-quarters of the household furniture. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\nbe bothered with it. It is now a quarter to\r\nsix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\xa6 You don\xe2\x80\x99t seem very sorry I am going, Richard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94perhaps not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like you much for how you have behaved. I won\xe2\x80\x99t be so affected as to say I love you,\r\nbecause you know I don\xe2\x80\x99t, except as a friend. But you do seem that to\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue was for a few moments a little tearful at these reflections, and\r\nthen the station omnibus came round to take her up. Phillotson saw her\r\nthings put on the top, handed her in, and was obliged to make an\r\nappearance of kissing her as he wished her good-bye, which she quite\r\nunderstood and imitated. From the cheerful manner in which they parted\r\nthe omnibus-man had no other idea than that she was going for a short\r\nvisit.\r\n\r\nWhen Phillotson got back into the house he went upstairs and opened the\r\nwindow in the direction the omnibus had taken. Soon the noise of its\r\nwheels died away. He came down then, his face compressed like that of\r\none bearing pain; he put on his hat and went out, following by the same\r\nroute for nearly a mile. Suddenly turning round he came home.\r\n\r\nHe had no sooner entered than the voice of his friend Gillingham\r\ngreeted him from the front room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could make nobody hear; so finding your door open I walked in, and\r\nmade myself comfortable. Whatever it may be she has my full\r\nconsent to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a stability, a ballast, in Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s pronouncement which\r\nrestrained his friend\xe2\x80\x99s comment. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I\xe2\x80\x94leave you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no. Would you help me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham assented; and having gone to the upper rooms the\r\nschoolmaster opened drawers, and began taking out all Sue\xe2\x80\x99s things that\r\nshe had left behind, and laying them in a large box. \xe2\x80\x9cShe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take\r\nall I wanted her to,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cBut when I made up my mind to her\r\ngoing to live in her own way I did make up my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome men would have stopped at an agreement to separate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve gone into all that, and don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to argue it. I was, and am,\r\nthe most old-fashioned man in the world on the question of marriage\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nfact I had never thought critically about its ethics at all. But\r\ncertain facts stared me in the face, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go against them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went on with the packing silently. When it was done Phillotson\r\nclosed the box and turned the key.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTo adorn her in somebody\xe2\x80\x99s eyes; never again in\r\nmine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nFour-and-twenty hours before this time Sue had written the following\r\nnote to Jude:\r\n\r\nIt is as I told you; and I am leaving to-morrow evening. Richard and I\r\nthought it could be done with less obtrusiveness after dark. I feel\r\nrather frightened, and therefore ask you to be sure you are on the\r\nMelchester platform to meet me. I arrive at a little to seven. I know\r\nyou will, of course, dear Jude; but I feel so timid that I can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nbegging you to be punctual. He has been so _very_ kind to me through it\r\nall!\r\n    Now to our meeting!\r\n\r\n\r\nS.\r\n\r\nAs she was carried by the omnibus farther and farther down from the\r\nmountain town\xe2\x80\x94the single passenger that evening\xe2\x80\x94she regarded the\r\nreceding road with a sad face. But no hesitation was apparent therein.\r\n\r\nThe up-train by which she was departing stopped by signal only. To Sue\r\nit seemed strange that such a powerful organization as a railway train\r\nshould be brought to a stand-still on purpose for her\xe2\x80\x94a fugitive from\r\nher lawful home.\r\n\r\nThe twenty minutes\xe2\x80\x99 journey drew towards its close, and Sue began\r\ngathering her things together to alight. At the moment that the train\r\ncame to a stand-still by the Melchester platform a hand was laid on the\r\ndoor and she beheld Jude. He entered the compartment promptly. He had a\r\nblack bag in his hand, and was dressed in the dark suit he wore on\r\nSundays and in the evening after work. Altogether he looked a very\r\nhandsome young fellow, his ardent affection for her burning in his\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d She clasped his hand with both hers, and her tense state\r\ncaused her to simmer over in a little succession of dry sobs. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I am\r\nso glad! I get out here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I get in, dear one! I\xe2\x80\x99ve packed. Aren\xe2\x80\x99t we going to stay here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t possibly, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see. We are known here\xe2\x80\x94I, at any rate,\r\nam well known. Perhaps not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere wasn\xe2\x80\x99t time for me to write and say the place I had decided on.\r\nAldbrickham is a much bigger town\xe2\x80\x94sixty or seventy thousand\r\ninhabitants\xe2\x80\x94and nobody knows anything about us there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have given up your cathedral work here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It was rather sudden\xe2\x80\x94your message coming unexpectedly. Strictly,\r\nI might have been made to finish out the week. But I pleaded urgency\r\nand I was let off. I would have deserted any day at your command, dear\r\nSue. I have deserted more than that for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear I am doing you a lot of harm. Ruining your prospects of the\r\nChurch; ruining your progress in your trade; everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Church is no more to me. Let it lie! _I_ am not to be one of\r\n\r\n    The soldier-saints who, row on row,\r\nBurn upward each to his point of bliss,\r\n\r\nif any such there be! My point of bliss is not upward, but here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh I seem so bad\xe2\x80\x94upsetting men\xe2\x80\x99s courses like this!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, taking\r\nup in her voice the emotion that had begun in his. On\r\nreconsideration she added, \xe2\x80\x9cHe was very resigned to letting me go\xe2\x80\x94too\r\nresigned almost! I never was so near being in love with him as when he\r\nmade such thoughtful arrangements for my being comfortable on my\r\njourney, and offering to provide money. Yet I was not. If I loved him\r\never so little as a wife, I\xe2\x80\x99d go back to him even now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true\xe2\x80\x94oh so terribly true!\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor me neither, I half-fear!\xe2\x80\x9d he said pettishly. \xe2\x80\x9cNor anybody perhaps!\r\nSue, sometimes, when I am vexed with you, I think you are incapable of\r\nreal love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not good and loyal of you!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and drawing away from him\r\nas far as she could, looked severely out into the darkness. She added\r\nin hurt tones, without turning round: \xe2\x80\x9cMy liking for you is not as some\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x99s perhaps. But it is a delight in being with you, of a supremely\r\ndelicate kind, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go further and risk it by\xe2\x80\x94an attempt\r\nto intensify it! And they\r\nsat side by side with joined hands, till she aroused herself at some\r\nthought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly go to that Temperance Inn, after your telegraphing\r\nthat message!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can see well enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; there\xe2\x80\x99ll be some other one open, no doubt. I have sometimes\r\nthought, since your marrying Phillotson because of a stupid scandal,\r\nthat under the affectation of independent views you are as enslaved to\r\nthe social code as any woman I know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot mentally. But sometimes a\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s _love of being loved_ gets the better of her conscience, and\r\nthough she is agonized at the thought of treating a man cruelly, she\r\nencourages him to love her while she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t love him at all. It was about ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock when they arrived at\r\nAldbrickham, the county town of North Wessex. As she would not go to\r\nthe Temperance Hotel because of the form of his telegram, Jude inquired\r\nfor another; and a youth who volunteered to find one wheeled their\r\nluggage to the George farther on, which proved to be the inn at which\r\nJude had stayed with Arabella on that one occasion of their meeting\r\nafter their division for years.\r\n\r\nOwing, however, to their now entering it by another door, and to his\r\npreoccupation, he did not at first recognize the place. How long ago was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout a month or two. A handsome, full-figured woman. They had this\r\nroom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Jude came back and sat down to supper Sue seemed moping and\r\nmiserable. \xe2\x80\x9cJude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him plaintively, at their parting that\r\nnight upon the landing, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not so nice and pleasant as it used to\r\nbe with us! It is more than this earthly wretch called Me\r\ndeserves\xe2\x80\x94you spirit, you disembodied creature, you dear, sweet,\r\ntantalizing phantom\xe2\x80\x94hardly flesh at all; so that when I put my arms\r\nround you I almost expect them to pass through you as through air!\r\nForgive me for being gross, as you call it! These are some of them:\r\n\r\nThere was a Being whom my spirit oft\r\nMet on its visioned wanderings far aloft.\r\n\r\n\r\n*    *    *    *    *    *    *    *    *\r\n\r\n\r\nA seraph of Heaven, too gentle to be human,\r\nVeiling beneath that radiant form of woman\xe2\x80\xa6\r\n\r\nOh it is too flattering, so I won\xe2\x80\x99t go on!
