And you shall kiss me just once there\xe2\x80\x94not very\r\nlong.\xe2\x80\x9d She put the tip of her finger gingerly to her cheek; and he did\r\nas commanded. \xe2\x80\x9cYou do care for me very much, don\xe2\x80\x99t you, in spite of my\r\nnot\xe2\x80\x94you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sweet!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a sigh; and bade her good-night.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nIn returning to his native town of Shaston as schoolmaster Phillotson\r\nhad won the interest and awakened the memories of the inhabitants, who,\r\nthough they did not honour him for his miscellaneous acquirements as he\r\nwould have been honoured elsewhere, retained for him a sincere regard.\r\nWhen, shortly after his arrival, he brought home a pretty\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x94awkwardly pretty for him, if he did not take care, they said\xe2\x80\x94they\r\nwere glad to have her settle among them.\r\n\r\nFor some time after her flight from that home Sue\xe2\x80\x99s absence did not\r\nexcite comment. Her place as monitor in the school was taken by another\r\nyoung woman within a few days of her vacating it, which substitution\r\nalso passed without remark, Sue\xe2\x80\x99s services having been of a provisional\r\nnature only. When, however, a month had passed, and Phillotson casually\r\nadmitted to an acquaintance that he did not know where his wife was\r\nstaying, curiosity began to be aroused; till, jumping to conclusions,\r\npeople ventured to affirm that Sue had played him false and run away\r\nfrom him. The schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s growing languor and listlessness over his\r\nwork gave countenance to the idea.\r\n\r\nThough Phillotson had held his tongue as long as he could, except to\r\nhis friend Gillingham, his honesty and directness would not allow him\r\nto do so when misapprehensions as to Sue\xe2\x80\x99s conduct spread abroad. On a\r\nMonday morning the chairman of the school committee called, and after\r\nattending to the business of the school drew Phillotson aside out of\r\nearshot of the children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll excuse my asking, Phillotson, since everybody is talking of it:\r\nis this true as to your domestic affairs\xe2\x80\x94that your wife\xe2\x80\x99s going away\r\nwas on no visit, but a secret elopement with a lover? If so, I condole\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was no secret about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone to visit friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what has happened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone away under circumstances that usually call for condolence\r\nwith the husband. But I gave my consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe chairman looked as if he had not apprehended the remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I say is quite true,\xe2\x80\x9d Phillotson continued testily. \xe2\x80\x9cShe asked\r\nleave to go away with her lover, and I let her. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I? A\r\nwoman of full age, it was a question of her own conscience\xe2\x80\x94not for me.\r\nI was not her gaoler. I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain any further. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to be\r\nquestioned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children observed that much seriousness marked the faces of the two\r\nmen, and went home and told their parents that something new had\r\nhappened about Mrs. Phillotson. Then Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s little maidservant,\r\nwho was a schoolgirl just out of her standards, said that Mr.\r\nPhillotson had helped in his wife\xe2\x80\x99s packing, had offered her what money\r\nshe required, and had written a friendly letter to her young man,\r\ntelling him to take care of her. The chairman of committee thought the\r\nmatter over, and talked to the other managers of the school, till a\r\nrequest came to Phillotson to meet them privately. The meeting lasted a\r\nlong time, and at the end the school-master came home, looking as usual\r\npale and worn. Gillingham was sitting in his house awaiting him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell; it is as you said,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Phillotson, flinging himself down\r\nwearily in a chair. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have requested me to send in my resignation\r\non account of my scandalous conduct in giving my tortured wife her\r\nliberty\xe2\x80\x94or, as they call it, condoning her adultery. But I shan\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nresign!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t. It is no business of theirs. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t affect me in my\r\npublic capacity at all. They may expel me if they like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you make a fuss it will get into the papers, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll never get\r\nappointed to another school. You see, they have to consider what you\r\ndid as done by a teacher of youth\xe2\x80\x94and its effects as such upon the\r\nmorals of the town; and, to ordinary opinion, your position is\r\nindefensible. You must let me say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this good advice, however, Phillotson would not listen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t go unless I am turned out. And for\r\nthis reason; that by resigning I acknowledge I have acted wrongly by\r\nher; when I am more and more convinced every day that in the sight of\r\nHeaven and by all natural, straightforward humanity, I have acted\r\nrightly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham saw that his rather headstrong friend would not be able to\r\nmaintain such a position as this; but he said nothing further, and in\r\ndue time\xe2\x80\x94indeed, in a quarter of an hour\xe2\x80\x94the formal letter of dismissal\r\narrived, the managers having remained behind to write it after\r\nPhillotson\xe2\x80\x99s withdrawal. The latter replied that he should not accept\r\ndismissal; and called a public meeting, which he attended, although he\r\nlooked so weak and ill that his friend implored him to stay at home.\r\nWhen he stood up to give his reasons for contesting the decision of the\r\nmanagers he advanced them firmly, as he had done to his friend, and\r\ncontended, moreover, that the matter was a domestic theory which did\r\nnot concern them. This they over-ruled, insisting that the private\r\neccentricities of a teacher came quite within their sphere of control,\r\nas it touched the morals of those he taught. Phillotson replied that he\r\ndid not see how an act of natural charity could injure morals.\r\n\r\nAll the respectable inhabitants and well-to-do fellow-natives of the\r\ntown were against Phillotson to a man. But, somewhat to his surprise,\r\nsome dozen or more champions rose up in his defence as from the ground.\r\n\r\nIt has been stated that Shaston was the anchorage of a curious and\r\ninteresting group of itinerants, who frequented the numerous fairs and\r\nmarkets held up and down Wessex during the summer and autumn months.\r\nAlthough Phillotson had never spoken to one of these gentlemen they now\r\nnobly led the forlorn hope in his defence. The body included two cheap\r\nJacks, a shooting-gallery proprietor and the ladies who loaded the\r\nguns, a pair of boxing-masters, a steam-roundabout manager, two\r\ntravelling broom-makers, who called themselves widows, a\r\ngingerbread-stall keeper, a swing-boat owner, and a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctest-your-strength\xe2\x80\x9d man.\r\n\r\nThis generous phalanx of supporters, and a few others of independent\r\njudgment, whose own domestic experiences had been not without\r\nvicissitude, came up and warmly shook hands with Phillotson; after\r\nwhich they expressed their thoughts so strongly to the meeting that\r\nissue was joined, the result being a general scuffle, wherein a black\r\nboard was split, three panes of the school windows were broken, an\r\ninkbottle was spilled over a town-councillor\xe2\x80\x99s shirt front, a\r\nchurchwarden was dealt such a topper with the map of Palestine that his\r\nhead went right through Samaria, and many black eyes and bleeding noses\r\nwere given, one of which, to everybody\xe2\x80\x99s horror, was the venerable\r\nincumbent\xe2\x80\x99s, owing to the zeal of an emancipated chimney-sweep, who\r\ntook the side of Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s party. When Phillotson saw the blood\r\nrunning down the rector\xe2\x80\x99s face he deplored almost in groans the\r\nuntoward and degrading circumstances, regretted that he had not\r\nresigned when called upon, and went home so ill that next morning he\r\ncould not leave his bed.\r\n\r\nThe farcical yet melancholy event was the beginning of a serious\r\nillness for him; and he lay in his lonely bed in the pathetic state of\r\nmind of a middle-aged man who perceives at length that his life,\r\nintellectual and domestic, is tending to failure and gloom. Gillingham\r\ncame to see him in the evenings, and on one occasion mentioned Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care anything about me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should\r\nshe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know you are ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better for both of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are her lover and she living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt Melchester\xe2\x80\x94I suppose; at least he was living there some time ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Gillingham reached home he sat and reflected, and at last wrote an\r\nanonymous line to Sue, on the bare chance of its reaching her, the\r\nletter being enclosed in an envelope addressed to Jude at the diocesan\r\ncapital. Arriving at that place it was forwarded to Marygreen in North\r\nWessex, and thence to Aldbrickham by the only person who knew his\r\npresent address\xe2\x80\x94the widow who had nursed his aunt.\r\n\r\nThree days later, in the evening, when the sun was going down in\r\nsplendour over the lowlands of Blackmoor, and making the Shaston\r\nwindows like tongues of fire to the eyes of the rustics in that vale,\r\nthe sick man fancied that he heard somebody come to the house, and a\r\nfew minutes after there was a tap at the bedroom door. Phillotson did\r\nnot speak; the door was hesitatingly opened, and there entered\xe2\x80\x94Sue.\r\n\r\nShe was in light spring clothing, and her advent seemed ghostly\xe2\x80\x94like\r\nthe flitting in of a moth. He turned his eyes upon her, and flushed;\r\nbut appeared to check his primary impulse to speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no business here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, bending her frightened face to\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I heard you were ill\xe2\x80\x94very ill; and\xe2\x80\x94and as I know that you\r\nrecognize other feelings between man and woman than physical love, I\r\nhave come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not very ill, my dear friend. Only unwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know that; and I am afraid that only a severe illness would\r\nhave justified my coming!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\xa6 yes. And I almost wish you had not come! It is a little too\r\nsoon\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all I mean. Still, let us make the best of it. You haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nheard about the school, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94what about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly that I am going away from here to another place. The managers and\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree, and we are going to part\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue did not for a moment, either now or later, suspect what troubles\r\nhad resulted to him from letting her go; it never once seemed to cross\r\nher mind, and she had received no news whatever from Shaston. They\r\ntalked on slight and ephemeral subjects, and when his tea was brought\r\nup he told the amazed little servant that a cup was to be set for Sue.\r\nThat young person was much more interested in their history than they\r\nsupposed, and as she descended the stairs she lifted her eyes and hands\r\nin grotesque amazement. While they sipped Sue went to the window and\r\nthoughtfully said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is such a beautiful sunset, Richard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are mostly beautiful from here, owing to the rays crossing the\r\nmist of the vale. But I lose them all, as they don\xe2\x80\x99t shine into this\r\ngloomy corner where I lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like to see this particular one? It is like heaven\r\nopened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes! But I can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll help you to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94the bedstead can\xe2\x80\x99t be shifted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut see how I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went to where a swing-glass stood, and taking it in her hands\r\ncarried it to a spot by the window where it could catch the sunshine,\r\nmoving the glass till the beams were reflected into Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94you can see the great red sun now!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am sure it\r\nwill cheer you\xe2\x80\x94I do so hope it will!\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke with a childlike,\r\nrepentant kindness, as if she could not do too much for him.\r\n\r\nPhillotson smiled sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are an odd creature!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured as the\r\nsun glowed in his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cThe idea of your coming to see me after what\r\nhas passed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let us go back upon that!\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have to catch\r\nthe omnibus for the train, as Jude doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know I have come; he was out\r\nwhen I started; so I must return home almost directly. Richard, I am so\r\nvery glad you are better. You don\xe2\x80\x99t hate me, do you? You have been such\r\na kind friend to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to know you think so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson huskily. \xe2\x80\x9cNo. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhate you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt grew dusk quickly in the gloomy room during their intermittent chat,\r\nand when candles were brought and it was time to leave she put her hand\r\nin his or rather allowed it to flit through his; for she was\r\nsignificantly light in touch. She had nearly closed the door when he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cSue!\xe2\x80\x9d He had noticed that, in turning away from him, tears were\r\non her face and a quiver in her lip.\r\n\r\nIt was bad policy to recall her\xe2\x80\x94he knew it while he pursued it. But he\r\ncould not help it. She came back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you wish to make it up, and stay? I\xe2\x80\x99ll forgive\r\nyou and condone everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh you can\xe2\x80\x99t, you can\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t condone it\r\nnow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_He_ is your husband now, in effect, you mean, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may assume it. He is obtaining a divorce from his wife Arabella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis wife! It is altogether news to me that he has a wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a bad marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike mine. He is not doing it so much on his own account as on hers.\r\nShe wrote and told him it would be a kindness to her, since then she\r\ncould marry and live respectably. And Jude has agreed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA wife\xe2\x80\xa6 A kindness to her. Ah, yes; a kindness to her to release her\r\naltogether\xe2\x80\xa6 But I don\xe2\x80\x99t like the sound of it. I can forgive, Sue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! You can\xe2\x80\x99t have me back now I have been so wicked\xe2\x80\x94as to do what\r\nI have done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere had arisen in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s face that incipient fright which showed\r\nitself whenever he changed from friend to husband, and which made her\r\nadopt any line of defence against marital feeling in him. \xe2\x80\x9cI _must_ go\r\nnow. I\xe2\x80\x99ll come again\xe2\x80\x94may I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to go, even now. I ask you to stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you, Richard; but I must. As you are not so ill as I thought,\r\nI _cannot_ stay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s his\xe2\x80\x94his from lips to heel!\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson; but so faintly that\r\nin closing the door she did not hear it. The dread of a reactionary\r\nchange in the schoolmaster\xe2\x80\x99s sentiments, coupled, perhaps, with a faint\r\nshamefacedness at letting even him know what a slipshod lack of\r\nthoroughness, from a man\xe2\x80\x99s point of view, characterized her transferred\r\nallegiance, prevented her telling him of her, thus far, incomplete\r\nrelations with Jude; and Phillotson lay writhing like a man in hell as\r\nhe pictured the prettily dressed, maddening compound of sympathy and\r\naverseness who bore his name, returning impatiently to the home of her\r\nlover.\r\n\r\nGillingham was so interested in Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s affairs, and so seriously\r\nconcerned about him, that he walked up the hill-side to Shaston two or\r\nthree times a week, although, there and back, it was a journey of nine\r\nmiles, which had to be performed between tea and supper, after a hard\r\nday\xe2\x80\x99s work in school. When he called on the next occasion after Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvisit his friend was downstairs, and Gillingham noticed that his\r\nrestless mood had been supplanted by a more fixed and composed one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s been here since you called last,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot Mrs. Phillotson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! You have made it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\xa6 She just came, patted my pillow with her little white hand, played\r\nthe thoughtful nurse for half an hour, and went away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m hanged! A little hussy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, what a tantalizing, capricious little woman! If she were not\r\nyour wife\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not; she\xe2\x80\x99s another man\xe2\x80\x99s except in name and law. And I have\r\nbeen thinking\xe2\x80\x94it was suggested to me by a conversation I had with\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94that, in kindness to her, I ought to dissolve the legal tie\r\naltogether; which, singularly enough, I think I can do, now she has\r\nbeen back, and refused my request to stay after I said I had forgiven\r\nher. I believe that fact would afford me opportunity of doing it,\r\nthough I did not see it at the moment. What\xe2\x80\x99s the use of keeping her\r\nchained on to me if she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to me? I know\xe2\x80\x94I feel absolutely\r\ncertain\xe2\x80\x94that she would welcome my taking such a step as the greatest\r\ncharity to her. For though as a fellow-creature she sympathizes with,\r\nand pities me, and even weeps for me, as a husband she cannot endure\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94she loathes me\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s no use in mincing words\xe2\x80\x94she loathes me, and\r\nmy only manly, and dignified, and merciful course is to complete what I\r\nhave begun\xe2\x80\xa6 And for worldly reasons, too, it will be better for her to\r\nbe independent. I have hopelessly ruined my prospects because of my\r\ndecision as to what was best for us, though she does not know it; I see\r\nonly dire poverty ahead from my feet to the grave; for I can be\r\naccepted as teacher no more. I shall probably have enough to do to make\r\nboth ends meet during the remainder of my life, now my occupation\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngone; and I shall be better able to bear it alone. I may as well tell\r\nyou that what has suggested my letting her go is some news she brought\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94the news that Fawley is doing the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94he had a spouse, too? A queer couple, these lovers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t want your opinion on that. What I was going to say is\r\nthat my liberating her can do her no possible harm, and will open up a\r\nchance of happiness for her which she has never dreamt of hitherto. For\r\nthen they\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to marry, as they ought to have done at first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham did not hurry to reply. \xe2\x80\x9cI may disagree with your motive,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said gently, for he respected views he could not share. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I think\r\nyou are right in your determination\xe2\x80\x94if you can carry it out. I doubt,\r\nhowever, if you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart Fifth AT ALDBRICKHAM AND ELSEWHERE\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cThy aerial part, and all the fiery parts which are mingled in thee,\r\nthough by nature they have an upward tendency, still in obedience to\r\nthe disposition of the universe they are over-powered here in the\r\ncompound mass the body.\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94M. ANTONINUS (Long).\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nHow Gillingham\xe2\x80\x99s doubts were disposed of will most quickly appear by\r\npassing over the series of dreary months and incidents that followed\r\nthe events of the last chapter, and coming on to a Sunday in the\r\nFebruary of the year following.\r\n\r\nSue and Jude were living in Aldbrickham, in precisely the same\r\nrelations that they had established between themselves when she left\r\nShaston to join him the year before. The proceedings in the law-courts\r\nhad reached their consciousness, but as a distant sound and an\r\noccasional missive which they hardly understood.\r\n\r\nThey had met, as usual, to breakfast together in the little house with\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s name on it, that he had taken at fifteen pounds a year, with\r\nthree-pounds-ten extra for rates and taxes, and furnished with his\r\naunt\xe2\x80\x99s ancient and lumbering goods, which had cost him about their full\r\nvalue to bring all the way from Marygreen. Sue kept house, and managed\r\neverything.\r\n\r\nAs he entered the room this morning Sue held up a letter she had just\r\nreceived.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell; and what is it about?\xe2\x80\x9d he said after kissing her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat the decree _nisi_ in the case of Phillotson _versus_ Phillotson\r\nand Fawley, pronounced six months ago, has just been made absolute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, as he sat down.\r\n\r\nThe same concluding incident in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s suit against Arabella had\r\noccurred about a month or two earlier. Both cases had been too\r\ninsignificant to be reported in the papers, further than by name in a\r\nlong list of other undefended cases.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, Sue, at any rate, you can do what you like!\xe2\x80\x9d He looked at\r\nhis sweetheart curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we\xe2\x80\x94you and I\xe2\x80\x94just as free now as if we had never married at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as free\xe2\x80\x94except, I believe, that a clergyman may object personally\r\nto remarry you, and hand the job on to somebody else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I wonder\xe2\x80\x94do you think it is really so with us? I know it is\r\ngenerally. But I have an uncomfortable feeling that my freedom has been\r\nobtained under false pretences!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94if the truth about us had been known, the decree wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nbeen pronounced. It is only, is it, because we have made no defence,\r\nand have led them into a false supposition? Therefore is my freedom\r\nlawful, however proper it may be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94why did you let it be under false pretences? You have only\r\nyourself to blame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said mischievously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t! You ought not to be touchy about that still. You must take\r\nme as I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, darling: so I will. Perhaps you were right. As to your\r\nquestion, we were not obliged to prove anything. That was their\r\nbusiness. Anyhow we are living together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Though not in their sense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing is certain, that however the decree may be brought about, a\r\nmarriage is dissolved when it is dissolved. There is this advantage in\r\nbeing poor obscure people like us\xe2\x80\x94that these things are done for us in\r\na rough and ready fashion. It was the same with me and Arabella. I was\r\nafraid her criminal second marriage would have been discovered, and she\r\npunished; but nobody took any interest in her\xe2\x80\x94nobody inquired, nobody\r\nsuspected it. If we\xe2\x80\x99d been patented nobilities we should have had\r\ninfinite trouble, and days and weeks would have been spent in\r\ninvestigations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy degrees Sue acquired her lover\xe2\x80\x99s cheerfulness at the sense of\r\nfreedom, and proposed that they should take a walk in the fields, even\r\nif they had to put up with a cold dinner on account of it. Jude agreed,\r\nand Sue went up-stairs and prepared to start, putting on a joyful\r\ncoloured gown in observance of her liberty; seeing which Jude put on a\r\nlighter tie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll strut arm and arm,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9clike any other engaged couple.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ve a legal right to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey rambled out of the town, and along a path over the low-lying lands\r\nthat bordered it, though these were frosty now, and the extensive\r\nseed-fields were bare of colour and produce. The pair, however, were so\r\nabsorbed in their own situation that their surroundings were little in\r\ntheir consciousness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dearest, the result of all this is that we can marry after a\r\ndecent interval.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I suppose we can,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, without enthusiasm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd aren\xe2\x80\x99t we going to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to say no, dear Jude; but I feel just the same about it\r\nnow as I have done all along. I have just the same dread lest an iron\r\ncontract should extinguish your tenderness for me, and mine for you, as\r\nit did between our unfortunate parents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, what can we do? I do love you, as you know, Sue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it abundantly. But I think I would much rather go on living\r\nalways as lovers, as we are living now, and only meeting by day. It is\r\nso much sweeter\xe2\x80\x94for the woman at least, and when she is sure of the\r\nman. And henceforward we needn\xe2\x80\x99t be so particular as we have been about\r\nappearances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur experiences of matrimony with others have not been encouraging, I\r\nown,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with some gloom; \xe2\x80\x9ceither owing to our own dissatisfied,\r\nunpractical natures, or by our misfortune. But we two\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould be two dissatisfied ones linked together, which would be twice\r\nas bad as before\xe2\x80\xa6 I think I should begin to be afraid of you, Jude, the\r\nmoment you had contracted to cherish me under a Government stamp, and I\r\nwas licensed to be loved on the premises by you\xe2\x80\x94Ugh, how horrible and\r\nsordid! Although, as you are, free, I trust you more than any other man\r\nin the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t say I should change!\xe2\x80\x9d he expostulated; yet there was\r\nmisgiving in his own voice also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApart from ourselves, and our unhappy peculiarities, it is foreign to\r\na man\xe2\x80\x99s nature to go on loving a person when he is told that he must\r\nand shall be that person\xe2\x80\x99s lover. There would be a much likelier chance\r\nof his doing it if he were told not to love. If the marriage ceremony\r\nconsisted in an oath and signed contract between the parties to cease\r\nloving from that day forward, in consideration of personal possession\r\nbeing given, and to avoid each other\xe2\x80\x99s society as much as possible in\r\npublic, there would be more loving couples than there are now. Fancy\r\nthe secret meetings between the perjuring husband and wife, the denials\r\nof having seen each other, the clambering in at bedroom windows, and\r\nthe hiding in closets! There\xe2\x80\x99d be little cooling then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but admitting this, or something like it, to be true, you are not\r\nthe only one in the world to see it, dear little Sue. People go on\r\nmarrying because they can\xe2\x80\x99t resist natural forces, although many of\r\nthem may know perfectly well that they are possibly buying a month\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npleasure with a life\xe2\x80\x99s discomfort. No doubt my father and mother, and\r\nyour father and mother, saw it, if they at all resembled us in habits\r\nof observation. But then they went and married just the same, because\r\nthey had ordinary passions. But you, Sue, are such a phantasmal,\r\nbodiless creature, one who\xe2\x80\x94if you\xe2\x80\x99ll allow me to say it\xe2\x80\x94has so little\r\nanimal passion in you, that you can act upon reason in the matter, when\r\nwe poor unfortunate wretches of grosser substance can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ve owned that it would probably end in misery\r\nfor us. And I am not so exceptional a woman as you think. Fewer women\r\nlike marriage than you suppose, only they enter into it for the dignity\r\nit is assumed to confer, and the social advantages it gains them\r\nsometimes\xe2\x80\x94a dignity and an advantage that I am quite willing to do\r\nwithout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude fell back upon his old complaint\xe2\x80\x94that, intimate as they were, he\r\nhad never once had from her an honest, candid declaration that she\r\nloved or could love him. \xe2\x80\x9cI really fear sometimes that you cannot,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, with a dubiousness approaching anger. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are so reticent. I\r\nknow that women are taught by other women that they must never admit\r\nthe full truth to a man. But the highest form of affection is based on\r\nfull sincerity on both sides. Not being men, these women don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthat in looking back on those he has had tender relations with, a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart returns closest to her who was the soul of truth in her conduct.\r\nThe better class of man, even if caught by airy affectations of dodging\r\nand parrying, is not retained by them. A Nemesis attends the woman who\r\nplays the game of elusiveness too often, in the utter contempt for her\r\nthat, sooner or later, her old admirers feel; under which they allow\r\nher to go unlamented to her grave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue, who was regarding the distance, had acquired a guilty look; and\r\nshe suddenly replied in a tragic voice: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I like you\r\nto-day so well as I did, Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you? Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well\xe2\x80\x94you are not nice\xe2\x80\x94too sermony. Though I suppose I am so bad\r\nand worthless that I deserve the utmost rigour of lecturing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you are not bad. You are a dear. But as slippery as an eel when I\r\nwant to get a confession from you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I am bad, and obstinate, and all sorts! It is no use your\r\npretending I am not! People who are good don\xe2\x80\x99t want scolding as I do\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nBut now that I have nobody but you, and nobody to defend me, it is very\r\nhard that I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t have my own way in deciding how I\xe2\x80\x99ll live with you,\r\nand whether I\xe2\x80\x99ll be married or no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue, my own comrade and sweetheart, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to force you either\r\nto marry or to do the other thing\xe2\x80\x94of course I don\xe2\x80\x99t! It is too wicked\r\nof you to be so pettish! Now we won\xe2\x80\x99t say any more about it, and go on\r\njust the same as we have done; and during the rest of our walk we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntalk of the meadows only, and the floods, and the prospect of the\r\nfarmers this coming year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter this the subject of marriage was not mentioned by them for\r\nseveral days, though living as they were with only a landing between\r\nthem it was constantly in their minds. Sue was assisting Jude very\r\nmaterially now: he had latterly occupied himself on his own account in\r\nworking and lettering headstones, which he kept in a little yard at the\r\nback of his little house, where in the intervals of domestic duties she\r\nmarked out the letters full size for him, and blacked them in after he\r\nhad cut them. It was a lower class of handicraft than were his former\r\nperformances as a cathedral mason, and his only patrons were the poor\r\npeople who lived in his own neighbourhood, and knew what a cheap man\r\nthis \xe2\x80\x9cJude Fawley: Monumental Mason\xe2\x80\x9d (as he called himself on his front\r\ndoor) was to employ for the simple memorials they required for their\r\ndead. But he seemed more independent than before, and it was the only\r\narrangement under which Sue, who particularly wished to be no burden on\r\nhim, could render any assistance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was an evening at the end of the month, and Jude had just returned\r\nhome from hearing a lecture on ancient history in the public hall not\r\nfar off. When he entered, Sue, who had been keeping indoors during his\r\nabsence, laid out supper for him. Contrary to custom she did not speak.\r\nJude had taken up some illustrated paper, which he perused till,\r\nraising his eyes, he saw that her face was troubled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you depressed, Sue?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nShe paused a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a message for you,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomebody has called?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. A woman.\xe2\x80\x9d Sue\xe2\x80\x99s voice quavered as she spoke, and she suddenly sat\r\ndown from her preparations, laid her hands in her lap, and looked into\r\nthe fire. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I did right or not!\xe2\x80\x9d she continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsaid you were not at home, and when she said she would wait, I said I\r\nthought you might not be able to see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you say that, dear? I suppose she wanted a headstone. Was she\r\nin mourning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. She wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in mourning, and she didn\xe2\x80\x99t want a headstone; and I\r\nthought you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see her.\xe2\x80\x9d Sue looked critically and imploringly at\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut who was she? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t she say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. She wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give her name. But I know who she was\xe2\x80\x94I think I do!\r\nIt was Arabella!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven save us! What should Arabella come for? What made you think it\r\nwas she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I can hardly tell. But I know it was! I feel perfectly certain it\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x94by the light in her eyes as she looked at me. She was a fleshy,\r\ncoarse woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I should not have called Arabella coarse exactly, except in\r\nspeech, though she may be getting so by this time under the duties of\r\nthe public house. She was rather handsome when I knew her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHandsome! But yes!\xe2\x80\x94so she is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I heard a quiver in your little mouth. Well, waiving that, as\r\nshe is nothing to me, and virtuously married to another man, why should\r\nshe come troubling us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure she\xe2\x80\x99s married? Have you definite news of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not definite news. But that was why she asked me to release her.\r\nShe and the man both wanted to lead a proper life, as I understood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Jude\xe2\x80\x94it was, it _was_ Arabella!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sue, covering her eyes with\r\nher hand. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am so miserable! It seems such an ill omen, whatever\r\nshe may have come for. You could not possibly see her, could you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t really think I could. It would be so very painful to talk to\r\nher now\xe2\x80\x94for her as much as for me. However, she\xe2\x80\x99s gone. Did she say she\r\nwould come again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But she went away very reluctantly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue, whom the least thing upset, could not eat any supper, and when\r\nJude had finished his he prepared to go to bed. He had no sooner raked\r\nout the fire, fastened the doors, and got to the top of the stairs than\r\nthere came a knock. Sue instantly emerged from her room, which she had\r\nbut just entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere she is again!\xe2\x80\x9d Sue whispered in appalled accents.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knocked like that last time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey listened, and the knocking came again. No servant was kept in the\r\nhouse, and if the summons were to be responded to one of them would\r\nhave to do it in person. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll open a window,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWhoever it\r\nis cannot be expected to be let in at this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe accordingly went into his bedroom and lifted the sash. The lonely\r\nstreet of early retiring workpeople was empty from end to end save of\r\none figure\xe2\x80\x94that of a woman walking up and down by the lamp a few yards\r\noff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s there?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that Mr. Fawley?\xe2\x80\x9d came up from the woman, in a voice which was\r\nunmistakably Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nJude replied that it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it she?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sue from the door, with lips apart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want, Arabella?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, Jude, for disturbing you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella humbly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I called earlier\xe2\x80\x94I wanted particularly to see you to-night, if I\r\ncould. I am in trouble, and have nobody to help me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn trouble, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a silence. An inconvenient sympathy seemed to be rising in\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s breast at the appeal. \xe2\x80\x9cBut aren\xe2\x80\x99t you married?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nArabella hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, Jude, I am not,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned. \xe2\x80\x9cHe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nafter all. And I am in great difficulty. I hope to get another\r\nsituation as barmaid soon. But it takes time, and I really am in great\r\ndistress because of a sudden responsibility that\xe2\x80\x99s been sprung upon me\r\nfrom Australia; or I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t trouble you\xe2\x80\x94believe me I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. I want\r\nto tell you about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue remained at gaze, in painful tension, hearing every word, but\r\nspeaking none.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not really in want of money, Arabella?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, in a\r\ndistinctly softened tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have enough to pay for the night\xe2\x80\x99s lodging I have obtained, but\r\nbarely enough to take me back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn London still.\xe2\x80\x9d She was about to give the address, but she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nam afraid somebody may hear, so I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to call out particulars of\r\nmyself so loud. If you could come down and walk a little way with me\r\ntowards the Prince Inn, where I am staying to-night, I would explain\r\nall. You may as well, for old time\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor thing! I must do her the kindness of hearing what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, I\r\nsuppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude in much perplexity. \xe2\x80\x9cAs she\xe2\x80\x99s going back to-morrow\r\nit can\xe2\x80\x99t make much difference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you can go and see her to-morrow, Jude! Don\xe2\x80\x99t go now, Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d came\r\nin plaintive accents from the doorway. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is only to entrap you, I\r\nknow it is, as she did before! Don\xe2\x80\x99t go, dear! She is such a\r\nlow-passioned woman\xe2\x80\x94I can see it in her shape, and hear it in her\r\nvoice!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I shall go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to detain me, Sue. God\r\nknows I love her little enough now, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be cruel to\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to the stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she\xe2\x80\x99s not your wife!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sue distractedly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are not either, dear, yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but are you going to her? Don\xe2\x80\x99t! Stay at home! Please, please stay\r\nat home, Jude, and not go to her, now she\xe2\x80\x99s not your wife any more than\r\nI!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she is, rather more than you, come to that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, taking his\r\nhat determinedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve wanted you to be, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve waited with the\r\npatience of Job, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that I\xe2\x80\x99ve got anything by my\r\nself-denial. I shall certainly give her something, and hear what it is\r\nshe is so anxious to tell me; no man could do less!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was that in his manner which she knew it would be futile to\r\noppose. She said no more, but, turning to her room as meekly as a\r\nmartyr, heard him go downstairs, unbolt the door, and close it behind\r\nhim. With a woman\xe2\x80\x99s disregard of her dignity when in the presence of\r\nnobody but herself, she also trotted down, sobbing articulately as she\r\nwent. She listened. She knew exactly how far it was to the inn that\r\nArabella had named as her lodging. It would occupy about seven minutes\r\nto get there at an ordinary walking pace; seven to come back again. If\r\nhe did not return in fourteen minutes he would have lingered. She\r\nlooked at the clock. It was twenty-five minutes to eleven. He _might_\r\nenter the inn with Arabella, as they would reach it before closing\r\ntime; she might get him to drink with her; and Heaven only knew what\r\ndisasters would befall him then.\r\n\r\nIn a still suspense she waited on. It seemed as if the whole time had\r\nnearly elapsed when the door was opened again, and Jude appeared.\r\n\r\nSue gave a little ecstatic cry. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I knew I could trust you!\xe2\x80\x94how good\r\nyou are!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t find her anywhere in this street, and I went out in my\r\nslippers only. She has walked on, thinking I\xe2\x80\x99ve been so hard-hearted as\r\nto refuse her requests entirely, poor woman. I\xe2\x80\x99ve come back for my\r\nboots, as it is beginning to rain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but why should you take such trouble for a woman who has served\r\nyou so badly!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue in a jealous burst of disappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Sue, she\xe2\x80\x99s a woman, and I once cared for her; and one can\xe2\x80\x99t be a\r\nbrute in such circumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe isn\xe2\x80\x99t your wife any longer!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sue, passionately excited.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _mustn\xe2\x80\x99t_ go out to find her! It isn\xe2\x80\x99t right! You _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ join\r\nher, now she\xe2\x80\x99s a stranger to you. How can you forget such a thing, my\r\ndear, dear one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe seems much the same as ever\xe2\x80\x94an erring, careless, unreflecting\r\nfellow-creature,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, continuing to pull on his boots. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat those\r\nlegal fellows have been playing at in London makes no difference in my\r\nreal relations to her. If she was my wife while she was away in\r\nAustralia with another husband, she\xe2\x80\x99s my wife now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t! That\xe2\x80\x99s just what I hold! There\xe2\x80\x99s the absurdity!\xe2\x80\x94\r\nWell\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll come straight back, after a few minutes, won\xe2\x80\x99t you, dear?\r\nShe is too low, too coarse for you to talk to long, Jude, and was\r\nalways!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I am coarse too, worse luck! I have the germs of every human\r\ninfirmity in me, I verily believe\xe2\x80\x94that was why I saw it was so\r\npreposterous of me to think of being a curate. I have cured myself of\r\ndrunkenness I think; but I never know in what new form a suppressed\r\nvice will break out in me! I do love you, Sue, though I have danced\r\nattendance on you so long for such poor returns! All that\xe2\x80\x99s best and\r\nnoblest in me loves you, and your freedom from everything that\xe2\x80\x99s gross\r\nhas elevated me, and enabled me to do what I should never have dreamt\r\nmyself capable of, or any man, a year or two ago. It is all very well\r\nto preach about self-control, and the wickedness of coercing a woman.\r\nBut I should just like a few virtuous people who have condemned me in\r\nthe past, about Arabella and other things, to have been in my\r\ntantalizing position with you through these late weeks!\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99d believe,\r\nI think, that I have exercised some little restraint in always giving\r\nin to your wishes\xe2\x80\x94living here in one house, and not a soul between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you have been good to me, Jude; I know you have, my dear\r\nprotector.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94Arabella has appealed to me for help. I must go out and speak to\r\nher, Sue, at least!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say any more!\xe2\x80\x94Oh, if you must, you must!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, bursting\r\nout into sobs that seemed to tear her heart. \xe2\x80\x9cI have nobody but you,\r\nJude, and you are deserting me! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were like this\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t bear it, I can\xe2\x80\x99t! If she were yours it would be different!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr if you were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well then\xe2\x80\x94if I must I must. Since you will have it so, I agree! I\r\nwill be. Only I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to! And I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to marry again,\r\neither! \xe2\x80\xa6 But, yes\xe2\x80\x94I agree, I agree! I do love you. I ought to have\r\nknown that you would conquer in the long run, living like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe ran across and flung her arms round his neck. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not a\r\ncold-natured, sexless creature, am I, for keeping you at such a\r\ndistance? I give in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll arrange for our marriage to-morrow, or as soon as ever you\r\nwish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Jude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99ll let her go,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, embracing Sue softly. \xe2\x80\x9cI do feel that\r\nit would be unfair to you to see her, and perhaps unfair to her. There; and there; and there!\xe2\x80\x9d He kissed her\r\non one side, and on the other, and in the middle, and rebolted the\r\nfront door.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next morning it was wet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude gaily at breakfast; \xe2\x80\x9cas this is Saturday I mean\r\nto call about the banns at once, so as to get the first publishing done\r\nto-morrow, or we shall lose a week. Banns will do? We shall save a\r\npound or two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue absently agreed to banns. But her mind for the moment was running\r\non something else. A glow had passed away from her, and depression sat\r\nupon her features.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel I was wickedly selfish last night!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was sheer\r\nunkindness in me\xe2\x80\x94or worse\xe2\x80\x94to treat Arabella as I did. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nabout her being in trouble, and what she wished to tell you! Perhaps it\r\nwas really something she was justified in telling you. That\xe2\x80\x99s some more\r\nof my badness, I suppose! Love has its own dark morality when rivalry\r\nenters in\xe2\x80\x94at least, mine has, if other people\xe2\x80\x99s hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\xa6 I wonder how\r\nshe got on? I hope she reached the inn all right, poor woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes: she got on all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t shut out, and that she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t to walk the streets in\r\nthe rain. Do you mind my putting on my waterproof and going to see if\r\nshe got in? I\xe2\x80\x99ve been thinking of her all the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94is it necessary? You haven\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea how Arabella is able\r\nto shift for herself. Still, darling, if you want to go and inquire you\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no limit to the strange and unnecessary penances which Sue\r\nwould meekly undertake when in a contrite mood; and this going to see\r\nall sorts of extraordinary persons whose relation to her was precisely\r\nof a kind that would have made other people shun them was her instinct\r\never, so that the request did not surprise him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when you come back,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be ready to go about the\r\nbanns. You\xe2\x80\x99ll come with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue agreed, and went off under cloak and umbrella letting Jude kiss her\r\nfreely, and returning his kisses in a way she had never done before.\r\nTimes had decidedly changed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe little bird is caught at last!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, a sadness showing in her smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94only nested,\xe2\x80\x9d he assured her.\r\n\r\nShe walked along the muddy street till she reached the public house\r\nmentioned by Arabella, which was not so very far off. She was informed\r\nthat Arabella had not yet left, and in doubt how to announce herself so\r\nthat her predecessor in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s affections would recognize her, she sent\r\nup word that a friend from Spring Street had called, naming the place\r\nof Jude\xe2\x80\x99s residence. She was asked to step upstairs, and on being shown\r\ninto a room found that it was Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s bedroom, and that the latter\r\nhad not yet risen. She halted on the turn of her toe till Arabella\r\ncried from the bed, \xe2\x80\x9cCome in and shut the door,\xe2\x80\x9d which Sue accordingly\r\ndid.\r\n\r\nArabella lay facing the window, and did not at once turn her head: and\r\nSue was wicked enough, despite her penitence, to wish for a moment that\r\nJude could behold her forerunner now, with the daylight full upon her.\r\nShe may have seemed handsome enough in profile under the lamps, but a\r\nfrowsiness was apparent this morning; and the sight of her own fresh\r\ncharms in the looking-glass made Sue\xe2\x80\x99s manner bright, till she\r\nreflected what a meanly sexual emotion this was in her, and hated\r\nherself for it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just looked in to see if you got back comfortably last night,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gently. \xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid afterwards that you might\r\nhave met with any mishap?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94how stupid this is! I thought my visitor was\xe2\x80\x94your friend\xe2\x80\x94your\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Fawley, as I suppose you call yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella,\r\nflinging her head back upon the pillows with a disappointed toss, and\r\nceasing to retain the dimple she had just taken the trouble to produce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I thought you might have, even if he\xe2\x80\x99s not really yours. Decency\r\nis decency, any hour of the twenty-four.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue stiffly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is mine, if you\r\ncome to that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue coloured roseate, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom your manner when you talked to me at the door. Well, my dear,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve been quick about it, and I expect my visit last night helped it\r\non\xe2\x80\x94ha-ha! But I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get him away from you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue looked out at the rain, and at the dirty toilet-cover, and at the\r\ndetached tail of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s hair hanging on the looking-glass, just as\r\nit had done in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s time; and wished she had not come. In the pause\r\nthere was a knock at the door, and the chambermaid brought in a\r\ntelegram for \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Cartlett.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella opened it as she lay, and her ruffled look disappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you for your anxiety about me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said blandly\r\nwhen the maid had gone; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is not necessary you should feel it. My\r\nman finds he can\xe2\x80\x99t do without me after all, and agrees to stand by the\r\npromise to marry again over here that he has made me all along. See\r\nhere! This is in answer to one from me.\xe2\x80\x9d She held out the telegram for\r\nSue to read, but Sue did not take it. \xe2\x80\x9cHe asks me to come back. His\r\nlittle corner public in Lambeth would go to pieces without me, he says.\r\nBut he isn\xe2\x80\x99t going to knock me about when he has had a drop, any more\r\nafter we are spliced by English law than before! \xe2\x80\xa6 As for you, I should\r\ncoax Jude to take me before the parson straight off, and have done with\r\nit, if I were in your place. Life with a man is more businesslike\r\nafter it, and money matters work better. And then, you see, if you have\r\nrows, and he turns you out of doors, you can get the law to protect\r\nyou, which you can\xe2\x80\x99t otherwise, unless he half-runs you through with a\r\nknife, or cracks your noddle with a poker. And if he bolts away from\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94I say it friendly, as woman to woman, for there\xe2\x80\x99s never any knowing\r\nwhat a man med do\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll have the sticks o\xe2\x80\x99 furniture, and won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nlooked upon as a thief. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find it an awful\r\nbother later on if you don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have told you he is asking me to marry him\xe2\x80\x94to make our natural\r\nmarriage a legal one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, with yet more dignity. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was quite\r\nby my wish that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t the moment I was free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes\xe2\x80\x94you are a oneyer too, like myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella, eyeing her\r\nvisitor with humorous criticism. \xe2\x80\x9cBolted from your first, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you,\r\nlike me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning!\xe2\x80\x94I must go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue hastily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I, too, must up and off!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the other, springing out of bed\r\nso suddenly that the soft parts of her person shook. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to write it, but never mind\xe2\x80\x94I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Sue reached home Jude was awaiting her at the door to take the\r\ninitial step towards their marriage. She clasped his arm, and they went\r\nalong silently together, as true comrades oft-times do. He saw that she\r\nwas preoccupied, and forbore to question her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Jude\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve been talking to her,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t!\r\nAnd yet it is best to be reminded of things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she was civil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I felt we were deceiving him before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue, you seem when you are like this to be one of the women of some\r\ngrand old civilization, whom I used to read about in my bygone, wasted,\r\nclassical days, rather than a denizen of a mere Christian country. I\r\nalmost expect you to say at these times that you have just been talking\r\nto some friend whom you met in the Via Sacra, about the latest news of\r\nOctavia or Livia; or have been listening to Aspasia\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence, or\r\nhave been watching Praxiteles chiselling away at his latest Venus,\r\nwhile Phryne made complaint that she was tired of posing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had now reached the house of the parish clerk. Sue stood back,\r\nwhile her lover went up to the door. His hand was raised to knock when\r\nshe said: \xe2\x80\x9cJude!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute, would you mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe came back to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust let us think,\xe2\x80\x9d she said timidly. \xe2\x80\x9cI had such a horrid dream one\r\nnight! \xe2\x80\xa6 And Arabella\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did Arabella say to you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she said that when people were tied up you could get the law of a\r\nman better if he beat you\xe2\x80\x94and how when couples quarrelled\xe2\x80\xa6 Jude, do you\r\nthink that when you must have me with you by law, we shall be so happy\r\nas we are now? The men and women of our family are very generous when\r\neverything depends upon their goodwill, but they always kick against\r\ncompulsion. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you dread the attitude that insensibly arises out of\r\nlegal obligation? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it is destructive to a passion whose\r\nessence is its gratuitousness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, love, you are beginning to frighten me, too, with all\r\nthis foreboding!
