And they turned from\r\nthe clerk\xe2\x80\x99s door, Sue taking his arm and murmuring as they walked on\r\nhomeward:\r\n\r\nCan you keep the bee from ranging,\r\nOr the ring-dove\xe2\x80\x99s neck from changing?\r\nNo! Nor fetter\xe2\x80\x99d love\xe2\x80\xa6\r\n\r\nThey thought it over, or postponed thinking. Certainly they postponed\r\naction, and seemed to live on in a dreamy paradise. At the end of a\r\nfortnight or three weeks matters remained unadvanced, and no banns were\r\nannounced to the ears of any Aldbrickham congregation.\r\n\r\nWhilst they were postponing and postponing thus a letter and a\r\nnewspaper arrived before breakfast one morning from Arabella. Seeing\r\nthe handwriting Jude went up to Sue\xe2\x80\x99s room and told her, and as soon as\r\nshe was dressed she hastened down. Sue opened the newspaper; Jude the\r\nletter. After glancing at the paper she held across the first page to\r\nhim with her finger on a paragraph; but he was so absorbed in his\r\nletter that he did not turn awhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\nHe looked and read. The paper was one that circulated in South London\r\nonly, and the marked advertisement was simply the announcement of a\r\nmarriage at St. John\xe2\x80\x99s Church, Waterloo Road, under the names,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCARTLETT\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94DONN\xe2\x80\x9d; the united pair being Arabella and the inn-keeper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is satisfactory,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue complacently. \xe2\x80\x9cThough, after this,\r\nit seems rather low to do likewise, and I am glad. However, she is\r\nprovided for now in a way, I suppose, whatever her faults, poor thing.\r\nIt is nicer that we are able to think that, than to be uneasy about\r\nher. I ought, too, to write to Richard and ask him how he is getting\r\non, perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Jude\xe2\x80\x99s attention was still absorbed. Having merely glanced at the\r\nannouncement he said in a disturbed voice: \xe2\x80\x9cListen to this letter. What\r\nshall I say or do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTHE THREE HORNS, LAMBETH.\r\n\r\n\r\nDEAR JUDE (I won\xe2\x80\x99t be so distant as to call you Mr. Fawley),\xe2\x80\x94I send\r\nto-day a newspaper, from which useful document you will learn that I\r\nwas married over again to Cartlett last Tuesday. So that business is\r\nsettled right and tight at last. But what I write about more particular\r\nis that private affair I wanted to speak to you on when I came down to\r\nAldbrickham. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t very well tell it to your lady friend, and\r\nshould much have liked to let you know it by word of mouth, as I could\r\nhave explained better than by letter. The fact is, Jude, that, though I\r\nhave never informed you before, there was a boy born of our marriage,\r\neight months after I left you, when I was at Sydney, living with my\r\nfather and mother. All that is easily provable. As I had separated from\r\nyou before I thought such a thing was going to happen, and I was over\r\nthere, and our quarrel had been sharp, I did not think it convenient to\r\nwrite about the birth. I was then looking out for a good situation, so\r\nmy parents took the child, and he has been with them ever since. That\r\nwas why I did not mention it when I met you in Christminster, nor at\r\nthe law proceedings. He is now of an intelligent age, of course, and my\r\nmother and father have lately written to say that, as they have rather\r\na hard struggle over there, and I am settled comfortably here, they\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see why they should be encumbered with the child any longer, his\r\nparents being alive. I would have him with me here in a moment, but he\r\nis not old enough to be of any use in the bar nor will be for years and\r\nyears, and naturally Cartlett might think him in the way. They have,\r\nhowever, packed him off to me in charge of some friends who happened to\r\nbe coming home, and I must ask you to take him when he arrives, for I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do with him. He is lawfully yours, that I solemnly\r\nswear. If anybody says he isn\xe2\x80\x99t, call them brimstone liars, for my\r\nsake. Whatever I may have done before or afterwards, I was honest to\r\nyou from the time we were married till I went away, and I remain,\r\nyours, &c.,\r\n\r\n\r\nARABELLA CARTLETT.\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s look was one of dismay. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked\r\nfaintly.\r\n\r\nJude did not reply, and Sue watched him anxiously, with heavy breaths.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt hits me hard!\xe2\x80\x9d said he in an under-voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt _may_ be true! I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t make it out. Certainly, if his birth was exactly when she says,\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s mine. I cannot think why she didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me when I met her at\r\nChristminster, and came on here that evening with her! \xe2\x80\xa6 Ah\xe2\x80\x94I do\r\nremember now that she said something about having a thing on her mind\r\nthat she would like me to know, if ever we lived together again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe poor child seems to be wanted by nobody!\xe2\x80\x9d Sue replied, and her\r\neyes filled.\r\n\r\nJude had by this time come to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a view of life he must\r\nhave, mine or not mine!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI must say that, if I were better\r\noff, I should not stop for a moment to think whose he might be. I would\r\ntake him and bring him up. The beggarly question of parentage\xe2\x80\x94what is\r\nit, after all? What does it matter, when you come to think of it,\r\nwhether a child is yours by blood or not? All the little ones of our\r\ntime are collectively the children of us adults of the time, and\r\nentitled to our general care. That excessive regard of parents for\r\ntheir own children, and their dislike of other people\xe2\x80\x99s, is, like\r\nclass-feeling, patriotism, save-your-own-soul-ism, and other virtues, a\r\nmean exclusiveness at bottom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue jumped up and kissed Jude with passionate devotion. \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94so it is,\r\ndearest! And we\xe2\x80\x99ll have him here! And if he isn\xe2\x80\x99t yours it makes it all\r\nthe better. I do hope he isn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94though perhaps I ought not to feel quite\r\nthat! If he isn\xe2\x80\x99t, I should like so much for us to have him as an\r\nadopted child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you must assume about him what is most pleasing to you, my\r\ncurious little comrade!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel that, anyhow, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to\r\nleave the unfortunate little fellow to neglect. Just think of his life\r\nin a Lambeth pothouse, and all its evil influences, with a parent who\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t want him, and has, indeed, hardly seen him, and a stepfather\r\nwho doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know him. \xe2\x80\x98Let the day perish wherein I was born, and the\r\nnight in which it was said, There is a man child conceived!\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat the boy\xe2\x80\x94_my_ boy, perhaps, will find himself saying before long!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I was the petitioner, I am really entitled to his custody, I\r\nsuppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhether or no, we must have him. I see that. I\xe2\x80\x99ll do the best I can to\r\nbe a mother to him, and we can afford to keep him somehow. I\xe2\x80\x99ll work\r\nharder. I wonder when he\xe2\x80\x99ll arrive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the course of a few weeks, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish\xe2\x80\x94When shall we have courage to marry, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you have it, I think I shall. It remains with you entirely,\r\ndear. Only say the word, and it\xe2\x80\x99s done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore the boy comes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would make a more natural home for him, perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\nJude thereupon wrote in purely formal terms to request that the boy\r\nshould be sent on to them as soon as he arrived, making no remark\r\nwhatever on the surprising nature of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s information, nor\r\nvouchsafing a single word of opinion on the boy\xe2\x80\x99s paternity, nor on\r\nwhether, had he known all this, his conduct towards her would have been\r\nquite the same.\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the down train that was timed to reach Aldbrickham station about ten\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock the next evening, a small, pale child\xe2\x80\x99s face could be seen in\r\nthe gloom of a third-class carriage. He had large, frightened eyes, and\r\nwore a white woollen cravat, over which a key was suspended round his\r\nneck by a piece of common string: the key attracting attention by its\r\noccasional shine in the lamplight. In the band of his hat his\r\nhalf-ticket was stuck. His eyes remained mostly fixed on the back of\r\nthe seat opposite, and never turned to the window even when a station\r\nwas reached and called. On the other seat were two or three passengers,\r\none of them a working woman who held a basket on her lap, in which was\r\na tabby kitten. The woman opened the cover now and then, whereupon the\r\nkitten would put out its head, and indulge in playful antics. At these\r\nthe fellow-passengers laughed, except the solitary boy bearing the key\r\nand ticket, who, regarding the kitten with his saucer eyes, seemed\r\nmutely to say: \xe2\x80\x9cAll laughing comes from misapprehension. Rightly looked\r\nat, there is no laughable thing under the sun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOccasionally, at a stoppage, the guard would look into the compartment\r\nand say to the boy, \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, my man. Your box is safe in the van.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe boy would say, \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d without animation, would try to smile, and\r\nfail.\r\n\r\nHe was Age masquerading as Juvenility, and doing it so badly that his\r\nreal self showed through crevices. A ground-swell from ancient years of\r\nnight seemed now and then to lift the child in this his morning-life,\r\nwhen his face took a back view over some great Atlantic of Time, and\r\nappeared not to care about what it saw.\r\n\r\nWhen the other travellers closed their eyes, which they did one by\r\none\xe2\x80\x94even the kitten curling itself up in the basket, weary of its too\r\ncircumscribed play\xe2\x80\x94the boy remained just as before. He then seemed to\r\nbe doubly awake, like an enslaved and dwarfed divinity, sitting passive\r\nand regarding his companions as if he saw their whole rounded lives\r\nrather than their immediate figures.\r\n\r\nThis was Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s boy. With her usual carelessness, she had postponed\r\nwriting to Jude about him till the eve of his landing, when she could\r\nabsolutely postpone no longer, though she had known for weeks of his\r\napproaching arrival, and had, as she truly said, visited Aldbrickham\r\nmainly to reveal the boy\xe2\x80\x99s existence and his near home-coming to Jude.\r\nThis very day on which she had received her former husband\xe2\x80\x99s answer at\r\nsome time in the afternoon, the child reached the London Docks, and the\r\nfamily in whose charge he had come, having put him into a cab for\r\nLambeth and directed the cabman to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s house, bade him\r\ngood-bye, and went their way.\r\n\r\nOn his arrival at the Three Horns, Arabella had looked him over with an\r\nexpression that was as good as saying, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very much what I\r\nexpected you to be,\xe2\x80\x9d had given him a good meal, a little money, and,\r\nlate as it was getting, dispatched him to Jude by the next train,\r\nwishing her husband Cartlett, who was out, not to see him.\r\n\r\nThe train reached Aldbrickham, and the boy was deposited on the lonely\r\nplatform beside his box. The collector took his ticket, and, with a\r\nmeditative sense of the unfitness of things, asked him where he was\r\ngoing by himself at that time of night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to Spring Street,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little one impassively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that\xe2\x80\x99s a long way from here; a\xe2\x80\x99most out in the country; and the\r\nfolks will be gone to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got to go there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have a fly for your box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I must walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh well: you\xe2\x80\x99d better leave your box here and send for it. There\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\n\xe2\x80\x98bus goes half-way, but you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to walk the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t your friends come to meet \xe2\x80\x99ee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose they didn\xe2\x80\x99t know I was coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is your friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish me to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll I can do, then, is to take charge of this. Now walk as fast as you\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSaying nothing further the boy came out into the street, looking round\r\nto see that nobody followed or observed him. When he had walked some\r\nlittle distance he asked for the street of his destination. He was told\r\nto go straight on quite into the outskirts of the place.\r\n\r\nThe child fell into a steady mechanical creep which had in it an\r\nimpersonal quality\xe2\x80\x94the movement of the wave, or of the breeze, or of\r\nthe cloud. He followed his directions literally, without an inquiring\r\ngaze at anything. It could have been seen that the boy\xe2\x80\x99s ideas of life\r\nwere different from those of the local boys. Children begin with\r\ndetail, and learn up to the general; they begin with the contiguous,\r\nand gradually comprehend the universal. The boy seemed to have begun\r\nwith the generals of life, and never to have concerned himself with the\r\nparticulars. To him the houses, the willows, the obscure fields beyond,\r\nwere apparently regarded not as brick residences, pollards, meadows;\r\nbut as human dwellings in the abstract, vegetation, and the wide dark\r\nworld.\r\n\r\nHe found the way to the little lane, and knocked at the door of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse. Jude had just retired to bed, and Sue was about to enter her\r\nchamber adjoining when she heard the knock and came down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this where Father lives?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Fawley, that\xe2\x80\x99s his name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue ran up to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s room and told him, and he hurried down as soon as\r\nhe could, though to her impatience he seemed long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94is it he\xe2\x80\x94so soon?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked as Jude came.\r\n\r\nShe scrutinized the child\xe2\x80\x99s features, and suddenly went away into the\r\nlittle sitting-room adjoining. Jude lifted the boy to a level with\r\nhimself, keenly regarded him with gloomy tenderness, and telling him he\r\nwould have been met if they had known of his coming so soon, set him\r\nprovisionally in a chair whilst he went to look for Sue, whose\r\nsupersensitiveness was disturbed, as he knew. He found her in the dark,\r\nbending over an arm-chair. He enclosed her with his arm, and putting\r\nhis face by hers, whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Arabella says is true\xe2\x80\x94true! I see you in him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell: that\xe2\x80\x99s one thing in my life as it should be, at any rate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the other half of him is\xe2\x80\x94_she_! And that\xe2\x80\x99s what I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear! But\r\nI ought to\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll try to get used to it; yes, I ought!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJealous little Sue! I withdraw all remarks about your sexlessness.\r\nNever mind! Time may right things\xe2\x80\xa6 And Sue, darling; I have an idea!\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll educate and train him with a view to the university. What I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t accomplish in my own person perhaps I can carry out through\r\nhim? They are making it easier for poor students now, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh you dreamer!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, and holding his hand returned to the child\r\nwith him. The boy looked at her as she had looked at him. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it you\r\nwho\xe2\x80\x99s my _real_ mother at last?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? Do I look like your father\xe2\x80\x99s wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes; \xe2\x80\x99cept he seems fond of you, and you of him. Can I call you\r\nMother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen a yearning look came over the child and he began to cry. Sue\r\nthereupon could not refrain from instantly doing likewise, being a harp\r\nwhich the least wind of emotion from another\xe2\x80\x99s heart could make to\r\nvibrate as readily as a radical stir in her own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may call me Mother, if you wish to, my poor dear!\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nbending her cheek against his to hide her tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this round your neck?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jude with affected calmness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe key of my box that\xe2\x80\x99s at the station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey bustled about and got him some supper, and made him up a temporary\r\nbed, where he soon fell asleep. Both went and looked at him as he lay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe called you Mother two or three times before he dropped off,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nmurmured Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t it odd that he should have wanted to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94it was significant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s more for us to think\r\nabout in that one little hungry heart than in all the stars of the sky\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nI suppose, dear, we must pluck up courage, and get that ceremony over?\r\nIt is no use struggling against the current, and I feel myself getting\r\nintertwined with my kind. Oh Jude, you\xe2\x80\x99ll love me dearly, won\xe2\x80\x99t you,\r\nafterwards? I do want to be kind to this child, and to be a mother to\r\nhim; and our adding the legal form to our marriage might make it easier\r\nfor me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\n\r\nTheir next and second attempt thereat was more deliberately made,\r\nthough it was begun on the morning following the singular child\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narrival at their home.\r\n\r\nHim they found to be in the habit of sitting silent, his quaint and\r\nweird face set, and his eyes resting on things they did not see in the\r\nsubstantial world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis face is like the tragic mask of Melpomene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is\r\nyour name, dear? Did you tell us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Father Time is what they always called me. It is a nickname;\r\nbecause I look so aged, they say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you talk so, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue tenderly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is strange, Jude, that\r\nthese preternaturally old boys almost always come from new countries.\r\nBut what were you christened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, if I died in damnation, \xe2\x80\x99twould save the expense of a\r\nChristian funeral.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94your name is not Jude, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said his father with some\r\ndisappointment.\r\n\r\nThe boy shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cNever heerd on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue quickly; \xe2\x80\x9csince she was hating you all the\r\ntime!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have him christened,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude; and privately to Sue: \xe2\x80\x9cThe day\r\nwe are married.\xe2\x80\x9d Yet the advent of the child disturbed him.\r\n\r\nTheir position lent them shyness, and having an impression that a\r\nmarriage at a superintendent registrar\xe2\x80\x99s office was more private than\r\nan ecclesiastical one, they decided to avoid a church this time. Both\r\nSue and Jude together went to the office of the district to give\r\nnotice: they had become such companions that they could hardly do\r\nanything of importance except in each other\xe2\x80\x99s company.\r\n\r\nJude Fawley signed the form of notice, Sue looking over his shoulder\r\nand watching his hand as it traced the words. As she read the\r\nfour-square undertaking, never before seen by her, into which her own\r\nand Jude\xe2\x80\x99s names were inserted, and by which that very volatile\r\nessence, their love for each other, was supposed to be made permanent,\r\nher face seemed to grow painfully apprehensive. \xe2\x80\x9cNames and Surnames of\r\nthe Parties\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94(they were to be parties now, not lovers, she thought).\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCondition\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94(a horrid idea)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cRank or Occupation\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAge\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDwelling\r\nat\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cLength of Residence\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cChurch or Building in which the Marriage is\r\nto be solemnized\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDistrict and County in which the Parties\r\nrespectively dwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt spoils the sentiment, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it!\xe2\x80\x9d she said on their way home. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nseems making a more sordid business of it even than signing the\r\ncontract in a vestry. There is a little poetry in a church. But we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntry to get through with it, dearest, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will. \xe2\x80\x98For what man is he that hath betrothed a wife and hath not\r\ntaken her? Let him go and return unto his house, lest he die in the\r\nbattle, and another man take her.\xe2\x80\x99 So said the Jewish law-giver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you know the Scriptures, Jude! You really ought to have been a\r\nparson. I can only quote profane writers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDuring the interval before the issuing of the certificate, Sue, in her\r\nhousekeeping errands, sometimes walked past the office, and furtively\r\nglancing in saw affixed to the wall the notice of the purposed clinch\r\nto their union. She could not bear its aspect. Coming after her\r\nprevious experience of matrimony, all the romance of their attachment\r\nseemed to be starved away by placing her present case in the same\r\ncategory. She was usually leading little Father Time by the hand, and\r\nfancied that people thought him hers, and regarded the intended\r\nceremony as the patching up of an old error.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Jude decided to link his present with his past in some slight\r\ndegree by inviting to the wedding the only person remaining on earth\r\nwho was associated with his early life at Marygreen\xe2\x80\x94the aged widow Mrs.\r\nEdlin, who had been his great-aunt\xe2\x80\x99s friend and nurse in her last\r\nillness. He hardly expected that she would come; but she did, bringing\r\nsingular presents, in the form of apples, jam, brass snuffers, an\r\nancient pewter dish, a warming-pan, and an enormous bag of goose\r\nfeathers towards a bed. She was allotted the spare room in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, whither she retired early, and where they could hear her through\r\nthe ceiling below, honestly saying the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s Prayer in a loud voice,\r\nas the Rubric directed.\r\n\r\nAs, however, she could not sleep, and discovered that Sue and Jude were\r\nstill sitting up\xe2\x80\x94it being in fact only ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock\xe2\x80\x94she dressed herself\r\nagain and came down, and they all sat by the fire till a late\r\nhour\xe2\x80\x94Father Time included; though, as he never spoke, they were hardly\r\nconscious of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I bain\xe2\x80\x99t set against marrying as your great-aunt was,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nwidow. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I hope \xe2\x80\x99twill be a jocund wedding for ye in all respects\r\nthis time. Nobody can hope it more, knowing what I do of your families,\r\nwhich is more, I suppose, than anybody else now living. For they have\r\nbeen unlucky that way, God knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue breathed uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey was always good-hearted people, too\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t kill a fly if they\r\nknowed it,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the wedding guest. \xe2\x80\x9cBut things happened to thwart\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em, and if everything wasn\xe2\x80\x99t vitty they were upset. No doubt that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhow he that the tale is told of came to do what \xe2\x80\x99a did\xe2\x80\x94if he _were_ one\r\nof your family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94that tale, ye know; he that was gibbeted just on the brow of the\r\nhill by the Brown House\xe2\x80\x94not far from the milestone between Marygreen\r\nand Alfredston, where the other road branches off. But Lord, \xe2\x80\x99twas in\r\nmy grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s time; and it medn\xe2\x80\x99 have been one of your folk at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know where the gibbet is said to have stood, very well,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nJude. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I never heard of this. What\xe2\x80\x94did this man\xe2\x80\x94my ancestor and\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94kill his wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twer not that exactly. She ran away from him, with their child, to\r\nher friends; and while she was there the child died. He wanted the\r\nbody, to bury it where his people lay, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give it up. Her\r\nhusband then came in the night with a cart, and broke into the house to\r\nsteal the coffin away; but he was catched, and being obstinate,\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell what he broke in for. They brought it in burglary, and\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s why he was hanged and gibbeted on Brown House Hill. His wife\r\nwent mad after he was dead. But it medn\xe2\x80\x99t be true that he belonged to\r\nye more than to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA small slow voice rose from the shade of the fireside, as if out of\r\nthe earth: \xe2\x80\x9cIf I was you, Mother, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry Father!\xe2\x80\x9d It came\r\nfrom little Time, and they started, for they had forgotten him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is only a tale,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue cheeringly.\r\n\r\nAfter this exhilarating tradition from the widow on the eve of the\r\nsolemnization they rose, and, wishing their guest good-night, retired.\r\n\r\nThe next morning Sue, whose nervousness intensified with the hours,\r\ntook Jude privately into the sitting-room before starting. \xe2\x80\x9cJude, I\r\nwant you to kiss me, as a lover, incorporeally,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, tremulously\r\nnestling up to him, with damp lashes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t be ever like this any\r\nmore, will it? I wish we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t begun the business. But I suppose we\r\nmust go on. How horrid that story was last night! It spoilt my thoughts\r\nof to-day. It makes me feel as if a tragic doom overhung our family, as\r\nit did the house of Atreus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr the house of Jeroboam,\xe2\x80\x9d said the quondam theologian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And it seems awful temerity in us two to go marrying! I am going\r\nto vow to you in the same words I vowed in to my other husband, and you\r\nto me in the same as you used to your other wife; regardless of the\r\ndeterrent lesson we were taught by those experiments!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are uneasy I am made unhappy,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI had hoped you would\r\nfeel quite joyful. But if you don\xe2\x80\x99t, you don\xe2\x80\x99t. It is no use\r\npretending. It is a dismal business to you, and that makes it so to\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is unpleasantly like that other morning\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go on now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey started arm in arm for the office aforesaid, no witness\r\naccompanying them except the Widow Edlin. The day was chilly and dull,\r\nand a clammy fog blew through the town from \xe2\x80\x9cRoyal-tower\xe2\x80\x99d Thame.\xe2\x80\x9d On\r\nthe steps of the office there were the muddy foot-marks of people who\r\nhad entered, and in the entry were damp umbrellas. Within the office\r\nseveral persons were gathered, and our couple perceived that a marriage\r\nbetween a soldier and a young woman was just in progress. Sue, Jude,\r\nand the widow stood in the background while this was going on, Sue\r\nreading the notices of marriage on the wall. The room was a dreary\r\nplace to two of their temperament, though to its usual frequenters it\r\ndoubtless seemed ordinary enough. Law-books in musty calf covered one\r\nwall, and elsewhere were post-office directories, and other books of\r\nreference. Papers in packets tied with red tape were pigeon-holed\r\naround, and some iron safes filled a recess, while the bare wood floor\r\nwas, like the door-step, stained by previous visitors.\r\n\r\nThe soldier was sullen and reluctant: the bride sad and timid; she was\r\nsoon, obviously, to become a mother, and she had a black eye. Their\r\nlittle business was soon done, and the twain and their friends\r\nstraggled out, one of the witnesses saying casually to Jude and Sue in\r\npassing, as if he had known them before: \xe2\x80\x9cSee the couple just come in?\r\nHa, ha! That fellow is just out of gaol this morning. She met him at\r\nthe gaol gates, and brought him straight here. She\xe2\x80\x99s paying for\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue turned her head and saw an ill-favoured man, closely cropped, with\r\na broad-faced, pock-marked woman on his arm, ruddy with liquor and the\r\nsatisfaction of being on the brink of a gratified desire. They jocosely\r\nsaluted the outgoing couple, and went forward in front of Jude and Sue,\r\nwhose diffidence was increasing. The latter drew back and turned to her\r\nlover, her mouth shaping itself like that of a child about to give way\r\nto grief:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it here! I wish we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come! The place gives me\r\nthe horrors: it seems so unnatural as the climax of our love! I wish it\r\nhad been at church, if it had to be at all. It is not so vulgar there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear little girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cHow troubled and pale you look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be performed here now, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94perhaps not necessarily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke to the clerk, and came back. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94we need not marry here or\r\nanywhere, unless we like, even now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can be married in a\r\nchurch, if not with the same certificate with another he\xe2\x80\x99ll give us, I\r\nthink. Anyhow, let us go out till you are calmer, dear, and I too, and\r\ntalk it over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went out stealthily and guiltily, as if they had committed a\r\nmisdemeanour, closing the door without noise, and telling the widow,\r\nwho had remained in the entry, to go home and await them; that they\r\nwould call in any casual passers as witnesses, if necessary. When in\r\nthe street they turned into an unfrequented side alley where they\r\nwalked up and down as they had done long ago in the market-house at\r\nMelchester.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, darling, what shall we do? We are making a mess of it, it strikes\r\nme. Still, _anything_ that pleases you will please me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Jude, dearest, I am worrying you! You wanted it to be there,\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, to tell the truth, when I got inside I felt as if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nmuch about it. The place depressed me almost as much as it did you\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwas ugly. And then I thought of what you had said this morning as to\r\nwhether we ought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked on vaguely, till she paused, and her little voice began\r\nanew: \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems so weak, too, to vacillate like this! And yet how much\r\nbetter than to act rashly a second time\xe2\x80\xa6 How terrible that scene was to\r\nme! The expression in that flabby woman\xe2\x80\x99s face, leading her on to give\r\nherself to that gaol-bird, not for a few hours, as she would, but for a\r\nlifetime, as she must. And the other poor soul\xe2\x80\x94to escape a nominal\r\nshame which was owing to the weakness of her character, degrading\r\nherself to the real shame of bondage to a tyrant who scorned her\xe2\x80\x94a man\r\nwhom to avoid for ever was her only chance of salvation\xe2\x80\xa6 This is our\r\nparish church, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? This is where it would have to be, if we did\r\nit in the usual way? A service or something seems to be going on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude went up and looked in at the door. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94it is a wedding here too,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cEverybody seems to be on our tack to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue said she supposed it was because Lent was just over, when there was\r\nalways a crowd of marriages. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us listen,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand find how\r\nit feels to us when performed in a church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stepped in, and entered a back seat, and watched the proceedings\r\nat the altar. The contracting couple appeared to belong to the\r\nwell-to-do middle class, and the wedding altogether was of ordinary\r\nprettiness and interest. They could see the flowers tremble in the\r\nbride\xe2\x80\x99s hand, even at that distance, and could hear her mechanical\r\nmurmur of words whose meaning her brain seemed to gather not at all\r\nunder the pressure of her self-consciousness. Sue and Jude listened,\r\nand severally saw themselves in time past going through the same form\r\nof self-committal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not the same to her, poor thing, as it would be to me doing it\r\nover again with my present knowledge,\xe2\x80\x9d Sue whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, they\r\nare fresh to it, and take the proceedings as a matter of course. But\r\nhaving been awakened to its awful solemnity as we have, or at least as\r\nI have, by experience, and to my own too squeamish feelings perhaps\r\nsometimes, it really does seem immoral in me to go and undertake the\r\nsame thing again with open eyes. Coming in here and seeing this has\r\nfrightened me from a church wedding as much as the other did from a\r\nregistry one\xe2\x80\xa6 We are a weak, tremulous pair, Jude, and what others may\r\nfeel confident in I feel doubts of\xe2\x80\x94my being proof against the sordid\r\nconditions of a business contract again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they tried to laugh, and went on debating in whispers the\r\nobject-lesson before them. And Jude said he also thought they were both\r\ntoo thin-skinned\xe2\x80\x94that they ought never to have been born\xe2\x80\x94much less have\r\ncome together for the most preposterous of all joint ventures for\r\n_them_\xe2\x80\x94matrimony.\r\n\r\nHis betrothed shuddered; and asked him earnestly if he indeed felt that\r\nthey ought not to go in cold blood and sign that life-undertaking\r\nagain? \xe2\x80\x9cIt is awful if you think we have found ourselves not strong\r\nenough for it, and knowing this, are proposing to perjure ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy I do think it\xe2\x80\x94since you ask me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cRemember I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\r\nit if you wish, own darling.\xe2\x80\x9d While she hesitated he went on to confess\r\nthat, though he thought they ought to be able to do it, he felt checked\r\nby the dread of incompetency just as she did\xe2\x80\x94from their peculiarities,\r\nperhaps, because they were unlike other people. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are horribly\r\nsensitive; that\xe2\x80\x99s really what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with us, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy more are like us than we think!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. The intention of the contract is good, and right\r\nfor many, no doubt; but in our case it may defeat its own ends because\r\nwe are the queer sort of people we are\xe2\x80\x94folk in whom domestic ties of a\r\nforced kind snuff out cordiality and spontaneousness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue still held that there was not much queer or exceptional in them:\r\nthat all were so. \xe2\x80\x9cEverybody is getting to feel as we do. We are a\r\nlittle beforehand, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. In fifty, a hundred, years the\r\ndescendants of these two will act and feel worse than we. They will see\r\nweltering humanity still more vividly than we do now, as\r\n\r\nShapes like our own selves hideously multiplied,\r\n\r\nand will be afraid to reproduce them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a terrible line of poetry! \xe2\x80\xa6 though I have felt it myself about\r\nmy fellow-creatures, at morbid times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus they murmured on, till Sue said more brightly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94the general question is not our business, and why should we\r\nplague ourselves about it? However different our reasons are, we come\r\nto the same conclusion: that for us particular two, an irrevocable oath\r\nis risky. Then, Jude, let us go home without killing our dream! Yes?\r\nHow good you are, my friend: you give way to all my whims!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey accord very much with my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave her a little kiss behind a pillar while the attention of\r\neverybody present was taken up in observing the bridal procession\r\nentering the vestry; and then they came outside the building. By the\r\ndoor they waited till two or three carriages, which had gone away for a\r\nwhile, returned, and the new husband and wife came into the open\r\ndaylight. Sue sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe flowers in the bride\xe2\x80\x99s hand are sadly like the garland which\r\ndecked the heifers of sacrifice in old times!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, Sue, it is no worse for the woman than for the man. That\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nsome women fail to see, and instead of protesting against the\r\nconditions they protest against the man, the other victim; just as a\r\nwoman in a crowd will abuse the man who crushes against her, when he is\r\nonly the helpless transmitter of the pressure put upon him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94some are like that, instead of uniting with the man against the\r\ncommon enemy, coercion.\xe2\x80\x9d The bride and bridegroom had by this time\r\ndriven off, and the two moved away with the rest of the idlers.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t let\xe2\x80\x99s do it,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least, just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey reached home, and passing the window arm in arm saw the widow\r\nlooking out at them. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d cried their guest when they entered, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsaid to myself when I zeed ye coming so loving up to the door, \xe2\x80\x98They\r\nmade up their minds at last, then!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey briefly hinted that they had not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94and ha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t ye really done it? Chok\xe2\x80\x99 it all, that I should have\r\nlived to see a good old saying like \xe2\x80\x98marry in haste and repent at\r\nleisure\xe2\x80\x99 spoiled like this by you two! \xe2\x80\x99Tis time I got back again to\r\nMarygreen\xe2\x80\x94sakes if tidden\xe2\x80\x94if this is what the new notions be leading us\r\nto! Nobody thought o\xe2\x80\x99 being afeard o\xe2\x80\x99 matrimony in my time, nor of much\r\nelse but a cannon-ball or empty cupboard! Why when I and my poor man\r\nwere married we thought no more o\xe2\x80\x99t than of a game o\xe2\x80\x99 dibs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell the child when he comes in,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Sue nervously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll think it has all gone on right, and it will be better that he\r\nshould not be surprised and puzzled. Of course it is only put off for\r\nreconsideration. If we are happy as we are, what does it matter to\r\nanybody?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe purpose of a chronicler of moods and deeds does not require him to\r\nexpress his personal views upon the grave controversy above given. That\r\nthe twain were happy\xe2\x80\x94between their times of sadness\xe2\x80\x94was indubitable.\r\nAnd when the unexpected apparition of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s child in the house had\r\nshown itself to be no such disturbing event as it had looked, but one\r\nthat brought into their lives a new and tender interest of an ennobling\r\nand unselfish kind, it rather helped than injured their happiness.\r\n\r\nTo be sure, with such pleasing anxious beings as they were, the boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncoming also brought with it much thought for the future, particularly\r\nas he seemed at present to be singularly deficient in all the usual\r\nhopes of childhood. But the pair tried to dismiss, for a while at\r\nleast, a too strenuously forward view.\r\n\r\nThere is in Upper Wessex an old town of nine or ten thousand souls; the\r\ntown may be called Stoke-Barehills. It stands with its gaunt,\r\nunattractive, ancient church, and its new red brick suburb, amid the\r\nopen, chalk-soiled cornlands, near the middle of an imaginary triangle\r\nwhich has for its three corners the towns of Aldbrickham and\r\nWintoncester, and the important military station of Quartershot. The\r\ngreat western highway from London passes through it, near a point where\r\nthe road branches into two, merely to unite again some twenty miles\r\nfurther westward. Out of this bifurcation and reunion there used to\r\narise among wheeled travellers, before railway days, endless questions\r\nof choice between the respective ways. But the question is now as dead\r\nas the scot-and-lot freeholder, the road waggoner, and the mail\r\ncoachman who disputed it; and probably not a single inhabitant of\r\nStoke-Barehills is now even aware that the two roads which part in his\r\ntown ever meet again; for nobody now drives up and down the great\r\nwestern highway dally.\r\n\r\nThe most familiar object in Stoke-Barehills nowadays is its cemetery,\r\nstanding among some picturesque medi\xc3\xa6val ruins beside the railway; the\r\nmodern chapels, modern tombs, and modern shrubs having a look of\r\nintrusiveness amid the crumbling and ivy-covered decay of the ancient\r\nwalls.\r\n\r\nOn a certain day, however, in the particular year which has now been\r\nreached by this narrative\xe2\x80\x94the month being early June\xe2\x80\x94the features of\r\nthe town excite little interest, though many visitors arrive by the\r\ntrains; some down-trains, in especial, nearly emptying themselves here.\r\nIt is the week of the Great Wessex Agricultural Show, whose vast\r\nencampment spreads over the open outskirts of the town like the tents\r\nof an investing army. Rows of marquees, huts, booths, pavilions,\r\narcades, porticoes\xe2\x80\x94every kind of structure short of a permanent\r\none\xe2\x80\x94cover the green field for the space of a square half-mile, and the\r\ncrowds of arrivals walk through the town in a mass, and make straight\r\nfor the exhibition ground. The way thereto is lined with shows, stalls,\r\nand hawkers on foot, who make a market-place of the whole roadway to\r\nthe show proper, and lead some of the improvident to lighten their\r\npockets appreciably before they reach the gates of the exhibition they\r\ncame expressly to see.\r\n\r\nIt is the popular day, the shilling day, and of the fast arriving\r\nexcursion trains two from different directions enter the two contiguous\r\nrailway stations at almost the same minute. One, like several which\r\nhave preceded it, comes from London: the other by a cross-line from\r\nAldbrickham; and from the London train alights a couple; a short,\r\nrather bloated man, with a globular stomach and small legs, resembling\r\na top on two pegs, accompanied by a woman of rather fine figure and\r\nrather red face, dressed in black material, and covered with beads from\r\nbonnet to skirt, that made her glisten as if clad in chain-mail.\r\n\r\nThey cast their eyes around. The man was about to hire a fly as some\r\nothers had done, when the woman said, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be in such a hurry,\r\nCartlett. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t so very far to the show-yard. Let us walk down the\r\nstreet into the place. Perhaps I can pick up a cheap bit of furniture\r\nor old china. It is years since I was here\xe2\x80\x94never since I lived as a\r\ngirl at Aldbrickham, and used to come across for a trip sometimes with\r\nmy young man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t carry home furniture by excursion train,\xe2\x80\x9d said, in a thick\r\nvoice, her husband, the landlord of The Three Horns, Lambeth; for they\r\nhad both come down from the tavern in that \xe2\x80\x9cexcellent, densely\r\npopulated, gin-drinking neighbourhood,\xe2\x80\x9d which they had occupied ever\r\nsince the advertisement in those words had attracted them thither. The\r\nconfiguration of the landlord showed that he, too, like his customers,\r\nwas becoming affected by the liquors he retailed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99ll get it sent, if I see any worth having,\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife.\r\n\r\nThey sauntered on, but had barely entered the town when her attention\r\nwas attracted by a young couple leading a child, who had come out from\r\nthe second platform, into which the train from Aldbrickham had steamed.\r\nThey were walking just in front of the inn-keepers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSakes alive!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Cartlett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho do you think that couple is? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you recognize the man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot from the photos I have showed you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Fawley?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well. I suppose he was inclined for a little sight-seeing like the\r\nrest of us.\xe2\x80\x9d Cartlett\xe2\x80\x99s interest in Jude whatever it might have been\r\nwhen Arabella was new to him, had plainly flagged since her charms and\r\nher idiosyncrasies, her supernumerary hair-coils, and her optional\r\ndimples, were becoming as a tale that is told.\r\n\r\nArabella so regulated her pace and her husband\xe2\x80\x99s as to keep just in the\r\nrear of the other three, which it was easy to do without notice in such\r\na stream of pedestrians. Her answers to Cartlett\xe2\x80\x99s remarks were vague\r\nand slight, for the group in front interested her more than all the\r\nrest of the spectacle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are rather fond of one another and of their child, seemingly,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncontinued the publican.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Their_ child! \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t their child,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella with a curious,\r\nsudden covetousness. \xe2\x80\x9cThey haven\xe2\x80\x99t been married long enough for it to\r\nbe theirs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut although the smouldering maternal instinct was strong enough in her\r\nto lead her to quash her husband\xe2\x80\x99s conjecture, she was not disposed on\r\nsecond thoughts to be more candid than necessary. Mr. Cartlett had no\r\nother idea than that his wife\xe2\x80\x99s child by her first husband was with his\r\ngrandparents at the Antipodes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh I suppose not. She looks quite a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are only lovers, or lately married, and have the child in charge,\r\nas anybody can see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll continued to move ahead. The unwitting Sue and Jude, the couple in\r\nquestion, had determined to make this agricultural exhibition within\r\ntwenty miles of their own town the occasion of a day\xe2\x80\x99s excursion which\r\nshould combine exercise and amusement with instruction, at small\r\nexpense. Not regardful of themselves alone, they had taken care to\r\nbring Father Time, to try every means of making him kindle and laugh\r\nlike other boys, though he was to some extent a hindrance to the\r\ndelightfully unreserved intercourse in their pilgrimages which they so\r\nmuch enjoyed. But they soon ceased to consider him an observer, and\r\nwent along with that tender attention to each other which the shyest\r\ncan scarcely disguise, and which these, among entire strangers as they\r\nimagined, took less trouble to disguise than they might have done at\r\nhome. Sue, in her new summer clothes, flexible and light as a bird, her\r\nlittle thumb stuck up by the stem of her white cotton sunshade, went\r\nalong as if she hardly touched ground, and as if a moderately strong\r\npuff of wind would float her over the hedge into the next field. Jude,\r\nin his light grey holiday-suit, was really proud of her companionship,\r\nnot more for her external attractiveness than for her sympathetic words\r\nand ways. That complete mutual understanding, in which every glance and\r\nmovement was as effectual as speech for conveying intelligence between\r\nthem, made them almost the two parts of a single whole.\r\n\r\nThe pair with their charge passed through the turnstiles, Arabella and\r\nher husband not far behind them. When inside the enclosure the\r\npublican\xe2\x80\x99s wife could see that the two ahead began to take trouble with\r\nthe youngster, pointing out and explaining the many objects of\r\ninterest, alive and dead; and a passing sadness would touch their faces\r\nat their every failure to disturb his indifference.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow she sticks to him!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94I fancy they are not\r\nmarried, or they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so much to one another as that\xe2\x80\xa6 I wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought you said he did marry her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard he was going to\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all, going to make another attempt,\r\nafter putting it off once or twice\xe2\x80\xa6 As far as they themselves are\r\nconcerned they are the only two in the show. I should be ashamed of\r\nmaking myself so silly if I were he!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see as how there\xe2\x80\x99s anything remarkable in their behaviour. I\r\nshould never have noticed their being in love, if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t said so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never see anything,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined. Nevertheless Cartlett\xe2\x80\x99s view of\r\nthe lovers\xe2\x80\x99 or married pair\xe2\x80\x99s conduct was undoubtedly that of the\r\ngeneral crowd, whose attention seemed to be in no way attracted by what\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s sharpened vision discerned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s charmed by her as if she were some fairy!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Arabella.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee how he looks round at her, and lets his eyes rest on her. I am\r\ninclined to think that she don\xe2\x80\x99t care for him quite so much as he does\r\nfor her. She\xe2\x80\x99s not a particular warm-hearted creature to my thinking,\r\nthough she cares for him pretty middling much\xe2\x80\x94as much as she\xe2\x80\x99s able to;\r\nand he could make her heart ache a bit if he liked to try\xe2\x80\x94which he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntoo simple to do. If we have come to see the show let us see it in our\r\nown way, as they do in theirs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94suppose we agree to meet somewhere in an hour\xe2\x80\x99s time\xe2\x80\x94say at that\r\nrefreshment tent over there, and go about independent? Then you can\r\nlook at what you choose to, and so can I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCartlett was not loath to agree to this, and they parted\xe2\x80\x94he proceeding\r\nto the shed where malting processes were being exhibited, and Arabella\r\nin the direction taken by Jude and Sue. Before, however, she had\r\nregained their wake a laughing face met her own, and she was confronted\r\nby Anny, the friend of her girlhood.\r\n\r\nAnny had burst out in hearty laughter at the mere fact of the chance\r\nencounter. \xe2\x80\x9cI am still living down there,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as soon as she was\r\ncomposed. \xe2\x80\x9cI am soon going to be married, but my intended couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nup here to-day. But there\xe2\x80\x99s lots of us come by excursion, though I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nlost the rest of \xe2\x80\x99em for the present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you met Jude and his young woman, or wife, or whatever she is? I\r\nsaw \xe2\x80\x99em by now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Not a glimpse of un for years!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they are close by here somewhere. Only two-and-three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe physician had produced a box from his waistcoat pocket, and\r\nArabella was induced to make the purchase.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the same time,\xe2\x80\x9d continued he, when the pills were paid for, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nhave the advantage of me, Mrs.\xe2\x80\x94 Surely not Mrs. Fawley, once Miss Donn,\r\nof the vicinity of Marygreen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.
