So her husband thought, no doubt, when he divorced her\xe2\x80\xa6 Shall we\r\nlook at the pictures, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe trio followed across the green and entered. Jude and Sue, with the\r\nchild, unaware of the interest they were exciting, had gone up to a\r\nmodel at one end of the building, which they regarded with considerable\r\nattention for a long while before they went on. Arabella and her\r\nfriends came to it in due course, and the inscription it bore was:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cModel of Cardinal College, Christminster; by J. Fawley and S. F. M.\r\nBridehead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmiring their own work,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cHow like Jude\xe2\x80\x94always\r\nthinking of colleges and Christminster, instead of attending to his\r\nbusiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey glanced cursorily at the pictures, and proceeded to the\r\nband-stand. When they had stood a little while listening to the music\r\nof the military performers, Jude, Sue, and the child came up on the\r\nother side. Arabella did not care if they should recognize her; but\r\nthey were too deeply absorbed in their own lives, as translated into\r\nemotion by the military band, to perceive her under her beaded veil.\r\nShe walked round the outside of the listening throng, passing behind\r\nthe lovers, whose movements had an unexpected fascination for her\r\nto-day. Scrutinizing them narrowly from the rear she noticed that\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s hand sought Sue\xe2\x80\x99s as they stood, the two standing close together\r\nso as to conceal, as they supposed, this tacit expression of their\r\nmutual responsiveness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSilly fools\xe2\x80\x94like two children!\xe2\x80\x9d Arabella whispered to herself\r\nmorosely, as she rejoined her companions, with whom she preserved a\r\npreoccupied silence.\r\n\r\nAnny meanwhile had jokingly remarked to Vilbert on Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s hankering\r\ninterest in her first husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the physician to Arabella, apart; \xe2\x80\x9cdo you want anything\r\nsuch as this, Mrs. Cartlett? It is not compounded out of my regular\r\npharmacop\xc5\x93ia, but I am sometimes asked for such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d He produced a\r\nsmall phial of clear liquid. \xe2\x80\x9cA love-philtre, such as was used by the\r\nancients with great effect. I found it out by study of their writings,\r\nand have never known it to fail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it made of?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Arabella curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94a distillation of the juices of doves\xe2\x80\x99 hearts\xe2\x80\x94otherwise\r\npigeons\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94is one of the ingredients. It took nearly a hundred hearts to\r\nproduce that small bottle full.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you get pigeons enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo tell a secret, I get a piece of rock-salt, of which pigeons are\r\ninordinately fond, and place it in a dovecot on my roof. In a few hours\r\nthe birds come to it from all points of the compass\xe2\x80\x94east, west, north,\r\nand south\xe2\x80\x94and thus I secure as many as I require. You use the liquid by\r\ncontriving that the desired man shall take about ten drops of it in his\r\ndrink. But remember, all this is told you because I gather from your\r\nquestions that you mean to be a purchaser. You must keep faith with\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind a bottle\xe2\x80\x94to give some friend or other to try it\r\non her young man.\xe2\x80\x9d She produced five shillings, the price asked, and\r\nslipped the phial in her capacious bosom. Saying presently that she was\r\ndue at an appointment with her husband, she sauntered away towards the\r\nrefreshment bar, Jude, his companion, and the child having gone on to\r\nthe horticultural tent, where Arabella caught a glimpse of them\r\nstanding before a group of roses in bloom.\r\n\r\nShe waited a few minutes observing them, and then proceeded to join her\r\nspouse with no very amiable sentiments. She found him seated on a stool\r\nby the bar, talking to one of the gaily dressed maids who had served\r\nhim with spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think you had enough of this business at home!\xe2\x80\x9d Arabella\r\nremarked gloomily. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely you didn\xe2\x80\x99t come fifty miles from your own\r\nbar to stick in another? Come, take me round the show, as other men do\r\ntheir wives! Dammy, one would think you were a young bachelor, with\r\nnobody to look after but yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we agreed to meet here; and what could I do but wait?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now we have met, come along,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned, ready to quarrel\r\nwith the sun for shining on her. And they left the tent together, this\r\npot-bellied man and florid woman, in the antipathetic, recriminatory\r\nmood of the average husband and wife of Christendom.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime the more exceptional couple and the boy still lingered\r\nin the pavilion of flowers\xe2\x80\x94an enchanted palace to their appreciative\r\ntaste\xe2\x80\x94Sue\xe2\x80\x99s usually pale cheeks reflecting the pink of the tinted roses\r\nat which she gazed; for the gay sights, the air, the music, and the\r\nexcitement of a day\xe2\x80\x99s outing with Jude had quickened her blood and made\r\nher eyes sparkle with vivacity. She adored roses, and what Arabella had\r\nwitnessed was Sue detaining Jude almost against his will while she\r\nlearnt the names of this variety and that, and put her face within an\r\ninch of their blooms to smell them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to push my face quite into them\xe2\x80\x94the dears!\xe2\x80\x9d she had\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I suppose it is against the rules to touch them\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t it,\r\nJude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you baby,\xe2\x80\x9d said he: and then playfully gave her a little push, so\r\nthat her nose went among the petals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe policeman will be down on us, and I shall say it was my husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfault!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she looked up at him, and smiled in a way that told so much to\r\nArabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy?\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? Because you have come to the great Wessex Agricultural Show\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nbecause _we_ have come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are always trying to make me confess to all sorts of absurdities.\r\nBecause I am improving my mind, of course, by seeing all these\r\nsteam-ploughs, and threshing-machines, and chaff-cutters, and cows, and\r\npigs, and sheep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was quite content with a baffle from his ever evasive companion.\r\nBut when he had forgotten that he had put the question, and because he\r\nno longer wished for an answer, she went on: \xe2\x80\x9cI feel that we have\r\nreturned to Greek joyousness, and have blinded ourselves to sickness\r\nand sorrow, and have forgotten what twenty-five centuries have taught\r\nthe race since their time, as one of your Christminster luminaries\r\nsays\xe2\x80\xa6 There is one immediate shadow, however\xe2\x80\x94only one.\xe2\x80\x9d And she looked\r\nat the aged child, whom, though they had taken him to everything likely\r\nto attract a young intelligence, they had utterly failed to interest.\r\n\r\nHe knew what they were saying and thinking. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very, very sorry,\r\nFather and Mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut please don\xe2\x80\x99t mind!\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. I\r\nshould like the flowers very very much, if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t keep on thinking\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d be all withered in a few days!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe unnoticed lives that the pair had hitherto led began, from the day\r\nof the suspended wedding onwards, to be observed and discussed by other\r\npersons than Arabella. The society of Spring Street and the\r\nneighbourhood generally did not understand, and probably could not have\r\nbeen made to understand, Sue and Jude\xe2\x80\x99s private minds, emotions,\r\npositions, and fears. The curious facts of a child coming to them\r\nunexpectedly, who called Jude \xe2\x80\x9cFather,\xe2\x80\x9d and Sue \xe2\x80\x9cMother,\xe2\x80\x9d and a hitch\r\nin a marriage ceremony intended for quietness to be performed at a\r\nregistrar\xe2\x80\x99s office, together with rumours of the undefended cases in\r\nthe law-courts, bore only one translation to plain minds.\r\n\r\nLittle Time\xe2\x80\x94for though he was formally turned into \xe2\x80\x9cJude,\xe2\x80\x9d the apt\r\nnickname stuck to him\xe2\x80\x94would come home from school in the evening, and\r\nrepeat inquiries and remarks that had been made to him by the other\r\nboys; and cause Sue, and Jude when he heard them, a great deal of pain\r\nand sadness.\r\n\r\nThe result was that shortly after the attempt at the registrar\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\npair went off\xe2\x80\x94to London it was believed\xe2\x80\x94for several days, hiring\r\nsomebody to look to the boy. When they came back they let it be\r\nunderstood indirectly, and with total indifference and weariness of\r\nmien, that they were legally married at last. Sue, who had previously\r\nbeen called Mrs. Bridehead now openly adopted the name of Mrs. Fawley.\r\nHer dull, cowed, and listless manner for days seemed to substantiate\r\nall this.\r\n\r\nBut the mistake (as it was called) of their going away so secretly to\r\ndo the business, kept up much of the mystery of their lives; and they\r\nfound that they made not such advances with their neighbours as they\r\nhad expected to do thereby. A living mystery was not much less\r\ninteresting than a dead scandal.\r\n\r\nThe baker\xe2\x80\x99s lad and the grocer\xe2\x80\x99s boy, who at first had used to lift\r\ntheir hats gallantly to Sue when they came to execute their errands, in\r\nthese days no longer took the trouble to render her that homage, and\r\nthe neighbouring artizans\xe2\x80\x99 wives looked straight along the pavement\r\nwhen they encountered her.\r\n\r\nNobody molested them, it is true; but an oppressive atmosphere began to\r\nencircle their souls, particularly after their excursion to the show,\r\nas if that visit had brought some evil influence to bear on them. And\r\ntheir temperaments were precisely of a kind to suffer from this\r\natmosphere, and to be indisposed to lighten it by vigorous and open\r\nstatements. Their apparent attempt at reparation had come too late to\r\nbe effective.\r\n\r\nThe headstone and epitaph orders fell off: and two or three months\r\nlater, when autumn came, Jude perceived that he would have to return to\r\njourney-work again, a course all the more unfortunate just now, in that\r\nhe had not as yet cleared off the debt he had unavoidably incurred in\r\nthe payment of the law-costs of the previous year.\r\n\r\nOne evening he sat down to share the common meal with Sue and the child\r\nas usual. \xe2\x80\x9cI am thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\xe2\x80\x99ll hold on here no\r\nlonger. The life suits us, certainly; but if we could get away to a\r\nplace where we are unknown, we should be lighter hearted, and have a\r\nbetter chance. And so I am afraid we must break it up here, however\r\nawkward for you, poor dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue was always much affected at a picture of herself as an object of\r\npity, and she saddened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I am not sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said she presently. \xe2\x80\x9cI am much depressed by the\r\nway they look at me here. And you have been keeping on this house and\r\nfurniture entirely for me and the boy! You don\xe2\x80\x99t want it yourself, and\r\nthe expense is unnecessary. But whatever we do, wherever we go, you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t take him away from me, Jude dear? I could not let him go now! The\r\ncloud upon his young mind makes him so pathetic to me; I do hope to\r\nlift it some day! And he loves me so. You won\xe2\x80\x99t take him away from me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I won\xe2\x80\x99t, dear little girl! We\xe2\x80\x99ll get nice lodgings, wherever\r\nwe go. I shall be moving about probably\xe2\x80\x94getting a job here and a job\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall do something too, of course, till\xe2\x80\x94till\xe2\x80\x94 Well, now I can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nuseful in the lettering it behoves me to turn my hand to something\r\nelse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t hurry about getting employment,\xe2\x80\x9d he said regretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant you to do that. I wish you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, Sue. The boy and yourself are\r\nenough for you to attend to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a knock at the door, and Jude answered it. Sue could hear the\r\nconversation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Mr. Fawley at home? \xe2\x80\xa6 Biles and Willis, the building contractors,\r\nsent me to know if you\xe2\x80\x99ll undertake the relettering of the ten\r\ncommandments in a little church they\xe2\x80\x99ve been restoring lately in the\r\ncountry near here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude reflected, and said he could undertake it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a very artistic job,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the messenger. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nclergyman is a very old-fashioned chap, and he has refused to let\r\nanything more be done to the church than cleaning and repairing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcellent old man!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue to herself, who was sentimentally opposed\r\nto the horrors of over-restoration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Ten Commandments are fixed to the east end,\xe2\x80\x9d the messenger went\r\non, \xe2\x80\x9cand they want doing up with the rest of the wall there, since he\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t have them carted off as old materials belonging to the contractor\r\nin the usual way of the trade.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA bargain as to terms was struck, and Jude came indoors. \xe2\x80\x9cThere, you\r\nsee,\xe2\x80\x9d he said cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cOne more job yet, at any rate, and you can\r\nhelp in it\xe2\x80\x94at least you can try. We shall have all the church to\r\nourselves, as the rest of the work is finished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext day Jude went out to the church, which was only two miles off. He\r\nfound that what the contractor\xe2\x80\x99s clerk had said was true. The tables of\r\nthe Jewish law towered sternly over the utensils of Christian grace, as\r\nthe chief ornament of the chancel end, in the fine dry style of the\r\nlast century. And as their framework was constructed of ornamental\r\nplaster they could not be taken down for repair. A portion, crumbled by\r\ndamp, required renewal; and when this had been done, and the whole\r\ncleansed, he began to renew the lettering. On the second morning Sue\r\ncame to see what assistance she could render, and also because they\r\nliked to be together.\r\n\r\nThe silence and emptiness of the building gave her confidence, and,\r\nstanding on a safe low platform erected by Jude, which she was\r\nnevertheless timid at mounting, she began painting in the letters of\r\nthe first Table while he set about mending a portion of the second. She\r\nwas quite pleased at her powers; she had acquired them in the days she\r\npainted illumined texts for the church-fitting shop at Christminster.\r\nNobody seemed likely to disturb them; and the pleasant twitter of\r\nbirds, and rustle of October leafage, came in through an open window,\r\nand mingled with their talk.\r\n\r\nThey were not, however, to be left thus snug and peaceful for long.\r\nAbout half-past twelve there came footsteps on the gravel without. The\r\nold vicar and his churchwarden entered, and, coming up to see what was\r\nbeing done, seemed surprised to discover that a young woman was\r\nassisting. They passed on into an aisle, at which time the door again\r\nopened, and another figure entered\xe2\x80\x94a small one, that of little Time,\r\nwho was crying. Sue had told him where he might find her between\r\nschool-hours, if he wished. She came down from her perch, and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay to eat my dinner in school, because they said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\ndescribed how some boys had taunted him about his nominal mother, and\r\nSue, grieved, expressed her indignation to Jude aloft. The child went\r\ninto the churchyard, and Sue returned to her work. Meanwhile the door\r\nhad opened again, and there shuffled in with a businesslike air the\r\nwhite-aproned woman who cleaned the church. Sue recognized her as one\r\nwho had friends in Spring Street, whom she visited. The church-cleaner\r\nlooked at Sue, gaped, and lifted her hands; she had evidently\r\nrecognized Jude\xe2\x80\x99s companion as the latter had recognized her. Next came\r\ntwo ladies, and after talking to the charwoman they also moved forward,\r\nand as Sue stood reaching upward, watched her hand tracing the letters,\r\nand critically regarded her person in relief against the white wall,\r\ntill she grew so nervous that she trembled visibly.\r\n\r\nThey went back to where the others were standing, talking in\r\nundertones: and one said\xe2\x80\x94Sue could not hear which\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s his wife, I\r\nsuppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome say Yes: some say No,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply from the charwoman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot? Then she ought to be, or somebody\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s very clear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve only been married a very few weeks, whether or no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA strange pair to be painting the Two Tables! I wonder Biles and\r\nWillis could think of such a thing as hiring those!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe churchwarden supposed that Biles and Willis knew of nothing wrong,\r\nand then the other, who had been talking to the old woman, explained\r\nwhat she meant by calling them strange people.\r\n\r\nThe probable drift of the subdued conversation which followed was made\r\nplain by the churchwarden breaking into an anecdote, in a voice that\r\neverybody in the church could hear, though obviously suggested by the\r\npresent situation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, it is a curious thing, but my grandfather told me a strange\r\ntale of a most immoral case that happened at the painting of the\r\nCommandments in a church out by Gaymead\xe2\x80\x94which is quite within a walk of\r\nthis one. In them days Commandments were mostly done in gilt letters on\r\na black ground, and that\xe2\x80\x99s how they were out where I say, before the\r\nowld church was rebuilded. It must have been somewhere about a hundred\r\nyears ago that them Commandments wanted doing up just as ours do here,\r\nand they had to get men from Aldbrickham to do \xe2\x80\x99em. Now they wished to\r\nget the job finished by a particular Sunday, so the men had to work\r\nlate Saturday night, against their will, for overtime was not paid then\r\nas \xe2\x80\x99tis now. There was no true religion in the country at that date,\r\nneither among pa\xe2\x80\x99sons, clerks, nor people, and to keep the men up to\r\ntheir work the vicar had to let \xe2\x80\x99em have plenty of drink during the\r\nafternoon. As evening drawed on they sent for some more themselves;\r\nrum, by all account. It got later and later, and they got more and more\r\nfuddled, till at last they went a-putting their rum-bottle and rummers\r\nupon the communion table, and drawed up a trestle or two, and sate\r\nround comfortable and poured out again right hearty bumpers. No sooner\r\nhad they tossed off their glasses than, so the story goes, they fell\r\ndown senseless, one and all. How long they bode so they didn\xe2\x80\x99t know,\r\nbut when they came to themselves there was a terrible thunder-storm\r\na-raging, and they seemed to see in the gloom a dark figure with very\r\nthin legs and a curious voot, a-standing on the ladder, and finishing\r\ntheir work. When it got daylight they could see that the work was\r\nreally finished, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t at all mind finishing it themselves. They\r\nwent home, and the next thing they heard was that a great scandal had\r\nbeen caused in the church that Sunday morning, for when the people came\r\nand service began, all saw that the Ten Commandments wez painted with\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x98nots\xe2\x80\x99 left out. Decent people wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t attend service there for a\r\nlong time, and the Bishop had to be sent for to reconsecrate the\r\nchurch. That\xe2\x80\x99s the tradition as I used to hear it as a child. You must\r\ntake it for what it is wo\xe2\x80\x99th, but this case to-day has reminded me o\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nas I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe visitors gave one more glance, as if to see whether Jude and Sue\r\nhad left the \xe2\x80\x9cnots\xe2\x80\x9d out likewise, and then severally left the church,\r\neven the old woman at last. Sue and Jude, who had not stopped working,\r\nsent back the child to school, and remained without speaking; till,\r\nlooking at her narrowly, he found she had been crying silently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, comrade!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t _bear_ that they, and everybody, should think people wicked\r\nbecause they may have chosen to live their own way! It is really these\r\nopinions that make the best intentioned people reckless, and actually\r\nbecome immoral!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever be cast down! It was only a funny story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but we suggested it! I am afraid I have done you mischief, Jude,\r\ninstead of helping you by coming!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo have suggested such a story was certainly not very exhilarating, in\r\na serious view of their position. However, in a few minutes Sue seemed\r\nto see that their position this morning had a ludicrous side, and\r\nwiping her eyes she laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is droll, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we two, of all people, with\r\nour queer history, should happen to be here painting the Ten\r\nCommandments! You a reprobate, and I\xe2\x80\x94in my condition\xe2\x80\xa6 O dear!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\xa6 And\r\nwith her hand over her eyes she laughed again silently and\r\nintermittently, till she was quite weak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we are right again, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we,\r\nlittle girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh but it is serious, all the same!\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed as she took up the\r\nbrush and righted herself. \xe2\x80\x9cBut do you see they don\xe2\x80\x99t think we are\r\nmarried? They _won\xe2\x80\x99t_ believe it! It is extraordinary!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care whether they think so or not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t take\r\nany more trouble to make them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sat down to lunch\xe2\x80\x94which they had brought with them not to hinder\r\ntime\xe2\x80\x94and having eaten it, were about to set to work anew when a man\r\nentered the church, and Jude recognized in him the contractor Willis.\r\nHe beckoned to Jude, and spoke to him apart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve just had a complaint about this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with rather\r\nbreathless awkwardness. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to go into the matter\xe2\x80\x94as of\r\ncourse I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what was going on\xe2\x80\x94but I am afraid I must ask you\r\nand her to leave off, and let somebody else finish this! It is best, to\r\navoid all unpleasantness. I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay you for the week, all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was too independent to make any fuss; and the contractor paid him,\r\nand left. Jude picked up his tools, and Sue cleansed her brush. Then\r\ntheir eyes met.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could we be so simple as to suppose we might do this!\xe2\x80\x9d said she,\r\ndropping to her tragic note. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course we ought not\xe2\x80\x94I ought not\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nhave come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea that anybody was going to intrude into such a lonely\r\nplace and see us!\xe2\x80\x9d Jude returned. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it can\xe2\x80\x99t be helped, dear; and\r\nof course I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to injure Willis\xe2\x80\x99s trade-connection by\r\nstaying.\xe2\x80\x9d They sat down passively for a few minutes, proceeded out of\r\nthe church, and overtaking the boy pursued their thoughtful way to\r\nAldbrickham.\r\n\r\nFawley had still a pretty zeal in the cause of education, and, as was\r\nnatural with his experiences, he was active in furthering \xe2\x80\x9cequality of\r\nopportunity\xe2\x80\x9d by any humble means open to him. He had joined an\r\nArtizans\xe2\x80\x99 Mutual Improvement Society established in the town about the\r\ntime of his arrival there; its members being young men of all creeds\r\nand denominations, including Churchmen, Congregationalists, Baptists,\r\nUnitarians, Positivists, and others\xe2\x80\x94Agnostics had scarcely been heard\r\nof at this time\xe2\x80\x94their one common wish to enlarge their minds forming a\r\nsufficiently close bond of union. The subscription was small, and the\r\nroom homely; and Jude\xe2\x80\x99s activity, uncustomary acquirements, and, above\r\nall, singular intuition on what to read and how to set about\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94begotten of his years of struggle against malignant stars\xe2\x80\x94had led to\r\nhis being placed on the committee.\r\n\r\nA few evenings after his dismissal from the church repairs, and before\r\nhe had obtained any more work to do, he went to attend a meeting of the\r\naforesaid committee. It was late when he arrived: all the others had\r\ncome, and as he entered they looked dubiously at him, and hardly\r\nuttered a word of greeting. He guessed that something bearing on\r\nhimself had been either discussed or mooted. Some ordinary business was\r\ntransacted, and it was disclosed that the number of subscriptions had\r\nshown a sudden falling off for that quarter. One member\xe2\x80\x94a really\r\nwell-meaning and upright man\xe2\x80\x94began speaking in enigmas about certain\r\npossible causes: that it behoved them to look well into their\r\nconstitution; for if the committee were not respected, and had not at\r\nleast, in their differences, a common standard of _conduct_, they would\r\nbring the institution to the ground. Nothing further was said in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence, but he knew what this meant; and turning to the table wrote a\r\nnote resigning his office there and then.\r\n\r\nThus the supersensitive couple were more and more impelled to go away.\r\nAnd then bills were sent in, and the question arose, what could Jude do\r\nwith his great-aunt\xe2\x80\x99s heavy old furniture, if he left the town to\r\ntravel he knew not whither? This, and the necessity of ready money,\r\ncompelled him to decide on an auction, much as he would have preferred\r\nto keep the venerable goods.\r\n\r\nThe day of the sale came on; and Sue for the last time cooked her own,\r\nthe child\xe2\x80\x99s, and Jude\xe2\x80\x99s breakfast in the little house he had furnished.\r\nIt chanced to be a wet day; moreover Sue was unwell, and not wishing to\r\ndesert her poor Jude in such gloomy circumstances, for he was compelled\r\nto stay awhile, she acted on the suggestion of the auctioneer\xe2\x80\x99s man,\r\nand ensconced herself in an upper room, which could be emptied of its\r\neffects, and so kept closed to the bidders. Here Jude discovered her;\r\nand with the child, and their few trunks, baskets, and bundles, and two\r\nchairs and a table that were not in the sale, the two sat in meditative\r\ntalk.\r\n\r\nFootsteps began stamping up and down the bare stairs, the comers\r\ninspecting the goods, some of which were of so quaint and ancient a\r\nmake as to acquire an adventitious value as art. Their door was tried\r\nonce or twice, and to guard themselves against intrusion Jude wrote\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrivate\xe2\x80\x9d on a scrap of paper, and stuck it upon the panel.\r\n\r\nThey soon found that, instead of the furniture, their own personal\r\nhistories and past conduct began to be discussed to an unexpected and\r\nintolerable extent by the intending bidders. It was not till now that\r\nthey really discovered what a fools\xe2\x80\x99 paradise of supposed unrecognition\r\nthey had been living in of late. Sue silently took her companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand, and with eyes on each other they heard these passing remarks\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nquaint and mysterious personality of Father Time being a subject which\r\nformed a large ingredient in the hints and innuendoes. At length the\r\nauction began in the room below, whence they could hear each familiar\r\narticle knocked down, the highly prized ones cheaply, the unconsidered\r\nat an unexpected price.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople don\xe2\x80\x99t understand us,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed heavily. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad we have\r\ndecided to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe question is, where to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ought to be to London. There one can live as one chooses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not London, dear! I know it well. We should be unhappy there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause Arabella is there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the chief reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut in the country I shall always be uneasy lest there should be some\r\nmore of our late experience. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t care to lessen it by\r\nexplaining, for one thing, all about the boy\xe2\x80\x99s history. To cut him off\r\nfrom his past I have determined to keep silence. I am sickened of\r\necclesiastical work now; and I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to accept it, if offered\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to have learnt classic. Gothic is barbaric art, after all.\r\nPugin was wrong, and Wren was right. Remember the interior of\r\nChristminster Cathedral\xe2\x80\x94almost the first place in which we looked in\r\neach other\xe2\x80\x99s faces. Under the picturesqueness of those Norman details\r\none can see the grotesque childishness of uncouth people trying to\r\nimitate the vanished Roman forms, remembered by dim tradition only.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94you have half-converted me to that view by what you have said\r\nbefore. But one can work, and despise what one does. I must do\r\nsomething, if not church-gothic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish we could both follow an occupation in which personal\r\ncircumstances don\xe2\x80\x99t count,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, smiling up wistfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI am as\r\ndisqualified for teaching as you are for ecclesiastical art.
