I grew up\r\nin the baking business with aunt, you know. But even a baker must be\r\nconventional, to get customers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnless he keeps a cake and gingerbread stall at markets and fairs,\r\nwhere people are gloriously indifferent to everything except the\r\nquality of the goods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir thoughts were diverted by the voice of the auctioneer: \xe2\x80\x9cNow this\r\nantique oak settle\xe2\x80\x94a unique example of old English furniture, worthy\r\nthe attention of all collectors!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was my great-grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish we could have\r\nkept the poor old thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne by one the articles went, and the afternoon passed away. Jude and\r\nthe other two were getting tired and hungry, but after the conversation\r\nthey had heard they were shy of going out while the purchasers were in\r\ntheir line of retreat. However, the later lots drew on, and it became\r\nnecessary to emerge into the rain soon, to take on Sue\xe2\x80\x99s things to\r\ntheir temporary lodging.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the next lot: two pairs of pigeons, all alive and plump\xe2\x80\x94a nice pie\r\nfor somebody for next Sunday\xe2\x80\x99s dinner!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe impending sale of these birds had been the most trying suspense of\r\nthe whole afternoon. They were Sue\xe2\x80\x99s pets, and when it was found that\r\nthey could not possibly be kept, more sadness was caused than by\r\nparting from all the furniture. Sue tried to think away her tears as\r\nshe heard the trifling sum that her dears were deemed to be worth\r\nadvanced by small stages to the price at which they were finally\r\nknocked down. The purchaser was a neighbouring poulterer, and they were\r\nunquestionably doomed to die before the next market day.\r\n\r\nNoting her dissembled distress Jude kissed her, and said it was time to\r\ngo and see if the lodgings were ready. He would go on with the boy, and\r\nfetch her soon.\r\n\r\nWhen she was left alone she waited patiently, but Jude did not come\r\nback. At last she started, the coast being clear, and on passing the\r\npoulterer\xe2\x80\x99s shop, not far off, she saw her pigeons in a hamper by the\r\ndoor. An emotion at sight of them, assisted by the growing dusk of\r\nevening, caused her to act on impulse, and first looking around her\r\nquickly, she pulled out the peg which fastened down the cover, and went\r\non. The cover was lifted from within, and the pigeons flew away with a\r\nclatter that brought the chagrined poulterer cursing and swearing to\r\nthe door.\r\n\r\nSue reached the lodging trembling, and found Jude and the boy making it\r\ncomfortable for her. \xe2\x80\x9cDo the buyers pay before they bring away the\r\nthings?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked breathlessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think. Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, then, I\xe2\x80\x99ve done such a wicked thing!\xe2\x80\x9d And she explained, in\r\nbitter contrition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to pay the poulterer for them, if he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t catch them,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cBut never mind. Don\xe2\x80\x99t fret about it, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was so foolish of me! Oh why should Nature\xe2\x80\x99s law be mutual\r\nbutchery!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it so, Mother?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the boy intently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue vehemently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they must take their chance, now, poor things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cAs\r\nsoon as the sale-account is wound up, and our bills paid, we go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do we go to?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Time, in suspense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must sail under sealed orders, that nobody may trace us\xe2\x80\xa6 We mustn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo to Alfredston, or to Melchester, or to Shaston, or to Christminster.\r\nApart from those we may go anywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy mustn\xe2\x80\x99t we go there, Father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause of a cloud that has gathered over us; though \xe2\x80\x98we have wronged\r\nno man, corrupted no man, defrauded no man!\xe2\x80\x99 Though perhaps we have\r\n\xe2\x80\x98done that which was right in our own eyes.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nFrom that week Jude Fawley and Sue walked no more in the town of\r\nAldbrickham.\r\n\r\nWhither they had gone nobody knew, chiefly because nobody cared to\r\nknow. Any one sufficiently curious to trace the steps of such an\r\nobscure pair might have discovered without great trouble that they had\r\ntaken advantage of his adaptive craftsmanship to enter on a shifting,\r\nalmost nomadic, life, which was not without its pleasantness for a\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nWherever Jude heard of free-stone work to be done, thither he went,\r\nchoosing by preference places remote from his old haunts and Sue\xe2\x80\x99s. He\r\nlaboured at a job, long or briefly, till it was finished; and then\r\nmoved on.\r\n\r\nTwo whole years and a half passed thus. Sometimes he might have been\r\nfound shaping the mullions of a country mansion, sometimes setting the\r\nparapet of a town-hall, sometimes ashlaring an hotel at Sandbourne,\r\nsometimes a museum at Casterbridge, sometimes as far down as Exonbury,\r\nsometimes at Stoke-Barehills. Later still he was at Kennetbridge, a\r\nthriving town not more than a dozen miles south of Marygreen, this\r\nbeing his nearest approach to the village where he was known; for he\r\nhad a sensitive dread of being questioned as to his life and fortunes\r\nby those who had been acquainted with him during his ardent young\r\nmanhood of study and promise, and his brief and unhappy married life at\r\nthat time.\r\n\r\nAt some of these places he would be detained for months, at others only\r\na few weeks. His curious and sudden antipathy to ecclesiastical work,\r\nboth episcopal and noncomformist, which had risen in him when suffering\r\nunder a smarting sense of misconception, remained with him in cold\r\nblood, less from any fear of renewed censure than from an\r\nultra-conscientiousness which would not allow him to seek a living out\r\nof those who would disapprove of his ways; also, too, from a sense of\r\ninconsistency between his former dogmas and his present practice,\r\nhardly a shred of the beliefs with which he had first gone up to\r\nChristminster now remaining with him. He was mentally approaching the\r\nposition which Sue had occupied when he first met her.\r\n\r\nOn a Saturday evening in May, nearly three years after Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrecognition of Sue and himself at the agricultural show, some of those\r\nwho there encountered each other met again.\r\n\r\nIt was the spring fair at Kennetbridge, and, though this ancient\r\ntrade-meeting had much dwindled from its dimensions of former times,\r\nthe long straight street of the borough presented a lively scene about\r\nmidday. At this hour a light trap, among other vehicles, was driven\r\ninto the town by the north road, and up to the door of a temperance\r\ninn. There alighted two women, one the driver, an ordinary country\r\nperson, the other a finely built figure in the deep mourning of a\r\nwidow. Her sombre suit, of pronounced cut, caused her to appear a\r\nlittle out of place in the medley and bustle of a provincial fair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will just find out where it is, Anny,\xe2\x80\x9d said the widow-lady to her\r\ncompanion, when the horse and cart had been taken by a man who came\r\nforward: \xe2\x80\x9cand then I\xe2\x80\x99ll come back, and meet you here; and we\xe2\x80\x99ll go in\r\nand have something to eat and drink. I begin to feel quite a sinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith all my heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other. \xe2\x80\x9cThough I would sooner have put up\r\nat the Chequers or The Jack. You can\xe2\x80\x99t get much at these temperance\r\nhouses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t you give way to gluttonous desires, my child,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nwoman in weeds reprovingly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the proper place. Very well: we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nmeet in half an hour, unless you come with me to find out where the\r\nsite of the new chapel is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to. You can tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe companions then went their several ways, the one in crape walking\r\nfirmly along with a mien of disconnection from her miscellaneous\r\nsurroundings. Making inquiries she came to a hoarding, within which\r\nwere excavations denoting the foundations of a building; and on the\r\nboards without one or two large posters announcing that the\r\nfoundation-stone of the chapel about to be erected would be laid that\r\nafternoon at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock by a London preacher of great popularity\r\namong his body.\r\n\r\nHaving ascertained thus much the immensely weeded widow retraced her\r\nsteps, and gave herself leisure to observe the movements of the fair.\r\nBy and by her attention was arrested by a little stall of cakes and\r\nginger-breads, standing between the more pretentious erections of\r\ntrestles and canvas. It was covered with an immaculate cloth, and\r\ntended by a young woman apparently unused to the business, she being\r\naccompanied by a boy with an octogenarian face, who assisted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my\xe2\x80\x94senses!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the widow to herself. \xe2\x80\x9cHis wife Sue\xe2\x80\x94if she\r\nis so!\xe2\x80\x9d She drew nearer to the stall. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do, Mrs. Fawley?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid blandly.\r\n\r\nSue changed colour and recognized Arabella through the crape veil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you, Mrs. Cartlett?\xe2\x80\x9d she said stiffly. And then perceiving\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s garb her voice grew sympathetic in spite of herself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x94you have lost\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor husband. Yes. He died suddenly, six weeks ago, leaving me none\r\ntoo well off, though he was a kind husband to me. But whatever profit\r\nthere is in public-house keeping goes to them that brew the liquors,\r\nand not to them that retail \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\xa6 And you, my little old man! You don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow me, I expect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do. You be the woman I thought wer my mother for a bit, till I\r\nfound you wasn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Father Time, who had learned to use the\r\nWessex tongue quite naturally by now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Never mind. I am a friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJuey,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cgo down to the station platform with this\r\ntray\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s another train coming in, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he was gone Arabella continued: \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll never be a beauty, will he,\r\npoor chap! Does he know I am his mother really?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He thinks there is some mystery about his parentage\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\r\nJude is going to tell him when he is a little older.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do you come to be doing this? I am surprised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only a temporary occupation\xe2\x80\x94a fancy of ours while we are in a\r\ndifficulty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are living with him still?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny children?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd another coming soon, I see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue writhed under the hard and direct questioning, and her tender\r\nlittle mouth began to quiver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord\xe2\x80\x94I mean goodness gracious\xe2\x80\x94what is there to cry about? Some folks\r\nwould be proud enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not that I am ashamed\xe2\x80\x94not as you think! But it seems such a\r\nterribly tragic thing to bring beings into the world\xe2\x80\x94so\r\npresumptuous\xe2\x80\x94that I question my right to do it sometimes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it easy, my dear\xe2\x80\xa6 But you don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me why you do such a thing\r\nas this? Jude used to be a proud sort of chap\xe2\x80\x94above any business\r\nalmost, leave alone keeping a standing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps my husband has altered a little since then. I am sure he is\r\nnot proud now!\xe2\x80\x9d And Sue\xe2\x80\x99s lips quivered again. \xe2\x80\x9cI am doing this because\r\nhe caught a chill early in the year while putting up some stonework of\r\na music-hall, at Quartershot, which he had to do in the rain, the work\r\nhaving to be executed by a fixed day. He is better than he was; but it\r\nhas been a long, weary time! We have had an old widow friend with us to\r\nhelp us through it; but she\xe2\x80\x99s leaving soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am respectable too, thank God, and of a serious way of\r\nthinking since my loss. Why did you choose to sell gingerbreads?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pure accident. He was brought up to the baking business, and\r\nit occurred to him to try his hand at these, which he can make without\r\ncoming out of doors. We call them Christminster cakes. They are a great\r\nsuccess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw any like \xe2\x80\x99em. Why, they are windows and towers, and\r\npinnacles! And upon my word they are very nice.\xe2\x80\x9d She had helped\r\nherself, and was unceremoniously munching one of the cakes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. They are reminiscences of the Christminster Colleges. Traceried\r\nwindows, and cloisters, you see. It was a whim of his to do them in\r\npastry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill harping on Christminster\xe2\x80\x94even in his cakes!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Arabella.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust like Jude. A ruling passion. What a queer fellow he is, and\r\nalways will be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue sighed, and she looked her distress at hearing him criticized.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think he is? Come now; you do, though you are so fond of\r\nhim!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course Christminster is a sort of fixed vision with him, which I\r\nsuppose he\xe2\x80\x99ll never be cured of believing in. He still thinks it a\r\ngreat centre of high and fearless thought, instead of what it is, a\r\nnest of commonplace schoolmasters whose characteristic is timid\r\nobsequiousness to tradition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella was quizzing Sue with more regard of how she was speaking than\r\nof what she was saying. \xe2\x80\x9cHow odd to hear a woman selling cakes talk\r\nlike that!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you go back to school-keeping?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cThey won\xe2\x80\x99t have me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause of the divorce, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat and other things. And there is no reason to wish it. We gave up\r\nall ambition, and were never so happy in our lives till his illness\r\ncame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere in Kennetbridge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s manner showed Arabella that her random guess was right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere comes the boy back again,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cMy boy and\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s eyes darted a spark. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t throw that in my face!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94though I half-feel as if I should like to have him with me!\r\n\xe2\x80\xa6 But Lord, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to take him from \xe2\x80\x99ee\xe2\x80\x94ever I should sin to\r\nspeak so profane\xe2\x80\x94though I should think you must have enough of your\r\nown! He\xe2\x80\x99s in very good hands, that I know; and I am not the woman to\r\nfind fault with what the Lord has ordained. I\xe2\x80\x99ve reached a more\r\nresigned frame of mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed! I wish I had been able to do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should try,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the widow, from the serene heights of a soul\r\nconscious not only of spiritual but of social superiority. \xe2\x80\x9cI make no\r\nboast of my awakening, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not what I was. After Cartlett\xe2\x80\x99s death I\r\nwas passing the chapel in the street next ours, and went into it for\r\nshelter from a shower of rain. I felt a need of some sort of support\r\nunder my loss, and, as \xe2\x80\x99twas righter than gin, I took to going there\r\nregular, and found it a great comfort. But I\xe2\x80\x99ve left London now, you\r\nknow, and at present I am living at Alfredston, with my friend Anny, to\r\nbe near my own old country. I\xe2\x80\x99m not come here to the fair to-day.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s to be the foundation-stone of a new chapel laid this afternoon\r\nby a popular London preacher, and I drove over with Anny. Now I must go\r\nback to meet her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Arabella wished Sue good-bye, and went on.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon Sue and the other people bustling about Kennetbridge\r\nfair could hear singing inside the placarded hoarding farther down the\r\nstreet. Those who peeped through the opening saw a crowd of persons in\r\nbroadcloth, with hymn-books in their hands, standing round the\r\nexcavations for the new chapel-walls. Arabella Cartlett and her weeds\r\nstood among them. She had a clear, powerful voice, which could be\r\ndistinctly heard with the rest, rising and falling to the tune, her\r\ninflated bosom being also seen doing likewise.\r\n\r\nIt was two hours later on the same day that Anny and Mrs. Cartlett,\r\nhaving had tea at the Temperance Hotel, started on their return journey\r\nacross the high and open country which stretches between Kennetbridge\r\nand Alfredston. Arabella was in a thoughtful mood; but her thoughts\r\nwere not of the new chapel, as Anny at first surmised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94it is something else,\xe2\x80\x9d at last said Arabella sullenly. \xe2\x80\x9cI came here\r\nto-day never thinking of anybody but poor Cartlett, or of anything but\r\nspreading the Gospel by means of this new tabernacle they\xe2\x80\x99ve begun this\r\nafternoon. But something has happened to turn my mind another way\r\nquite. Anny, I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard of un again, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen _her_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard of Jude, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen his wife. And ever since, do what I\r\nwill, and though I sung the hymns wi\xe2\x80\x99 all my strength, I have not been\r\nable to help thinking about \xe2\x80\x99n; which I\xe2\x80\x99ve no right to do as a chapel\r\nmember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t ye fix your mind upon what was said by the London preacher\r\nto-day, and try to get rid of your wandering fancies that way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do. But my wicked heart will ramble off in spite of myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I know what it is to have a wanton mind o\xe2\x80\x99 my own, too! If you\r\non\xe2\x80\x99y knew what I do dream sometimes o\xe2\x80\x99 nights quite against my wishes,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d say I had my struggles!\xe2\x80\x9d (Anny, too, had grown rather serious of\r\nlate, her lover having jilted her.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I do about it?\xe2\x80\x9d urged Arabella morbidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou could take a lock of your late-lost husband\xe2\x80\x99s hair, and have it\r\nmade into a mourning brooch, and look at it every hour of the day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t a morsel!\xe2\x80\x94and if I had \xe2\x80\x99twould be no good\xe2\x80\xa6 After all that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsaid about the comforts of this religion, I wish I had Jude back\r\nagain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must fight valiant against the feeling, since he\xe2\x80\x99s another\xe2\x80\x99s. And\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard that another good thing for it, when it afflicts volupshious\r\nwidows, is to go to your husband\xe2\x80\x99s grave in the dusk of evening, and\r\nstand a long while a-bowed down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh! I know as well as you what I should do; only I don\xe2\x80\x99t do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey drove in silence along the straight road till they were within the\r\nhorizon of Marygreen, which lay not far to the left of their route.\r\nThey came to the junction of the highway and the cross-lane leading to\r\nthat village, whose church-tower could be seen athwart the hollow. When\r\nthey got yet farther on, and were passing the lonely house in which\r\nArabella and Jude had lived during the first months of their marriage,\r\nand where the pig-killing had taken place, she could control herself no\r\nlonger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s more mine than hers!\xe2\x80\x9d she burst out. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat right has she to him,\r\nI should like to know! I\xe2\x80\x99d take him from her if I could!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFie, Abby! And your husband only six weeks gone! Pray against it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe damned if I do! Feelings are feelings! I won\xe2\x80\x99t be a creeping\r\nhypocrite any longer\xe2\x80\x94so there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella had hastily drawn from her pocket a bundle of tracts which she\r\nhad brought with her to distribute at the fair, and of which she had\r\ngiven away several. As she spoke she flung the whole remainder of the\r\npacket into the hedge. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve tried that sort o\xe2\x80\x99 physic and have failed\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 it. I must be as I was born!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! You be excited, dear! Now you come along home quiet, and have a\r\ncup of tea, and don\xe2\x80\x99t let us talk about un no more. We won\xe2\x80\x99t come out\r\nthis road again, as it leads to where he is, because it inflames \xe2\x80\x99ee\r\nso. You\xe2\x80\x99ll be all right again soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella did calm herself down by degrees; and they crossed the\r\nridge-way. When they began to descend the long, straight hill, they saw\r\nplodding along in front of them an elderly man of spare stature and\r\nthoughtful gait. In his hand he carried a basket; and there was a touch\r\nof slovenliness in his attire, together with that indefinable something\r\nin his whole appearance which suggested one who was his own\r\nhousekeeper, purveyor, confidant, and friend, through possessing nobody\r\nelse at all in the world to act in those capacities for him. The\r\nremainder of the journey was down-hill, and guessing him to be going to\r\nAlfredston they offered him a lift, which he accepted.\r\n\r\nArabella looked at him, and looked again, till at length she spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t mistake I am talking to Mr. Phillotson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wayfarer faced round and regarded her in turn. \xe2\x80\x9cYes; my name is\r\nPhillotson,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t recognize you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember you well enough when you used to be schoolmaster out at\r\nMarygreen, and I one of your scholars. I used to walk up there from\r\nCresscombe every day, because we had only a mistress down at our place,\r\nand you taught better. But you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t remember me as I should\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x94Arabella Donn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said politely, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t recall the name.\r\nAnd I should hardly recognize in your present portly self the slim\r\nschool child no doubt you were then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I always had plenty of flesh on my bones. However, I am staying\r\ndown here with some friends at present. You know, I suppose, who I\r\nmarried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJude Fawley\xe2\x80\x94also a scholar of yours\xe2\x80\x94at least a night scholar\xe2\x80\x94for some\r\nlittle time, I think? And known to you afterwards, if I am not\r\nmistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, dear me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson, starting out of his stiffness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ Fawley\xe2\x80\x99s wife? To be sure\xe2\x80\x94he had a wife! And he\xe2\x80\x94I understood\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDivorced her\xe2\x80\x94as you did yours\xe2\x80\x94perhaps for better reasons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94he med have been right in doing it\xe2\x80\x94right for both; for I soon\r\nmarried again, and all went pretty straight till my husband died\r\nlately. But you\xe2\x80\x94you were decidedly wrong!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson, with sudden testiness. \xe2\x80\x9cI would rather not talk\r\nof this, but\xe2\x80\x94I am convinced I did only what was right, and just, and\r\nmoral. I have suffered for my act and opinions, but I hold to them;\r\nthough her loss was a loss to me in more ways than one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou lost your school and good income through her, did you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to talk of it. I have recently come back here\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nMarygreen. I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are keeping the school there again, just as formerly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pressure of a sadness that would out unsealed him. \xe2\x80\x9cI am there,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nreplied. \xe2\x80\x9cJust as formerly, no. Merely on sufferance. It was a last\r\nresource\xe2\x80\x94a small thing to return to after my move upwards, and my long\r\nindulged hopes\xe2\x80\x94a returning to zero, with all its humiliations. But it\r\nis a refuge. I like the seclusion of the place, and the vicar having\r\nknown me before my so-called eccentric conduct towards my wife had\r\nruined my reputation as a schoolmaster, he accepted my services when\r\nall other schools were closed against me. However, although I take\r\nfifty pounds a year here after taking above two hundred elsewhere, I\r\nprefer it to running the risk of having my old domestic experiences\r\nraked up against me, as I should do if I tried to make a move.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight you are. A contented mind is a continual feast. She has done no\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not doing well, you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI met her by accident at Kennetbridge this very day, and she is\r\nanything but thriving. Her husband is ill, and she anxious. You made a\r\nfool of a mistake about her, I tell \xe2\x80\x99ee again, and the harm you did\r\nyourself by dirting your own nest serves you right, excusing the\r\nliberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was innocent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut nonsense! They did not even defend the case!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was because they didn\xe2\x80\x99t care to. She was quite innocent of what\r\nobtained you your freedom, at the time you obtained it. I saw her just\r\nafterwards, and proved it to myself completely by talking to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson grasped the edge of the spring-cart, and appeared to be much\r\nstressed and worried by the information. \xe2\x80\x9cStill\xe2\x80\x94she wanted to go,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. But you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have let her. That\xe2\x80\x99s the only way with these\r\nfanciful women that chaw high\xe2\x80\x94innocent or guilty. She\xe2\x80\x99d have come round\r\nin time. We all do! Custom does it! It\xe2\x80\x99s all the same in the end!\r\nHowever, I think she\xe2\x80\x99s fond of her man still\xe2\x80\x94whatever he med be of her.\r\nYou were too quick about her. _I_ shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have let her go! I should\r\nhave kept her chained on\xe2\x80\x94her spirit for kicking would have been broke\r\nsoon enough! There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing like bondage and a stone-deaf taskmaster\r\nfor taming us women. Besides, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got the laws on your side. Moses\r\nknew. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you call to mind what he says?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for the moment, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I regret to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall yourself a schoolmaster! I used to think o\xe2\x80\x99t when they read it in\r\nchurch, and I was carrying on a bit. \xe2\x80\x98Then shall the man be guiltless;\r\nbut the woman shall bear her iniquity.\xe2\x80\x99 Damn rough on us women; but we\r\nmust grin and put up wi\xe2\x80\x99 it! Haw haw! Well; she\xe2\x80\x99s got her deserts now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson, with biting sadness. \xe2\x80\x9cCruelty is the law\r\npervading all nature and society; and we can\xe2\x80\x99t get out of it if we\r\nwould!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you forget to try it next time, old man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot answer you, madam. I have never known much of womankind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had now reached the low levels bordering Alfredston, and passing\r\nthrough the outskirts approached a mill, to which Phillotson said his\r\nerrand led him; whereupon they drew up, and he alighted, bidding them\r\ngood-night in a preoccupied mood.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime Sue, though remarkably successful in her cake-selling\r\nexperiment at Kennetbridge fair, had lost the temporary brightness\r\nwhich had begun to sit upon her sadness on account of that success.\r\nWhen all her \xe2\x80\x9cChristminster\xe2\x80\x9d cakes had been disposed of she took upon\r\nher arm the empty basket, and the cloth which had covered the standing\r\nshe had hired, and giving the other things to the boy left the street\r\nwith him. They followed a lane to a distance of half a mile, till they\r\nmet an old woman carrying a child in short clothes, and leading a\r\ntoddler in the other hand.\r\n\r\nSue kissed the children, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cHow is he now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill better!\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Edlin cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cBefore you are\r\nupstairs again your husband will be well enough\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ee trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey turned, and came to some old, dun-tiled cottages with gardens and\r\nfruit-trees. Into one of these they entered by lifting the latch\r\nwithout knocking, and were at once in the general living-room. Here\r\nthey greeted Jude, who was sitting in an arm-chair, the increased\r\ndelicacy of his normally delicate features, and the childishly\r\nexpectant look in his eyes, being alone sufficient to show that he had\r\nbeen passing through a severe illness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94you have sold them all?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, a gleam of interest lighting up\r\nhis face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Arcades, gables, east windows and all.\xe2\x80\x9d She told him the\r\npecuniary results, and then hesitated. At last, when they were left\r\nalone, she informed him of the unexpected meeting with Arabella, and\r\nthe latter\xe2\x80\x99s widowhood.\r\n\r\nJude was discomposed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94is she living here?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; at Alfredston,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue.\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s countenance remained clouded. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I had better tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe continued, kissing him anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\xa6 Dear me! Arabella not in the depths of London, but down here! It\r\nis only a little over a dozen miles across the country to Alfredston.\r\nWhat is she doing there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe told him all she knew. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has taken to chapel-going,\xe2\x80\x9d Sue added;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand talks accordingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps it is for the best that we have almost\r\ndecided to move on. I feel much better to-day, and shall be well enough\r\nto leave in a week or two. Then Mrs. Edlin can go home again\xe2\x80\x94dear\r\nfaithful old soul\xe2\x80\x94the only friend we have in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you think to go to?\xe2\x80\x9d Sue asked, a troublousness in her tones.\r\n\r\nThen Jude confessed what was in his mind. He said it would surprise\r\nher, perhaps, after his having resolutely avoided all the old places\r\nfor so long. But one thing and another had made him think a great deal\r\nof Christminster lately, and, if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind, he would like to go\r\nback there. Why should they care if they were known? It was\r\noversensitive of them to mind so much. They could go on selling cakes\r\nthere, for that matter, if he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t work. He had no sense of shame\r\nat mere poverty; and perhaps he would be as strong as ever soon, and\r\nable to set up stone-cutting for himself there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you care so much for Christminster?\xe2\x80\x9d she said pensively.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristminster cares nothing for you, poor dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I do, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. I love the place\xe2\x80\x94although I know how it\r\nhates all men like me\xe2\x80\x94the so-called self-taught\xe2\x80\x94how it scorns our\r\nlaboured acquisitions, when it should be the first to respect them; how\r\nit sneers at our false quantities and mispronunciations, when it should\r\nsay, I see you want help, my poor friend! \xe2\x80\xa6 Nevertheless, it is the\r\ncentre of the universe to me, because of my early dream: and nothing\r\ncan alter it. Perhaps it will soon wake up, and be generous. I pray so!\r\n\xe2\x80\xa6 I should like to go back to live there\xe2\x80\x94perhaps to die there! In two\r\nor three weeks I might, I think. It will then be June, and I should\r\nlike to be there by a particular day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis hope that he was recovering proved so far well grounded that in\r\nthree weeks they had arrived in the city of many memories; were\r\nactually treading its pavements, receiving the reflection of the\r\nsunshine from its wasting walls.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPart Sixth AT CHRISTMINSTER AGAIN\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\xa6 And she humbled her body greatly, and all the places of her joy she\r\nfilled with her torn hair.\xe2\x80\x9d_\xe2\x80\x94ESTHER (Apoc.).\r\n\r\n    _\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two who decline, a woman and I,_\r\n    _And enjoy our death in the darkness here.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n                \xe2\x80\x94R. BROWNING.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nOn their arrival the station was lively with straw-hatted young men,\r\nwelcoming young girls who bore a remarkable family likeness to their\r\nwelcomers, and who were dressed up in the brightest and lightest of\r\nraiment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe place seems gay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94it is Remembrance Day!\xe2\x80\x94Jude\xe2\x80\x94how\r\nsly of you\xe2\x80\x94you came to-day on purpose!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude quietly, as he took charge of the small child, and\r\ntold Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s boy to keep close to them, Sue attending to their own\r\neldest. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought we might as well come to-day as on any other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am afraid it will depress you!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking anxiously at\r\nhim up and down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t let it interfere with our business; and we have a good\r\ndeal to do before we shall be settled here. The first thing is\r\nlodgings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving left their luggage and his tools at the station they proceeded\r\non foot up the familiar street, the holiday people all drifting in the\r\nsame direction. Reaching the Fourways they were about to turn off to\r\nwhere accommodation was likely to be found when, looking at the clock\r\nand the hurrying crowd, Jude said: \xe2\x80\x9cLet us go and see the procession,\r\nand never mind the lodgings just now. We can get them afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOughtn\xe2\x80\x99t we to get a house over our heads first?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nBut his soul seemed full of the anniversary, and together they went\r\ndown Chief Street, their smallest child in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s arms, Sue leading her\r\nlittle girl, and Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s boy walking thoughtfully and silently\r\nbeside them. Crowds of pretty sisters in airy costumes, and meekly\r\nignorant parents who had known no college in their youth, were under\r\nconvoy in the same direction by brothers and sons bearing the opinion\r\nwritten large on them that no properly qualified human beings had lived\r\non earth till they came to grace it here and now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy failure is reflected on me by every one of those young fellows,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cA lesson on presumption is awaiting me to-day!\xe2\x80\x94Humiliation\r\nDay for me! \xe2\x80\xa6 If you, my dear darling, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come to my rescue, I\r\nshould have gone to the dogs with despair!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe saw from his face that he was getting into one of his tempestuous,\r\nself-harrowing moods. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been better if we had gone at once\r\nabout our own affairs, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure this sight will\r\nawaken old sorrows in you, and do no good!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94we are near; we will see it now,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nThey turned in on the left by the church with the Italian porch, whose\r\nhelical columns were heavily draped with creepers, and pursued the lane\r\ntill there arose on Jude\xe2\x80\x99s sight the circular theatre with that\r\nwell-known lantern above it, which stood in his mind as the sad symbol\r\nof his abandoned hopes, for it was from that outlook that he had\r\nfinally surveyed the City of Colleges on the afternoon of his great\r\nmeditation, which convinced him at last of the futility of his attempt\r\nto be a son of the university.\r\n\r\nTo-day, in the open space stretching between this building and the\r\nnearest college, stood a crowd of expectant people. A passage was kept\r\nclear through their midst by two barriers of timber, extending from the\r\ndoor of the college to the door of the large building between it and\r\nthe theatre.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is the place\xe2\x80\x94they are just going to pass!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude in sudden\r\nexcitement. And pushing his way to the front he took up a position\r\nclose to the barrier, still hugging the youngest child in his arms,\r\nwhile Sue and the others kept immediately behind him. The crowd filled\r\nin at their back, and fell to talking, joking, and laughing as carriage\r\nafter carriage drew up at the lower door of the college, and solemn\r\nstately figures in blood-red robes began to alight. The sky had grown\r\novercast and livid, and thunder rumbled now and then.\r\n\r\nFather Time shuddered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt do seem like the Judgment Day!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwhispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are only learned Doctors,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue.\r\n\r\nWhile they waited big drops of rain fell on their heads and shoulders,\r\nand the delay grew tedious. Sue again wished not to stay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey won\xe2\x80\x99t be long now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, without turning his head.\r\n\r\nBut the procession did not come forth, and somebody in the crowd, to\r\npass the time, looked at the fa\xc3\xa7ade of the nearest college, and said he\r\nwondered what was meant by the Latin inscription in its midst. Jude,\r\nwho stood near the inquirer, explained it, and finding that the people\r\nall round him were listening with interest, went on to describe the\r\ncarving of the frieze (which he had studied years before), and to\r\ncriticize some details of masonry in other college fronts about the\r\ncity.\r\n\r\nThe idle crowd, including the two policemen at the doors, stared like\r\nthe Lycaonians at Paul, for Jude was apt to get too enthusiastic over\r\nany subject in hand, and they seemed to wonder how the stranger should\r\nknow more about the buildings of their town than they themselves did;\r\ntill one of them said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I know that man; he used to work here\r\nyears ago\xe2\x80\x94Jude Fawley, that\xe2\x80\x99s his name! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you mind he used to be\r\nnicknamed Tutor of St. Slums, d\xe2\x80\x99ye mind?\xe2\x80\x94because he aimed at that line\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 business? He\xe2\x80\x99s married, I suppose, then, and that\xe2\x80\x99s his child he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncarrying. Taylor would know him, as he knows everybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe speaker was a man named Jack Stagg, with whom Jude had formerly\r\nworked in repairing the college masonries; Tinker Taylor was seen to be\r\nstanding near. Having his attention called the latter cried across the\r\nbarriers to Jude: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve honoured us by coming back again, my friend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have done any great things for yourself by going\r\naway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude assented to this also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept found more mouths to fill!\xe2\x80\x9d This came in a new voice, and Jude\r\nrecognized its owner to be Uncle Joe, another mason whom he had known.\r\n\r\nJude replied good-humouredly that he could not dispute it; and from\r\nremark to remark something like a general conversation arose between\r\nhim and the crowd of idlers, during which Tinker Taylor asked Jude if\r\nhe remembered the Apostles\xe2\x80\x99 Creed in Latin still, and the night of the\r\nchallenge in the public house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Fortune didn\xe2\x80\x99t lie that way?\xe2\x80\x9d threw in Joe. \xe2\x80\x9cYer powers wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nenough to carry \xe2\x80\x99ee through?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t answer them any more!\xe2\x80\x9d entreated Sue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I like Christminster!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured little Time mournfully,\r\nas he stood submerged and invisible in the crowd.\r\n\r\nBut finding himself the centre of curiosity, quizzing, and comment,\r\nJude was not inclined to shrink from open declarations of what he had\r\nno great reason to be ashamed of; and in a little while was stimulated\r\nto say in a loud voice to the listening throng generally:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a difficult question, my friends, for any young man\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nquestion I had to grapple with, and which thousands are weighing at the\r\npresent moment in these uprising times\xe2\x80\x94whether to follow uncritically\r\nthe track he finds himself in, without considering his aptness for it,\r\nor to consider what his aptness or bent may be, and re-shape his course\r\naccordingly. I tried to do the latter, and I failed. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t admit\r\nthat my failure proved my view to be a wrong one, or that my success\r\nwould have made it a right one; though that\xe2\x80\x99s how we appraise such\r\nattempts nowadays\xe2\x80\x94I mean, not by their essential soundness, but by\r\ntheir accidental outcomes. If I had ended by becoming like one of these\r\ngentlemen in red and black that we saw dropping in here by now,\r\neverybody would have said: \xe2\x80\x98See how wise that young man was, to follow\r\nthe bent of his nature!\xe2\x80\x99 But having ended no better than I began they\r\nsay: \xe2\x80\x98See what a fool that fellow was in following a freak of his\r\nfancy!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever it was my poverty and not my will that consented to be beaten.\r\nIt takes two or three generations to do what I tried to do in one; and\r\nmy impulses\xe2\x80\x94affections\xe2\x80\x94vices perhaps they should be called\xe2\x80\x94were too\r\nstrong not to hamper a man without advantages; who should be as\r\ncold-blooded as a fish and as selfish as a pig to have a really good\r\nchance of being one of his country\xe2\x80\x99s worthies. You may ridicule me\xe2\x80\x94I am\r\nquite willing that you should\xe2\x80\x94I am a fit subject, no doubt. But I think\r\nif you knew what I have gone through these last few years you would\r\nrather pity me. And if they knew\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he nodded towards the college at\r\nwhich the dons were severally arriving\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cit is just possible they would\r\ndo the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe do look ill and worn-out, it is true!\xe2\x80\x9d said a woman.\r\n\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x99s face grew more emotional; but though she stood close to Jude she\r\nwas screened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may do some good before I am dead\xe2\x80\x94be a sort of success as a\r\nfrightful example of what not to do; and so illustrate a moral story,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncontinued Jude, beginning to grow bitter, though he had opened serenely\r\nenough. \xe2\x80\x9cI was, perhaps, after all, a paltry victim to the spirit of\r\nmental and social restlessness that makes so many unhappy in these\r\ndays!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell them that!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Sue with tears, at perceiving Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstate of mind. \xe2\x80\x9cYou weren\xe2\x80\x99t that. You struggled nobly to acquire\r\nknowledge, and only the meanest souls in the world would blame you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude shifted the child into a more easy position on his arm, and\r\nconcluded: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what I appear, a sick and poor man, is not the worst of\r\nme. I am in a chaos of principles\xe2\x80\x94groping in the dark\xe2\x80\x94acting by\r\ninstinct and not after example. Eight or nine years ago when I came\r\nhere first, I had a neat stock of fixed opinions, but they dropped away\r\none by one; and the further I get the less sure I am. I doubt if I have\r\nanything more for my present rule of life than following inclinations\r\nwhich do me and nobody else any harm, and actually give pleasure to\r\nthose I love best. There, gentlemen, since you wanted to know how I was\r\ngetting on, I have told you. Much good may it do you! I cannot explain\r\nfurther here. I perceive there is something wrong somewhere in our\r\nsocial formulas: what it is can only be discovered by men or women with\r\ngreater insight than mine\xe2\x80\x94if, indeed, they ever discover it\xe2\x80\x94at least in\r\nour time. \xe2\x80\x98For who knoweth what is good for man in this life?\xe2\x80\x94and who\r\ncan tell a man what shall be after him under the sun?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the populace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell preached!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tinker Taylor. And privately to his neighbours:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, one of them jobbing pa\xe2\x80\x99sons swarming about here, that takes the\r\nservices when our head reverends want a holiday, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndiscoursed such doctrine for less than a guinea down. Hey? I\xe2\x80\x99ll take my\r\noath not one o\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em would! And then he must have had it wrote down for\r\n\xe2\x80\x99n. And this only a working-man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs a sort of objective commentary on Jude\xe2\x80\x99s remarks there drove up at\r\nthis moment with a belated Doctor, robed and panting, a cab whose horse\r\nfailed to stop at the exact point required for setting down the hirer,\r\nwho jumped out and entered the door. The driver, alighting, began to\r\nkick the animal in the belly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf that can be done,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, \xe2\x80\x9cat college gates in the most\r\nreligious and educational city in the world, what shall we say as to\r\nhow far we\xe2\x80\x99ve got?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOrder!\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the policemen, who had been engaged with a comrade\r\nin opening the large doors opposite the college. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep yer tongue\r\nquiet, my man, while the procession passes.\xe2\x80\x9d The rain came on more\r\nheavily, and all who had umbrellas opened them. Jude was not one of\r\nthese, and Sue only possessed a small one, half sunshade. She had grown\r\npale, though Jude did not notice it then.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go on, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, endeavouring to shelter him. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t any lodgings yet, remember, and all our things are at the\r\nstation; and you are by no means well yet. I am afraid this wet will\r\nhurt you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are coming now. Just a moment, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll go!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nA peal of six bells struck out, human faces began to crowd the windows\r\naround, and the procession of heads of houses and new Doctors emerged,\r\ntheir red and black gowned forms passing across the field of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvision like inaccessible planets across an object-glass.\r\n\r\nAs they went their names were called by knowing informants, and when\r\nthey reached the old round theatre of Wren a cheer rose high.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go that way!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jude, and though it now rained steadily he\r\nseemed not to know it, and took them round to the theatre. Here they\r\nstood upon the straw that was laid to drown the discordant noise of\r\nwheels, where the quaint and frost-eaten stone busts encircling the\r\nbuilding looked with pallid grimness on the proceedings, and in\r\nparticular at the bedraggled Jude, Sue, and their children, as at\r\nludicrous persons who had no business there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could get in!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her fervidly. \xe2\x80\x9cListen\xe2\x80\x94I may catch a\r\nfew words of the Latin speech by staying here; the windows are open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, beyond the peals of the organ, and the shouts and hurrahs\r\nbetween each piece of oratory, Jude\xe2\x80\x99s standing in the wet did not bring\r\nmuch Latin to his intelligence more than, now and then, a sonorous word\r\nin _um_ or _ibus_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m an outsider to the end of my days!\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed after a while.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99ll go, my patient Sue. How good of you to wait in the rain all\r\nthis time\xe2\x80\x94to gratify my infatuation! I\xe2\x80\x99ll never care any more about the\r\ninfernal cursed place, upon my soul I won\xe2\x80\x99t! But what made you tremble\r\nso when we were at the barrier? And how pale you are, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw Richard amongst the people on the other side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94did you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is evidently come up to Jerusalem to see the festival like the rest\r\nof us: and on that account is probably living not so very far away. He\r\nhad the same hankering for the university that you had, in a milder\r\nform. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think he saw me, though he must have heard you speaking\r\nto the crowd. But he seemed not to notice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94suppose he did. Your mind is free from worries about him now, my\r\nSue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so. But I am weak. Although I know it is all right with\r\nour plans, I felt a curious dread of him; an awe, or terror, of\r\nconventions I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in. It comes over me at times like a sort\r\nof creeping paralysis, and makes me so sad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are getting tired, Sue. Oh\xe2\x80\x94I forgot, darling! Yes, we\xe2\x80\x99ll go on at\r\nonce.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey started in quest of the lodging, and at last found something that\r\nseemed to promise well, in Mildew Lane\xe2\x80\x94a spot which to Jude was\r\nirresistible\xe2\x80\x94though to Sue it was not so fascinating\xe2\x80\x94a narrow lane\r\nclose to the back of a college, but having no communication with it.\r\nThe little houses were darkened to gloom by the high collegiate\r\nbuildings, within which life was so far removed from that of the people\r\nin the lane as if it had been on opposite sides of the globe; yet only\r\na thickness of wall divided them. Two or three of the houses had\r\nnotices of rooms to let, and the newcomers knocked at the door of one,\r\nwhich a woman opened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94listen!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude suddenly, instead of addressing her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy the bells\xe2\x80\x94what church can that be? The tones are familiar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother peal of bells had begun to sound out at some distance off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d said the landlady tartly. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you knock to ask that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; for lodgings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, coming to himself.\r\n\r\nThe householder scrutinized Sue\xe2\x80\x99s figure a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t any to\r\nlet,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, shutting the door.\r\n\r\nJude looked discomfited, and the boy distressed. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9clet me try. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey found a second place hard by; but here the occupier, observing not\r\nonly Sue, but the boy and the small children, said civilly, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry\r\nto say we don\xe2\x80\x99t let where there are children\xe2\x80\x9d; and also closed the\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\nThe small child squared its mouth and cried silently, with an instinct\r\nthat trouble loomed. The boy sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like Christminster!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cAre the great old houses gaols?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; colleges,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude; \xe2\x80\x9cwhich perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll study in some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather not!\xe2\x80\x9d the boy rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll try again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pull my cloak more round me\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nLeaving Kennetbridge for this place is like coming from Caiaphas to\r\nPilate! \xe2\x80\xa6 How do I look now, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody would notice it now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\nThere was one other house, and they tried a third time. The woman here\r\nwas more amiable; but she had little room to spare, and could only\r\nagree to take in Sue and the children if her husband could go\r\nelsewhere. This arrangement they perforce adopted, in the stress from\r\ndelaying their search till so late. They came to terms with her, though\r\nher price was rather high for their pockets. But they could not afford\r\nto be critical till Jude had time to get a more permanent abode; and in\r\nthis house Sue took possession of a back room on the second floor with\r\nan inner closet-room for the children. Jude stayed and had a cup of\r\ntea; and was pleased to find that the window commanded the back of\r\nanother of the colleges. Kissing all four he went to get a few\r\nnecessaries and look for lodgings for himself.\r\n\r\nWhen he was gone the landlady came up to talk a little with Sue, and\r\ngather something of the circumstances of the family she had taken in.\r\nSue had not the art of prevarication, and, after admitting several\r\nfacts as to their late difficulties and wanderings, she was startled by\r\nthe landlady saying suddenly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you really a married woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue hesitated; and then impulsively told the woman that her husband and\r\nherself had each been unhappy in their first marriages, after which,\r\nterrified at the thought of a second irrevocable union, and lest the\r\nconditions of the contract should kill their love, yet wishing to be\r\ntogether, they had literally not found the courage to repeat it, though\r\nthey had attempted it two or three times. Therefore, though in her own\r\nsense of the words she was a married woman, in the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s sense she\r\nwas not.\r\n\r\nThe housewife looked embarrassed, and went downstairs. Sue sat by the\r\nwindow in a reverie, watching the rain. Her quiet was broken by the\r\nnoise of someone entering the house, and then the voices of a man and\r\nwoman in conversation in the passage below. The landlady\xe2\x80\x99s husband had\r\narrived, and she was explaining to him the incoming of the lodgers\r\nduring his absence.\r\n\r\nHis voice rose in sudden anger. \xe2\x80\x9cNow who wants such a woman here? and\r\nperhaps a confinement! \xe2\x80\xa6 Besides, didn\xe2\x80\x99t I say I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nchildren? The hall and stairs fresh painted, to be kicked about by\r\nthem! You must have known all was not straight with \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94coming like\r\nthat. Taking in a family when I said a single man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wife expostulated, but, as it seemed, the husband insisted on his\r\npoint; for presently a tap came to Sue\xe2\x80\x99s door, and the woman appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to tell you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I can\xe2\x80\x99t let you have\r\nthe room for the week after all. My husband objects; and therefore I\r\nmust ask you to go. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind your staying over to-night, as it is\r\ngetting late in the afternoon; but I shall be glad if you can leave\r\nearly in the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThough she knew that she was entitled to the lodging for a week, Sue\r\ndid not wish to create a disturbance between the wife and husband, and\r\nshe said she would leave as requested. When the landlady had gone Sue\r\nlooked out of the window again. Finding that the rain had ceased she\r\nproposed to the boy that, after putting the little ones to bed, they\r\nshould go out and search about for another place, and bespeak it for\r\nthe morrow, so as not to be so hard-driven then as they had been that\r\nday.\r\n\r\nTherefore, instead of unpacking her boxes, which had just been sent on\r\nfrom the station by Jude, they sallied out into the damp though not\r\nunpleasant streets, Sue resolving not to disturb her husband with the\r\nnews of her notice to quit while he was perhaps worried in obtaining a\r\nlodging for himself. In the company of the boy she wandered into this\r\nstreet and into that; but though she tried a dozen different houses she\r\nfared far worse alone than she had fared in Jude\xe2\x80\x99s company, and could\r\nget nobody to promise her a room for the following day. Every\r\nhouseholder looked askance at such a woman and child inquiring for\r\naccommodation in the gloom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought not to be born, ought I?\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy with misgiving.\r\n\r\nThoroughly tired at last Sue returned to the place where she was not\r\nwelcome, but where at least she had temporary shelter. In her absence\r\nJude had left his address; but knowing how weak he still was she\r\nadhered to her determination not to disturb him till the next day.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nSue sat looking at the bare floor of the room, the house being little\r\nmore than an old intramural cottage, and then she regarded the scene\r\noutside the uncurtained window. At some distance opposite, the outer\r\nwalls of Sarcophagus College\xe2\x80\x94silent, black, and windowless\xe2\x80\x94threw their\r\nfour centuries of gloom, bigotry, and decay into the little room she\r\noccupied, shutting out the moonlight by night and the sun by day. The\r\noutlines of Rubric College also were discernible beyond the other, and\r\nthe tower of a third farther off still. She thought of the strange\r\noperation of a simple-minded man\xe2\x80\x99s ruling passion, that it should have\r\nled Jude, who loved her and the children so tenderly, to place them\r\nhere in this depressing purlieu, because he was still haunted by his\r\ndream. Even now he did not distinctly hear the freezing negative that\r\nthose scholared walls had echoed to his desire.\r\n\r\nThe failure to find another lodging, and the lack of room in this house\r\nfor his father, had made a deep impression on the boy\xe2\x80\x94a brooding\r\nundemonstrative horror seemed to have seized him. The silence was\r\nbroken by his saying: \xe2\x80\x9cMother, _what_ shall we do to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue despondently. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid this will trouble\r\nyour father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish Father was quite well, and there had been room for him! Then it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t matter so much! Poor Father!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I do anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! All is trouble, adversity, and suffering!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather went away to give us children room, didn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPartly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be better to be out o\xe2\x80\x99 the world than in it, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would almost, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis because of us children, too, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it, that you can\xe2\x80\x99t get a good\r\nlodging?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94people do object to children sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen if children make so much trouble, why do people have \xe2\x80\x99em?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94because it is a law of nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we don\xe2\x80\x99t ask to be born?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what makes it worse with me is that you are not my real mother,\r\nand you needn\xe2\x80\x99t have had me unless you liked. I oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have come\r\nto \xe2\x80\x99ee\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the real truth! I troubled \xe2\x80\x99em in Australia, and I\r\ntrouble folk here. I wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been born!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that whenever children be born that are not wanted they should\r\nbe killed directly, before their souls come to \xe2\x80\x99em, and not allowed to\r\ngrow big and walk about!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue did not reply. She was doubtfully pondering how to treat this too\r\nreflective child.\r\n\r\nShe at last concluded that, so far as circumstances permitted, she\r\nwould be honest and candid with one who entered into her difficulties\r\nlike an aged friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is going to be another in our family soon,\xe2\x80\x9d she hesitatingly\r\nremarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is going to be another baby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d The boy jumped up wildly. \xe2\x80\x9cOh God, Mother, you\xe2\x80\x99ve never a-sent\r\nfor another; and such trouble with what you\xe2\x80\x99ve got!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have, I am sorry to say!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Sue, her eyes glistening\r\nwith suspended tears.\r\n\r\nThe boy burst out weeping. \xe2\x80\x9cOh you don\xe2\x80\x99t care, you don\xe2\x80\x99t care!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried in bitter reproach. \xe2\x80\x9cHow _ever_ could you, Mother, be so wicked\r\nand cruel as this, when you needn\xe2\x80\x99t have done it till we was better\r\noff, and Father well! To bring us all into _more_ trouble! No room for\r\nus, and Father a-forced to go away, and we turned out to-morrow; and\r\nyet you be going to have another of us soon! \xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x99Tis done o\xe2\x80\x99\r\npurpose!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis!\xe2\x80\x9d He walked up and down sobbing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cY-you must forgive me, little Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded, her bosom heaving\r\nnow as much as the boy\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t explain\xe2\x80\x94I will when you are older.\r\nIt does seem\xe2\x80\x94as if I had done it on purpose, now we are in these\r\ndifficulties! I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain, dear! But it\xe2\x80\x94is not quite on purpose\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t help it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it is\xe2\x80\x94it must be! For nobody would interfere with us, like that,\r\nunless you agreed! I won\xe2\x80\x99t forgive you, ever, ever! I\xe2\x80\x99ll never believe\r\nyou care for me, or Father, or any of us any more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe got up, and went away into the closet adjoining her room, in which a\r\nbed had been spread on the floor. There she heard him say: \xe2\x80\x9cIf we\r\nchildren was gone there\xe2\x80\x99d be no trouble at all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think that, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, rather peremptorily. \xe2\x80\x9cBut go to\r\nsleep!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe following morning she awoke at a little past six, and decided to\r\nget up and run across before breakfast to the inn which Jude had\r\ninformed her to be his quarters, to tell him what had happened before\r\nhe went out. She arose softly, to avoid disturbing the children, who,\r\nas she knew, must be fatigued by their exertions of yesterday.\r\n\r\nShe found Jude at breakfast in the obscure tavern he had chosen as a\r\ncounterpoise to the expense of her lodging: and she explained to him\r\nher homelessness. He had been so anxious about her all night, he said.\r\nSomehow, now it was morning, the request to leave the lodgings did not\r\nseem such a depressing incident as it had seemed the night before, nor\r\ndid even her failure to find another place affect her so deeply as at\r\nfirst. Jude agreed with her that it would not be worth while to insist\r\nupon her right to stay a week, but to take immediate steps for removal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must all come to this inn for a day or two,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a\r\nrough place, and it will not be so nice for the children, but we shall\r\nhave more time to look round. There are plenty of lodgings in the\r\nsuburbs\xe2\x80\x94in my old quarter of Beersheba. Have breakfast with me now you\r\nare here, my bird. You are sure you are well? There will be plenty of\r\ntime to get back and prepare the children\xe2\x80\x99s meal before they wake. In\r\nfact, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe joined Jude in a hasty meal, and in a quarter of an hour they\r\nstarted together, resolving to clear out from Sue\xe2\x80\x99s too respectable\r\nlodging immediately. On reaching the place and going upstairs she found\r\nthat all was quiet in the children\xe2\x80\x99s room, and called to the landlady\r\nin timorous tones to please bring up the tea-kettle and something for\r\ntheir breakfast. This was perfunctorily done, and producing a couple of\r\neggs which she had brought with her she put them into the boiling\r\nkettle, and summoned Jude to watch them for the youngsters, while she\r\nwent to call them, it being now about half-past eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nJude stood bending over the kettle, with his watch in his hand, timing\r\nthe eggs, so that his back was turned to the little inner chamber where\r\nthe children lay. A shriek from Sue suddenly caused him to start round.\r\nHe saw that the door of the room, or rather closet\xe2\x80\x94which had seemed to\r\ngo heavily upon its hinges as she pushed it back\xe2\x80\x94was open, and that Sue\r\nhad sunk to the floor just within it. Hastening forward to pick her up\r\nhe turned his eyes to the little bed spread on the boards; no children\r\nwere there. He looked in bewilderment round the room. At the back of\r\nthe door were fixed two hooks for hanging garments, and from these the\r\nforms of the two youngest children were suspended, by a piece of\r\nbox-cord round each of their necks, while from a nail a few yards off\r\nthe body of little Jude was hanging in a similar manner. An overturned\r\nchair was near the elder boy, and his glazed eyes were slanted into the\r\nroom; but those of the girl and the baby boy were closed.\r\n\r\nHalf-paralyzed by the strange and consummate horror of the scene, he\r\nlet Sue lie, cut the cords with his pocket-knife and threw the three\r\nchildren on the bed; but the feel of their bodies in the momentary\r\nhandling seemed to say that they were dead. He caught up Sue, who was\r\nin fainting fits, and put her on the bed in the other room, after which\r\nhe breathlessly summoned the landlady and ran out for a doctor.\r\n\r\nWhen he got back Sue had come to herself, and the two helpless women,\r\nbending over the children in wild efforts to restore them, and the\r\ntriplet of little corpses, formed a sight which overthrew his\r\nself-command. The nearest surgeon came in, but, as Jude had inferred,\r\nhis presence was superfluous. The children were past saving, for though\r\ntheir bodies were still barely cold it was conjectured that they had\r\nbeen hanging more than an hour. The probability held by the parents\r\nlater on, when they were able to reason on the case, was that the elder\r\nboy, on waking, looked into the outer room for Sue, and, finding her\r\nabsent, was thrown into a fit of aggravated despondency that the events\r\nand information of the evening before had induced in his morbid\r\ntemperament. Moreover a piece of paper was found upon the floor, on\r\nwhich was written, in the boy\xe2\x80\x99s hand, with the bit of lead pencil that\r\nhe carried:\r\n\r\n_Done because we are too menny._\r\n\r\nAt sight of this Sue\xe2\x80\x99s nerves utterly gave way, an awful conviction\r\nthat her discourse with the boy had been the main cause of the tragedy,\r\nthrowing her into a convulsive agony which knew no abatement. They\r\ncarried her away against her wish to a room on the lower floor; and\r\nthere she lay, her slight figure shaken with her gasps, and her eyes\r\nstaring at the ceiling, the woman of the house vainly trying to soothe\r\nher.\r\n\r\nThey could hear from this chamber the people moving about above, and\r\nshe implored to be allowed to go back, and was only kept from doing so\r\nby the assurance that, if there were any hope, her presence might do\r\nharm, and the reminder that it was necessary to take care of herself\r\nlest she should endanger a coming life. Her inquiries were incessant,\r\nand at last Jude came down and told her there was no hope. As soon as\r\nshe could speak she informed him what she had said to the boy, and how\r\nshe thought herself the cause of this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was in his nature to do it. The Doctor says there\r\nare such boys springing up amongst us\xe2\x80\x94boys of a sort unknown in the\r\nlast generation\xe2\x80\x94the outcome of new views of life. They seem to see all\r\nits terrors before they are old enough to have staying power to resist\r\nthem. He says it is the beginning of the coming universal wish not to\r\nlive. He\xe2\x80\x99s an advanced man, the Doctor: but he can give no consolation\r\nto\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude had kept back his own grief on account of her; but he now broke\r\ndown; and this stimulated Sue to efforts of sympathy which in some\r\ndegree distracted her from her poignant self-reproach. When everybody\r\nwas gone, she was allowed to see the children.\r\n\r\nThe boy\xe2\x80\x99s face expressed the whole tale of their situation. On that\r\nlittle shape had converged all the inauspiciousness and shadow which\r\nhad darkened the first union of Jude, and all the accidents, mistakes,\r\nfears, errors of the last. He was their nodal point, their focus, their\r\nexpression in a single term. For the rashness of those parents he had\r\ngroaned, for their ill assortment he had quaked, and for the\r\nmisfortunes of these he had died.\r\n\r\nWhen the house was silent, and they could do nothing but await the\r\ncoroner\xe2\x80\x99s inquest, a subdued, large, low voice spread into the air of\r\nthe room from behind the heavy walls at the back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue, her spasmodic breathing suspended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe organ of the college chapel. The organist practising I suppose.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s the anthem from the seventy-third Psalm; \xe2\x80\x98Truly God is loving unto\r\nIsrael.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sobbed again. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, oh my babies! They had done no harm! Why should\r\nthey have been taken away, and not I!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was another stillness\xe2\x80\x94broken at last by two persons in\r\nconversation somewhere without.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are talking about us, no doubt!\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Sue. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98We are made a\r\nspectacle unto the world, and to angels, and to men!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude listened\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94they are not talking of us,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are two\r\nclergymen of different views, arguing about the eastward position. Good\r\nGod\xe2\x80\x94the eastward position, and all creation groaning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen another silence, till she was seized with another uncontrollable\r\nfit of grief. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is something external to us which says, \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nshan\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x99 First it said, \xe2\x80\x98You shan\xe2\x80\x99t learn!\xe2\x80\x99 Then it said, \xe2\x80\x98You shan\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlabour!\xe2\x80\x99 Now it says, \xe2\x80\x98You shan\xe2\x80\x99t love!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tried to soothe her by saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s bitter of you, darling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus they waited, and she went back again to her room. The baby\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfrock, shoes, and socks, which had been lying on a chair at the time of\r\nhis death, she would not now have removed, though Jude would fain have\r\ngot them out of her sight. But whenever he touched them she implored\r\nhim to let them lie, and burst out almost savagely at the woman of the\r\nhouse when she also attempted to put them away.\r\n\r\nJude dreaded her dull apathetic silences almost more than her\r\nparoxysms. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak to me, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out, after one\r\nof these. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t turn away from me! I talked to the child as one should only talk to people of\r\nmature age. I said the world was against us, that it was better to be\r\nout of life than in it at this price; and he took it literally. And I\r\ntold him I was going to have another child. It upset him. Oh how\r\nbitterly he upbraided me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you do it, Sue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell. It was that I wanted to be truthful. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear\r\ndeceiving him as to the facts of life. And yet I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t truthful, for\r\nwith a false delicacy I told him too obscurely.\xe2\x80\x94Why was I half-wiser\r\nthan my fellow-women? And not entirely wiser! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell him\r\npleasant untruths, instead of half-realities? It was my want of\r\nself-control, so that I could neither conceal things nor reveal them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour plan might have been a good one for the majority of cases; only\r\nin our peculiar case it chanced to work badly perhaps. He must have\r\nknown sooner or later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I was just making my baby darling a new frock; and now I shall\r\nnever see him in it, and never talk to him any more! \xe2\x80\xa6 My eyes are so\r\nswollen that I can scarcely see; and yet little more than a year ago I\r\ncalled myself happy! We went about loving each other too much\xe2\x80\x94indulging\r\nourselves to utter selfishness with each other! We said\xe2\x80\x94do you\r\nremember?\xe2\x80\x94that we would make a virtue of joy. I said it was Nature\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintention, Nature\xe2\x80\x99s law and _raison d\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xaatre_ that we should be joyful in\r\nwhat instincts she afforded us\xe2\x80\x94instincts which civilization had taken\r\nupon itself to thwart. What dreadful things I said! And now Fate has\r\ngiven us this stab in the back for being such fools as to take Nature\r\nat her word!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sank into a quiet contemplation, till she said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is best,\r\nperhaps, that they should be gone.\xe2\x80\x94Yes\xe2\x80\x94I see it is! Better that they\r\nshould be plucked fresh than stay to wither away miserably!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cSome say that the elders should rejoice when\r\ntheir children die in infancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they don\xe2\x80\x99t know! \xe2\x80\xa6 Oh my babies, my babies, could you be alive\r\nnow! You may say the boy wished to be out of life, or he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\ndone it. It was not unreasonable for him to die: it was part of his\r\nincurably sad nature, poor little fellow! But then the others\xe2\x80\x94my _own_\r\nchildren and yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Sue looked at the hanging little frock and at the socks and\r\nshoes; and her figure quivered like a string. \xe2\x80\x9cI am a pitiable\r\ncreature,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cgood neither for earth nor heaven any more!
