What ought to be done?\xe2\x80\x9d She stared at\r\nJude, and tightly held his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing can be done,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cThings are as they are, and will be\r\nbrought to their destined issue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe paused. \xe2\x80\x9cYes! Who said that?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked heavily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt comes in the chorus of the _Agamemnon_. It has been in my mind\r\ncontinually since this happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor Jude\xe2\x80\x94how you\xe2\x80\x99ve missed everything!\xe2\x80\x94you more than I, for I did\r\nget you! To think you should know that by your unassisted reading, and\r\nyet be in poverty and despair!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter such momentary diversions her grief would return in a wave.\r\n\r\nThe jury duly came and viewed the bodies, the inquest was held; and\r\nnext arrived the melancholy morning of the funeral. Accounts in the\r\nnewspapers had brought to the spot curious idlers, who stood apparently\r\ncounting the window-panes and the stones of the walls. Doubt of the\r\nreal relations of the couple added zest to their curiosity. Sue had\r\ndeclared that she would follow the two little ones to the grave, but at\r\nthe last moment she gave way, and the coffins were quietly carried out\r\nof the house while she was lying down. Jude got into the vehicle, and\r\nit drove away, much to the relief of the landlord, who now had only Sue\r\nand her luggage remaining on his hands, which he hoped to be also clear\r\nof later on in the day, and so to have freed his house from the\r\nexasperating notoriety it had acquired during the week through his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s unlucky admission of these strangers. In the afternoon he\r\nprivately consulted with the owner of the house, and they agreed that\r\nif any objection to it arose from the tragedy which had occurred there\r\nthey would try to get its number changed.\r\n\r\nWhen Jude had seen the two little boxes\xe2\x80\x94one containing little Jude, and\r\nthe other the two smallest\xe2\x80\x94deposited in the earth he hastened back to\r\nSue, who was still in her room, and he therefore did not disturb her\r\njust then. Feeling anxious, however, he went again about four o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\nThe woman thought she was still lying down, but returned to him to say\r\nthat she was not in her bedroom after all. Her hat and jacket, too,\r\nwere missing: she had gone out. Jude hurried off to the public house\r\nwhere he was sleeping. She had not been there. Then bethinking himself\r\nof possibilities he went along the road to the cemetery, which he\r\nentered, and crossed to where the interments had recently taken place.\r\nThe idlers who had followed to the spot by reason of the tragedy were\r\nall gone now. A man with a shovel in his hands was attempting to earth\r\nin the common grave of the three children, but his arm was held back by\r\nan expostulating woman who stood in the half-filled hole. It was Sue,\r\nwhose coloured clothing, which she had never thought of changing for\r\nthe mourning he had bought, suggested to the eye a deeper grief than\r\nthe conventional garb of bereavement could express.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s filling them in, and he shan\xe2\x80\x99t till I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen my little ones\r\nagain!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried wildly when she saw Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to see them once\r\nmore. Oh Jude\xe2\x80\x94please Jude\xe2\x80\x94I want to see them! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you would\r\nlet them be taken away while I was asleep! You said perhaps I should\r\nsee them once more before they were screwed down; and then you didn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nbut took them away! Oh Jude, you are cruel to me too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s been wanting me to dig out the grave again, and let her get to\r\nthe coffins,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man with the spade. \xe2\x80\x9cShe ought to be took home,\r\nby the look o\xe2\x80\x99 her. She is hardly responsible, poor thing, seemingly.\r\nCan\xe2\x80\x99t dig \xe2\x80\x99em up again now, ma\xe2\x80\x99am. Do ye go home with your husband, and\r\ntake it quiet, and thank God that there\xe2\x80\x99ll be another soon to swage yer\r\ngrief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sue kept asking piteously: \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t I see them once more\xe2\x80\x94just once!\r\nCan\xe2\x80\x99t I? Only just one little minute, Jude? It would not take long! And\r\nI should be so glad, Jude! I will be so good, and not disobey you ever\r\nany more, Jude, if you will let me? I would go home quietly afterwards,\r\nand not want to see them any more! Can\xe2\x80\x99t I? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus she went on. Jude was thrown into such acute sorrow that he almost\r\nfelt he would try to get the man to accede. But it could do no good,\r\nand might make her still worse; and he saw that it was imperative to\r\nget her home at once. So he coaxed her, and whispered tenderly, and put\r\nhis arm round her to support her; till she helplessly gave in, and was\r\ninduced to leave the cemetery.\r\n\r\nHe wished to obtain a fly to take her back in, but economy being so\r\nimperative she deprecated his doing so, and they walked along slowly,\r\nJude in black crape, she in brown and red clothing. They were to have\r\ngone to a new lodging that afternoon, but Jude saw that it was not\r\npracticable, and in course of time they entered the now hated house.\r\nSue was at once got to bed, and the Doctor sent for.\r\n\r\nJude waited all the evening downstairs. At a very late hour the\r\nintelligence was brought to him that a child had been prematurely born,\r\nand that it, like the others, was a corpse.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\n\r\nSue was convalescent, though she had hoped for death, and Jude had\r\nagain obtained work at his old trade. They were in other lodgings now,\r\nin the direction of Beersheba, and not far from the Church of\r\nCeremonies\xe2\x80\x94Saint Silas.\r\n\r\nThey would sit silent, more bodeful of the direct antagonism of things\r\nthan of their insensate and stolid obstructiveness. Vague and quaint\r\nimaginings had haunted Sue in the days when her intellect scintillated\r\nlike a star, that the world resembled a stanza or melody composed in a\r\ndream; it was wonderfully excellent to the half-aroused intelligence,\r\nbut hopelessly absurd at the full waking; that the First Cause worked\r\nautomatically like a somnambulist, and not reflectively like a sage;\r\nthat at the framing of the terrestrial conditions there seemed never to\r\nhave been contemplated such a development of emotional perceptiveness\r\namong the creatures subject to those conditions as that reached by\r\nthinking and educated humanity. But affliction makes opposing forces\r\nloom anthropomorphous; and those ideas were now exchanged for a sense\r\nof Jude and herself fleeing from a persecutor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must conform!\xe2\x80\x9d she said mournfully. \xe2\x80\x9cAll the ancient wrath of the\r\nPower above us has been vented upon us, His poor creatures, and we must\r\nsubmit. There is no choice. We must. It is no use fighting against\r\nGod!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only against man and senseless circumstance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I been thinking of! I am getting as\r\nsuperstitious as a savage! \xe2\x80\xa6 But whoever or whatever our foe may be, I\r\nam cowed into submission. I have no more fighting strength left; no\r\nmore enterprise. I am beaten, beaten! \xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x98We are made a spectacle unto\r\nthe world, and to angels, and to men!\xe2\x80\x99 I am always saying that now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel the same!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall we do? You are in work now; but remember, it may only be\r\nbecause our history and relations are not absolutely known\xe2\x80\xa6 Possibly,\r\nif they knew our marriage had not been formalized they would turn you\r\nout of your job as they did at Aldbrickham!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly know. Perhaps they would hardly do that. However, I think\r\nthat we ought to make it legal now\xe2\x80\x94as soon as you are able to go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think we ought?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Jude fell into thought. \xe2\x80\x9cI have seemed to myself lately,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cto belong to that vast band of men shunned by the virtuous\xe2\x80\x94the men\r\ncalled seducers. It amazes me when I think of it! I have not been\r\nconscious of it, or of any wrongdoing towards you, whom I love more\r\nthan myself. Yet I am one of those men! I wonder if any other of them\r\nare the same purblind, simple creatures as I? \xe2\x80\xa6 Yes, Sue\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what I\r\nam. I seduced you\xe2\x80\xa6 You were a distinct type\xe2\x80\x94a refined creature,\r\nintended by Nature to be left intact. But I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t leave you alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Jude!\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t reproach yourself with being\r\nwhat you are not. If anybody is to blame it is I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI supported you in your resolve to leave Phillotson; and without me\r\nperhaps you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have urged him to let you go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have, just the same. As to ourselves, the fact of our not\r\nhaving entered into a legal contract is the saving feature in our\r\nunion. We have thereby avoided insulting, as it were, the solemnity of\r\nour first marriages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSolemnity?\xe2\x80\x9d Jude looked at her with some surprise, and grew conscious\r\nthat she was not the Sue of their earlier time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a little quiver in her words, \xe2\x80\x9cI have had\r\ndreadful fears, a dreadful sense of my own insolence of action. I have\r\nthought\xe2\x80\x94that I am still his wife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichard\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God, dearest!\xe2\x80\x94why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain! Only the thought comes to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is your weakness\xe2\x80\x94a sick fancy, without reason or meaning! Don\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nit trouble you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue sighed uneasily.\r\n\r\nAs a set-off against such discussions as these there had come an\r\nimprovement in their pecuniary position, which earlier in their\r\nexperience would have made them cheerful. Jude had quite unexpectedly\r\nfound good employment at his old trade almost directly he arrived, the\r\nsummer weather suiting his fragile constitution; and outwardly his days\r\nwent on with that monotonous uniformity which is in itself so grateful\r\nafter vicissitude. People seemed to have forgotten that he had ever\r\nshown any awkward aberrancies, and he daily mounted to the parapets and\r\ncopings of colleges he could never enter, and renewed the crumbling\r\nfreestones of mullioned windows he would never look from, as if he had\r\nknown no wish to do otherwise.\r\n\r\nThere was this change in him; that he did not often go to any service\r\nat the churches now. One thing troubled him more than any other; that\r\nSue and himself had mentally travelled in opposite directions since the\r\ntragedy: events which had enlarged his own views of life, laws,\r\ncustoms, and dogmas, had not operated in the same manner on Sue\xe2\x80\x99s. She\r\nwas no longer the same as in the independent days, when her intellect\r\nplayed like lambent lightning over conventions and formalities which he\r\nat that time respected, though he did not now.\r\n\r\nOn a particular Sunday evening he came in rather late. She was not at\r\nhome, but she soon returned, when he found her silent and meditative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you thinking of, little woman?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell clearly! I have thought that we have been selfish,\r\ncareless, even impious, in our courses, you and I. Our life has been a\r\nvain attempt at self-delight. But self-abnegation is the higher road.\r\nWe should mortify the flesh\xe2\x80\x94the terrible flesh\xe2\x80\x94the curse of Adam!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has come over you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to be continually sacrificing ourselves on the altar of duty!\r\nBut I have always striven to do what has pleased me. I well deserved\r\nthe scourging I have got! I wish something would take the evil right\r\nout of me, and all my monstrous errors, and all my sinful ways!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue\xe2\x80\x94my own too suffering dear!\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s no evil woman in you. Your\r\nnatural instincts are perfectly healthy; not quite so impassioned,\r\nperhaps, as I could wish; but good, and dear, and pure. And as I have\r\noften said, you are absolutely the most ethereal, least sensual woman I\r\never knew to exist without inhuman sexlessness. Why do you talk in such\r\na changed way? We have not been selfish, except when no one could\r\nprofit by our being otherwise. You used to say that human nature was\r\nnoble and long-suffering, not vile and corrupt, and at last I thought\r\nyou spoke truly. And now you seem to take such a much lower view!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want a humble heart; and a chastened mind; and I have never had them\r\nyet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been fearless, both as a thinker and as a feeler, and you\r\ndeserved more admiration than I gave. I was too full of narrow dogmas\r\nat that time to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that, Jude! I wish my every fearless word and thought could\r\nbe rooted out of my history. Self-renunciation\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s everything! I\r\ncannot humiliate myself too much. I should like to prick myself all\r\nover with pins and bleed out the badness that\xe2\x80\x99s in me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pressing her little face against his breast as if she\r\nwere an infant. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is bereavement that has brought you to this! Such\r\nremorse is not for you, my sensitive plant, but for the wicked ones of\r\nthe earth\xe2\x80\x94who never feel it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought not to stay like this,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, when she had remained in\r\nthe position a long while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is indulgence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill on the same tack! But is there anything better on earth than\r\nthat we should love one another?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It depends on the sort of love; and yours\xe2\x80\x94ours\xe2\x80\x94is the wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t have it, Sue! Come, when do you wish our marriage to be signed\r\nin a vestry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe paused, and looked up uneasily. \xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered.\r\n\r\nNot knowing the whole of her meaning he took the objection serenely,\r\nand said nothing. Several minutes elapsed, and he thought she had\r\nfallen asleep; but he spoke softly, and found that she was wide awake\r\nall the time. She sat upright and sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a strange, indescribable perfume or atmosphere about you\r\nto-night, Sue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean not only mentally, but about your\r\nclothes, also. A sort of vegetable scent, which I seem to know, yet\r\ncannot remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is incense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIncense?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been to the service at St. Silas\xe2\x80\x99, and I was in the fumes of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94St. Silas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I go there sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed. You go there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Jude, it is lonely here in the weekday mornings, when you are\r\nat work, and I think and think of\xe2\x80\x94of my\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She stopped till she could\r\ncontrol the lumpiness of her throat. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have taken to go in there,\r\nas it is so near.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh well\xe2\x80\x94of course, I say nothing against it. Only it is odd, for you.\r\nThey little think what sort of chiel is amang them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94a sceptic, to be plain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you pain me so, dear Jude, in my trouble! Yet I know you\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it. But you ought not to say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t. But I am much surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I want to tell you something else, Jude. You won\xe2\x80\x99t be angry, will\r\nyou? I have thought of it a good deal since my babies died. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I ought to be your wife\xe2\x80\x94or as your wife\xe2\x80\x94any longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? \xe2\x80\xa6 But you _are_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom your point of view; but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course we were afraid of the ceremony, and a good many others would\r\nhave been in our places, with such strong reasons for fears. But\r\nexperience has proved how we misjudged ourselves, and overrated our\r\ninfirmities; and if you are beginning to respect rites and ceremonies,\r\nas you seem to be, I wonder you don\xe2\x80\x99t say it shall be carried out\r\ninstantly? You certainly _are_ my wife, Sue, in all but law. What do\r\nyou mean by what you said?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot? But suppose we _had_ gone through the ceremony? Would you feel\r\nthat you were then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I should not feel even then that I was. I should feel worse than I\r\ndo now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so\xe2\x80\x94in the name of all that\xe2\x80\x99s perverse, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I am Richard\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94you hinted that absurd fancy to me before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was only an impression with me then; I feel more and more convinced\r\nas time goes on that\xe2\x80\x94I belong to him, or to nobody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy good heavens\xe2\x80\x94how we are changing places!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Perhaps so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome few days later, in the dusk of the summer evening, they were\r\nsitting in the same small room downstairs, when a knock came to the\r\nfront door of the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s house where they were lodging, and in a\r\nfew moments there was a tap at the door of their room. Before they\r\ncould open it the comer did so, and a woman\xe2\x80\x99s form appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Mr. Fawley here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude and Sue started as he mechanically replied in the affirmative, for\r\nthe voice was Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nHe formally requested her to come in, and she sat down in the window\r\nbench, where they could distinctly see her outline against the light;\r\nbut no characteristic that enabled them to estimate her general aspect\r\nand air. Yet something seemed to denote that she was not quite so\r\ncomfortably circumstanced, nor so bouncingly attired, as she had been\r\nduring Cartlett\xe2\x80\x99s lifetime.\r\n\r\nThe three attempted an awkward conversation about the tragedy, of which\r\nJude had felt it to be his duty to inform her immediately, though she\r\nhad never replied to his letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just come from the cemetery,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI inquired and found\r\nthe child\xe2\x80\x99s grave. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come to the funeral\xe2\x80\x94thank you for\r\ninviting me all the same. I read all about it in the papers, and I felt\r\nI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t wanted\xe2\x80\xa6 No\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come to the funeral,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Arabella,\r\nwho, seeming utterly unable to reach the ideal of a catastrophic\r\nmanner, fumbled with iterations. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am glad I found the grave. As\r\n\xe2\x80\x99tis your trade, Jude, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to put up a handsome stone to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall put up a headstone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude drearily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was my child, and naturally I feel for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so. We all did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe others that weren\xe2\x80\x99t mine I didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel so much for, as was\r\nnatural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sigh came from the dark corner where Sue sat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had often wished I had mine with me,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mrs. Cartlett.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps \xe2\x80\x99twouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have happened then! But of course I didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to\r\ntake him away from your wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not his wife,\xe2\x80\x9d came from Sue.\r\n\r\nThe unexpectedness of her words struck Jude silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I beg your pardon, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you were!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude had known from the quality of Sue\xe2\x80\x99s tone that her new and\r\ntranscendental views lurked in her words; but all except their obvious\r\nmeaning was, naturally, missed by Arabella. The latter, after evincing\r\nthat she was struck by Sue\xe2\x80\x99s avowal, recovered herself, and went on to\r\ntalk with placid bluntness about \xe2\x80\x9cher\xe2\x80\x9d boy, for whom, though in his\r\nlifetime she had shown no care at all, she now exhibited a ceremonial\r\nmournfulness that was apparently sustaining to the conscience. She\r\nalluded to the past, and in making some remark appealed again to Sue.\r\nThere was no answer: Sue had invisibly left the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said she was not your wife?\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Arabella in another voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should she do that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot inform you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude shortly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is, isn\xe2\x80\x99t she? She once told me so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t criticize what she says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94I see! Well, my time is up. I am staying here to-night, and thought\r\nI could do no less than call, after our mutual affliction. I am\r\nsleeping at the place where I used to be barmaid, and to-morrow I go\r\nback to Alfredston. Father is come home again, and I am living with\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has returned from Australia?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude with languid curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get on there. Had a rough time of it. Mother died of\r\ndys\xe2\x80\x94what do you call it\xe2\x80\x94in the hot weather, and Father and two of the\r\nyoung ones have just got back. He has got a cottage near the old place,\r\nand for the present I am keeping house for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s former wife had maintained a stereotyped manner of strict good\r\nbreeding even now that Sue was gone, and limited her stay to a number\r\nof minutes that should accord with the highest respectability. When she\r\nhad departed Jude, much relieved, went to the stairs and called\r\nSue\xe2\x80\x94feeling anxious as to what had become of her.\r\n\r\nThere was no answer, and the carpenter who kept the lodgings said she\r\nhad not come in. Jude was puzzled, and became quite alarmed at her\r\nabsence, for the hour was growing late. The carpenter called his wife,\r\nwho conjectured that Sue might have gone to St. Silas\xe2\x80\x99 church, as she\r\noften went there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely not at this time o\xe2\x80\x99 night?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is shut.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knows somebody who keeps the key, and she has it whenever she\r\nwants it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long has she been going on with this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, some few weeks, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude went vaguely in the direction of the church, which he had never\r\nonce approached since he lived out that way years before, when his\r\nyoung opinions were more mystical than they were now. The spot was\r\ndeserted, but the door was certainly unfastened; he lifted the latch\r\nwithout noise, and pushing to the door behind him, stood absolutely\r\nstill inside. The prevalent silence seemed to contain a faint sound,\r\nexplicable as a breathing, or a sobbing, which came from the other end\r\nof the building. The floor-cloth deadened his footsteps as he moved in\r\nthat direction through the obscurity, which was broken only by the\r\nfaintest reflected night-light from without.\r\n\r\nHigh overhead, above the chancel steps, Jude could discern a huge,\r\nsolidly constructed Latin cross\xe2\x80\x94as large, probably, as the original it\r\nwas designed to commemorate. It seemed to be suspended in the air by\r\ninvisible wires; it was set with large jewels, which faintly glimmered\r\nin some weak ray caught from outside, as the cross swayed to and fro in\r\na silent and scarcely perceptible motion. Underneath, upon the floor,\r\nlay what appeared to be a heap of black clothes, and from this was\r\nrepeated the sobbing that he had heard before. It was his Sue\xe2\x80\x99s form,\r\nprostrate on the paving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\nSomething white disclosed itself; she had turned up her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94do you want with me here, Jude?\xe2\x80\x9d she said almost sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come! I wanted to be alone! Why did you intrude here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you ask!\xe2\x80\x9d he retorted in quick reproach, for his full heart\r\nwas wounded to its centre at this attitude of hers towards him. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do\r\nI come? Who has a right to come, I should like to know, if I have not!\r\nI, who love you better than my own self\xe2\x80\x94better\xe2\x80\x94far better\xe2\x80\x94than you have\r\nloved me! What made you leave me to come here alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t criticize me, Jude\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear it!\xe2\x80\x94I have often told you so.\r\nYou must take me as I am. I am a wretch\xe2\x80\x94broken by my distractions! I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _bear_ it when Arabella came\xe2\x80\x94I felt so utterly miserable I had\r\nto come away. She seems to be your wife still, and Richard to be my\r\nhusband!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they are nothing to us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear friend, they are. I see marriage differently now. My babies\r\nhave been taken from me to show me this! Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s child killing mine\r\nwas a judgement\xe2\x80\x94the right slaying the wrong. What, __ shall I do! I am\r\nsuch a vile creature\xe2\x80\x94too worthless to mix with ordinary human beings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is terrible!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude, verging on tears. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is monstrous and\r\nunnatural for you to be so remorseful when you have done no wrong!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t know my badness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe returned vehemently: \xe2\x80\x9cI do! Every atom and dreg of it! You make me\r\nhate Christianity, or mysticism, or Sacerdotalism, or whatever it may\r\nbe called, if it\xe2\x80\x99s that which has caused this deterioration in you.\r\nThat a woman-poet, a woman-seer, a woman whose soul shone like a\r\ndiamond\xe2\x80\x94whom all the wise of the world would have been proud of, if\r\nthey could have known you\xe2\x80\x94should degrade herself like this! I am glad I\r\nhad nothing to do with Divinity\xe2\x80\x94damn glad\xe2\x80\x94if it\xe2\x80\x99s going to ruin you in\r\nthis way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are angry, Jude, and unkind to me, and don\xe2\x80\x99t see how things are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen come along home with me, dearest, and perhaps I shall. I am\r\noverburdened\xe2\x80\x94and you, too, are unhinged just now.\xe2\x80\x9d He put his arm round\r\nher and lifted her; but though she came, she preferred to walk without\r\nhis support.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t dislike you, Jude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a sweet and imploring voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you as much as ever! Only\xe2\x80\x94I ought not to love you\xe2\x80\x94any more. Oh\r\nI must not any more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t own it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have made up my mind that I am not your wife! Nothing can alter it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely we are man and wife, if ever two people were in this world?\r\nNature\xe2\x80\x99s own marriage it is, unquestionably!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut not Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s. Another was made for me there, and ratified\r\neternally in the church at Melchester.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue, Sue\xe2\x80\x94affliction has brought you to this unreasonable state! After\r\nconverting me to your views on so many things, to find you suddenly\r\nturn to the right-about like this\xe2\x80\x94for no reason whatever, confounding\r\nall you have formerly said through sentiment merely! You root out of me\r\nwhat little affection and reverence I had left in me for the Church as\r\nan old acquaintance\xe2\x80\xa6 What I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand in you is your\r\nextraordinary blindness now to your old logic. Is it peculiar to you,\r\nor is it common to woman? Is a woman a thinking unit at all, or a\r\nfraction always wanting its integer? How you argued that marriage was\r\nonly a clumsy contract\xe2\x80\x94which it is\xe2\x80\x94how you showed all the objections to\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94all the absurdities! If two and two made four when we were happy\r\ntogether, surely they make four now? I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand it, I repeat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, dear Jude; that\xe2\x80\x99s because you are like a totally deaf man\r\nobserving people listening to music. You say \xe2\x80\x98What are they regarding?\r\nNothing is there.\xe2\x80\x99 But something is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a hard saying from you; and not a true parallel! You threw off\r\nold husks of prejudices, and taught me to do it; and now you go back\r\nupon yourself. I confess I am utterly stultified in my estimate of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear friend, my only friend, don\xe2\x80\x99t be hard with me! I can\xe2\x80\x99t help being\r\nas I am, I am convinced I am right\xe2\x80\x94that I see the light at last. But\r\noh, how to profit by it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked along a few more steps till they were outside the building\r\nand she had returned the key. \xe2\x80\x9cCan this be the girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude when\r\nshe came back, feeling a slight renewal of elasticity now that he was\r\nin the open street; \xe2\x80\x9ccan this be the girl who brought the pagan deities\r\ninto this most Christian city?\xe2\x80\x94who mimicked Miss Fontover when she\r\ncrushed them with her heel?\xe2\x80\x94quoted Gibbon, and Shelley, and Mill? Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s coming was the finish.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t satirize me: it cuts like a knife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe flung his arms round her and kissed her passionately there in the\r\nsilent street, before she could hinder him. They went on till they came\r\nto a little coffee-house. \xe2\x80\x9cJude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with suppressed tears,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwould you mind getting a lodging here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will\xe2\x80\x94if, if you really wish? Turning she found that Jude\r\nhad followed her, and was standing at the chamber door. She went to\r\nhim, put her hand in his, and said \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Sue! Don\xe2\x80\x99t we live here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said you would do as I wished!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Very well! \xe2\x80\xa6 Perhaps it was wrong of me to argue distastefully as\r\nI have done! Perhaps as we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t conscientiously marry at first in\r\nthe old-fashioned way, we ought to have parted. Perhaps the world is\r\nnot illuminated enough for such experiments as ours! Who were we, to\r\nthink we could act as pioneers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad you see that much, at any rate. I never deliberately\r\nmeant to do as I did. I had thought I ought in charity to let\r\nyou approach me\xe2\x80\x94that it was damnably selfish to torture you as I did my\r\nother friend. You have never loved me as I love\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94never\xe2\x80\x94never! Yours is not a passionate heart\xe2\x80\x94your heart does not\r\nburn in a flame! But he had said nothing of it at the moment\r\nto his companion Gillingham, who, being an old friend, was staying with\r\nhim at the village aforesaid, and had, indeed, suggested the day\xe2\x80\x99s trip\r\nto Christminster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you thinking of?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gillingham, as they went home. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nuniversity degree you never obtained?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson gruffly. \xe2\x80\x9cOf somebody I saw to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d In a\r\nmoment he added, \xe2\x80\x9cSusanna.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw her, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to draw your attention to her. But, as you did see her,\r\nyou should have said: \xe2\x80\x98How d\xe2\x80\x99ye do, my dear-that-was?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well. I might have. Awkward, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has taken care to set you right since, anyhow, apparently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m. That\xe2\x80\x99s a cheap sneer. I ought to have waited, unquestionably.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the end of the week, when Gillingham had gone back to his school\r\nnear Shaston, Phillotson, as was his custom, went to Alfredston market;\r\nruminating again on Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s intelligence as he walked down the long\r\nhill which he had known before Jude knew it, though his history had not\r\nbeaten so intensely upon its incline. Arrived in the town he bought his\r\nusual weekly local paper; and when he had sat down in an inn to refresh\r\nhimself for the five miles\xe2\x80\x99 walk back, he pulled the paper from his\r\npocket and read awhile. The account of the \xe2\x80\x9cstrange suicide of a\r\nstone-mason\xe2\x80\x99s children\xe2\x80\x9d met his eye.\r\n\r\nUnimpassioned as he was, it impressed him painfully, and puzzled him\r\nnot a little, for he could not understand the age of the elder child\r\nbeing what it was stated to be. However, there was no doubt that the\r\nnewspaper report was in some way true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTheir cup of sorrow is now full!\xe2\x80\x9d he said: and thought and thought of\r\nSue, and what she had gained by leaving him.\r\n\r\nArabella having made her home at Alfredston, and the schoolmaster\r\ncoming to market there every Saturday, it was not wonderful that in a\r\nfew weeks they met again\xe2\x80\x94the precise time being just after her return\r\nfrom Christminster, where she had stayed much longer than she had at\r\nfirst intended, keeping an interested eye on Jude, though Jude had seen\r\nno more of her. Phillotson was on his way homeward when he encountered\r\nArabella, and she was approaching the town.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou like walking out this way, Mrs. Cartlett?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just begun to again,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is where I lived as maid\r\nand wife, and all the past things of my life that are interesting to my\r\nfeelings are mixed up with this road. And they have been stirred up in\r\nme too, lately; for I\xe2\x80\x99ve been visiting at Christminster. Yes; I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen\r\nJude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! How do they bear their terrible affliction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a ve-ry strange way\xe2\x80\x94ve-ry strange! She don\xe2\x80\x99t live with him any\r\nlonger. I only heard of it as a certainty just before I left; though I\r\nhad thought things were drifting that way from their manner when I\r\ncalled on them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot live with her husband? Why, I should have thought \xe2\x80\x99twould have\r\nunited them more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not her husband, after all. She has never really married him\r\nalthough they have passed as man and wife so long. And now, instead of\r\nthis sad event making \xe2\x80\x99em hurry up, and get the thing done legally,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s took in a queer religious way, just as I was in my affliction at\r\nlosing Cartlett, only hers is of a more \xe2\x80\x99sterical sort than mine. And\r\nshe says, so I was told, that she\xe2\x80\x99s your wife in the eye of Heaven and\r\nthe Church\xe2\x80\x94yours only; and can\xe2\x80\x99t be anybody else\xe2\x80\x99s by any act of man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94indeed? \xe2\x80\xa6 Separated, have they!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, the eldest boy was mine\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, poor little fellow\xe2\x80\x94born in lawful wedlock, thank God. And perhaps\r\nshe feels, over and above other things, that I ought to have been in\r\nher place. I can\xe2\x80\x99t say. However, as for me, I am soon off from here.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got Father to look after now, and we can\xe2\x80\x99t live in such a hum-drum\r\nplace as this. I hope soon to be in a bar again at Christminster, or\r\nsome other big town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey parted. When Phillotson had ascended the hill a few steps he\r\nstopped, hastened back, and called her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is, or was, their address?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella gave it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. Good afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella smiled grimly as she resumed her way, and practised\r\ndimple-making all along the road from where the pollard willows begin\r\nto the old almshouses in the first street of the town.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Phillotson ascended to Marygreen, and for the first time\r\nduring a lengthened period he lived with a forward eye. On crossing\r\nunder the large trees of the green to the humble schoolhouse to which\r\nhe had been reduced he stood a moment, and pictured Sue coming out of\r\nthe door to meet him. No man had ever suffered more inconvenience from\r\nhis own charity, Christian or heathen, than Phillotson had done in\r\nletting Sue go. He had been knocked about from pillar to post at the\r\nhands of the virtuous almost beyond endurance; he had been nearly\r\nstarved, and was now dependent entirely upon the very small stipend\r\nfrom the school of this village (where the parson had got ill-spoken of\r\nfor befriending him). He had often thought of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s remarks that\r\nhe should have been more severe with Sue, that her recalcitrant spirit\r\nwould soon have been broken. Yet such was his obstinate and illogical\r\ndisregard of opinion, and of the principles in which he had been\r\ntrained, that his convictions on the rightness of his course with his\r\nwife had not been disturbed.\r\n\r\nPrinciples which could be subverted by feeling in one direction were\r\nliable to the same catastrophe in another. The instincts which had\r\nallowed him to give Sue her liberty now enabled him to regard her as\r\nnone the worse for her life with Jude. He wished for her still, in his\r\ncurious way, if he did not love her, and, apart from policy, soon felt\r\nthat he would be gratified to have her again as his, always provided\r\nthat she came willingly.\r\n\r\nBut artifice was necessary, he had found, for stemming the cold and\r\ninhumane blast of the world\xe2\x80\x99s contempt. And here were the materials\r\nready made. By getting Sue back and remarrying her on the respectable\r\nplea of having entertained erroneous views of her, and gained his\r\ndivorce wrongfully, he might acquire some comfort, resume his old\r\ncourses, perhaps return to the Shaston school, if not even to the\r\nChurch as a licentiate.\r\n\r\nHe thought he would write to Gillingham to inquire his views, and what\r\nhe thought of his, Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s, sending a letter to her. Gillingham\r\nreplied, naturally, that now she was gone it were best to let her be,\r\nand considered that if she were anybody\xe2\x80\x99s wife she was the wife of the\r\nman to whom she had borne three children and owed such tragical\r\nadventures. Probably, as his attachment to her seemed unusually strong,\r\nthe singular pair would make their union legal in course of time, and\r\nall would be well, and decent, and in order.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they won\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94Sue won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Phillotson to himself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGillingham is so matter of fact. She\xe2\x80\x99s affected by Christminster\r\nsentiment and teaching. I can see her views on the indissolubility of\r\nmarriage well enough, and I know where she got them. They are not mine;\r\nbut I shall make use of them to further mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wrote a brief reply to Gillingham. \xe2\x80\x9cI know I am entirely wrong, but\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you. As to her having lived with and had three\r\nchildren by him, my feeling is (though I can advance no logical or\r\nmoral defence of it, on the old lines) that it has done little more\r\nthan finish her education. I shall write to her, and learn whether what\r\nthat woman said is true or no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he had made up his mind to do this before he had written to his\r\nfriend, there had not been much reason for writing to the latter at\r\nall. However, it was Phillotson\xe2\x80\x99s way to act thus.\r\n\r\nHe accordingly addressed a carefully considered epistle to Sue, and,\r\nknowing her emotional temperament, threw a Rhadamanthine strictness\r\ninto the lines here and there, carefully hiding his heterodox feelings,\r\nnot to frighten her. He stated that, it having come to his knowledge\r\nthat her views had considerably changed, he felt compelled to say that\r\nhis own, too, were largely modified by events subsequent to their\r\nparting. He would not conceal from her that passionate love had little\r\nto do with his communication. It arose from a wish to make their lives,\r\nif not a success, at least no such disastrous failure as they\r\nthreatened to become, through his acting on what he had considered at\r\nthe time a principle of justice, charity, and reason.\r\n\r\nTo indulge one\xe2\x80\x99s instinctive and uncontrolled sense of justice and\r\nright, was not, he had found, permitted with impunity in an old\r\ncivilization like ours. It was necessary to act under an acquired and\r\ncultivated sense of the same, if you wished to enjoy an average share\r\nof comfort and honour; and to let crude loving kindness take care of\r\nitself.\r\n\r\nHe suggested that she should come to him there at Marygreen.\r\n\r\nOn second thoughts he took out the last paragraph but one; and having\r\nrewritten the letter he dispatched it immediately, and in some\r\nexcitement awaited the issue.\r\n\r\n\r\nA few days after a figure moved through the white fog which enveloped\r\nthe Beersheba suburb of Christminster, towards the quarter in which\r\nJude Fawley had taken up his lodging since his division from Sue. A\r\ntimid knock sounded upon the door of his abode.\r\n\r\nIt was evening\xe2\x80\x94so he was at home; and by a species of divination he\r\njumped up and rushed to the door himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you come out with me? I would rather not come in. I want to\xe2\x80\x94to\r\ntalk with you\xe2\x80\x94and to go with you to the cemetery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt had been in the trembling accents of Sue that these words came. Jude\r\nput on his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is dreary for you to be out,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you\r\nprefer not to come in, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I do. I shall not keep you long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was too much affected to go on talking at first; she, too, was now\r\nsuch a mere cluster of nerves that all initiatory power seemed to have\r\nleft her, and they proceeded through the fog like Acherontic shades for\r\na long while, without sound or gesture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she presently said, her voice now quick, now\r\nslow, \xe2\x80\x9cso that you may not hear of it by chance. I am going back to\r\nRichard. He has\xe2\x80\x94so magnanimously\xe2\x80\x94agreed to forgive all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing back? How can you go\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is going to marry me again. That is for form\xe2\x80\x99s sake, and to satisfy\r\nthe world, which does not see things as they are. But of course I _am_\r\nhis wife already. Nothing has changed that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned upon her with an anguish that was well-nigh fierce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are _my_ wife! Yes, you are. You know it. I have always\r\nregretted that feint of ours in going away and pretending to come back\r\nlegally married, to save appearances. I loved you, and you loved me;\r\nand we closed with each other; and that made the marriage. We still\r\nlove\xe2\x80\x94you as well as I\xe2\x80\x94_know_ it, Sue! Therefore our marriage is not\r\ncancelled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I know how you see it,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered with despairing\r\nself-suppression. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am going to marry him again, as it would be\r\ncalled by you. Strictly speaking you, too\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t mind my saying it,\r\nJude!\xe2\x80\x94you should take back\xe2\x80\x94Arabella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should? Good God\xe2\x80\x94what next! But how if you and I had married\r\nlegally, as we were on the point of doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have felt just the same\xe2\x80\x94that ours was not a marriage. And I\r\nwould go back to Richard without repeating the sacrament, if he asked\r\nme. But \xe2\x80\x98the world and its ways have a certain worth\xe2\x80\x99 (I suppose),\r\ntherefore I concede a repetition of the ceremony\xe2\x80\xa6 Don\xe2\x80\x99t crush all the\r\nlife out of me by satire and argument, I implore you! I was strongest\r\nonce, I know, and perhaps I treated you cruelly. But Jude, return good\r\nfor evil! I am the weaker now. Don\xe2\x80\x99t retaliate upon me, but be kind. Oh\r\nbe kind to me\xe2\x80\x94a poor wicked woman who is trying to mend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook his head hopelessly, his eyes wet. The blow of her bereavement\r\nseemed to have destroyed her reasoning faculty. The once keen vision\r\nwas dimmed. \xe2\x80\x9cAll wrong, all wrong!\xe2\x80\x9d he said huskily. \xe2\x80\x9cError\xe2\x80\x94perversity!\r\nIt drives me out of my senses. Do you care for him? Do you love him?\r\nYou know you don\xe2\x80\x99t! It will be a fanatic prostitution\xe2\x80\x94God forgive me,\r\nyes\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what it will be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t love him\xe2\x80\x94I must, must, own it, in deepest remorse! But I shall\r\ntry to learn to love him by obeying him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude argued, urged, implored; but her conviction was proof against all.\r\nIt seemed to be the one thing on earth on which she was firm, and that\r\nher firmness in this had left her tottering in every other impulse and\r\nwish she possessed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been considerate enough to let you know the whole truth, and to\r\ntell it you myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in cut tones; \xe2\x80\x9cthat you might not\r\nconsider yourself slighted by hearing of it at second hand. I have even\r\nowned the extreme fact that I do not love him. I did not think you\r\nwould be so rough with me for doing so! I was going to ask you\xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo give you away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. To send\xe2\x80\x94my boxes to me\xe2\x80\x94if you would. But I suppose you won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course I will. What\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t he coming to fetch you\xe2\x80\x94to marry you\r\nfrom here? He won\xe2\x80\x99t condescend to do that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t let him. I go to him voluntarily, just as I went away from\r\nhim. We are to be married at his little church at Marygreen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was so sadly sweet in what he called her wrong-headedness that Jude\r\ncould not help being moved to tears more than once for pity of her. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nnever knew such a woman for doing impulsive penances, as you, Sue! No\r\nsooner does one expect you to go straight on, as the one rational\r\nproceeding, than you double round the corner!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well; let that go! \xe2\x80\xa6 Jude, I must say good-bye! But I wanted you\r\nto go to the cemetery with me. Let our farewell be there\xe2\x80\x94beside the\r\ngraves of those who died to bring home to me the error of my views.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey turned in the direction of the place, and the gate was opened to\r\nthem on application. Sue had been there often, and she knew the way to\r\nthe spot in the dark. They reached it, and stood still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is here\xe2\x80\x94I should like to part,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think me hard because I have acted on conviction. Your generous\r\ndevotion to me is unparalleled, Jude! Your worldly failure, if you have\r\nfailed, is to your credit rather than to your blame. Remember that the\r\nbest and greatest among mankind are those who do themselves no worldly\r\ngood. Every successful man is more or less a selfish man. The devoted\r\nfail\xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x98Charity seeketh not her own.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that chapter we are at one, ever beloved darling, and on it we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\npart friends. Its verses will stand fast when all the rest that you\r\ncall religion has passed away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t discuss it. Good-bye, Jude; my fellow-sinner, and kindest\r\nfriend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, my mistaken wife. Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next afternoon the familiar Christminster fog still hung over all\r\nthings. Sue\xe2\x80\x99s slim shape was only just discernible going towards the\r\nstation.\r\n\r\nJude had no heart to go to his work that day. Neither could he go\r\nanywhere in the direction by which she would be likely to pass. He went\r\nin an opposite one, to a dreary, strange, flat scene, where boughs\r\ndripped, and coughs and consumption lurked, and where he had never been\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue\xe2\x80\x99s gone from me\xe2\x80\x94gone!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured miserably.\r\n\r\nShe in the meantime had left by the train, and reached Alfredston Road,\r\nwhere she entered the steam-tram and was conveyed into the town. It had\r\nbeen her request to Phillotson that he should not meet her. She wished,\r\nshe said, to come to him voluntarily, to his very house and\r\nhearthstone.\r\n\r\nIt was Friday evening, which had been chosen because the schoolmaster\r\nwas disengaged at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock that day till the Monday morning\r\nfollowing. The little car she hired at the Bear to drive her to\r\nMarygreen set her down at the end of the lane, half a mile from the\r\nvillage, by her desire, and preceded her to the schoolhouse with such\r\nportion of her luggage as she had brought. On its return she\r\nencountered it, and asked the driver if he had found the master\xe2\x80\x99s house\r\nopen. The man informed her that he had, and that her things had been\r\ntaken in by the schoolmaster himself.\r\n\r\nShe could now enter Marygreen without exciting much observation. She\r\ncrossed by the well and under the trees to the pretty new school on the\r\nother side, and lifted the latch of the dwelling without knocking.\r\nPhillotson stood in the middle of the room, awaiting her, as requested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come, Richard,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, looking pale and shaken, and sinking\r\ninto a chair. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot believe\xe2\x80\x94you forgive your\xe2\x80\x94wife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything, darling Susanna,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson.\r\n\r\nShe started at the endearment, though it had been spoken advisedly\r\nwithout fervour. Then she nerved herself again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy children\xe2\x80\x94are dead\xe2\x80\x94and it is right that they should be! I am\r\nglad\xe2\x80\x94almost. They were sin-begotten. They were sacrificed to teach me\r\nhow to live! Their death was the first stage of my purification. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhy they have not died in vain! \xe2\x80\xa6 You will take me back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was so stirred by her pitiful words and tone that he did more than\r\nhe had meant to do. He bent and kissed her cheek.\r\n\r\nSue imperceptibly shrank away, her flesh quivering under the touch of\r\nhis lips.\r\n\r\nPhillotson\xe2\x80\x99s heart sank, for desire was renascent in him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou still\r\nhave an aversion to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, dear\xe2\x80\x94I have been driving through the damp, and I was chilly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, with a hurried smile of apprehension. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen are we going to\r\nhave the marriage? Soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow morning, early, I thought\xe2\x80\x94if you really wish. I am sending\r\nround to the vicar to let him know you are come. I have told him all,\r\nand he highly approves\xe2\x80\x94he says it will bring our lives to a triumphant\r\nand satisfactory issue. But\xe2\x80\x94are you sure of yourself? It is not too\r\nlate to refuse now if\xe2\x80\x94you think you can\xe2\x80\x99t bring yourself to it, you\r\nknow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, I can! I want it done quick. Tell him, tell him at once! My\r\nstrength is tried by the undertaking\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t wait long!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave something to eat and drink then, and go over to your room at Mrs.\r\nEdlin\xe2\x80\x99s. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell the vicar half-past eight to-morrow, before anybody\r\nis about\xe2\x80\x94if that\xe2\x80\x99s not too soon for you? My friend Gillingham is here\r\nto help us in the ceremony. He\xe2\x80\x99s been good enough to come all the way\r\nfrom Shaston at great inconvenience to himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnlike a woman in ordinary, whose eye is so keen for material things,\r\nSue seemed to see nothing of the room they were in, or any detail of\r\nher environment. But on moving across the parlour to put down her muff\r\nshe uttered a little \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d and grew paler than before. Her look was\r\nthat of the condemned criminal who catches sight of his coffin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson.\r\n\r\nThe flap of the bureau chanced to be open, and in placing her muff upon\r\nit her eye had caught a document which lay there. \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94only a\xe2\x80\x94funny\r\nsurprise!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, trying to laugh away her cry as she came back to\r\nthe table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson. \xe2\x80\x9cThe licence\xe2\x80\xa6 It has just come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham now joined them from his room above, and Sue nervously made\r\nherself agreeable to him by talking on whatever she thought likely to\r\ninterest him, except herself, though that interested him most of all.\r\nShe obediently ate some supper, and prepared to leave for her lodging\r\nhard by. Phillotson crossed the green with her, bidding her good-night\r\nat Mrs. Edlin\xe2\x80\x99s door.\r\n\r\nThe old woman accompanied Sue to her temporary quarters, and helped her\r\nto unpack. Among other things she laid out a night-gown tastefully\r\nembroidered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know _that_ was put in!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean\r\nit to be. Here is a different one.\xe2\x80\x9d She handed a new and absolutely\r\nplain garment, of coarse and unbleached calico.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is the prettiest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Edlin. \xe2\x80\x9cThat one is no better\r\nthan very sackcloth o\xe2\x80\x99 Scripture!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I meant it to be. Give me the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took it, and began rending it with all her might, the tears\r\nresounding through the house like a screech-owl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my dear, dear!\xe2\x80\x94whatever....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is adulterous! It signifies what I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel\xe2\x80\x94I bought it long\r\nago\xe2\x80\x94to please Jude. It must be destroyed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Edlin lifted her hands, and Sue excitedly continued to tear the\r\nlinen into strips, laying the pieces in the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou med ha\xe2\x80\x99 give it to me!\xe2\x80\x9d said the widow. \xe2\x80\x9cIt do make my heart ache\r\nto see such pretty open-work as that a-burned by the flames\xe2\x80\x94not that\r\nornamental night-rails can be much use to a\xe2\x80\x99 ould \xe2\x80\x99ooman like I. My\r\ndays for such be all past and gone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an accursed thing\xe2\x80\x94it reminds me of what I want to forget!\xe2\x80\x9d Sue\r\nrepeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is only fit for the fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, you be too strict! What do ye use such words for, and condemn to\r\nhell your dear little innocent children that\xe2\x80\x99s lost to \xe2\x80\x99ee! Upon my\r\nlife I don\xe2\x80\x99t call that religion!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSue flung her face upon the bed, sobbing. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t! That kills\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d She remained shaken with her grief, and slipped down upon her\r\nknees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell \xe2\x80\x99ee what\xe2\x80\x94you ought not to marry this man again!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nEdlin indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are in love wi\xe2\x80\x99 t\xe2\x80\x99 other still!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes I must\xe2\x80\x94I am his already!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshoo! You be t\xe2\x80\x99 other man\xe2\x80\x99s. If you didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to commit yourselves\r\nto the binding vow again, just at first, \xe2\x80\x99twas all the more credit to\r\nyour consciences, considering your reasons, and you med ha\xe2\x80\x99 lived on,\r\nand made it all right at last. After all, it concerned nobody but your\r\nown two selves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichard says he\xe2\x80\x99ll have me back, and I\xe2\x80\x99m bound to go! If he had\r\nrefused, it might not have been so much my duty to\xe2\x80\x94give up Jude. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe remained with her face in the bed-clothes, and Mrs. Edlin left the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\nPhillotson in the interval had gone back to his friend Gillingham, who\r\nstill sat over the supper-table. They soon rose, and walked out on the\r\ngreen to smoke awhile. A light was burning in Sue\xe2\x80\x99s room, a shadow\r\nmoving now and then across the blind.\r\n\r\nGillingham had evidently been impressed with the indefinable charm of\r\nSue, and after a silence he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell: you\xe2\x80\x99ve all but got her again\r\nat last. She can\xe2\x80\x99t very well go a second time. The pear has dropped\r\ninto your hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! \xe2\x80\xa6 I suppose I am right in taking her at her word. I confess there\r\nseems a touch of selfishness in it. Apart from her being what she is,\r\nof course, a luxury for a fogey like me, it will set me right in the\r\neyes of the clergy and orthodox laity, who have never forgiven me for\r\nletting her go. So I may get back in some degree into my old track.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94if you\xe2\x80\x99ve got any sound reason for marrying her again, do it now\r\nin God\xe2\x80\x99s name! I was always against your opening the cage-door and\r\nletting the bird go in such an obviously suicidal way. You might have\r\nbeen a school inspector by this time, or a reverend, if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nso weak about her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did myself irreparable damage\xe2\x80\x94I know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce you\xe2\x80\x99ve got her housed again, stick to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhillotson was more evasive to-night. He did not care to admit clearly\r\nthat his taking Sue to him again had at bottom nothing to do with\r\nrepentance of letting her go, but was, primarily, a human instinct\r\nflying in the face of custom and profession. He said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I shall do\r\nthat. I know woman better now. Whatever justice there was in releasing\r\nher, there was little logic, for one holding my views on other\r\nsubjects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGillingham looked at him, and wondered whether it would ever happen\r\nthat the reactionary spirit induced by the world\xe2\x80\x99s sneers and his own\r\nphysical wishes would make Phillotson more orthodoxly cruel to her than\r\nhe had erstwhile been informally and perversely kind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI perceive it won\xe2\x80\x99t do to give way to impulse,\xe2\x80\x9d Phillotson resumed,\r\nfeeling more and more every minute the necessity of acting up to his\r\nposition. \xe2\x80\x9cI flew in the face of the Church\xe2\x80\x99s teaching; but I did it\r\nwithout malice prepense. A little judicious severity, perhaps\xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but you must tighten the reins by degrees only. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be too\r\nstrenuous at first. She\xe2\x80\x99ll come to any terms in time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe caution was unnecessary, though Phillotson did not say so. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nremember what my vicar at Shaston said, when I left after the row that\r\nwas made about my agreeing to her elopement. \xe2\x80\x98The only thing you can do\r\nto retrieve your position and hers is to admit your error in not\r\nrestraining her with a wise and strong hand, and to get her back again\r\nif she\xe2\x80\x99ll come, and be firm in the future.\xe2\x80\x99 But I was so headstrong at\r\nthat time that I paid no heed. And that after the divorce she should\r\nhave thought of doing so I did not dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gate of Mrs. Edlin\xe2\x80\x99s cottage clicked, and somebody began crossing\r\nin the direction of the school. Phillotson said \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is that Mr. Phillotson,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Edlin. \xe2\x80\x9cI was going over to see\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee. In my time we took it more\r\ncareless; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that we was any the worse for it! When I and\r\nmy poor man were jined in it we kept up the junketing all the week, and\r\ndrunk the parish dry, and had to borrow half a crown to begin\r\nhousekeeping!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mrs. Edlin had gone back to her cottage Phillotson spoke moodily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I ought to do it\xe2\x80\x94at any rate quite so rapidly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she is really compelling herself to this against her\r\ninstincts\xe2\x80\x94merely from this new sense of duty or religion\xe2\x80\x94I ought\r\nperhaps to let her wait a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve got so far you ought not to back out of it. The fact of her having lived with\r\nFawley goes for nothing. At the time she left me for him she thought\r\nshe was quite within her right. Now she thinks otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next morning came, and the self-sacrifice of the woman on the altar\r\nof what she was pleased to call her principles was acquiesced in by\r\nthese two friends, each from his own point of view. Phillotson went\r\nacross to the Widow Edlin\xe2\x80\x99s to fetch Sue a few minutes after eight\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock. The fog of the previous day or two on the low-lands had\r\ntravelled up here by now, and the trees on the green caught armfuls,\r\nand turned them into showers of big drops. The bride was waiting,\r\nready; bonnet and all on. She had never in her life looked so much like\r\nthe lily her name connoted as she did in that pallid morning light.\r\nChastened, world-weary, remorseful, the strain on her nerves had preyed\r\nupon her flesh and bones, and she appeared smaller in outline than she\r\nhad formerly done, though Sue had not been a large woman in her days of\r\nrudest health.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrompt,\xe2\x80\x9d said the schoolmaster, magnanimously taking her hand. But he\r\nchecked his impulse to kiss her, remembering her start of yesterday,\r\nwhich unpleasantly lingered in his mind.\r\n\r\nGillingham joined them, and they left the house, Widow Edlin continuing\r\nsteadfast in her refusal to assist in the ceremony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the church?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sue. She had not lived there for any length\r\nof time since the old church was pulled down, and in her preoccupation\r\nforgot the new one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phillotson; and presently the tower loomed large and\r\nsolemn in the fog. In\r\nthe nave of the edifice were two or three villagers, and when the\r\nclergyman came to the words, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat God hath joined,\xe2\x80\x9d a woman\xe2\x80\x99s voice\r\nfrom among these was heard to utter audibly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod hath jined indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was like a re-enactment by the ghosts of their former selves of the\r\nsimilar scene which had taken place at Melchester years before. When\r\nthe books were signed the vicar congratulated the husband and wife on\r\nhaving performed a noble, and righteous, and mutually forgiving act.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll\xe2\x80\x99s well that ends well,\xe2\x80\x9d he said smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cMay you long be happy\r\ntogether, after thus having been \xe2\x80\x98saved as by fire.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey came down the nearly empty building, and crossed to the\r\nschoolhouse. Gillingham wanted to get home that night, and left early.\r\nHe, too, congratulated the couple. \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in parting from\r\nPhillotson, who walked out a little way, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be able to tell the\r\npeople in your native place a good round tale; and they\xe2\x80\x99ll all say\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well done,\xe2\x80\x99 depend on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the schoolmaster got back Sue was making a pretence of doing some\r\nhousewifery as if she lived there. But she seemed timid at his\r\napproach, and compunction wrought on him at sight of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, my dear, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t expect to intrude upon your personal\r\nprivacy any more than I did before,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is for our\r\ngood socially to do this, and that\xe2\x80\x99s its justification, if it was not\r\nmy reason.\xe2\x80\x9d Sue brightened a little.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe place was the door of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s lodging in the out-skirts of\r\nChristminster\xe2\x80\x94far from the precincts of St. Silas\xe2\x80\x99 where he had\r\nformerly lived, which saddened him to sickness. The rain was coming\r\ndown. A woman in shabby black stood on the doorstep talking to Jude,\r\nwho held the door in his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am lonely, destitute, and houseless\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what I am! Father has\r\nturned me out of doors after borrowing every penny I\xe2\x80\x99d got, to put it\r\ninto his business, and then accusing me of laziness when I was only\r\nwaiting for a situation. I am at the mercy of the world! If you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake me and help me, Jude, I must go to the workhouse, or to something\r\nworse. Only just now two undergraduates winked at me as I came along.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis hard for a woman to keep virtuous where there\xe2\x80\x99s so many young\r\nmen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman in the rain who spoke thus was Arabella, the evening being\r\nthat of the day after Sue\xe2\x80\x99s remarriage with Phillotson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for you, but I am only in lodgings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude coldly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you turn me away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give you enough to get food and lodging for a few days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but can\xe2\x80\x99t you have the kindness to take me in? Please, Jude, for\r\nold times\xe2\x80\x99 sake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be reminded of those\r\nthings; and if you talk about them I shall not help you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I suppose I must go!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella.
