She had just heard that\r\nthe wedding was to be yesterday: but she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know if it had come\r\noff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to talk of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no: of course you don\xe2\x80\x99t. Only it shows what kind of woman\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t speak of her I say! She\xe2\x80\x99s a fool! And she\xe2\x80\x99s an angel, too, poor\r\ndear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it\xe2\x80\x99s done, he\xe2\x80\x99ll have a chance of getting back to his old position,\r\nby everybody\xe2\x80\x99s account, so Anny says. All his well-wishers will be\r\npleased, including the bishop himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo spare me, Arabella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella was duly installed in the little attic, and at first she did\r\nnot come near Jude at all. She went to and fro about her own business,\r\nwhich, when they met for a moment on the stairs or in the passage, she\r\ninformed him was that of obtaining another place in the occupation she\r\nunderstood best. When Jude suggested London as affording the most\r\nlikely opening in the liquor trade, she shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ntemptations are too many,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAny humble tavern in the country\r\nbefore that for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the Sunday morning following, when he breakfasted later than on\r\nother days, she meekly asked him if she might come in to breakfast with\r\nhim, as she had broken her teapot, and could not replace it\r\nimmediately, the shops being shut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if you like,\xe2\x80\x9d he said indifferently.\r\n\r\nWhile they sat without speaking she suddenly observed: \xe2\x80\x9cYou seem all in\r\na brood, old man. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am all in a brood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is about her, I know. It\xe2\x80\x99s no business of mine, but I could find\r\nout all about the wedding\xe2\x80\x94if it really did take place\xe2\x80\x94if you wanted to\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to go to Alfredston to get a few things I left there. And I\r\ncould see Anny, who\xe2\x80\x99ll be sure to have heard all about it, as she has\r\nfriends at Marygreen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude could not bear to acquiesce in this proposal; but his suspense\r\npitted itself against his discretion, and won in the struggle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\nask about it if you like,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve not heard a sound from there.\r\nIt must have been very private, if\xe2\x80\x94they have married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I haven\xe2\x80\x99t enough cash to take me there and back, or I\r\nshould have gone before. I must wait till I have earned some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I can pay the journey for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said impatiently. And thus his\r\nsuspense as to Sue\xe2\x80\x99s welfare, and the possible marriage, moved him to\r\ndispatch for intelligence the last emissary he would have thought of\r\nchoosing deliberately.\r\n\r\nArabella went, Jude requesting her to be home not later than by the\r\nseven o\xe2\x80\x99clock train. When she had gone he said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I have\r\ncharged her to be back by a particular time! She\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to me\xe2\x80\x94nor\r\nthe other neither!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut having finished work he could not help going to the station to meet\r\nArabella, dragged thither by feverish haste to get the news she might\r\nbring, and know the worst. Arabella had made dimples most successfully\r\nall the way home, and when she stepped out of the railway carriage she\r\nsmiled. He merely said \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d with the very reverse of a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94of course they are!\xe2\x80\x9d he returned. She observed, however, the hard\r\nstrain upon his lip as he spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnny says she has heard from Belinda, her relation out at Marygreen,\r\nthat it was very sad, and curious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean sad? She wanted to marry him again, didn\xe2\x80\x99t she? And he\r\nher!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that was it. She wanted to in one sense, but not in the other.\r\nMrs. Edlin was much upset by it all, and spoke out her mind at\r\nPhillotson. But Sue was that excited about it that she burnt her best\r\nembroidery that she\xe2\x80\x99d worn with you, to blot you out entirely. Well\xe2\x80\x94if\r\na woman feels like it, she ought to do it. I commend her for it, though\r\nothers don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d Arabella sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cShe felt he was her only husband, and\r\nthat she belonged to nobody else in the sight of God A\xe2\x80\x99mighty while he\r\nlived. Perhaps another woman feels the same about herself, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nArabella sighed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any cant!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t cant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel exactly the same as she!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed that issue by remarking abruptly: \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94now I know all I\r\nwanted to know. Many thanks for your information. I am not going back\r\nto my lodgings just yet.\xe2\x80\x9d And he left her straightway.\r\n\r\nIn his misery and depression Jude walked to well-nigh every spot in the\r\ncity that he had visited with Sue; thence he did not know whither, and\r\nthen thought of going home to his usual evening meal. But having all\r\nthe vices of his virtues, and some to spare, he turned into a public\r\nhouse, for the first time during many months. Among the possible\r\nconsequences of her marriage Sue had not dwelt on this.\r\n\r\nArabella, meanwhile, had gone back. The evening passed, and Jude did\r\nnot return. At half-past nine Arabella herself went out, first\r\nproceeding to an outlying district near the river where her father\r\nlived, and had opened a small and precarious pork-shop lately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him, \xe2\x80\x9cfor all your rowing me that night, I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ncalled in, for I have something to tell you. I think I shall get\r\nmarried and settled again. Only you must help me: and you can do no\r\nless, after what I\xe2\x80\x99ve stood \xe2\x80\x99ee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything to get thee off my hands!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I am now going to look for my young man. He\xe2\x80\x99s on the loose\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, and I must get him home. All I want you to do to-night is\r\nnot to fasten the door, in case I should want to sleep here, and should\r\nbe late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you\xe2\x80\x99d soon get tired of giving yourself airs and keeping\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t do the door. That\xe2\x80\x99s all I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe then sallied out again, and first hastening back to Jude\xe2\x80\x99s to make\r\nsure that he had not returned, began her search for him. A shrewd guess\r\nas to his probable course took her straight to the tavern which Jude\r\nhad formerly frequented, and where she had been barmaid for a brief\r\nterm. She had no sooner opened the door of the \xe2\x80\x9cPrivate Bar\xe2\x80\x9d than her\r\neyes fell upon him\xe2\x80\x94sitting in the shade at the back of the compartment,\r\nwith his eyes fixed on the floor in a blank stare. He was drinking\r\nnothing stronger than ale just then. He did not observe her, and she\r\nentered and sat beside him.\r\n\r\nJude looked up, and said without surprise: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve come to have\r\nsomething, Arabella? \xe2\x80\xa6 I\xe2\x80\x99m trying to forget her: that\xe2\x80\x99s all! But I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t; and I am going home.\xe2\x80\x9d She saw that he was a little way on in\r\nliquor, but only a little as yet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come entirely to look for you, dear boy. You are not well. Now\r\nyou must have something better than that.\xe2\x80\x9d Arabella held up her finger\r\nto the barmaid. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shall have a liqueur\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s better fit for a man\r\nof education than beer. You shall have maraschino, or cura\xc3\xa7ao dry or\r\nsweet, or cherry brandy. I\xe2\x80\x99ll treat you, poor chap!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care which! Say cherry brandy\xe2\x80\xa6 Sue has served me badly, very\r\nbadly. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it of Sue! I stuck to her, and she ought to have\r\nstuck to me. I\xe2\x80\x99d have sold my soul for her sake, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t risk\r\nhers a jot for me. To save her own soul she lets mine go damn! \xe2\x80\xa6 But it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t her fault, poor little girl\xe2\x80\x94I am sure it isn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHow Arabella had obtained money did not appear, but she ordered a\r\nliqueur each, and paid for them. When they had drunk these Arabella\r\nsuggested another; and Jude had the pleasure of being, as it were,\r\npersonally conducted through the varieties of spirituous delectation by\r\none who knew the landmarks well. Arabella kept very considerably in the\r\nrear of Jude; but though she only sipped where he drank, she took as\r\nmuch as she could safely take without losing her head\xe2\x80\x94which was not a\r\nlittle, as the crimson upon her countenance showed.\r\n\r\nHer tone towards him to-night was uniformly soothing and cajoling; and\r\nwhenever he said \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care what happens to me,\xe2\x80\x9d a thing he did\r\ncontinually, she replied, \xe2\x80\x9cBut I do very much!\xe2\x80\x9d The closing hour came,\r\nand they were compelled to turn out; whereupon Arabella put her arm\r\nround his waist, and guided his unsteady footsteps.\r\n\r\nWhen they were in the streets she said: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what our landlord\r\nwill say to my bringing you home in this state. I expect we are\r\nfastened out, so that he\xe2\x80\x99ll have to come down and let us in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the worst of not having a home of your own. I tell you, Jude,\r\nwhat we had best do. Come round to my father\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94I made it up with him a\r\nbit to-day. I can let you in, and nobody will see you at all; and by\r\nto-morrow morning you\xe2\x80\x99ll be all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything\xe2\x80\x94anywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil does it matter to\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went along together, like any other fuddling couple, her arm still\r\nround his waist, and his, at last, round hers; though with no amatory\r\nintent; but merely because he was weary, unstable, and in need of\r\nsupport.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis\xe2\x80\x94is th\xe2\x80\x99 Martyrs\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94burning-place,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered as they dragged\r\nacross a broad street. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember\xe2\x80\x94in old Fuller\xe2\x80\x99s _Holy State_\xe2\x80\x94and I\r\nam reminded of it\xe2\x80\x94by our passing by here\xe2\x80\x94old Fuller in his _Holy State_\r\nsays, that at the burning of Ridley, Doctor Smith\xe2\x80\x94preached sermon, and\r\ntook as his text _\xe2\x80\x98Though I give my body to be burned, and have not\r\ncharity, it profiteth me nothing.\xe2\x80\x99_\xe2\x80\x94Often think of it as I pass here.\r\nRidley was a\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Exactly. Very thoughtful of you, deary, even though it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmuch to do with our present business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes it has! I\xe2\x80\x99m giving my body to be burned! But\xe2\x80\x94ah you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand!\xe2\x80\x94it wants Sue to understand such things! And I was her\r\nseducer\xe2\x80\x94poor little girl! And she\xe2\x80\x99s gone\xe2\x80\x94and I don\xe2\x80\x99t care about myself!\r\nDo what you like with me! \xe2\x80\xa6 And yet she did it for conscience\xe2\x80\x99 sake,\r\npoor little Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang her!\xe2\x80\x94I mean, I think she was right,\xe2\x80\x9d hiccuped Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve my\r\nfeelings too, like her; and I feel I belong to you in Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s eye, and\r\nto nobody else, till death us do part! It is\xe2\x80\x94hic\xe2\x80\x94never too late\xe2\x80\x94hic to\r\nmend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had reached her father\xe2\x80\x99s house, and she softly unfastened the\r\ndoor, groping about for a light within.\r\n\r\nThe circumstances were not altogether unlike those of their entry into\r\nthe cottage at Cresscombe, such a long time before. Nor were perhaps\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s motives. But Jude did not think of that, though she did.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t find the matches, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she said when she had fastened up the\r\ndoor. \xe2\x80\x9cBut never mind\xe2\x80\x94this way. As quiet as you can, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is as dark as pitch,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me your hand, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll lead you. That\xe2\x80\x99s it. He\xe2\x80\x99d make a row, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe pulled off his boots. \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9ctake hold of me\xe2\x80\x94never\r\nmind your weight. Now\xe2\x80\x94first stair, second stair\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94are we out in our old house by Marygreen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the stupefied\r\nJude. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t been inside it for years till now! Hey? And where are\r\nmy books? That\xe2\x80\x99s what I want to know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are at my house, dear, where there\xe2\x80\x99s nobody to spy out how ill you\r\nare. Now\xe2\x80\x94third stair, fourth stair\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s it. Now we shall get on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nArabella was preparing breakfast in the downstairs back room of this\r\nsmall, recently hired tenement of her father\xe2\x80\x99s. She put her head into\r\nthe little pork-shop in front, and told Mr. Donn it was ready. Donn,\r\nendeavouring to look like a master pork-butcher, in a greasy blue\r\nblouse, and with a strap round his waist from which a steel dangled,\r\ncame in promptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must mind the shop this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d he said casually. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve to go\r\nand get some inwards and half a pig from Lumsdon, and to call\r\nelsewhere. If you live here you must put your shoulder to the wheel, at\r\nleast till I get the business started!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, for to-day I can\xe2\x80\x99t say.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked deedily into his face. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngot a prize upstairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh? What\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA husband\xe2\x80\x94almost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It\xe2\x80\x99s Jude. He\xe2\x80\x99s come back to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour old original one? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m damned!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I always did like him, that I will say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how does he come to be up there?\xe2\x80\x9d said Donn, humour-struck, and\r\nnodding to the ceiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask inconvenient questions, Father. What we\xe2\x80\x99ve to do is to keep\r\nhim here till he and I are\xe2\x80\x94as we were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\xa6 Well it is the rummest thing I ever heard of\xe2\x80\x94marrying an old\r\nhusband again, and so much new blood in the world! He\xe2\x80\x99s no catch, to my\r\nthinking. I\xe2\x80\x99d have had a new one while I was about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t rum for a woman to want her old husband back for\r\nrespectability, though for a man to want his old wife back\xe2\x80\x94well,\r\nperhaps it is funny, rather!\xe2\x80\x9d And Arabella was suddenly seized with a\r\nfit of loud laughter, in which her father joined more moderately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe civil to him, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do the rest,\xe2\x80\x9d she said when she had\r\nrecovered seriousness. \xe2\x80\x9cHe told me this morning that his head ached fit\r\nto burst, and he hardly seemed to know where he was. And no wonder,\r\nconsidering how he mixed his drink last night. We must keep him jolly\r\nand cheerful here for a day or two, and not let him go back to his\r\nlodging. Whatever you advance I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay back to you again. But I must go\r\nup and see how he is now, poor deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella ascended the stairs, softly opened the door of the first\r\nbedroom, and peeped in. Finding that her shorn Samson was asleep she\r\nentered to the bedside and stood regarding him. The fevered flush on\r\nhis face from the debauch of the previous evening lessened the\r\nfragility of his ordinary appearance, and his long lashes, dark brows,\r\nand curly back hair and beard against the white pillow completed the\r\nphysiognomy of one whom Arabella, as a woman of rank passions, still\r\nfelt it worth while to recapture, highly important to recapture as a\r\nwoman straitened both in means and in reputation. Her ardent gaze\r\nseemed to affect him; his quick breathing became suspended, and he\r\nopened his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you now, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is I\xe2\x80\x94Arabella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x94where\xe2\x80\x94oh yes, I remember! You gave me shelter\xe2\x80\xa6 I am\r\nstranded\xe2\x80\x94ill\xe2\x80\x94demoralized\xe2\x80\x94damn bad! That\xe2\x80\x99s what I am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen do stay here. There\xe2\x80\x99s nobody in the house but father and me, and\r\nyou can rest till you are thoroughly well. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell them at the\r\nstoneworks that you are knocked up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what they are thinking at the lodgings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go round and explain. Perhaps you had better let me pay up, or\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll think we\xe2\x80\x99ve run away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find enough money in my pocket there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite indifferent, and shutting his eyes because he could not bear the\r\ndaylight in his throbbing eye-balls, Jude seemed to doze again.\r\nArabella took his purse, softly left the room, and putting on her\r\noutdoor things went off to the lodgings she and he had quitted the\r\nevening before.\r\n\r\nScarcely half an hour had elapsed ere she reappeared round the corner,\r\nwalking beside a lad wheeling a truck on which were piled all Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhousehold possessions, and also the few of Arabella\xe2\x80\x99s things which she\r\nhad taken to the lodging for her short sojourn there. Jude was in such\r\nphysical pain from his unfortunate break-down of the previous night,\r\nand in such mental pain from the loss of Sue and from having yielded in\r\nhis half-somnolent state to Arabella, that when he saw his few chattels\r\nunpacked and standing before his eyes in this strange bedroom,\r\nintermixed with woman\xe2\x80\x99s apparel, he scarcely considered how they had\r\ncome there, or what their coming signalized.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella to her father downstairs, \xe2\x80\x9cwe must keep plenty of\r\ngood liquor going in the house these next few days. I know his nature,\r\nand if he once gets into that fearfully low state that he does get into\r\nsometimes, he\xe2\x80\x99ll never do the honourable thing by me in this world, and\r\nI shall be left in the lurch. He must be kept cheerful. He has a little\r\nmoney in the savings bank, and he has given me his purse to pay for\r\nanything necessary. Well, that will be the licence; for I must have\r\nthat ready at hand, to catch him the moment he\xe2\x80\x99s in the humour. You\r\nmust pay for the liquor. A few friends, and a quiet convivial party\r\nwould be the thing, if we could get it up. It would advertise the shop,\r\nand help me too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat can be got up easy enough by anybody who\xe2\x80\x99ll afford victuals and\r\ndrink\xe2\x80\xa6 Well yes\xe2\x80\x94it would advertise the shop\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThree days later, when Jude had recovered somewhat from the fearful\r\nthrobbing of his eyes and brain, but was still considerably confused in\r\nhis mind by what had been supplied to him by Arabella during the\r\ninterval\xe2\x80\x94to keep him, jolly, as she expressed it\xe2\x80\x94the quiet convivial\r\ngathering, suggested by her, to wind Jude up to the striking point,\r\ntook place.\r\n\r\nDonn had only just opened his miserable little pork and sausage shop,\r\nwhich had as yet scarce any customers; nevertheless that party\r\nadvertised it well, and the Donns acquired a real notoriety among a\r\ncertain class in Christminster who knew not the colleges, nor their\r\nworks, nor their ways. Jude was asked if he could suggest any guest in\r\naddition to those named by Arabella and her father, and in a saturnine\r\nhumour of perfect recklessness mentioned Uncle Joe, and Stagg, and the\r\ndecayed auctioneer, and others whom he remembered as having been\r\nfrequenters of the well-known tavern during his bout therein years\r\nbefore. He also suggested Freckles and Bower o\xe2\x80\x99 Bliss. Arabella took\r\nhim at his word so far as the men went, but drew the line at the\r\nladies.\r\n\r\nAnother man they knew, Tinker Taylor, though he lived in the same\r\nstreet, was not invited; but as he went homeward from a late job on the\r\nevening of the party, he had occasion to call at the shop for trotters.\r\nThere were none in, but he was promised some the next morning. While\r\nmaking his inquiry Taylor glanced into the back room, and saw the\r\nguests sitting round, card-playing, and drinking, and otherwise\r\nenjoying themselves at Donn\xe2\x80\x99s expense. He went home to bed, and on his\r\nway out next morning wondered how the party went off. He thought it\r\nhardly worth while to call at the shop for his provisions at that hour,\r\nDonn and his daughter being probably not up, if they caroused late the\r\nnight before. However, he found in passing that the door was open, and\r\nhe could hear voices within, though the shutters of the meat-stall were\r\nnot down. He went and tapped at the sitting-room door, and opened it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94to be sure!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, astonished.\r\n\r\nHosts and guests were sitting card-playing, smoking, and talking,\r\nprecisely as he had left them eleven hours earlier; the gas was burning\r\nand the curtains drawn, though it had been broad daylight for two hours\r\nout of doors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Arabella, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cHere we are, just the same. We ought\r\nto be ashamed of ourselves, oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t we? But it is a sort of\r\nhousewarming, you see; and our friends are in no hurry. Come in, Mr.\r\nTaylor, and sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tinker, or rather reduced ironmonger, was nothing loath, and\r\nentered and took a seat. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall lose a quarter, but never mind,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, really, I could hardly believe my eyes when I looked in!\r\nIt seemed as if I was flung back again into last night, all of a\r\nsudden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are. Pour out for Mr. Taylor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe now perceived that she was sitting beside Jude, her arm being round\r\nhis waist. Jude, like the rest of the company, bore on his face the\r\nsigns of how deeply he had been indulging.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99ve been waiting for certain legal hours to arrive, to tell\r\nthe truth,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued bashfully, and making her spirituous crimson\r\nlook as much like a maiden blush as possible. \xe2\x80\x9cJude and I have decided\r\nto make up matters between us by tying the knot again, as we find we\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t do without one another after all. So, as a bright notion, we\r\nagreed to sit on till it was late enough, and go and do it off-hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude seemed to pay no great heed to what she was announcing, or indeed\r\nto anything whatever. The entrance of Taylor infused fresh spirit into\r\nthe company, and they remained sitting, till Arabella whispered to her\r\nfather: \xe2\x80\x9cNow we may as well go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the parson don\xe2\x80\x99t know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I told him last night that we might come between eight and nine,\r\nas there were reasons of decency for doing it as early and quiet as\r\npossible; on account of it being our second marriage, which might make\r\npeople curious to look on if they knew. He highly approved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh very well, I\xe2\x80\x99m ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father, getting up and shaking\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, old darling,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cCome along, as you promised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did I promise anything?\xe2\x80\x9d asked he, whom she had made so tipsy by\r\nher special knowledge of that line of business as almost to have made\r\nhim sober again\xe2\x80\x94or to seem so to those who did not know him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella, affecting dismay. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve promised to marry me\r\nseveral times as we\xe2\x80\x99ve sat here to-night. These gentlemen have heard\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude doggedly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s only one woman\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nI won\xe2\x80\x99t mention her in this Capharnaum!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella looked towards her father. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Fawley be honourable,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Donn. \xe2\x80\x9cYou and my daughter have been living here together these\r\nthree or four days, quite on the understanding that you were going to\r\nmarry her. Of course I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have had such goings on in my house if\r\nI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t understood that. As a point of honour you must do it now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say anything against my honour!\xe2\x80\x9d enjoined Jude hotly, standing\r\nup. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d marry the W\xe2\x80\x93\xe2\x80\x93\xe2\x80\x93\xe2\x80\x93 of Babylon rather than do anything\r\ndishonourable! No reflection on you, my dear. It is a mere rhetorical\r\nfigure\xe2\x80\x94what they call in the books, hyperbole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep your figures for your debts to friends who shelter you,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDonn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am bound in honour to marry her\xe2\x80\x94as I suppose I am\xe2\x80\x94though how I\r\ncame to be here with her I know no more than a dead man\xe2\x80\x94marry her I\r\nwill, so help me God! I have never behaved dishonourably to a woman or\r\nto any living thing. I am not a man who wants to save himself at the\r\nexpense of the weaker among us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94never mind him, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, putting her cheek against\r\nJude\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cCome up and wash your face, and just put yourself tidy, and\r\noff we\xe2\x80\x99ll go. Make it up with Father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey shook hands. Jude went upstairs with her, and soon came down\r\nlooking tidy and calm. Arabella, too, had hastily arranged herself, and\r\naccompanied by Donn away they went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to the guests at parting. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve told the little\r\nmaid to get the breakfast while we are gone; and when we come back\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll all have some. A good strong cup of tea will set everybody right\r\nfor going home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Arabella, Jude, and Donn had disappeared on their matrimonial\r\nerrand the assembled guests yawned themselves wider awake, and\r\ndiscussed the situation with great interest. Tinker Taylor, being the\r\nmost sober, reasoned the most lucidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to speak against friends,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it do seem a\r\nrare curiosity for a couple to marry over again! If they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\non the first time when their minds were limp, they won\xe2\x80\x99t the second, by\r\nmy reckoning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he\xe2\x80\x99ll do it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been put upon his honour by the woman, so he med.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d hardly do it straight off like this. He\xe2\x80\x99s got no licence nor\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s got that, bless you. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you hear her say so to her father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tinker Taylor, relighting his pipe at the gas-jet. \xe2\x80\x9cTake\r\nher all together, limb by limb, she\xe2\x80\x99s not such a bad-looking\r\npiece\xe2\x80\x94particular by candlelight. To be sure, halfpence that have been\r\nin circulation can\xe2\x80\x99t be expected to look like new ones from the mint.\r\nBut for a woman that\xe2\x80\x99s been knocking about the four hemispheres for\r\nsome time, she\xe2\x80\x99s passable enough. A little bit thick in the flitch\r\nperhaps: but I like a woman that a puff o\xe2\x80\x99 wind won\xe2\x80\x99t blow down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir eyes followed the movements of the little girl as she spread the\r\nbreakfast-cloth on the table they had been using, without wiping up the\r\nslops of the liquor. The curtains were undrawn, and the expression of\r\nthe house made to look like morning. Some of the guests, however, fell\r\nasleep in their chairs. One or two went to the door, and gazed along\r\nthe street more than once. Tinker Taylor was the chief of these, and\r\nafter a time he came in with a leer on his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Gad, they are coming! I think the deed\xe2\x80\x99s done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Joe, following him in. \xe2\x80\x9cTake my word, he turned rusty\r\nat the last minute. They are walking in a very unusual way; and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe meaning of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey waited in silence till the wedding-party could be heard entering\r\nthe house. First into the room came Arabella boisterously; and her face\r\nwas enough to show that her strategy had succeeded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Fawley, I presume?\xe2\x80\x9d said Tinker Taylor with mock courtesy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Mrs. Fawley again,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Arabella blandly, pulling off\r\nher glove and holding out her left hand. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the padlock, see\xe2\x80\xa6\r\nWell, he was a very nice, gentlemanly man indeed. I mean the clergyman.\r\nHe said to me as gentle as a babe when all was done: \xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Fawley, I\r\ncongratulate you heartily,\xe2\x80\x99 he says. \xe2\x80\x98For having heard your history,\r\nand that of your husband, I think you have both done the right and\r\nproper thing. And for your past errors as a wife, and his as a husband,\r\nI think you ought now to be forgiven by the world, as you have forgiven\r\neach other,\xe2\x80\x99 says he. Yes; he was a very nice, gentlemanly man. \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nChurch don\xe2\x80\x99t recognize divorce in her dogma, strictly speaking,\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nsays: \xe2\x80\x98and bear in mind the words of the service in your goings out and\r\nyour comings in: What God hath joined together let no man put asunder.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nYes; he was a very nice, gentlemanly man\xe2\x80\xa6 But, Jude, my dear, you were\r\nenough to make a cat laugh! You walked that straight, and held yourself\r\nthat steady, that one would have thought you were going \xe2\x80\x99prentice to a\r\njudge; though I knew you were seeing double all the time, from the way\r\nyou fumbled with my finger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said I\xe2\x80\x99d do anything to\xe2\x80\x94save a woman\xe2\x80\x99s honour,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Jude. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve done it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, old deary, come along and have some breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want\xe2\x80\x94some\xe2\x80\x94more whisky,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jude stolidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, dear. Not now! There\xe2\x80\x99s no more left. The tea will take the\r\nmuddle out of our heads, and we shall be as fresh as larks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\xe2\x80\x94married you. She said I ought to marry you again, and\r\nI have straightway. It is true religion! Ha\xe2\x80\x94ha\xe2\x80\x94ha!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nMichaelmas came and passed, and Jude and his wife, who had lived but a\r\nshort time in her father\xe2\x80\x99s house after their remarriage, were in\r\nlodgings on the top floor of a dwelling nearer to the centre of the\r\ncity.\r\n\r\nHe had done a few days\xe2\x80\x99 work during the two or three months since the\r\nevent, but his health had been indifferent, and it was now precarious.\r\nHe was sitting in an arm-chair before the fire, and coughed a good\r\ndeal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a bargain for my trouble in marrying thee over again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nArabella was saying to him. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to keep \xe2\x80\x99ee entirely\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat \xe2\x80\x99twill come to! I shall have to make black-pot and sausages, and\r\nhawk \xe2\x80\x99em about the street, all to support an invalid husband I\xe2\x80\x99d no\r\nbusiness to be saddled with at all. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you keep your health,\r\ndeceiving one like this? You were well enough when the wedding was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, laughing acridly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been thinking of my\r\nfoolish feeling about the pig you and I killed during our first\r\nmarriage. I feel now that the greatest mercy that could be vouchsafed\r\nto me would be that something should serve me as I served that animal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was the sort of discourse that went on between them every day now.\r\nThe landlord of the lodging, who had heard that they were a queer\r\ncouple, had doubted if they were married at all, especially as he had\r\nseen Arabella kiss Jude one evening when she had taken a little\r\ncordial; and he was about to give them notice to quit, till by chance\r\noverhearing her one night haranguing Jude in rattling terms, and\r\nultimately flinging a shoe at his head, he recognized the note of\r\ngenuine wedlock; and concluding that they must be respectable, said no\r\nmore.\r\n\r\nJude did not get any better, and one day he requested Arabella, with\r\nconsiderable hesitation, to execute a commission for him. She asked him\r\nindifferently what it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo write to Sue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the name\xe2\x80\x94do you want me to write to her for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo ask how she is, and if she\xe2\x80\x99ll come to see me, because I\xe2\x80\x99m ill, and\r\nshould like to see her\xe2\x80\x94once again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is like you to insult a lawful wife by asking such a thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is just in order not to insult you that I ask you to do it. You\r\nknow I love Sue. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to mince the matter\xe2\x80\x94there stands the\r\nfact: I love her. I could find a dozen ways of sending a letter to her\r\nwithout your knowledge. But I wish to be quite above-board with you,\r\nand with her husband. A message through you asking her to come is at\r\nleast free from any odour of intrigue. If she retains any of her old\r\nnature at all, she\xe2\x80\x99ll come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve no respect for marriage whatever, or its rights and duties!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _does_ it matter what my opinions are\xe2\x80\x94a wretch like me! Can it\r\nmatter to anybody in the world who comes to see me for half an\r\nhour\xe2\x80\x94here with one foot in the grave! \xe2\x80\xa6 Come, please write, Arabella!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe pleaded. \xe2\x80\x9cRepay my candour by a little generosity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think _not_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot just once?\xe2\x80\x94Oh do!\xe2\x80\x9d He felt that his physical weakness had taken\r\naway all his dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want _her_ to know how you are for? She don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee. She\xe2\x80\x99s the rat that forsook the sinking ship!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I stuck to un\xe2\x80\x94the more fool I! Have that strumpet in the house\r\nindeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlmost as soon as the words were spoken Jude sprang from the chair, and\r\nbefore Arabella knew where she was he had her on her back upon a little\r\ncouch which stood there, he kneeling above her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay another word of that sort,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll kill you\xe2\x80\x94here\r\nand now! I\xe2\x80\x99ve everything to gain by it\xe2\x80\x94my own death not being the least\r\npart. So don\xe2\x80\x99t think there\xe2\x80\x99s no meaning in what I say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want me to do?\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromise never to speak of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take your word,\xe2\x80\x9d he said scornfully as he loosened her. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what it\r\nis worth I can\xe2\x80\x99t say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t kill the pig, but you could kill me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94there you have me! No\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t kill you\xe2\x80\x94even in a passion. Taunt\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe then began coughing very much, and she estimated his life with an\r\nappraiser\xe2\x80\x99s eye as he sank back ghastly pale. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll send for her,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nArabella murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cif you\xe2\x80\x99ll agree to my being in the room with you\r\nall the time she\xe2\x80\x99s here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe softer side of his nature, the desire to see Sue, made him unable\r\nto resist the offer even now, provoked as he had been; and he replied\r\nbreathlessly: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I agree. Only send for her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the evening he inquired if she had written.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI wrote a note telling her you were ill, and asking\r\nher to come to-morrow or the day after. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t posted it yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next day Jude wondered if she really did post it, but would not ask\r\nher; and foolish Hope, that lives on a drop and a crumb, made him\r\nrestless with expectation. He knew the times of the possible trains,\r\nand listened on each occasion for sounds of her.\r\n\r\nShe did not come; but Jude would not address Arabella again thereon. He\r\nhoped and expected all the next day; but no Sue appeared; neither was\r\nthere any note of reply. Then Jude decided in the privacy of his mind\r\nthat Arabella had never posted hers, although she had written it. There\r\nwas something in her manner which told it. His physical weakness was\r\nsuch that he shed tears at the disappointment when she was not there to\r\nsee. His suspicions were, in fact, well founded. Arabella, like some\r\nother nurses, thought that your duty towards your invalid was to pacify\r\nhim by any means short of really acting upon his fancies.\r\n\r\nHe never said another word to her about his wish or his conjecture. A\r\nsilent, undiscerned resolve grew up in him, which gave him, if not\r\nstrength, stability and calm. One midday when, after an absence of two\r\nhours, she came into the room, she beheld the chair empty.\r\n\r\nDown she flopped on the bed, and sitting, meditated. \xe2\x80\x9cNow where the\r\ndevil is my man gone to!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nA driving rain from the north-east had been falling with more or less\r\nintermission all the morning, and looking from the window at the\r\ndripping spouts it seemed impossible to believe that any sick man would\r\nhave ventured out to almost certain death. Yet a conviction possessed\r\nArabella that he had gone out, and it became a certainty when she had\r\nsearched the house. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he\xe2\x80\x99s such a fool, let him be!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\r\ndo no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJude was at that moment in a railway train that was drawing near to\r\nAlfredston, oddly swathed, pale as a monumental figure in alabaster,\r\nand much stared at by other passengers. An hour later his thin form, in\r\nthe long great-coat and blanket he had come with, but without an\r\numbrella, could have been seen walking along the five-mile road to\r\nMarygreen. On his face showed the determined purpose that alone\r\nsustained him, but to which has weakness afforded a sorry foundation.\r\nBy the up-hill walk he was quite blown, but he pressed on; and at\r\nhalf-past three o\xe2\x80\x99clock stood by the familiar well at Marygreen. The\r\nrain was keeping everybody indoors; Jude crossed the green to the\r\nchurch without observation, and found the building open. Here he stood,\r\nlooking forth at the school, whence he could hear the usual sing-song\r\ntones of the little voices that had not learnt Creation\xe2\x80\x99s groan.\r\n\r\nHe waited till a small boy came from the school\xe2\x80\x94one evidently allowed\r\nout before hours for some reason or other. Jude held up his hand, and\r\nthe child came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease call at the schoolhouse and ask Mrs. Phillotson if she will be\r\nkind enough to come to the church for a few minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child departed, and Jude heard him knock at the door of the\r\ndwelling. He himself went further into the church. Everything was new,\r\nexcept a few pieces of carving preserved from the wrecked old fabric,\r\nnow fixed against the new walls. He stood by these: they seemed akin to\r\nthe perished people of that place who were his ancestors and Sue\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nA light footstep, which might have been accounted no more than an added\r\ndrip to the rainfall, sounded in the porch, and he looked round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think it was you! He turned and\r\nlooked back, once, at the building which still contained Sue; and then\r\nwent on, knowing that his eyes would light on that scene no more.\r\n\r\nThere are cold spots up and down Wessex in autumn and winter weather;\r\nbut the coldest of all when a north or east wind is blowing is the\r\ncrest of the down by the Brown House, where the road to Alfredston\r\ncrosses the old Ridgeway. Here the first winter sleets and snows fall\r\nand lie, and here the spring frost lingers last unthawed. Here in the\r\nteeth of the north-east wind and rain Jude now pursued his way, wet\r\nthrough, the necessary slowness of his walk from lack of his former\r\nstrength being insufficent to maintain his heat. He came to the\r\nmilestone, and, raining as it was, spread his blanket and lay down\r\nthere to rest. Before moving on he went and felt at the back of the\r\nstone for his own carving. It was still there; but nearly obliterated\r\nby moss. He passed the spot where the gibbet of his ancestor and Sue\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhad stood, and descended the hill.\r\n\r\nIt was dark when he reached Alfredston, where he had a cup of tea, the\r\ndeadly chill that began to creep into his bones being too much for him\r\nto endure fasting. He did\r\nnot reach Christminster till ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the platform stood Arabella. He assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring it to you here in the passage,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nobody\r\nbut me about the house to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe brought him a bottle and a glass, and he drank.\r\n\r\nArabella began shaking with suppressed laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this, my\r\ndear?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, smacking his lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94a drop of wine\xe2\x80\x94and something in it.\xe2\x80\x9d Laughing again she said: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\npoured your own love-philtre into it, that you sold me at the\r\nagricultural show, don\xe2\x80\x99t you re-member?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, I do! Clever woman! But you must be prepared for the\r\nconsequences.\xe2\x80\x9d Putting his arm round her shoulders he kissed her there\r\nand then.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, laughing good-humouredly. \xe2\x80\x9cMy man will\r\nhear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe let him out of the house, and as she went back she said to herself:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! Weak women must provide for a rainy day. And if my poor fellow\r\nupstairs do go off\xe2\x80\x94as I suppose he will soon\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s well to keep chances\r\nopen. And I can\xe2\x80\x99t pick and choose now as I could when I was younger.\r\nAnd one must take the old if one can\xe2\x80\x99t get the young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe last pages to which the chronicler of these lives would ask the\r\nreader\xe2\x80\x99s attention are concerned with the scene in and out of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedroom when leafy summer came round again.\r\n\r\nHis face was now so thin that his old friends would hardly have known\r\nhim. It was afternoon, and Arabella was at the looking-glass curling\r\nher hair, which operation she performed by heating an umbrella-stay in\r\nthe flame of a candle she had lighted, and using it upon the flowing\r\nlock. When she had finished this, practised a dimple, and put on her\r\nthings, she cast her eyes round upon Jude. He seemed to be sleeping,\r\nthough his position was an elevated one, his malady preventing him\r\nlying down.\r\n\r\nArabella, hatted, gloved, and ready, sat down and waited, as if\r\nexpecting some one to come and take her place as nurse.\r\n\r\nCertain sounds from without revealed that the town was in festivity,\r\nthough little of the festival, whatever it might have been, could be\r\nseen here. Bells began to ring, and the notes came into the room\r\nthrough the open window, and travelled round Jude\xe2\x80\x99s head in a hum. They\r\nmade her restless, and at last she said to herself: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy ever doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nFather come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked again at Jude, critically gauged his ebbing life, as she had\r\ndone so many times during the late months, and glancing at his watch,\r\nwhich was hung up by way of timepiece, rose impatiently. Still he\r\nslept, and coming to a resolution she slipped from the room, closed the\r\ndoor noiselessly, and descended the stairs. The house was empty. The\r\nattraction which moved Arabella to go abroad had evidently drawn away\r\nthe other inmates long before.\r\n\r\nIt was a warm, cloudless, enticing day. She shut the front door, and\r\nhastened round into Chief Street, and when near the theatre could hear\r\nthe notes of the organ, a rehearsal for a coming concert being in\r\nprogress. She entered under the archway of Oldgate College, where men\r\nwere putting up awnings round the quadrangle for a ball in the hall\r\nthat evening. People who had come up from the country for the day were\r\npicnicking on the grass, and Arabella walked along the gravel paths and\r\nunder the aged limes. But finding this place rather dull she returned\r\nto the streets, and watched the carriages drawing up for the concert,\r\nnumerous Dons and their wives, and undergraduates with gay female\r\ncompanions, crowding up likewise. When the doors were closed, and the\r\nconcert began, she moved on.\r\n\r\nThe powerful notes of that concert rolled forth through the swinging\r\nyellow blinds of the open windows, over the housetops, and into the\r\nstill air of the lanes. They reached so far as to the room in which\r\nJude lay; and it was about this time that his cough began again and\r\nawakened him.\r\n\r\nAs soon as he could speak he murmured, his eyes still closed: \xe2\x80\x9cA little\r\nwater, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing but the deserted room received his appeal, and he coughed to\r\nexhaustion again\xe2\x80\x94saying still more feebly: \xe2\x80\x9cWater\xe2\x80\x94some\r\nwater\xe2\x80\x94Sue\xe2\x80\x94Arabella!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe room remained still as before. Presently he gasped again:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThroat\xe2\x80\x94water\xe2\x80\x94Sue\xe2\x80\x94darling\xe2\x80\x94drop of water\xe2\x80\x94please\xe2\x80\x94oh please!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo water came, and the organ notes, faint as a bee\xe2\x80\x99s hum, rolled in as\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nWhile he remained, his face changing, shouts and hurrahs came from\r\nsomewhere in the direction of the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94yes! The Remembrance games,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I here. And Sue\r\ndefiled!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hurrahs were repeated, drowning the faint organ notes. Jude\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nchanged more: he whispered slowly, his parched lips scarcely moving:\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cLet the day perish wherein I was born, and the night in which it was\r\nsaid, There is a man-child conceived.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cLet that day be darkness; let not God regard it from above, neither\r\nlet the light shine upon it. Lo, let that night be solitary, let no\r\njoyful voice come therein.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cWhy died I not from the womb? Why did I not give up the ghost when I\r\ncame out of the belly? \xe2\x80\xa6 For now should I have lain still and been\r\nquiet. I should have slept: then had I been at rest!\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cThere the prisoners rest together; they hear not the voice of the\r\noppressor\xe2\x80\xa6 The small and the great are there; and the servant is free\r\nfrom his master. Wherefore is light given to him that is in misery, and\r\nlife unto the bitter in soul?\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Arabella, in her journey to discover what was going on, took\r\na short cut down a narrow street and through an obscure nook into the\r\nquad of Cardinal. It was full of bustle, and brilliant in the sunlight\r\nwith flowers and other preparations for a ball here also. A carpenter\r\nnodded to her, one who had formerly been a fellow-workman of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s. A\r\ncorridor was in course of erection from the entrance to the hall\r\nstaircase, of gay red and buff bunting. Waggon-loads of boxes\r\ncontaining bright plants in full bloom were being placed about, and the\r\ngreat staircase was covered with red cloth. She nodded to one workman\r\nand another, and ascended to the hall on the strength of their\r\nacquaintance, where they were putting down a new floor and decorating\r\nfor the dance.\r\n\r\nThe cathedral bell close at hand was sounding for five o\xe2\x80\x99clock service.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not mind having a spin there with a fellow\xe2\x80\x99s arm round my\r\nwaist,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to one of the men. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Lord, I must be getting home\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s a lot to do. No dancing for me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she reached home she was met at the door by Stagg, and one or two\r\nother of Jude\xe2\x80\x99s fellow stoneworkers. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are just going down to the\r\nriver,\xe2\x80\x9d said the former, \xe2\x80\x9cto see the boat-bumping. But we\xe2\x80\x99ve called\r\nround on our way to ask how your husband is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s sleeping nicely, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right. Well now, can\xe2\x80\x99t you give yourself half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrelaxation, Mrs. Fawley, and come along with us? \xe2\x80\x99Twould do you good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to go,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen the boat-racing, and\r\nI hear it is good fun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow I _wish_ I could!\xe2\x80\x9d She looked longingly down the street. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a\r\nminute, then. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just run up and see how he is now. Father is with\r\nhim, I believe; so I can most likely come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey waited, and she entered. Downstairs the inmates were absent as\r\nbefore, having, in fact, gone in a body to the river where the\r\nprocession of boats was to pass. When she reached the bedroom she found\r\nthat her father had not even now come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy couldn\xe2\x80\x99t he have been here!\xe2\x80\x9d she said impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cHe wants to\r\nsee the boats himself\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what it is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, on looking round to the bed she brightened, for she saw that\r\nJude was apparently sleeping, though he was not in the usual\r\nhalf-elevated posture necessitated by his cough. He had slipped down,\r\nand lay flat. A second glance caused her to start, and she went to the\r\nbed. His face was quite white, and gradually becoming rigid. She\r\ntouched his fingers; they were cold, though his body was still warm.\r\nShe listened at his chest. All was still within. The bumping of near\r\nthirty years had ceased.\r\n\r\nAfter her first appalled sense of what had happened, the faint notes of\r\na military or other brass band from the river reached her ears; and in\r\na provoked tone she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cTo think he should die just now! Why\r\ndid he die just now!\xe2\x80\x9d Then meditating another moment or two she went to\r\nthe door, softly closed it as before, and again descended the stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere she is!\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the workmen. \xe2\x80\x9cWe wondered if you were coming\r\nafter all. Come along; we must be quick to get a good place\xe2\x80\xa6 Well, how\r\nis he? Sleeping well still? Of course, we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to drag \xe2\x80\x99ee away\r\nif\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes\xe2\x80\x94sleeping quite sound. He won\xe2\x80\x99t wake yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said hurriedly.\r\n\r\nThey went with the crowd down Cardinal Street, where they presently\r\nreached the bridge, and the gay barges burst upon their view. Thence\r\nthey passed by a narrow slit down to the riverside path\xe2\x80\x94now dusty, hot,\r\nand thronged. Almost as soon as they had arrived the grand procession\r\nof boats began; the oars smacking with a loud kiss on the face of the\r\nstream, as they were lowered from the perpendicular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I say\xe2\x80\x94how jolly! I\xe2\x80\x99m glad I\xe2\x80\x99ve come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\xe2\x80\x94it can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhurt my husband\xe2\x80\x94my being away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the opposite side of the river, on the crowded barges, were gorgeous\r\nnosegays of feminine beauty, fashionably arrayed in green, pink, blue,\r\nand white. The blue flag of the boat club denoted the centre of\r\ninterest, beneath which a band in red uniform gave out the notes she\r\nhad already heard in the death-chamber. Collegians of all sorts, in\r\ncanoes with ladies, watching keenly for \xe2\x80\x9cour\xe2\x80\x9d boat, darted up and down.\r\nWhile she regarded the lively scene somebody touched Arabella in the\r\nribs, and looking round she saw Vilbert.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat philtre is operating, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a leer. \xe2\x80\x9cShame on\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee to wreck a heart so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t talk of love to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? It is a general holiday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not reply. Vilbert\xe2\x80\x99s arm stole round her waist, which act could\r\nbe performed unobserved in the crowd. An arch expression overspread\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s face at the feel of the arm, but she kept her eyes on the\r\nriver as if she did not know of the embrace.\r\n\r\nThe crowd surged, pushing Arabella and her friends sometimes nearly\r\ninto the river, and she would have laughed heartily at the horse-play\r\nthat succeeded, if the imprint on her mind\xe2\x80\x99s eye of a pale, statuesque\r\ncountenance she had lately gazed upon had not sobered her a little.\r\n\r\nThe fun on the water reached the acme of excitement; there were\r\nimmersions, there were shouts: the race was lost and won, the pink and\r\nblue and yellow ladies retired from the barges, and the people who had\r\nwatched began to move.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s been awfully good,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I think I must get\r\nback to my poor man. Father is there, so far as I know; but I had\r\nbetter get back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your hurry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must go\xe2\x80\xa6 Dear, dear, this is awkward!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the narrow gangway where the people ascended from the riverside path\r\nto the bridge the crowd was literally jammed into one hot mass\xe2\x80\x94Arabella\r\nand Vilbert with the rest; and here they remained motionless, Arabella\r\nexclaiming, \xe2\x80\x9cDear, dear!\xe2\x80\x9d more and more impatiently; for it had just\r\noccurred to her mind that if Jude were discovered to have died alone an\r\ninquest might be deemed necessary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a fidget you are, my love,\xe2\x80\x9d said the physician, who, being\r\npressed close against her by the throng, had no need of personal effort\r\nfor contact. \xe2\x80\x9cJust as well have patience: there\xe2\x80\x99s no getting away yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was nearly ten minutes before the wedged multitude moved\r\nsufficiently to let them pass through. As soon as she got up into the\r\nstreet Arabella hastened on, forbidding the physician to accompany her\r\nfurther that day. She did not go straight to her house; but to the\r\nabode of a woman who performed the last necessary offices for the\r\npoorer dead; where she knocked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy husband has just gone, poor soul,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cCan you come and lay\r\nhim out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArabella waited a few minutes; and the two women went along, elbowing\r\ntheir way through the stream of fashionable people pouring out of\r\nCardinal meadow, and being nearly knocked down by the carriages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must call at the sexton\xe2\x80\x99s about the bell, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nis just round here, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I\xe2\x80\x99ll meet you at my door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock that night Jude was lying on the bedstead at his lodging\r\ncovered with a sheet, and straight as an arrow. Through the partly\r\nopened window the joyous throb of a waltz entered from the ball-room at\r\nCardinal.\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo days later, when the sky was equally cloudless, and the air equally\r\nstill, two persons stood beside Jude\xe2\x80\x99s open coffin in the same little\r\nbedroom. On one side was Arabella, on the other the Widow Edlin. They\r\nwere both looking at Jude\xe2\x80\x99s face, the worn old eyelids of Mrs. Edlin\r\nbeing red.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow beautiful he is!\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. He\xe2\x80\x99s a \xe2\x80\x99andsome corpse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella.\r\n\r\nThe window was still open to ventilate the room, and it being about\r\nnoontide the clear air was motionless and quiet without. From a\r\ndistance came voices; and an apparent noise of persons stamping.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the old woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s the Doctors in the theatre, conferring Honorary degrees on\r\nthe Duke of Hamptonshire and a lot more illustrious gents of that sort.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s Remembrance Week, you know. The cheers come from the young men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye; young and strong-lunged! Not like our poor boy here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn occasional word, as from some one making a speech, floated from the\r\nopen windows of the theatre across to this quiet corner, at which there\r\nseemed to be a smile of some sort upon the marble features of Jude;\r\nwhile the old, superseded, Delphin editions of Virgil and Horace, and\r\nthe dog-eared Greek Testament on the neighbouring shelf, and the few\r\nother volumes of the sort that he had not parted with, roughened with\r\nstone-dust where he had been in the habit of catching them up for a few\r\nminutes between his labours, seemed to pale to a sickly cast at the\r\nsounds. The bells struck out joyously; and their reverberations\r\ntravelled round the bed-room.\r\n\r\nArabella\xe2\x80\x99s eyes removed from Jude to Mrs. Edlin. \xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99ye think she will\r\ncome?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not say. She swore not to see him again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is she looking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTired and miserable, poor heart. Years and years older than when you\r\nsaw her last. Quite a staid, worn woman now. \xe2\x80\x99Tis the man\xe2\x80\x94she can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstomach un, even now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Jude had been alive to see her, he would hardly have cared for her\r\nany more, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what we don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\xa6 Didn\xe2\x80\x99t he ever ask you to send for her,\r\nsince he came to see her in that strange way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Quite the contrary. I offered to send, and he said I was not to\r\nlet her know how ill he was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he forgive her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94poor little thing, \xe2\x80\x99tis to be believed she\xe2\x80\x99s found forgiveness\r\nsomewhere! She said she had found peace!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe may swear that on her knees to the holy cross upon her necklace\r\ntill she\xe2\x80\x99s hoarse, but it won\xe2\x80\x99t be true!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arabella. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s never\r\nfound peace since she left his arms, and never will again till she\xe2\x80\x99s as\r\nhe is now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK JUDE THE OBSCURE ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
